# 冲虚至德真经解

*The Explication of the Perfect Scripture of the Utterly Empty and Supreme Virtue*

**Title:** *Explication of the Perfect Scripture of the Utterly Empty and Supreme Virtue*. Composed by Jiang Yu of the Song dynasty. Twenty fascicles. Base text from the *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, *Wondrous Writings* section, *Jade Instructions* category. (An alternate version in the *Complete Library of the Four Treasuries* comprises eight fascicles.)

**Fascicle One of the Explication of the Perfect Scripture of the Utterly Empty and Supreme Virtue**
Submitted by your subject Jiang Yu, Inner College Student of Hangzhou Prefecture, Song dynasty

**Heavenly Signs**
Master Liezi dwelt in the gardens of Zheng for forty years, yet no one knew him.

**Explication:** Master Liezi was a man of antiquity skilled in the Way of the scholar. Subtle, wondrous, and profoundly penetrating, his depths were unfathomable. Thus, though he dwelt in the gardens of Zheng for forty years, none knew him. The gardens and marshes teemed with worthies, yet after forty years without recognition, one perceives the profundity of his reclusion—what the *Book of Changes* calls "withdrawing into concealment." Even the lord of the state and his ministers regarded him as one of the common folk.

**Explication:** Those whose virtue suffices to govern a state and whose Way suffices to nurture the people ought to toil in the search for worthies. Those whose wisdom suffices to lead the multitude ought to be wise enough to know men. That the lord of the state and his ministers dismissed him as one of the common folk—this is why he was so profoundly unknowable.

When the state was impoverished, he prepared to take refuge in Wei.

**Explication:** "The state was impoverished" refers to a year of famine. Those who study Liezi suppose that because he could ride the wind, he must have abstained from the five grains and subsisted on wind and dew. Little do they know that once one enters the human world, one cannot escape the afflictions of human existence. Thus, the text begins with this to instruct later scholars to seek the Way rather than pursue the bizarre to shock the vulgar. The *Sayings Aligned* also states: "Master Liezi’s appearance was that of one in poverty, his countenance that of one suffering hunger."

His disciples said: "Master, you depart without a set time for return. Your disciples dare to make a request—what teaching will you leave us?"

"Have you not heard the words of Master Huqiu Zilin?"

**Explication:** One who embodies emptiness as his substance and the Mean as his Way, who encompasses the myriad things—this is Master Huqiu Zilin, and thus he was Master Liezi’s teacher.

Master Liezi laughed and said: "What did Master Hu say? Nevertheless, he once spoke to Bohun Maoren. I overheard it in passing—let me tell it to you."

**Explication:** Master Liezi’s teacher, Master Hu, exchanged smiles with him, their hearts in perfect accord. As for Bohun Maoren, though advanced in years, his clarity had waned, so Master Hu, out of necessity, spoke to him, and Master Liezi overheard it. As *Zhuangzi* says: "To know and not speak is the way to reach Heaven." Such was Master Liezi’s relationship with Master Hu. It also says: "To know and to speak is the way to reach man." This is why Master Hu spoke thus to Bohun Maoren.

His words were: "There is that which is born yet not born; there is that which transforms yet not transforms. That which is not born can give birth to birth; that which does not transform can give rise to transformation. That which is born cannot but be born; that which transforms cannot but transform."

**Explication:** The spirit of Heaven, the abundance of Earth, the reason sages are sages, the reason things are things—these can be summed up in a single word: birth and transformation. Thus, the *Heavenly Signs* begins by clarifying this.

The forms of bodies are distinct, shifting and passing without cease—this is what is meant by "there is birth and transformation." The Great Simplicity before any sign, the true and constant that does not change—this is what is meant by "not born and not transformed." Confined to what is born, one rushes toward transformation—how can one give birth to birth? Enslaved to what transforms, one ultimately perishes—how can one give rise to transformation? That which is born undergoes transformation—what does that which is not born have to do with transformation? That which transforms ultimately ceases—yet that which does not transform never had arising or ceasing.

The teeming births are all manifestations of the true heart—this is what is meant by "can give birth to birth." The bustling changes are all issuances of the wondrous heart—this is what is meant by "can give rise to transformation." Once there is birth, one cannot but be born. Once there is transformation, one cannot but transform. Though Heaven and Earth are vast, and the sun and moon bright, once confined to the realm of birth and transformation, they are like a mechanism that cannot stop itself, or a turning that cannot cease. The seasons change and the years pass, unending through all time—how much more so for the myriad things?

That which is born cannot but be born, so that which gives birth to birth also cannot but give birth to birth. That which transforms cannot but transform, so that which gives rise to transformation also cannot but give rise to transformation. The giving of birth to birth and the giving rise to transformation have no end. Now birth, now transformation—no one knows their limit. This is how Heaven and Earth contain the myriad things without exhaustion, and how the Way contains Heaven and Earth without end.

Nevertheless, what is called "not born and not transformed" cannot initially be named. Because there is form, we force a name upon it. Thus, beyond that which is born and transformed, there is not another that is not born and not transformed. Rather, the wonder of not being born and not transformed resides within birth and transformation. Therefore, his words say: "There is that which is born yet not born; there is that which transforms yet not transforms." This means that what is born has never truly been born, and what transforms has never truly transformed. Yet that by which there is birth and transformation is neither external nor internal—it simply gives birth to itself and transforms itself.

Observing that he first speaks of "that which is born yet not born" and "that which transforms yet not transforms," he has already exhausted the Way. Of necessity, he clarifies the principle of giving birth to birth and giving rise to transformation, which ultimately must return to self-birth and self-transformation. As for dwelling in the realm of birth and transformation yet following its course, or being within birth and transformation yet not controlled by them—then the birth of the myriad things is the true substance of my heart, and the transformation of the myriad things is the wondrous function of my heart. This is why sages are sages, and the purport of Master Liezi’s teachings.

Thus, there is constant birth and constant transformation. "Constant birth and constant transformation" means there is no time without birth, no time without transformation.

A commentary states: What is called "constant birth and constant transformation" speaks of the supreme principle of creation and transformation across all things, past and present. Observing even a single thing, it is born through transformation and dies through transformation. If we take the temporary aggregation of qi as birth, then it cannot be called "constant birth"; if we take the passing away of form as transformation, then it cannot be called "constant transformation." For in creation and transformation’s relation to the myriad things, as soon as something is born, its transformation does not cease; even after it dies, its transformation continues unabated. Transformation is indeed constant, and birth is likewise uninterrupted. If we were to imagine a single thing within creation and transformation, its birth and transformation occurring in the space of a hair’s breadth, then the principle of birth and transformation would nearly be extinguished.

It has been said that the life and death of things are like the day and night of the sun: the sun’s rising makes day, its setting makes night. How can day be called "birth" or night be called "extinction"? This is what is meant by "no time without birth, no time without transformation." The first chapter of the *Daoist Classic* of Lord Lao speaks of the constant Dao, constant names, constant non-being, and constant being. If discourse on the Dao does not reach constancy, it is insufficient to serve as the gate of all wonders.

It is but yin and yang, but the four seasons. A commentary states: Yin and yang unfold into the four seasons. All that belongs to the realm of the living cannot but follow their revolutions, yet the Dao disperses into yin and yang, and its birth and transformation are merely lodged in the realm of form. The wonder of constant birth and constant transformation is not to be seen in these alone. To say "it is but yin and yang, but the four seasons" is to belittle it.

That which does not birth is the solitary mystery; that which does not transform is the cycle of return. Its boundary cannot be exhausted; the solitary mystery—its Dao cannot be fathomed. A commentary states: Only the solitary can pair and respond, becoming the source to which all movement belongs. This is what the myriad things depend upon and what all transformation awaits. Yet the Dao does not pair with things; things pair with the Dao themselves. What Lord Lao called "seeming to be the ancestor of the myriad things" is what is here called the solitary mystery. "Cycle of return" refers to the endless thread. That its boundary cannot be exhausted and its Dao cannot be fathomed is why there is constant birth and constant transformation.

The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: "The valley spirit does not die; it is called the mysterious female. The gate of the mysterious female is called the root of heaven and earth. It flows continuously, as if existing; its use is never exhausted." A commentary states: The valley is empty yet responsive, responding without storing. Human life arises as the vital qi circulates through the body, entering and exiting through the nose and mouth—this is the image of the valley. What is called the "valley spirit" is the spirit of the valley, signifying attainment of the One, its numinous wonder beyond measure. "The valley spirit does not die" is the Dao of long life and enduring vision. To call it "does not die" is because all living kinds receive their mandate from creation and transformation and are enslaved by yin and yang. In their birth, they cannot but be born; in their death, they cannot but die. Only humans, as the most numinous of the myriad things, though they share with all things a mandate from creation and transformation to be born, once born, possess what creation and transformation cannot cause to die. For we share a single qi with heaven and earth and govern ourselves, guarding our original root. Thus, our mandate lies within us, not with heaven and earth. If one can constantly preserve the valley spirit, then one’s breath reaches the heels, the harmonious qi pervades the body, and there are those who cultivate the body for twelve hundred years without their form ever declining. Though they have life, they enter into a state beyond death and birth, enduring together with the Dao. Therefore, speaking of the valley spirit, it is not called "life" but "does not die."

"Mysterious" is the color of heaven. "Female" is the kind of earth. Flying creatures are called male and female; walking creatures are called female and male. The female is the utmost yin yet possesses the power of ceaseless generation. In the realm beyond form, yin precedes yang, which is why it is metaphorically ascribed to this generative power of things. The ceaseless generation of the valley spirit is called the mysterious female. For the wonder of the valley spirit, when applied to the body, gives birth to the body; when extended to others, gives birth to others. If one can constantly preserve this spirit, how could its generation ever be exhausted? Its essential wonder is such that nothing but the mysterious female could name it.

The "gate" refers to the coming and going, the entry and exit. It speaks of the valley spirit within me, whose coming and going, entry and exit, leave no gap. If one can constantly live without dying, then the waxing and waning, the filling and emptying of the single body are not controlled by creation and transformation, and creation and transformation lie within me. That heaven and earth can be long and enduring is fundamentally due to this Dao. Thus it is said: "The gate of the mysterious female is called the root of heaven and earth." "Flowing continuously, as if existing" describes the substance of the valley spirit. "Its use is never exhausted" describes the Dao of preserving the spirit.

"Flowing continuously" speaks of weakness without cessation. A single breath’s coming and going within the body may be called weak, yet to constantly live without dying is to be without cessation. Only by flowing continuously can it seem to exist yet be neither being nor non-being. "Its use is never exhausted" is what Mencius meant by "nourishing appropriately without harm." As for this qi, it cannot be abandoned and left unused, nor can its use be forced. Not to use it is like the man of Song who did not weed his seedlings; to use it with force is like the man of Song who pulled up his seedlings. Only by using it without force and yet filling all between heaven and earth can the valley spirit not die.

The *Transmission of the Dao* says: "The Great Simplicity is transmitted as the Dao’s words of vast emptiness shining within the scene." This is what Lord Lao called the valley spirit. If the valley spirit is thus, it is how the root of birth and death is extinguished and one constantly lives without dying. Therefore, that which gives birth to things does not itself birth; that which transforms things does not itself transform. It births itself and transforms itself, forms itself and colors itself, knows itself and empowers itself, dissolves itself and rests itself. To call these "birth and transformation," "form and color," "knowledge and power," "dissolution and rest" is incorrect.

A commentary states: Following the statement that "the valley spirit does not die," this is said: if one can preserve the valley spirit, then within the birth and transformation of one’s own body there is the wonder of neither birth nor transformation, no longer requiring the process of birth giving birth and transformation giving transformation. This is self-birth and self-transformation.

From the self-birth and self-transformation of one’s own body, one knows that all things likewise self-birth and self-transform. Tracing the Dao of birth and transformation to its origin, it is fundamentally rooted in spontaneity. To call it "birth giving birth and transformation giving transformation" is to posit a duality of birth and transformation. To regard it as self-birth and self-transformation seems to imply no external dependence, yet this still falls within the realm of discourse. For the Dao of birth and transformation transcends speech and defies designation. To call it "birth and transformation" is already far removed from the Dao; only when one recognizes that even this designation is inadequate does one approach the ultimate truth.

Moreover, the transformations of the myriad things are inexhaustible, yet all are but instances of birth and transformation. When distinguished in detail, they exhibit differences in form, color, knowledge, power, dissolution, and rest. This specificity is articulated to illustrate how the Dao’s abundance suffices for all things, leaving nothing, however small, unaccounted for.

Liezi said: "Of old, the sages followed yin and yang to unify Heaven and Earth." A commentary states: To speak of the birth and transformation of the myriad things is necessarily to trace them to yin and yang. Yet the unfolding of yin and yang is confined within Heaven and Earth. To fathom the creative transformations of Heaven and Earth is to recognize their unity in the sage. Though Heaven and Earth are vast, they do not transcend form and thus cannot be independent of what unifies them. The sage, though but one instance of what is unified, cannot unify Heaven and Earth without relying on their inherent principles. In unifying them, the sage cannot but follow the proper balance of yin and yang.

The Dao of Heaven and Earth originally requires no external dependence and cannot be unified. Yet the transformations of Heaven and Earth, though necessarily rooted in yin and yang, cannot be without dependence, and thus the sage is able to unify them by following their inherent principles. By aligning with yin and yang without contravening the natural tendencies of things, each thing attains its proper Dao and rectifies its nature and destiny.

All things submit to the mandate of creation, yet only through the sage do they each rectify their nature and destiny. If creation merely follows the sage rather than unifying Heaven and Earth, what else could unify them? This is to say, when one is raised, the other follows.

Taking the body as an example: the head and feet correspond to Heaven and Earth, while breath resembles yin and yang. By nurturing with uprightness and without harm, the qi of the body becomes vast and fills the space between Heaven and Earth. If one does not nurture it, the qi runs wild and disturbs the heart. If one nurtures it without uprightness, it becomes an inauspicious excess of life. Thus, unifying the body must be rooted in one’s own upright nurturing, just as unifying Heaven and Earth depends on the sage’s alignment with yin and yang.

Though the creative transformations of Heaven and Earth remain unchanged from ancient times to the present, when the sage governs above, yin and yang are harmonized, sun and moon shine brightly, and governance brings peace and prosperity. Thus, the divine archer caused things to be free from blight, and the harvests ripened. When the Yellow Emperor sought the essence of Heaven and Earth to aid the five grains, was there any other Dao? It was simply a matter of following yin and yang.

Alas, in their birth, all humans are shaped by Heaven and Earth and receive qi from yin and yang. Even the sage differs from others in no fundamental way. Yin and yang may control us, yet the sage can follow them. Heaven and Earth may give us life, yet the sage can unify them. Thus, it is no exaggeration to say that the sage’s husk and chaff suffice to mold a Yao or Shun.

That which has form arises from the formless—then from whence do Heaven and Earth arise? Therefore, it is said: There is the Supreme Simplicity, the Supreme Beginning, the Supreme Origin, and the Supreme Plainness.

A commentary states: The luminous arises from the obscure, the ordered from the formless. All things are the transformations of Heaven and Earth. Yet Heaven and Earth, though capable of generating the myriad things, have not transcended form. Having form, they cannot arise from nothing. If they too have an origin, from whence do they arise? If their origin is unknown, to say that the formed arises from the formless is also unconvincing.

Heaven and Earth are the greatest of the formed, yet their origins are unfathomable. One cannot say they arise from nothing, nor can one perceive their source. Yet by investigating the principle of birth within one’s own body, one realizes that Heaven and Earth are born alongside oneself—what then remains unknowable?

However, the formless of the Supreme Beginning cannot be discussed in words. What can be spoken of is only that which exists yet remains unformed. Thus, from the Supreme Simplicity onward, their sequence is clarified.

What, then, are the Supreme Simplicity, the Supreme Beginning, the Supreme Origin, and the Supreme Plainness? They are but provisional names assigned to the stages by which the Dao emerges from non-being into being, likened to forms and given forced designations.

The Supreme Simplicity is the state before qi is manifest. The Supreme Beginning is the inception of qi. The Supreme Origin is the inception of form. The Supreme Plainness is the inception of substance. When qi, form, and substance are present yet undifferentiated, this is called "primordial chaos." Primordial chaos describes the state in which the myriad things are intermingled and undivided. It cannot be seen, heard, or grasped—hence it is called "Simplicity." Simplicity has no boundaries of form.

A commentary states: "Supreme" is an emphatic expression of greatness. As forms and names descend, they are inadequate to describe its magnitude, so it is called "Supreme." "Simplicity" denotes inexhaustible transformation. Thus, the state before qi is manifest is named the Supreme Simplicity. All things share the Supreme Beginning, after which each has its own beginning. Once there is a beginning but no form, the inception of qi is called the Supreme Beginning.

All things take shape in Heaven and form on Earth. The character for "beginning" (*shi*) is composed of "woman" (*nü*) and "platform" (*tai*), suggesting gestation. Gestation is the inception of form, so the inception of form is called the Supreme Origin. As for "plainness" (*su*), it refers to color before it is dyed. Though it can be called plain, color has not yet manifested, so it is named the Supreme Plainness, denoting the inception of substance.

Though the inception of qi, form, and substance is complete, their sequence may be inferred, yet the Dao remains undivided. As Heaven, as Earth, as sages, as creatures—all share in uncarved simplicity and have not yet departed from it, this is called the Primal Chaos.

Forms upon forms, yet nothing exists—how can one see and trace sound? Sounds without hearing—how can one listen and know? Nevertheless, since the inception of qi, form, and substance is already complete, it has departed far from the Supreme Simplicity. Yet it remains in a state of chaos, not yet separated from simplicity, so though it cannot be named Supreme Simplicity, it is still called simplicity.

Simplicity transforms into the One, the One transforms into the Seven, the Seven transforms into the Nine. The Nine is the ultimate transformation, which then reverts to the One.

Explanation: The One is the generative number of water. The Seven is the consummate number of fire. Once essence and spirit are complete, fire ascends and water descends, Kan and Li intermingle, and the myriad transformations thereby manifest. The Seven further transforms into the Nine, and the transformations of the myriad things are exhausted. Nine is the ultimate number of Heaven; when it reaches its limit, it transforms, thus reverting to the One. The changes of numbers are inexhaustible, like a ring without end—this is the supreme principle of Heaven and Earth’s creative transformation.

Regarding water, we speak of its generative number; regarding fire, its consummate number. Water is the phase of things’ generation; fire, the phase of things’ flourishing. Moreover, the generative number of fire is the secondary Two, for the Two cannot transform.

The *Virtue Classic* of the Old Master says: “The One gives birth to the Two, the Two gives birth to the Three, and the Three gives birth to the myriad things.” This speaks of water as the One and fire as the secondary Two, essence being complete and spirit following. Heaven begins with the One in the North, Earth pairs with the Two in the South, and humans attain position in the Three. Once the Three Powers are complete, the myriad images are distinguished.

The Old Master speaks of the generation of things; Liezi speaks of the transformation of things. Though their numbers differ, all begin with the One and rely on the functions of water and fire. From this, we know that the teachings of the sages emerge and return as one, mutually illuminating the Dao.

The One is the inception of form’s transformation.

Explanation: As a number, the One has departed from the Dao yet is not yet paired with things, so the One is the inception of form’s transformation. Heaven’s One generates water, the abode of essence. Human life begins with Heaven’s One residing in the viscera, establishing the root and inception. The transformation and generation of the myriad things all originate from Heaven and Earth containing essence—this is why the One is the inception of form’s transformation. *Zhuangzi* says that when one is in accord with the One, all affairs are complete—is this not because one has grasped the inception of form’s transformation?

The pure and light ascend to become Heaven; the turbid and heavy descend to become Earth; the harmonious qi becomes humans. Thus, Heaven and Earth contain essence, and the myriad things transform and generate.

Explanation: Heaven attains the One and accumulates qi, so it is pure and light, covering all things. Earth attains the One and accumulates mass, so it is turbid and heavy, bearing all things. The solemn emerges from Heaven, the radiant issues from Earth—when the two intermingle, they form harmony, and the harmonious qi becomes humans. The transformation and generation of the myriad things all depend on attaining the One, yet cannot be without reliance on the ceaseless process of generation and transformation. Thus, only after Heaven and Earth contain essence do the myriad things transform and generate. It is not called “generation and transformation” but “transformation and generation,” for what has ended returns to generation, expressing the inexhaustibility of generation and transformation.

Of humans and things, all receive the harmony of yin and yang to complete their lives and bear their forms. Yet it is specifically said that the harmonious qi becomes humans because, though all share in life, the generation of the myriad things includes distinctions of flying and burrowing, moving and still, feathered and scaled, shelled and bare—each inevitably leans toward yin or yang. Only humans embody the harmonious qi.

Master Liezi said: “Heaven and Earth have no complete functions, sages have no complete abilities, and the myriad things have no complete uses.” Thus, Heaven’s duty is to generate and cover, Earth’s duty is to shape and bear, the sage’s duty is to teach and transform, and things’ duty is to fulfill what is fitting.

Yet Heaven has its shortcomings, Earth has its strengths, sages have their limitations, and things have their capacities. Why? What generates and covers cannot shape and bear; what shapes and bears cannot teach and transform; what teaches and transforms cannot defy what is fitting; and what is fittingly determined does not depart from its position.

Thus, the Dao of Heaven and Earth is either yin or yang; the teachings of sages are either benevolence or righteousness; the fittingness of the myriad things is either soft or hard. All follow what is fitting and cannot depart from their positions.

Explanation: Discussing the Dao of Heaven, Earth, and sages, there is nothing not encompassed, nothing not pervaded. Yet speaking of the functions of Heaven, Earth, and sages, they have already descended to their roots and flowed to their branches, confined within the realm of form’s transformation, each having its own duty. Thus, Heaven can act as Heaven but not as Earth; Earth can act as Earth but not as Heaven. Sages should be all-encompassing yet have their limitations; things have their limitations yet also their capacities.

If one seeks the Dao of Heaven through yin and yang, the sun of a thousand years may be attained while seated. If one seeks the teachings of sages through benevolence and righteousness, the achievements of emperors and kings may be discussed with reverence. If one seeks the myriad things through softness and hardness, the countless changes may be enumerated one by one. For once something is bound to form, it cannot depart from its fittingly determined position.

Nevertheless, without defying what is fitting, even the subtlest of the myriad things has its wondrous use. To seek perfection is to deny Heaven and Earth’s greatness of having no complete functions. If one can constantly abide in one’s fittingly determined position without opposing one’s natural nature, then every thing will share in the same function as Heaven and Earth. Though the myriad changes differ, all will be harmonized under a single governance.

Thus, there are those that generate, and those that generate generation; those that take form, and those that form forms; those that produce sound, and those that sound sounds; those that bear color, and those that color colors; those that have flavor, and those that flavor flavors.

What is generated by generation perishes, yet that which generates generation never ends. What is formed by forms is substantial, yet that which forms forms never exists. What is sounded by sounds is heard, yet that which sounds sounds never emits.

What is colored by colors is manifest, yet that which colors colors never appears. What is flavored by flavors is tasted, yet that which flavors flavors never presents itself.

The explanation states: The Classic says: "There is no absence without presence, no presence without absence." Now, what is called presence and absence does not take presence as presence, nor absence as absence. From non-presence arises presence, from non-absence arises absence—presence is thus absence, absence is thus presence. Hence, there are those that generate, and those that generate generation. Not generation, yet there is presence; not generating generation, yet there is absence. What is generated does not depart from generating generation, and generating generation does not lie beyond what is generated. Its presence is not presence, its absence is not absence. Forms, sounds, colors, and flavors are no different.

Thus, without the presence of forms, colors, sounds, and flavors, the transformations of absence would not be revealed. Without the presence of generating generation, forming forms, sounding sounds, coloring colors, and flavoring flavors, the operations of presence would not proceed. Therefore, what is generated cannot be called subtle, nor can generating generation be called wondrous. Though forms, sounds, colors, and flavors are the coarsest of things, their continuity from beginning to end ensures their generation is inexhaustible, and their provision to humanity is unceasing.

Moreover, forms, colors, sounds, and flavors are of one body, sufficiently encompassing all, yet they are repeatedly spoken of because the generation of the myriad things is often partial to sounds, colors, scents, and flavors. This is stated to clarify that all living kinds—whether sentient or insentient—operate under the office of non-action. All are the office of non-action.

They can be yin, they can be yang; they can be soft, they can be hard; they can be short, they can be long; they can be round, they can be square; they can generate life, they can bring death; they can be hot, they can be cool; they can float, they can sink; they can be *gong*, they can be *shang*; they can emerge, they can submerge; they can be dark, they can be yellow; they can be sweet, they can be bitter; they can be rank, they can be fragrant. They have no knowledge, yet nothing is unknown to them; they have no ability, yet nothing is beyond their capacity.

The explanation states: The Dao constantly practices non-action, yet nothing is left undone. The office of non-action is the master of the myriad transformations, what *Zhuangzi* calls the "true sovereign." Only through non-action is one unbound by yin and yang, unrestrained by softness and hardness, neither square nor form-bound, neither dead nor alive, untethered to time, unattached to things, unconfined to sounds and colors, ungoverned by scents and flavors. It issues forth qi and things, envelops forms and spirits, and all things rely on its function, yet I never depart from it—hence, nothing is unknown, nothing is beyond its capacity. As Lord Lao said, "Its function cannot be exhausted." Therefore, those who govern transformation must root themselves in non-action.

When Master Liezi traveled to Wei, he ate by the roadside. His followers saw a century-old skull, plucked some mugwort, and pointed at it, turning to his disciple Bai Feng to say: "Only you and that one know that you have never been born and never died. Is this not surpassing nourishment? Is this not surpassing joy?"

The manifold transformations of species are as follows:

When a frog becomes a quail, it may turn into *ji* upon encountering water; at the border of water and earth, it becomes the raiment of frogs and toads. Born on mounds, it becomes *lingxi*. *Lingxi* meeting *yuqi* becomes *wuzu*. The root of *wuzu* becomes *qichan*, while its leaves become butterflies. Butterflies, in their multitude, transform into insects, born beneath the stove, their form like a cast-off skin, named *niaogouduo*. After a thousand days, *niaogouduo* transforms into a bird called *ganyugu*. The foam of *ganyugu* becomes *simi*. *Simi* becomes *shixiyilu*, and *shixiyilu* gives rise to *shixihuanghuang*. *Shixihuanghuang* generates *jiuyou*, *jiuyou* generates *maorui*, and *maorui* arises from *fuxuan*.

Sheep’s liver transforms into *digao*; horse’s blood into *zhaolin*; human blood into wildfire. The sparrow hawk becomes the goshawk, the goshawk becomes the cuckoo, and the cuckoo in time reverts to the sparrow hawk. Swallows become clams; field mice become quails; rotten melons become fish; old leeks become amaranth; aged rams become apes; and fish roe becomes insects.

The beast *danyuan* conceives of itself and is called *lei*. The bird of rivers and marshes is born from gazing and is called *yi*. A purely female creature is named *dayao*; a purely male one, *zhifeng*. A longing scholar, though unwed, may be moved to conception; a yearning maiden, though husbandless, may become pregnant. Hou Ji was born from a giant’s footprint; Yi Yin from the hollow mulberry; Jue Zhao from dampness; and vinegar flies from wine.

When *yangxi* pairs with the non-sprouting bamboo, old bamboo gives rise to *qingning*. *Qingning* generates *cheng*, *cheng* generates horses, horses generate humans, and humans in time enter the mechanism. All things emerge from the mechanism and all things return to the mechanism.

**Explanation:**
Though a skull may lie for a hundred years, its wandering soul may pass into another form, becoming human again for another century before death. If it transforms into other beings, its deaths and rebirths would be beyond counting. Even as an empty skull, its form remains, and the transformations of its wandering soul are already manifold. Liezi alone grasped the marvel of his own body, and since the skull had undergone a century of changes, he pointed to it and said: "Only you and that one know that you have never died and never been born." Thus, Liezi, drawing on what he had seen and heard, questioned the century-old skull to illustrate the transformations of all things.

The arising of the myriad beings may stem from emotion or from thought. Those born from dampness arise through union and resonance; those formed through transformation respond through separation. Emotion and thought, union and separation, alternate and shift. Some, through sentience, transform into the insentient—like the frog becoming *lingxi*; others, through insentience, transform into the sentient—like *maorui* arising from *fuxuan*. Some grow from small to large, others from large to small. The swallow becomes a clam, and the flying creature sinks; the mouse becomes a quail, and the walking creature takes flight. Some transform by mutual gaze, like the birds of rivers and marshes; others conceive of themselves, like the beasts *zhan* and *yuan*. A single species may undergo diverse transformations—like *wuzu* becoming *qichan* or butterflies, or the frog becoming *ji*, the raiment of frogs and toads, or *lingxi*—so varied are its changes. Among these, Hou Ji’s birth from a giant’s footprint stands as a particularly extraordinary transformation, as recorded in the *Book of Songs* and thus verifiable.

In essence, whether sentient or insentient, large or small, all things emerge from and return to the mechanism, equally abiding in the supreme principle, none able to alter it. What, then, is this "mechanism"? How do the myriad things emerge from and return to it?

The *Plain Questions* states: "When exit and entry cease, the spiritual mechanism of transformation perishes." For the success and failure of all things depend on movement; movement unceasing gives rise to change. Thus, without exit and entry, there would be no birth, growth, maturity, or aging. Though exit and entry differ, the mechanism remains one; though their paths diverge, the mechanism is wondrous. The mechanism is revealed through exit and entry, and exit and entry operate by virtue of the mechanism. One cannot say that exit and entry *are* the mechanism, nor can one seek the mechanism apart from exit and entry. With each exit and entry, the spiritual mechanism silently operates.

The *Book of Changes* deems the knower of the mechanism "spiritual"—is this not the intent? The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: "Heavenly nature is human; the human heart is the mechanism." This text speaks of all things emerging from and returning to the mechanism, yet it particularly notes that "humans in time enter the mechanism"—is this not because humans are the most spiritually endowed of all beings, and thus cited to encompass all?

*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Profound Virtue*, Scroll One
*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Profound Virtue*, Scroll Two
Presented by Jiang Yue, Inner Student of the Hangzhou Prefecture School, Song Dynasty

The *Book of the Yellow Emperor* states: "When form moves, it does not generate form but generates shadow; when sound moves, it does not generate sound but generates echo; when non-being moves, it does not generate non-being but generates being." The explication says: Abiding in stillness without movement, one observes the subtlety of constant non-being and the boundary of constant being. Observing both boundary and subtlety, one sees they do not intersect. Yet when stirred by response, non-being cannot remain so. Constant non-being must enter into being, and being cannot remain constant, for it must return to non-being—such is the natural movement, which none can resist.

Though form and sound are not entirely absent in their reflections, when they rest in yin to cease their echoes, over time their sounds fade, and they too must ultimately return to nothingness. Since non-being cannot be spoken of as generating, the text speaks of form, sound, and their reflections to illustrate non-being.

*Zhuangzi* says: "All things generate one another through form." Here, however, it is said that form does not generate form. Though forms generate one another, in truth, form cannot generate itself. There is that which generates life, and it arises from non-being. Form is necessarily finite—does heaven and earth also end? They end together with me. Do they advance beyond ending? One cannot know. The Dao ends in what originally had no beginning and advances in what originally had no duration.

The explication says: That which exists must come to an end, so what has form must perish. Heaven, earth, and I are equally confined by form. Once it is clear how they arise, one should also know whether they end. Those who embody the Dao say that heaven, earth, and I are born together, so their end must also coincide with mine. Do they advance beyond ending? One cannot know—this is to say, there has never been an end. Since heaven, earth, and I do not depart from form, the discussion stops at "one cannot know." As for the Dao, it departs from form and generation, having neither beginning nor end. Beginning and end are correlatives—if the Dao originally had no beginning, how can one perceive its end? Duration and proximity are correlatives—if the Dao originally had no duration, how can one perceive its limit?

That which is born must return to non-birth; that which has form must return to formlessness. The non-born is not originally non-born; the formless is not originally formless. The born is that which by principle must end. The ending cannot but end, just as the born cannot but be born. Yet to desire only its birth and exhaust its end is to be deluded by fate.

The explication says: That which is originally non-born has no arising or ceasing; that which is originally formless has no gathering or dispersing. Once there is birth, there must be a return to non-birth; once there is form, there must be a return to formlessness. Waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness—these are the natural patterns. In life, fate arises and one cannot but be born; in death, fate is exhausted and one cannot but end. The coming and going of life and death do not depend on me. The deluded, however, cling to the body of conditioned phenomena, seeking long life without death—this is to fail to recognize the principle of inevitability.

Spirit belongs to heaven’s portion; bones and flesh belong to earth’s portion. That which belongs to heaven is clear and disperses; that which belongs to earth is turbid and gathers. When spirit departs from form, each returns to its true nature—hence it is called *gui* (ghost). *Gui* means "return," returning to its true abode.

The Yellow Emperor said: "When spirit enters its gate and bones return to their root, what of me remains?" The explication says: In human life, the hundred bones, nine orifices, and six viscera are all present, temporarily gathered by qi—this is not originally my body. The clear and light belong to heaven; the turbid and heavy belong to earth. At life’s end, spirit departs from form, warmth disperses, movement ceases, the clear returns to heaven, the turbid returns to earth, each returning to its true nature. What is called "I"—what remains of it? The value of the Dao in mastering death lies in the moment when all things return to their true nature without losing one’s self. If one does not lose what abides in oneself at this moment, then life is governed by oneself, and the transformations of creation cannot cause one to sink.

Confucius said: "If one hears the Dao in the morning, one may die in the evening." Mencius said: "Neither long life nor early death sways one’s resolve." Yan Hui said: "I have forgotten myself in sitting." All refer to this Dao.

From birth to death, the great transformations of life have four stages: infancy, youth and prime, old age, and death. In infancy, qi is concentrated and the will is unified—this is the height of harmony. Things do not harm it, and virtue cannot surpass it. In youth and prime, blood and qi overflow, desires and thoughts arise abundantly—things assail it, and virtue thus declines. In old age, desires and thoughts soften, the body prepares to rest—things do not precede it. Though it does not attain the wholeness of infancy, it surpasses youth and prime. In death, one comes to rest, returning to the ultimate.

The explication says: Human life belongs to the transformations of creation, ever renewing without cease, thoughts shifting moment by moment. Taking its broad outline, there are four stages. Their sequence is natural, neither increased nor diminished by nobility or baseness. Examining virtue and form, inner and outer do not correspond. In human life, the flourishing and decline of virtue rise and fall with the body—what is the principle behind this?

Human life is the gathering of qi. Qi is concentrated in infancy, overflows in youth and prime, rests in old age, and ceases in death. If one cannot preserve one’s self and the will is insufficient to command qi, how little remains within that is not swept away by things? In life, one is merely enslaved by transformation; in death, one is merely altered by it—one knows not when this will end. Only those who embody the Dao can transform things without being transformed by them. Though they follow the changes of transformation, they do not drift with creation’s flow. In infancy, they share the same concentration with others. As for youth, prime, and old age, they never lose the heart of a newborn child.

Though death comes, it is precisely the means by which one attains the constant joy of quiescence—this is what is called "in exhalation returning to the utmost."

Confucius once traveled to Mount Tai and saw Rong Qiqi walking in the wilds of Cheng, clad in a deerskin robe tied with a rope, playing the zither and singing. Confucius asked him, "Sir, what is the source of your joy?" He replied, "My joys are many. Heaven gives birth to the myriad things, and only humans are noble—yet I have been born a human: this is my first joy. Among men and women, men are honored while women are lowly, and thus men are noble—I have been born a man: this is my second joy. Some in life never see the sun or moon, never leave their swaddling clothes; I have already lived to ninety years: this is my third joy. Poverty is the constant lot of the scholar; death is the end of all people. Abiding in constancy and meeting one’s end—what is there to grieve?" Confucius said, "Excellent! He is one who can console himself."

An explanation: The name Rong Qiqi—*rong* suggests blossoming without fruition, *qi* suggests openness to engagement. He grasps the flourishing splendor of the Dao, his form open to interacting with things, and seeks only the joy of following life’s course, without delving into the Dao’s deep-rooted foundation. This is why he finds contentment in constancy and takes joy in his end.

Yet what is truly prized in the Dao is not merely the solitary joy of life—it is the means by which one perfects one’s death. Though such a person does not drown in the path of profit and harm, nor meet an untimely end midway, he is still distinct from the common crowd. As for his attainment of the Dao—it has not yet come to pass. This is what is called "consoling oneself to find joy," not the true joy that knows no lack of joy.

Lin Lei was nearly a hundred years old. In late spring, he donned a fur robe, gleaned stray grains from old fields, and sang as he walked. Confucius, passing through Wei, saw him in the wilds and said to his disciples, "That old man may be worth speaking to—go and question him." Zigong volunteered and, meeting him at the edge of a ridge, sighed as he faced him, "Sir, do you have no regrets, singing and gleaning as you go?" Lin Lei walked on without stopping, his song unbroken. Zigong pressed him repeatedly, and at last he looked up and replied, "What should I regret?" Zigong said, "In youth, you did not strive in conduct; in maturity, you did not compete with the times; in old age, you have no wife or children; death approaches—what joy is there in gleaning and singing?" Lin Lei laughed and said, "The source of my joy is what all people possess, yet they turn it into sorrow. In youth, I did not strive in conduct; in maturity, I did not compete with the times—thus I have lived so long. In old age, I have no wife or children; death approaches—thus I can be so joyful." Zigong said, "Longevity is what people desire; death is what they detest. Why do you take joy in death?" Lin Lei said, "Death and life are but departure and return. How do I know that one who dies here does not live again there? Thus I know they are not the same, and how do I know that busily seeking life is not delusion? How do I know that my death now is not better than my past life?" Zigong, hearing this, did not grasp his meaning and returned to report it to the Master. The Master said, "I knew he was one worth speaking to—indeed, so! Yet what he has attained is not yet complete."

The Explanation states: In youth, one strives in conduct to guide oneself forward with reputation. In maturity, one competes with the times, swept away externally by profit and gain. Not coveting fame, thus one does not act for show; not pursuing wealth, thus one does not vie with the times. Free from the pursuit of fame and profit within, unburdened by wife and children without—at ease in old age, approaching rest—knowing that death is but a temporary departure, deeming the frantic pursuit of life as delusion: this is the joy of Lin Lei. Zigong, still delighting in life and dreading death, how could he grasp this man’s heart?

Yet even Lin Lei, finding joy in the approach of death, has not attained the Way. One who has attained the Way is utterly indifferent to life and death, regarding them as discarded and entrusted to fate—why would they suddenly feel sorrow or joy between them? When Confucius said he had attained it but not fully, was this not the reason?

Moreover, his view that the frantic pursuit of life is delusion is what he has attained; his claim that present death is better than past life is what he has not yet fully grasped. As for Lin Lei—"Lin" meaning "wild" in the wilderness, signifying separation from things and estrangement from others, dwelling in the vast and desolate realm—he resembles the Dao of the sage. Thus he finds joy in death, having attained it yet not fully.

Zigong, weary of study, told Confucius: "I wish to rest." Confucius said: "With life, there is no rest." Zigong asked: "Then shall I, Si, have no rest at all?" Confucius replied: "There is rest for you."

The Explanation states: Zigong, skilled in rhetoric, heard the Master say Lin Lei was one worth speaking to and thus first realized the folly of excelling in debate. Seeking to diminish his excesses in pursuit of the Dao, he told Confucius he wished to rest. Yet he did not understand that the course of life, entrusted to the great transformation, follows an order that cannot be disordered. While life labors us, how can there be rest?

Nevertheless, for Zigong, enlightenment was easy—upon hearing Confucius’ words, he understood the rest of the noble man and the submission of the petty man. Thus Confucius approved, saying: "Si, you have understood." Gazing at the grave mounds—some towering like watchtowers, some smooth like sacrificial vessels, some rounded like hills, some segmented like tripods—he knew where rest lay.

The Explanation states: The shapes of the mounds—towering, smooth, rounded, segmented—reflect the different ranks of the noble and base, yet all alike find rest there. Zigong said: "Death is great indeed! The noble man rests there; the petty man submits there." Confucius said: "Si, you have understood. People all know the joy of life but not its suffering; they know the weariness of old age but not its ease; they know the dread of death but not its rest."

Yanzi said: "How excellent that the ancients had death! The virtuous rest there; the unvirtuous submit there."

The Explanation states: Rest is like the breath—once exhaled, it returns. Submission is like overpowering things and forcing them down, their movement and stillness governed by another. The noble man’s rest, though transformed, does not perish; the petty man’s submission is blindly rushing toward transformation. The noble man is the fulfillment of benevolence; the petty man is the opposite. Thus Yanzi also said: "The benevolent rest there; the unbenevolent submit there."

The Laozi treasures compassion above all. To transcend the realm of life and death, nothing precedes the capacity for benevolence and universal love—hence the reference to the noble man’s benevolence. Death is the culmination of virtue. The ancients called the dead "those who have returned." If the dead are called "those who have returned," then the living are "those who journey." One who journeys without knowing to return has lost their home. If one person loses their home, the world condemns them; if the whole world loses its home, none know to condemn it.

The Explanation states: Human life in the world is like a traveler at an inn—one must ultimately have a place to return. Yet those who cling to life and lose sight of principle are many, while those who early awaken to it are few. The many and the few oppose each other; what is not right is deemed right, and none awaken in the end. Liezi spoke of this not to condemn one man or one generation but simply to unify life and death in a single thread.

Is there one who leaves their homeland, parts from their six relations, abandons their family estate, and roams the four directions without returning—what manner of person is this? The world would surely call them a reckless and dissolute person. And is there one who clings to worldly worth, clings to worldly worth (should say "cherishes form and life"), prides themselves on skill and ability, cultivates fame and reputation, and boasts in the world without self-knowledge—what manner of person is this? The world would surely deem them a wise and strategic man. Both are lost, yet the world approves one and not the other. Only the sage knows whom to approve and whom to reject.

The Explanation states: The reckless person abandons their homeland and neglects their family estate; the strategic man cultivates fame and boasts in the world. The former’s recklessness is deemed petty by custom; the latter’s pursuit of strategy is deemed noble. Yet they do not realize that the reckless one, by leaving their homeland and parting from kin, merely neglects their family estate, while the strategic one, by harming life and injuring nature, strays from their true dwelling—how then can one distinguish noble from petty between them? Zhuangzi spoke of Zang Ke clutching his books and reading, or gambling and wandering, as equally lost like the sheep that strayed—this is what is meant by both being lost.

Liezi, following this, spoke of the value of emptiness, taking stillness and emptiness as finding one’s place, and grasping or relinquishing as losing it—thus the sage’s approval and rejection can be known. Someone asked Liezi: "Why do you value emptiness?" Liezi said: "The empty has no value." Liezi said: "Not in name—nothing compares to stillness, nothing compares to emptiness."

Stillness and emptiness—these are to find one’s dwelling; grasping and relinquishing—these are to lose one’s place. Those who, after shattering stone to fashion implements, then dance with benevolence and righteousness cannot restore what was broken.

Explanation: Emptiness and stillness are the root of the ten thousand things. Empty, one is thus sufficient to receive the full; still, one is thus sufficient to respond to movement. When emptiness and stillness reside within me, though the myriad changes arise, my heart remains ever tranquil; though the myriad forms differ, my heart remains ever clear—this is the root of responding to things. Thus it is said: nothing compares to stillness, nothing compares to emptiness. Empty yet able to contain, still and ever tranquil, one moves through change and misfortune without departing from the dwelling of one’s nature—this is to find one’s place. To have something to grasp is no longer empty; to have something to give is no longer still. When grasping and relinquishing contend, the ears and eyes turn outward, the mind races like fire and does not return—this is to lose one’s place. *Place* here means the dwelling of one’s nature, in accord with what the Old Master called "not losing one’s place for long." Once one has lost one’s place, only then does one drum up benevolence and righteousness in the wake of things’ withering and ruin—this is merely what strategists use to parade their cleverness before the world. They harm their own nature—how then could they help others restore their original state?

Zhu Xiong said: The turning of the cosmos never ceases; heaven and earth shift imperceptibly—who notices? Thus what is diminished there is filled here; what is completed here is depleted there. Loss and fullness, completion and depletion follow the world and follow death. Coming and going meet in succession, with no gap to spare—who notices? For qi does not advance all at once, nor does form deplete all at once; one does not perceive its completion, nor perceive its depletion. It is like a person from youth to old age: countenance, complexion, wisdom, and bearing change without a day’s pause; skin, nails, and hair fall away with the passing world, no different from infancy when nothing halted or remained unchanged. The change is imperceptible until later, when one knows. Explanation: Things and transformation form a single body; the body follows transformation and shifts. Within a hundred years, the great transformation occurs four times, and within it, seasons change and years pass, days alter and months transform. Even in the space of a single breath, the state before and after a sigh differs—old and new are not the same. It is like the wind striking water: the wave before is not the wave after; like a lamp in a jar: the flame before is not the flame after. In the space between lowering and raising one’s gaze, one’s form has already changed ten thousand times. The deluded are secretly shifted by creation yet see only the traces of their own form, unaware of the subtle workings of the great transformation. They rejoice in a thing’s completion, not knowing the principle of its depletion has already begun; they see a matter’s depletion, not knowing the principle of its completion has already advanced. In the *Yuan Jing*, Master Yang spoke of the moon’s waning: its breadth does not compare to the dawn’s radiance in the west—this refers to the subtle shifting of creation and the alternating advance of completion and depletion. Applied to the body, in the toil of a hundred years, countenance, complexion, wisdom, bearing, skin, nails, and hair change without a day’s pause—how could this be clearer? Why then does one not awaken to it? This is the meaning of Zhuangzi’s "hiding a boat in a ravine." The Master, standing by a river, said: "What passes is like this!"—this too makes it clear.

In the state of Qi, there was a man who worried that heaven and earth would collapse, leaving him nowhere to dwell, and so he neglected sleep and food. Another, worried about his worry, went to enlighten him, saying: "Heaven is but accumulated qi—there is no place without qi. In your bending and stretching, breathing, and moving about all day, you are always within heaven—why worry about collapse?" The man said: "If heaven is indeed accumulated qi, then the sun, moon, and stars should not fall?" The enlightener said: "The sun, moon, and stars are but luminous things within accumulated qi. Even if they fell, they could not harm anything." The man said: "What of the earth’s ruin?" The enlightener said: "The earth is but accumulated clods, filling the four directions—there is no place without clods. In your pacing and treading, you move about all day upon the earth—why worry about its ruin?" The man was suddenly greatly relieved. The enlightener was also suddenly greatly relieved.

Changluzi heard this and laughed, saying: "The rainbow, the clouds and mist, the wind and rain, the four seasons—these are the forms taken by accumulated qi in the heavens. The mountains and peaks, the rivers and seas, the metals and stones, the fire and wood—these are the forms taken by accumulated matter on the earth. Knowing that they are but accumulated qi and accumulated clods, how can one say they will not perish?

"Now, the heavens and earth are but a tiny thing within the void, the greatest of all things within the bounded. That they are difficult to exhaust, difficult to fathom—this is certainly so. That they are difficult to measure, difficult to comprehend—this is certainly so. To worry over their ruin is indeed too remote, yet to say they will not ruin is also incorrect. Since the heavens and earth cannot but perish, they will eventually return to ruin. When that time comes, how can one not grieve?

Ziliezi heard this and laughed, saying: "To say the heavens and earth will perish is also mistaken; to say they will not perish is also mistaken. Whether they perish or not, I cannot know. Nevertheless, that is one state, and this is another. Thus, the living do not know death, the dead do not know life; the coming does not know the going, the going does not know the coming. Whether they perish or not, why should I concern myself?"

The commentary says: The number of things is called the "ten thousand," yet man is but one among them. Compared to the ten thousand things, is he not like a hair’s tip upon a horse’s body? Thus, the human body, lodged between heaven and earth, is exceedingly insignificant. Moreover, the vault of heaven arches above, its warp forming the sun and moon, its weft forming the stars, and the ten thousand things are covered by it. The earth spreads vast below, its knots forming mountains and peaks, its flows forming rivers and seas, and the ten thousand things are borne upon it. The heavens’ longevity, the earth’s endurance—these are not what man can hastily measure. Yet the man of the state of Qi worried that his body would have nowhere to lodge, even to the point of abandoning sleep and food. Was the vastness of heaven and earth truly insufficient to contain his body? Rather, the man had no way to contain himself. Why did he not reflect that from the time heaven and earth have existed until now, there have been countless deaths and births, beyond reckoning? Why should he alone come to worry over this? His worry was truly lamentable, for it was a self-loss of man. Yet to worry over what he worried about was to lament the lamenter’s lament.

Having already worried, and then being further enlightened by such crude and shallow principles of things, only to suddenly rejoice—alas! Heaven is eternal, earth is enduring; what do they care for the fewness or multitude of such men? Why should such a man hastily allow his deluded heart to swing between worry and joy?

Changluzi heard this and laughed, and his words seemed apt. To say the heavens and earth are but a tiny thing within the void, the greatest within the bounded—this principle cannot be refuted. Yet to say, 'When their time of ruin comes, how can one not grieve?'—this is to grasp the truth without exhausting it. For where the principle of completion lies, there is unity everywhere. Whether it is that or this, in the end there is no duality. To use duality to reach unity is to have no other unity. If one understands that the One is not two, then life and death, coming and going, each do not know the other. Dwelling within the universe, drinking the harmony of heaven, eating the virtue of earth, one may peacefully live out one’s life—why need one know whether they perish or not? This is why Changluzi was further laughed at by Ziliezi.

Shun asked Cheng (in the *Zhuangzi*, written as "Cheng"): "Can the Dao be obtained and possessed?"

Cheng said: "Your body is not your own—how can you possess the Dao?"

Shun said: "If my body is not my own, then who possesses it?"

Cheng said: "It is the entrusted form of heaven and earth. Life is not your own—it is the entrusted harmony of heaven and earth. Nature and destiny are not your own—they are the entrusted compliance of heaven and earth. Children and grandchildren are not your own—they are the entrusted shedding of heaven and earth. Thus, in walking you do not know where you go, in dwelling you do not know what you hold, in eating you do not know why. Heaven and earth are but the strong yang qi—how can you possess anything?"

The commentary says: The Dao and I are of different names but the same reality. The Dao is I, and I am the Dao—there is no difference or separation. The realms of being and non-being, the contrast of emptiness and fullness—if it can be obtained and possessed, it can also be lost and absent. The Dao does not leave me—how can there be a separate 'heavenly Dao'? To say the Dao can be obtained and possessed is to make the Dao and me two. Shun’s question to Cheng was to claim the Dao exclusively for his own body. Yet this body of mine is but the accumulation of heaven and earth’s strong yang qi, temporarily gathered and soon to disperse—it is not truly mine. How can I possess the Dao?

Examining this body of mine: Heaven endows it with nature, and with nature comes life; with life comes the body. Nature and destiny arise from heaven and earth’s entrusted compliance; life arises from heaven and earth’s entrusted harmony; the body arises from heaven and earth’s entrusted form. As for children and grandchildren, that is even more distant—they are but the entrusted shedding of heaven and earth. Thus, whether walking, dwelling, or tasting, I always depend on that which I do not know. If one wishes to know one’s body, one may observe it as a shadow knows its form. The body’s relation to me is like the shadow’s to the form—where the strong yang is, I am with it. How can the strong yang be possessed?

Laozi said: "My great trouble is that I have a body." For he sought to return to the state before birth, without a body, and thus unite with the Dao. Shun was an ancient great sage, yet he asked Cheng about the Dao. This was because Shun, though compelled to govern the world after Yao, had already departed from the Dao in manifesting virtue. Thus, he spoke as he did. Cheng was one who assisted rulers with the Dao.

In Qi, the Guo family was immensely wealthy; in Song, the Xiang family was desperately poor. The Xiang family traveled from Song to Qi to ask for their method. The Guo family told them: "I am skilled in theft."

At first when I practiced theft, within a year I had enough, within two years I was self-sufficient, and within three years I had grown vastly prosperous. From then on, my bounty extended to the towns and villages.

The Xiang family rejoiced greatly, understanding his words about theft but not grasping the Dao of theft. They then scaled walls and broke into houses, taking whatever their hands and eyes could reach. Before long, they were caught with stolen goods and punished, their ancestral wealth confiscated. The Xiang family, believing the Guo family had deceived them, went to complain. The Guo family asked: "How did you practice theft?" The Xiang family described what they had done. The Guo family exclaimed: "Alas! Have you lost the Dao of theft to such an extent? Now I will tell you.

I have heard that Heaven has its seasons, Earth its advantages. I steal Heaven and Earth’s timely benefits—the soaking nourishment of clouds and rain, the produce of mountains and marshes—to grow my grain, cultivate my crops, build my walls, and construct my dwellings. On land, I steal from birds and beasts; in water, I steal from fish and turtles—nothing is not stolen. Yet grain, crops, trees, birds, beasts, fish, and turtles are all born of Heaven. How can they be mine? Yet when I steal from Heaven, I incur no misfortune.

Gold, jade, precious treasures, grain, silk, and wealth are what people amass. How can they be Heaven’s gifts? If you steal them and are punished, whom can you blame?"

The Xiang family was deeply perplexed, thinking the Guo family was again deceiving them. They went to consult Master Dongguo, who said: "Is not your very body itself theft? You steal the harmony of yin and yang to complete your life and sustain your form. How much more so with external things—are they not also theft?

Truly, Heaven, Earth, and the myriad things are inseparable. To claim possession of them is delusion. The Guo family’s theft is the public Dao, so they incur no misfortune. Your theft is driven by private desire, so you were punished. Those who act with public or private intent are both thieves; those who act without public or private intent are also thieves. The interplay of public and private is the virtue of Heaven and Earth. Who can say what is theft and what is not?

The explanation states: *The Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: Heaven and Earth are the thieves of the myriad things; the myriad things are the thieves of man; man is the thief of the myriad things. When the three thieves are in harmony, the Three Powers are at peace.

For Heaven bestows, Earth gives birth, and the labor of the myriad things transforms them. The myriad things rely on human effort to grow and flourish, while humans rely on the myriad things for sustenance. Heaven and Earth are not wholly efficacious, the sage is not wholly capable, and the myriad things are not wholly useful. What they lack, they borrow from without—is this not theft? Thus, anything taken that is not one’s own is theft. Why must one take only what hands and eyes can reach to be called a thief?

Yet Heaven and Earth’s creative transformations are boundless, the myriad things born of them are infinite, while human efforts to seize and claim them are limited. The Guo family steals from Heaven and Earth—this is the public Dao, so they prospered greatly and their bounty extended to the towns and villages. The Xiang family stole from what others had amassed—this was private desire. Since they had already amassed it with private intent, for you to profit from it was fitting that you were punished and your ancestral wealth confiscated.

However, if one regards having public or private intent as theft, then having neither public nor private intent should not be theft. Yet once one assumes human form, the body is not one’s own—where can one go that is not theft? Even without intent toward public or private, how can one escape the constraints of form?

Moreover, theft is one, yet the theft of the public Dao differs from that of private desire and avoids the calamities of human conduct. The theft without public or private intent, though not yet free of form, differs from those who value life and cherish the body through the public Dao.

As for the virtue of Heaven and Earth, it does not intend to be public—it lets the myriad things be public of themselves. It does not intend to be without private intent—it lets the myriad things be private of themselves. To speak of this is to ask: Are not the myriad things’ theft from man, and man’s theft from the myriad things, merely the accumulation of Heaven and Earth’s great theft? Heaven and Earth’s creation and transformation of the myriad things begin and end here. Thus, *The Celestial Axis* concludes at this point.

Let us consider this: When a single rabbit runs into the wild, a hundred people chase it, and no one faults them. But when a child catches it, even if a strong man could overpower a hundred, he dare not take it by force. Why? Because the division of names and duties is fixed."

The fleeting life of man, with its myriad branching and divergent intentions, clings to self-interest amidst Heaven and Earth—like those who chase the rabbit, each drawing lines to claim possession. Those who seize what chance brings them truly believe it their own; those who fail to seize it likewise believe it truly belongs to another. Thus, they covet more, strive to acquire, hoard fame and profit, delight in them all their lives, and fear only that others might glimpse their gain. How would they know that their very selves are but thieves?

Alas! Heaven and Earth’s generation of the myriad things is not the Dao of Heaven and Earth. For their generation is boundless, their transformation inexhaustible, allowing men to steal from them so that households grow rich without calamity. How much more so is the Dao of Heaven and Earth vast and all-embracing? If one could grasp it, its abundance would be beyond all reckoning.

*The Celestial Axis* elucidates the Six Classics as records of the Dao for later ages. It embeds the marvel of spirit within forms, names, measures, and numbers, and lodges the subtleties of the heart’s workings amid benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music. In its manifest aspect, even the foolishness of common men and women can partake of it. In its marvelous aspect, even the wisdom of sages cannot fully comprehend it. What can be grasped lies in its words being near at hand; what cannot be known lies in its meaning pointing afar. Such is the transmission of the Classics.

Beyond the Six Classics, those who establish teachings and compose books exhaustively expound the wondrous workings of the Dao, gathering its scattered aspects into unity, shedding its blossoms to reveal its fruit, discerning things and restoring nature, aspiring to tranquility and simplicity, and illuminating the inner and outer teachings of the Six Classics—none surpass the books of Laozi, Zhuangzi, and Liezi.

Though the three perfected ones’ scriptures all transmit the marvelous principle, their aims in establishing teachings differ, yet the Dao may still be spoken of thus: The ninety-nine chapters of the *Laozi* clarify the essential return of virtue and the Dao. In just a few thousand words, it examines its beginning and end, declaring the Dao that can be spoken not the eternal Dao, calling the highest virtue no-virtue, and taking the sage’s Dao as non-contention. Though it speaks of the sage’s way of responding to the world, it never touches upon the sovereigns of high antiquity; though it sets forth the principles of the myriad things’ generation and transformation, it never touches upon the realm of plants and animals. This is why the *Laozi* is supremely marvelous and concise.

As for the *Zhuangzi*, it employs "goblet words" to unfold endlessly, "repeated words" to convey truth, and "parable words" to broaden its reach. Its text flows freely without harm, its language strange and wondrous yet worthy of contemplation. The *Laozi*’s concision gains clarity through the *Zhuangzi*, and its Dao becomes all the more luminous.

As for the teachings of the *Liezi*, their primal marvel lies in the *Laozi*’s virtue and Dao, and their strangeness likewise resides in the *Zhuangzi*’s parables. They elucidate the *Laozi*’s essential marvels without indulging in the *Zhuangzi*’s expansiveness. The three scriptures’ aims converge in the Dao, with the *Liezi*’s teachings appropriately situated between them. Only by understanding this can one read the *Liezi*.

What, then, is the Dao? It is that which is marvelous and unknowable. By "marvelous," we do not mean that it is marvelous because it is empty, nonexistent, or non-active. Rather, it is marvelous because it operates above the myriad things yet cannot be fathomed.

Great is the Dao! As the primordial resource nurturing the myriad things, its spontaneity necessarily takes generation and transformation as primary; its shaping necessarily takes yin and yang as its origin. Thus, this scripture begins by clarifying the generation and transformation of celestial portents. "Heaven" refers to the Dao that is not made by anything; "portent" refers to things that accord with verification. "Celestial portent" is the tally of spontaneity.

Tracing the Dao of generation and transformation, it cannot be made clear apart from things, nor can it be exhausted by clinging to things. Clinging to things is insufficient to speak of generation and transformation, for generation and transformation are not exhausted by activity. Departing from things, there is no way to manifest generation and transformation, for generation and transformation do not abide in inactivity. It says: There is generation that does not generate, transformation that does not transform. That which does not generate and does not transform originates in Heaven’s spontaneity; that which generates and transforms accords with portents as verification. The Dao of generation and transformation is fully contained herein.

Since the Great Simplicity already existed, the Great Beginning followed it; the Great Commencement appeared, and the Great Prime presaged it. Once the beginnings of qi, form, and substance were complete, it could be named "Change." Change alters to become the One; the One is the beginning of form’s alterations. In the obscure depths, its sequence already lacks even a hair’s breadth of disorder. Once it has fallen into form’s alterations, the clear and turbid diverge at their source, Heaven and Earth settle into their positions, man embodies their harmony, and things contain their essence.

Though Heaven and Earth are vast, their Dao can be matched to yin and yang; though the sage’s Dao is marvelous, its teachings can be verified in benevolence and righteousness; though the myriad things are many, their transformations can be united in softness and hardness. Thus, even when shaping man’s form, none can escape the changes of creation.

As for the myriad things’ emergence from and return to the mechanism—whether sentient or insentient, alternating and transforming—this may indeed be called marvelous. Once transformation has settled into constancy, it cannot disrupt the tally of spontaneity. Thus, the transformations of the mole cricket’s feet may produce a grub but cannot produce a butterfly; the transformations of the frog may produce a quail but cannot produce a wren. The principle of generation and transformation accords thus, which is why the people of Qi, seeing that what has form must end, and observing that Heaven and Earth manifestly contain things, fell into petty and excessive calculations.

Yet the tally of spontaneity, in its beginning, must have something from which it arises; in its arising, must have a fitting and fixed position; in its end, must have a means by which it returns. Beginning must end, and ending must return to the beginning. The changes of one, seven, and nine cycle endlessly without terminus—this is why the principle of generation and transformation endures eternally without cease.

It is fitting that things without awareness sink into the depths of creation. But man, the spirit of the myriad things, though not exempt from generation and transformation, also possesses the principle of neither generating nor transforming within himself.

Why not turn inward to seek sincerity within oneself? Why willingly remain within the realm of creation, drifting and migrating alongside the myriad things? The teachings of Liezi aim to enable scholars to perceive the marvel of ceaseless generation and transformation even amid generation and transformation itself, and to follow the tally of creation without being constrained by it.

Thus, at the close of the chapter, he attributes the words to Shun inquiring of Cheng about the Dao, in order to dispel the burdens of having life. Yet this is still deemed insufficient—he goes so far as to declare that this very body of ours is a thief of Heaven and Earth. Even Heaven and Earth, in their generation and transformation, are likewise called thieves. Theft is what all people revile, yet I have come into being through theft.

If life bears this form, then what makes one value life or cherish the body, thereby transgressing what others detest? Here, *Heavenly Portents* concludes.

*Explication of the Perfect Classic of the Dao and Virtue of the Most Vacuous* [Volume Two] ends.

*Explication of the Perfect Classic of the Dao and Virtue of the Most Vacuous* [Volume Three]
Presented by Jiang Yu, Inner Student of the Hangzhou Prefecture School of the Song Dynasty. [Yellow Emperor]
