When the Yellow Emperor had reigned for fifteen years, he rejoiced that all under Heaven bore him willingly, nurturing his proper destiny, delighting his ears and eyes, and gratifying his nose and mouth—yet his complexion grew parched and sallow, and his five faculties dimmed in confusion. After another fifteen years, he fretted that all under Heaven remained untended, exhausting his keen hearing and bright vision, advancing his wisdom and strength to govern the hundred clans—yet his complexion grew parched and sallow, and his five faculties dimmed in confusion.

The Yellow Emperor then sighed in lament, saying: "My excesses have run deep indeed. Nurturing the self alone brings such affliction; governing the myriad things brings such affliction."

*Explication states:* The principle of life values unrestrained spontaneity. To harbor intent in nurturing the self is to tread the path of its undoing. The hundred clans call me natural, yet to harbor intent in governing the myriad things is likewise to tread the path of affliction. Thus, delighting the ears and eyes, gratifying the nose and mouth, and indulging in joyful exultation—this is not tranquility. Exhausting keen hearing and bright vision, advancing wisdom and strength, and dwelling in anxious sorrow—this is not contentment. Without tranquility and contentment, there is no virtue. Though the nurturing of the body and the governing of things differ, and though sorrow and joy diverge, their harm to life and injury to nature are the same. Harm to life thus parches the complexion and sallows the skin; injury to nature thus dims the five faculties in confusion. For fifteen years make a generation—half spent in sorrow, half in joy, outwardly wearing the body, inwardly wounding nature—where then lies the joy of life? This is why the Yellow Emperor sighed deeply over his excesses and sought the Perfect Dao.

Thereupon, he relinquished all affairs of state, abandoned his palace chambers, dismissed his attendants, silenced the bells and chimes, reduced his kitchen provisions, and retired to dwell in leisure at the Hall of the Great Court. There, he purified his heart and disciplined his form, abstaining from governance for three months. One day, as he napped in broad daylight, he dreamed of wandering in the land of Huaxu.

*Explication states:* Relinquishing all affairs of state severs entanglements; abandoning palace chambers ensures unwearied movement; dismissing attendants ensures independence and constancy...

Silencing the bells and chimes, reducing kitchen provisions—thus are the pleasures of ear, eye, nose, and mouth removed. Dwelling in leisure at the Hall of the Great Court, one roams freely and abides in the vast dwelling. Purifying the heart and disciplining the form, clarity, wisdom, and strength are not employed. Abstaining from governance for three months, one remains still and unmoving; passing through time’s changes yet unaltered. Napping in broad daylight and dreaming, the form does not engage with things, yet luminously communes with spirit.

The land of Huaxu is where spirit resides. Forced to name it, yet it has no substance—only those who embrace spirit with their nature may wander there. Thus was the realm where the Yellow Emperor dreamed of traveling. The land of Huaxu lies west of Yan Prefecture and north of Tai Prefecture. Who knows how many thousands of *li* distant it is from this world? It is not a place reachable by boat, carriage, or foot—only by spirit can one travel there.

*Explication states:* The northwest is the profound recess of Heaven and Earth, the primal gate of inner illumination—hence the land of Huaxu is situated there. Though forced to name it and locate it in a direction, it is not bound by direction, nor constrained by measure. Thus it is said: "Who knows how many thousands of *li* distant it is from this world? Only by spirit can one travel there."

In that land, there are no teachers or rulers—it is so of itself. Its people have no desires—it is so of itself. They do not delight in life, nor do they abhor death, and so there is no premature demise. They do not favor themselves, nor do they distance others, and so there is no love or hatred. They do not oppose or comply, and so there is no profit or harm. They cherish nothing, nor do they fear anything. Entering water, they do not drown; entering fire, they do not burn. Cut or struck, they feel no pain; poked or prodded, they feel no itch. Treading emptiness as though it were solid, resting in the void as though on a bed. Clouds and mist do not obstruct their sight; thunder and lightning do not disturb their hearing. Beauty and ugliness do not unsettle their hearts; mountains and valleys do not trip their steps—only spirit moves.

*Explication states:* As the saying goes: "Without clothing and food, the way of ruler and minister ceases." In that land, there are no teachers or rulers, and its people have no desires—it is so of itself. Moreover, the absence of rulers is not because the people are governed by non-desire; it is so of itself. The absence of desires is not because of a sagely ruler’s governance; it is so of itself. When the Dao reaches the state of spontaneity, then what the world calls desire and aversion, closeness and distance, opposition and compliance, love and fear—all are forgotten. How then could there be the misfortune of premature death, the passions of love and hatred, or the choices of profit and harm? Thus one may enter water and fire, forget pain and itch, tread emptiness as though it were solid, abide in solidity as though it were void, see and hear with perfect clarity, remain unperturbed in heart, and walk without stumbling.

True emptiness is not empty—treading emptiness is like reality, yet not truly real, hence it is said "as though treading solid ground." True void is not void—resting in the void is like the void, yet not truly void, hence it is said "as though on a bed." Who but one of utmost spirit could attain this? The true persons of old slept without dreaming and awoke without sorrow. The Yellow Emperor was one who discerned waking and dreaming—thus his wandering in Huaxu was a true dream. That he entrusted it to a dream was not merely to follow a fleeting moment.

When the Yellow Emperor awoke, he was joyfully content. He summoned Tian Lao, Li Mu, and Tai Shan Ji and said to them: "I have dwelled in leisure for three months, purifying my heart and disciplining my form, seeking the Dao to nurture the body and govern things, yet I did not attain its method. Exhausted, I slept, and dreamed thus. Now I know the Perfect Dao cannot be sought through emotion. I have understood it, I have attained it—but I cannot explain it to you."

Twenty-eight years later, the world was greatly ordered, nearly like the land of Huaxu.

*Explication states:* Tian Lao embodies the effortless way of Heaven. Li Mu is the skilled steward of human governance. Tai Shan Ji represents the greatest of earthly categories. One who attains the Dao can command the Three Powers and employ the transformations of creation—thus the Yellow Emperor, joyfully content, summoned these three to inform them.

The Dao has emotion and sincerity, yet the Perfect Dao cannot be sought through emotion—for the Dao does not reject emotion, yet it cannot be attained by seeking. The so-called Perfect Dao is the Dao that does not depart from the true. How could there be a method to be pondered and sought? Thus, seeking the Perfect Dao through emotion will never suffice to attain the Dao. Only after exhaustion and sleep, dreaming thus, and then awakening, does one become joyfully content. For in exhaustion and sleep, the limbs relax and wisdom and strength are not employed—then in dreams, one truly communes with spirit.

Only one who has attained the Perfect Dao can govern the world without governing, so that it is ordered. Thus, twenty-eight years later, the world was greatly ordered, nearly like the land of Huaxu. Twenty-eight is the number of four sevens. Seven times seven is the number of *tian gui*, reaching its mean and utmost flourishing. Though the Yellow Emperor’s governance did not depart from effort, his rule could not escape this number. Moreover, seven times seven is a yin number, and the Yellow Emperor was then gathering brilliance to return to the root—hence he particularly spoke of the flourishing of yin numbers.

If the Dao does not reach the true, no one sleeps without dreaming—how then could one reach Huaxu? Upon awakening, one is joyfully content. For what one loves by day, one dreams of by night. If the Yellow Emperor had purified his heart and disciplined his form, his waking thoughts and dreams would have dissolved. If he dreamed, it could only have been of Huaxu. But if one cannot align the heart and discipline the form, one is enslaved by thoughts and constrained by yin and yang. One who dreams of drinking wine wakes to weep; one who dreams of weeping wakes to hunt. Thus are waking and dreaming inverted, and gain and loss, sorrow and joy remain unsettled—how many thousands of *li* distant is one then from the land of Huaxu?

Moreover, that daytime thoughts give rise to nighttime dreams is the natural principle. Yet here it is framed as a dream occurring during daytime slumber—for daytime is the midpoint of the sun’s course, the time when one is most entangled in worldly affairs. If the spirit wanders even while the body is engaged in such affairs, then the spirit never departs from the body for even an instant.

Let us consider this: the achievements of emperors and kings are but the incidental undertakings of sages. The sage acts only when unavoidable, and even then, surrenders action to things, allowing them to act on their own—thus should there be no burden of deliberate doing. Yet once action is taken, the achievements of emperors and kings are accomplished, and the Great Dao recedes. When Liezi sought to clarify how the sage responds to the role of emperor or king while never straying from the ultimate Dao, he began his discourse with the Yellow Emperor, for in speaking of the Dao, the Yellow Emperor represents its highest attainment. Yet upon ascending the throne and engaging with the world, the tumult of worldly entanglements arises. Whether governing or in chaos, joy or sorrow, such involvements become unavoidable.

Nevertheless, the Yellow Emperor, emerging from the great source to respond to things, constantly embodied the inexhaustible and wandered without obstruction. Thus, he entrusted his worldly engagements to the dream of Huaxu to dispel their traces. When he aligned his heart and disciplined his form, gathering the traces of worldly response to return to the ultimate Dao, he nearly attained the governance of the Huaxu clan—such was the Yellow Emperor that the world never beheld. People merely cling to his outdated traces, pondering without enlightenment.

Therefore, this chapter concludes with Jixian’s physiognomy of Master Hu, revealing to him that which had never departed from the source—whereupon Jixian, unable to divine anything, fled in disarray. This discourse thus elucidates the ultimate principle of rulership, which is why *Zhuangzi’s* “Responding to Emperors and Kings” also concludes with this passage.

Mount Liegushe lies amidst the islands of the sea and rivers. Upon its summit dwells a spirit-person who inhales the wind and drinks the dew, partaking not of the five grains. The heart is like a deep spring, the form like a maiden’s; neither clinging nor cherishing, immortals and sages serve as ministers; neither fearing nor angering, the sincere and guileless act as envoys. Without bestowing or favoring, things are naturally sufficient; without amassing or hoarding, one remains free of fault. Yin and yang are ever harmonized, sun and moon ever bright, the four seasons ever orderly, wind and rain ever balanced, nurturance ever timely, grains ever abundant. The land knows no blight, people no premature death, creatures no blemish or plague, and ghosts no eerie manifestations.

Explanation: *Gu* means “for the time being,” and *she* means “to be sated.”

Mount Guye is where those who, wearied by worldly entanglements, dwell in temporary response to what cannot be avoided. Water nearly approaches the Dao, and the sea is where the waters of rivers return. Mount Guye lies amid its isles, signifying its place at the center of the Dao—who but a spirit-person could abide here? Not eating the five grains, inhaling the wind and drinking the dew, one savors the Dao’s blandness, which is flavorless. The heart like a deep spring is still and concentrated, never straying. The form like a maiden responds to things without initiating. Neither clinging nor cherishing, one treats the myriad things as straw dogs. Neither fearing nor angering, one guards the purity of qi. Without bestowing or favoring, things are naturally sufficient—this is what is meant by "no one loses a single hair." Without amassing or hoarding, one remains free of fault—this is what is meant by "no one seeks gain from the world." From the ever-harmonized yin and yang to the absence of eerie manifestations among ghosts, this is what Lord Lao called "peace and great stability."

Liezi studied under Master Lao Shang and befriended Bo Gaozi. *Shang* means the sound of metal; *Lao Shang* refers to one who returns to nature, restores destiny, and practices non-action. *Bo Gaozi* refers to one whose years grow ever higher and whose virtue ever more expansive. Thus did Liezi take such figures as teachers and friends. Liezi once studied under Master Huqiu and befriended Bohun Maoren. Since the Dao is present everywhere, what fixed teacher could there be? Advancing the Way of these two masters, he rode the wind and returned.

Yin Sheng heard of this and lived with Liezi, yet for months he did not attend to his own dwelling. Finding an opportunity, he requested to learn his arts, but after ten inquiries, Liezi gave no answer. Yin Sheng, resentful, asked to leave, but Liezi did not respond. Yin Sheng withdrew. Months later, his resolve unshaken, he returned to follow Liezi again. Liezi said, "Why do you come and go so frequently?" Yin Sheng replied, "Before, when Zhang Dai asked you, you did not tell me, so I harbored resentment. Now, my grievance has dissolved, and so I have returned." Liezi said, "Formerly, I took you for one who understood, but now your vulgarity has reached this point? Ji, I will tell you what I learned from my master."

*Zhuangzi* says: If the Dao could be conveyed to others, none would fail to tell their brothers. Ten inquiries met with ten refusals—it was not that he did not wish to tell, but that he did not know how to tell. When Nie Que questioned Wang Ni, four questions met with four non-answers. To know that questioning is futile is to say that three questions met with three non-answers would be excessive. As for Zhang Dai, he was one who exalted literary refinement and placed it above all else—how could he be fit to speak of Master Lao Shang’s non-action? Thus, having followed Liezi, he left in resentment, and after months away, returned with his grievance dissolved. His comings and goings were inconsistent, his resentments repeated—so vulgar and unenlightened in the Dao, he would never draw near. Thus, Liezi was compelled to tell him:

"Since I began serving my master and befriending such men, after three years, my heart dared not entertain right or wrong, my mouth dared not speak of benefit or harm, and only then did my master deign to glance at me. After five years, my heart again pondered right and wrong, my mouth again spoke of benefit and harm, and only then did my master relax his expression and smile. After seven years, my heart followed its thoughts without right or wrong, my mouth spoke without benefit or harm, and only then did my master draw me to sit beside him. After nine years, my heart roamed freely in its thoughts, my mouth spoke freely, yet I knew not whether my right and wrong, benefit and harm were mine or another’s; I knew not whether my master was my teacher or those men my friends—inner and outer had merged. Only then were my eyes like ears, my ears like nose, my nose like mouth, all the same. My heart congealed, my form dissolved, bones and flesh fused entirely. I did not sense the body’s support or the feet’s tread, drifting with the wind east and west like a leaf or dry husk—did the wind ride me, or did I ride the wind?

Now you, having entered the master’s gate for barely a moment, have already harbored resentment twice. Your limbs will not bear qi, your joints will not be supported by the earth. To tread emptiness and ride the wind—how could you hope for this?" Yin Sheng was deeply shaken, held his breath for a long while, and dared not speak again.

A commentary states: Right and wrong reside in principle; benefit and harm lie in affairs. What was once deemed right may now be considered wrong—thus right and wrong originally have no fixed form. What I deem beneficial, others may find harmful—thus benefit and harm originally have no constant truth. The reason right and wrong, benefit and harm arise is this: in human life, desire arises from having a body, and love arises from the body. Desire gives rise to love, and the seven emotions intermingle. The eyes pursue form, the ears follow sound, the nose drifts toward fragrance, the tongue craves flavor. Within a single body, each part remains unaware of the others. Though all share in the perceptions of ears and eyes, left and right occupy different positions, so brightness and darkness may differ—how then can there be no divergence? From this we know that right and wrong, benefit and harm all emerge from the perceptions of ears, eyes, nose, and mouth. Yet these perceptions arise from the luminous and perfect nature. When nature confronts things, things align with the self, thereby forming the body. Though the body is not unified, nature ultimately remains undivided.

When this singular nature splits into the seven emotions, it does not originally belong to them. If it then severs from the luminous whole, why should it depend on external things? If one can awaken to even a single truth amid sounds, forms, scents, and flavors, and return to rest in the real, then the seven apertures all withdraw. Ears, eyes, nose, and mouth merge into one. Looking back upon the myriad things of the world, their bustling confusion appears like snowflakes glimpsed through a veil—all dissolve and unite with true essence. How much more so for this single body of ours? This is the Way by which Liezi treads emptiness and rides the wind. Liezi possessed innate knowledge of this Way and attained it naturally—what need had he for study? Yet to transmit instruction, he necessarily placed it within the context of a learner, guiding them step by step through ordered practice.

If the heart dares not entertain thoughts of right and wrong, and the mouth dares not speak of benefit and harm, then one can cast off cunning and artifice. This marks the progression from a year in the wild to three years of penetrating understanding. The "dare not" does not mean the absence of such thoughts—it means one has not yet reached the state where the Way is present at a single glance. Thus, one first receives but a glance from Old Shang. By the fifth year, the heart again contemplates right and wrong, the mouth again speaks of benefit and harm. Yet these thoughts and words arise naturally, without pride or reluctance, so the Way flows unimpeded through the heart. Thus, the master relaxes his countenance and smiles, no longer selecting among the heart’s thoughts or the mouth’s words.

Until the seventh year of celestial completion, one cannot yet act freely without constraint—such a one is not yet fit to weigh alternatives. How could they keep pace with Huzi, matching his level and driving abreast? Only when thoughts follow the heart and words follow the mouth, utterly devoid of right and wrong, benefit and harm, does the master draw them near to share his seat. Yet even then, this is merely acting freely—though one may say there is no right and wrong, benefit and harm, one has not yet attained the state of "no nothingness."

Only after advancing to the ninth year of great subtlety do the heart’s thoughts and the tongue’s words flow unbounded. One no longer knows others’ right and wrong, benefit and harm, nor one’s own—all becomes the same. When the Way reaches this point, one values neither natural endowment nor teacher. Thus, one no longer knows whether the master is one’s teacher or those men one’s friends—inner and outer merge. Only then do the seven apertures return to the one real, the hundred bones harmonize with the great void. Dwelling between heaven and earth, one drifts with the wind east and west like a leaf or dry husk—does the wind ride me, or do I ride the wind?

To say I ride the wind—does the drifting leaf also have a mind to ride the wind? To say the wind rides me—does the wind’s blowing ever depend on me? Yet riding the wind and traveling, one moves with effortless grace, not knowing why it is so, only that it is so. Though it is said that bones and flesh entirely fuse, one still compares them to leaves and husks. For there is the qi of life and the form of existence—one can make them empty but not make them nothing. Though there is form, one is not burdened by it—this may be called emptiness. Though still as light as a leaf or husk, one has not yet departed from "having something." This is why *Zhuangzi* says Liezi still had something to depend on—this is the great subtlety of the Way. If it were merely nothingness, what value would the Way hold?

As for one like Yin Sheng, harboring resentment within, his qi of anger and frustration disrupts the great harmony. His life is but a wart or tumor upon heaven and earth. Having entered the master’s gate for barely a moment, he has already expressed resentment twice. If he carries these deluded emotions to the end of his days, he will only sink deeper. Though the earth’s vast capacity bears all, it cannot support even a single joint of his sinking body.

Alas! All living beings share the same mandate from creation, yet a single divergence in emotion and thought leads to such vast differences in ascent and descent. Thus, Liezi took special care to address this. Liezi asked Guan Yin: "The perfected person moves through water without obstruction, treads fire without heat, walks above the myriad things without fear. May I ask how one attains this?" Guan Yin replied: "This is the preservation of pure qi—it does not belong to the realm of cleverness, skill, or boldness."

A commentary states: The perfected person does not depart from the real. With the real within, what thing could alter it? This is why the perfected person enters water without getting wet, enters fire without heat, ascends heights without fear. Guan Yin speaks of attaining this through the preservation of pure qi—"pure" means not diminishing one’s spirit. Not diminishing one’s spirit is how one remains real. As for cleverness, skill, and boldness, these involve a mind set on overcoming things. With suspicion and calculation within and blustering qi without, even a single joint or part of the body would not be tolerated between heaven and earth—how could one draw near to water or fire?

Only when pure qi is inwardly preserved do knowledge and perception revert, sight and hearing withdraw inward, and none can discern their agency—water and fire become one with them. This is why there is no entry where they do not find themselves at ease.

The Old Master said: *"One whose virtue is profoundly contained is like a newborn babe."* This is precisely what is meant by the preservation of pure qi. Thus, poisonous creatures do not sting, fierce beasts do not seize, and grasping birds do not strike. Ji, the fish, speaks to you: All that has form, image, sound, or color is but *thing*—how can one thing be distant from another? Yet how could this suffice to reach the primordial? It is but *form* after all.

A commentary states: Form, image, sound, and color are all called *form*. All things arise from non-being into being, beginning with movement and giving rise to form. When primordial simplicity is complete, physical form first becomes manifest. Yet primordial simplicity does not reach the Great Beginning—how then could it attain the primordial? Only one who treats things as things yet is not a thing can reach the primordial. In reaching the primordial, does this not resemble what the Old Master called *"a thing unformed and complete"*? It is the creation of things before form, resting where there is no transformation. Yet those who attain this and exhaust it—how could they be called *upright*?

A commentary states: All that has form belongs to creation and transformation. Only that which creates before form rests where there is no transformation. Those who attain this Dao and exhaust it depart from form, transcend transformation, and reach the state before things, dissolving distinctions of right and wrong. Though uniquely upright, this cannot be named. This is how the perfected person remains real.

They dwell in a measure not profound, conceal themselves in a principle without end, and roam through the beginning and end of the myriad things.

A commentary states: What is most easy to know and most easy to practice—this is the measure not profound, for it does not violate the natural course of the myriad things. The Old Master took *"following and meeting without seeing its head or tail"* as the principle of the Dao—this is what is meant by the principle without end. Moving among the myriad things, the myriad things receive it to wax and wane, fill and empty. Its boundary cannot be exhausted—this is roaming through the beginning and end of the myriad things. Inwardly able to dwell and conceal, outwardly able to roam among things—this is the perfection and fullness of the Dao.

Unify their nature, nurture their qi, contain their virtue, and thereby connect with the source of things.

A commentary states: Unifying their nature prevents division; nurturing their qi prevents depletion; containing their virtue prevents dispersion. Thus, they can ascend and roam with the Creator—this is what is meant by connecting with the source of things. Those who are thus have their heavenly endowment intact, their spirit unobstructed—how could things enter them?

A commentary states: From what could things arise? How could uprightness emerge? Roaming through the beginning and end of the myriad things, they connect with the source of things. Connecting with the source of things, they attain heaven; attaining heaven, they attain spirit. Their heavenly endowment intact, their spirit has no division. With their spirit undivided, the preservation of pure qi is firmly established—thus, nothing can enter them.

When a drunkard falls from a cart, though the speed be great, he does not die. His bones and joints are the same as others’, yet the harm he suffers differs—his spirit is whole. He does not know he is riding, nor does he know he has fallen. Life and death, alarm and fear do not enter his breast; thus, he does not tremble before things. If one can be whole through wine and yet be thus, how much more so when whole through heaven? The sage conceals himself in heaven—thus, nothing can harm him.

A commentary states: Spirit gives life to form, and form completes spirit. Without spirit, form cannot live; without form, spirit cannot be realized. For spirit to remain whole and undiminished, its loss comes from knowledge. For form to remain whole and unharmed, its injury comes from resisting things. Yet spirit gives life to form—those who cherish their form do not necessarily preserve their spirit, but when spirit is preserved, nothing can resist it. The drunkard on the cart, unaware of riding or falling—this is how his spirit remains whole. With spirit whole, life and death, alarm and fear cannot move his center. Though he falls from the cart, it can only harm his form, not his spirit—this is why his harm differs from others’, and though the speed be great, he does not die. Yet being whole through wine is merely drowning in intoxication and confusing one’s knowledge—it is not knowledge that forgets emotion. As for sobriety, knowledge remains clear and never forgotten for a moment—thus, though he does not die, he may still suffer harm. But if true knowledge is without knowledge, then even water and fire cannot scorch or drown, let alone a fall from a cart. This is how being whole through heaven means nothing can harm one. The sage conceals himself in this—this is the way of long life and enduring vision.

Liezi drew his bow to the full for Bohun Wuren, placing a cup of water on his elbow and loosing the arrow. The arrowhead followed in succession, and the next arrow found its place. At that moment, he was like a wooden figure.

Bohun Wuren said: *"This is the shooting of one who shoots, not the shooting of one who does not shoot. Try standing with me atop a high mountain, treading on perilous rocks, facing an abyss of a hundred fathoms—could you shoot then?"*

Thereupon, Wuren ascended the high mountain, trod on perilous rocks, faced the hundred-fathom abyss, and turned his back, hesitating, with two parts of his feet hanging over the edge. He beckoned Liezi forward. Liezi prostrated himself on the ground, sweating to his heels.

Bohun Wuren said: *"The perfected person gazes above into the blue sky, plunges below into the yellow springs, roams to the eight limits, yet their spirit and qi remain unchanged. Now you tremble with fear in your eyes—your center is in peril."*

A commentary states: *"Drew his bow to the full"* means he drew it to the arrowhead’s limit. *"Placing a cup of water on his elbow"* means his stance was steady and precise. *"The arrowhead followed in succession"* means his skill was exact. *"The next arrow found its place"* means his shooting was swift.

The reason he could do this was that his intent remained undivided, like a wooden figurine. Yet this is the archery of archery, not the archery of non-archery. In the archery of archery, what one aims for lies without; in the archery of non-archery, what one holds fast to lies within. The archery of archery may or may not succeed; the archery of non-archery admits no success or failure. When one is like a wooden figurine, there is nothing to fear without. The reason he prostrated himself and sweated was that his heart clung to something. The mountain’s height, the rock’s peril, the abyss’s depth—none of these harbored intent to harm; not all who climb or tread encounter disaster. It is only because of clinging to life, submitting outwardly to pride and stinginess, that one goes to every extreme. In the end, things cannot harm me, but my own heart harms itself, leading to the loss of life without ever awakening.

As for the perfected person, who does not depart from the true, their climbing and treading differ not from others’—only their spirit and qi are inwardly guarded. They do not know the height or depth to be feared, and wherever they go, they remain like a wooden figurine. They roam to the eight limits, yet their spirit and qi remain unchanged—nothing more.

Liezi once rode the wind to travel—what difficulty would he have in advancing this Way? The text speaks thus to urge learners not to regard flattery and fame as the world’s highest beauty, all contained within themselves, but to devote themselves to wholeness. Confucius did not claim his sageliness—it was like this as well.

As for Bohun Wuren, he dwelled at the head of things, turning clarity into obscurity, and became a person of non-being. This is how he could ascend heights and face depths without fear—this was the teaching Liezi received.

The Fan clan had a son named Zihua, who was skilled at cultivating his private reputation. The entire state submitted to him; he was favored by the Lord of Jin yet held no office, ranking above the Three Ministers. Whatever he glanced at, the state of Jin ennobled; whatever he spoke of, the state of Jin demoted. Those who frequented his courtyard filled the court. Zihua sent his knights to attack one another with cunning and vulgarity, to oppress the weak with the strong. Though they were wounded before him, he paid no heed. Day and night, he made this his sport and pleasure, until the state nearly became a custom.

When He Sheng and Zi Bo—both esteemed guests of the Fan clan—went on a journey, they passed through the fields outside the village and lodged at the hut of Tian Geng, a field warden named Shang Qiu Kai. In the dead of night, He Sheng and Zi Bo spoke to each other of Zihua’s fame and power, how he could make the living perish and the dead live, the rich poor and the poor rich. Shang Qiu Kai, who had long suffered from hunger and cold, secretly listened from behind the north window. Thereupon, he borrowed grain and carried a basket to Zihua’s gate.

It is said: He Sheng and Zi Bo were both high-ranking guests of the Fan clan, so their knowledge of the clan’s fame and power was precise. That they spoke of Zihua’s influence in the middle of the night shows they were not boasting to others. Shang Qiu Kai, who overheard from beside the window, knew their words were unintentional and not false. Thus, taking their words as truth, he feared only that sincerity might fall short or action might not keep pace. Zihua’s followers were all from noble families, clad in white silk, riding in carriages, walking with slow steps and haughty gazes. When they saw Shang Qiu Kai—old, frail, dark-faced, and careless in dress—they all sneered at him. Soon, they grew familiar, mocking and deceiving him, pushing and shoving, doing whatever they pleased. Shang Qiu Kai never showed anger, and the guests’ tricks soon wore thin, their jesting exhausted.

It is said: Zihua’s followers took their mockery and deceitful tricks as amusement, seeking to outdo each other and humiliate others. Had Shang Qiu Kai taken their insults as lies and resented them, Zihua’s ambitions would have soared while Shang Qiu Kai would have truly been shamed. But now, meeting them with sincerity and never showing anger, though their tricks were many, they soon grew feeble; though their laughter was merry, it soon tired. In the end, they could not shame Shang Qiu Kai. Looking back, were not their past mockery and jesting merely futile and self-humiliating? Thereupon, they ascended a high terrace with Shang Qiu Kai and, before the crowd, declared: “Whoever can leap down will be rewarded with a hundred gold pieces.” The crowd all clamored to respond. Shang Qiu Kai, taking their words as true, leapt first. His form was like a flying bird, soaring to the ground, yet his bones were nearly unharmed.

It is said: When suspicion and doubt vanish and sincerity is inwardly preserved, the body becomes weightless. Thus, his lightness resembled a bird’s flight from the high terrace, his bones nearly unscathed—just as a drunkard falls from a carriage, suffering harm differently from others. The Fan clan’s followers thought it a fluke and did not yet find it strange. Then, pointing to a secluded bend in the river, they said: “There is a treasure pearl within; you may dive to retrieve it.” Shang Qiu Kai dove again and, upon emerging, indeed found a pearl. The crowd began to doubt in unison. Zihua then ordered that he be given meat, fine clothes, and silk.

It is said: Believing their words and diving into the water without drowning—this was loyalty and trust placing his body amidst the waves. They deceived him into thinking there was a treasure pearl within; he dove and emerged, indeed finding a pearl. What principle was this? It was proof that sincerity can move things. Moreover, the treasure pearl, in its essence and wondrous use, becomes clearer the deeper one goes, present everywhere. Sought with cleverness or demanded with scorn, the loss grows greater. Only Shang Qiu Kai’s sincerity matched the formless, so how was his success any different?

Soon after, a great fire broke out in the Fan clan’s treasury. Zihua said: “Whoever can enter the fire and retrieve brocade will be rewarded according to what they bring back.” Shang Qiu Kai went without hesitation, entering and exiting the flames unscathed—no dust clung to him, his body was not scorched. The Fan clan’s followers, believing him to possess the Dao, apologized together: “We did not know you possessed the Dao and deceived you; we did not know you were a divine person and humiliated you. You must think us fools, deaf, blind. We dare ask: what is your Dao?” Shang Qiu Kai replied: “I possess no Dao. Even my own heart does not know the reason. Nevertheless, there is one thing—I will try to tell you.”

When the two guests you sent once lodged at my home, they extolled the power of the Fan clan, claiming it could make the living perish and the dead live, the rich poor and the poor rich. I believed them without doubt, so I came without hesitation. Upon arrival, I took your followers’ words as truth, fearing only that my sincerity might fall short or my actions prove insufficient. I knew not where to place my body or where profit and harm lay—my heart was simply one. When things do not resist, it is like this alone.

Now that I know your followers deceived me, I harbor suspicion within and display arrogance without, clinging to past luck that spared me from burning or drowning. Yet my heart seethes with distress, trembling with fear. How could I dare approach water or fire again?

**Explanation:** To dive and retrieve a treasure pearl is already strange, and the Fan clan’s followers grew wary and suspicious. Only after entering and exiting the fire unscathed—no dust clinging, no body scorched—did they deem him possessed of the Dao and call him a divine person. Yet this may be like tossing an object high without a stone to strike it, or merely coinciding with circumstances by chance. Emerging from water is something even a practiced swimmer can do, but fire, raging and fierce, consumes all—whether fine or coarse, soft or hard—reducing everything to ash in its flames. Unless one’s body harmonizes with the spirit and unites with the formless, how could a mere clod of earth dare approach firewood and flame?

Shang Qiu Kai said he possessed no Dao, and even his own heart did not know the reason. Only by forgetting emotions and attaining non-knowledge does the heart become one and undivided—this is the Dao, and nothing can resist it. But if one harbors suspicion and displays arrogance, distress seethes within, burning inner harmony, so how could one enter fire without scorching? Trembling with fear, one’s nature is drowned within, so how could one enter water without getting wet?

After this, when the disciples of the Fan clan encountered beggars or horse doctors on the road, they dared not insult them but would alight from their carriages to bow. **Explanation:** Though Zi Hua’s fame and power made him disdain not even beggars or horse doctors, he recognized that fame and power were not the Dao. The noble person of great virtue appears foolish, and thus forgets his own status, daring not to insult even the lowly. This is the transformation wrought by utmost sincerity.

I once said that when Shang Qiu Kai was sincere toward things without a divided heart, even the purest qi of a perfected person could not surpass him. But when he harbored suspicion and displayed arrogance, he was still one of the deluded masses. From this, we know the supreme Dao does not await external seeking, nor does attainment follow gradual steps. The difference between madness and sagehood lies solely in mastering one’s thoughts—whether distraction arises even for a moment. How could one not be cautious?

Zai Wo heard this and told Confucius. Confucius said: “Did you not know? The person of utmost sincerity can move things, stir heaven and earth, move ghosts and spirits, traverse the six directions without resistance—how could it be merely treading peril, entering water and fire?” **Explanation:** Facing others with sincerity is but a small, unproven trust. Sincerity without expectation is the purest trust, like unalloyed gold. The person of utmost sincerity does not doubt within or without, is utterly empty and unopposed, utterly pure and unadulterated. Thus they can partake in the vastness of heaven and earth, assist in their transformative nurturing. They can journey to the lands of the southern barbarians or reach even the lowliest creatures like pigs and fish—this is what is meant by stirring heaven and earth, moving ghosts and spirits, traversing the six directions without resistance.

If Weisheng drowned or Bao Jiao burned, was that the fault of sincerity? They blocked their own paths with false trust, not the utmost sincerity. If Shang Qiu Kai did not resist even when others deceived him, how much less would he resist when both sides were sincere? Remember this, young one.

**Explanation:** When sincerity resides within, even false things become true; when delusion lingers inside, even favorable circumstances remain opposed. In dealing with oneself, one must focus solely on self-sincerity, and the sage’s skill in salvation aims only at mutual sincerity between self and other. As in the *Questions of Tang*, where the seed of immortals and sages flew back and forth between the five mountains in a single day or night—was this not mutual sincerity? To tell the young one to remember this is to say the Dao in a person knows no gap between youth and age. The young one should strive—why wait to hear the great Dao late in life?

As for the one called Zi Hua, his name means “glory without substance,” so it is fitting he would use fame and power to lord over others. *Shang* (merchant) suggests the gathering of sound, *qiu* (mound) the center rising from the earth, and *kai* (open) the emergence of things—thus “Shang Qiu Kai,” one who at first could enter water and fire, but in the end declared water and fire no longer approachable.

King Xuan of Zhou had a herdsman named Liang Yang, a laborer who could raise wild birds and beasts, feeding them in the palace gardens. Even tigers, wolves, eagles, and osprey became gentle—male and female before him, breeding in groups, different species living together without attacking or biting. The king, fearing the art would die with him, ordered Mao Qiu Yuan to learn it. Liang Yang said: “I am but a lowly laborer—what art could I teach you? I fear the king will think I am hiding it from you.” So he spoke briefly of his method for raising tigers: “All things with blood and qi rejoice when followed and rage when opposed—this is their nature. But when do joy and anger arise without cause? They are always provoked by opposition. When feeding a tiger, one must not give it live prey, lest it grow angry at having to kill, nor whole prey, lest it grow angry at having to tear it apart. One must time its hunger and satiety, understand its anger, and though tigers and humans are different species, they fawn on those who nurture them—this is following. To kill them is opposition.”

Then how could I dare oppose them to provoke their anger? Yet neither do I follow them to elicit their joy. For joy inevitably gives way to anger, and anger in turn often yields to joy—both miss the mark. Now, my heart harbors neither opposition nor compliance, so that birds and beasts regard me as one of their own. Thus, those that roam my enclosures do not long for lofty forests or vast marshes; those that rest in my courtyard do not yearn for deep mountains or secluded valleys—this is the principle at work.

**Explanation:** All living things possess the nature of blood and qi. From a human perspective, blood is stored in the heart, while qi resides in the lungs; qi moves, and blood follows. In a single day and night, there are thirteen thousand five hundred breaths, with blood and qi circulating ceaselessly—awake, they course through the body; asleep, they flow through the organs. Not a hair’s breadth of delay is permitted: too much or too little, and illness arises; if severe, death follows. Thus, blood and qi may be followed but never opposed.

From this nature of blood and qi arise the conditions of opposition and compliance, giving rise to the emotions of joy and anger. Resentment and gratitude, life and death—nothing is beyond their reach. Yet though humans cannot reverse their qi, they can command it; though they cannot defy their nature, they can regulate it—this is what makes humans possessors of the Dao. Even with the emotions of joy and anger, their expression never fails to strike the proper measure.

As for the creatures that fly and burrow, those with wings and horns, fangs and claws, that soar or crawl—knowing only the advantage of life, they are ruled by blood and qi. Thus, birds at their limit snatch, and beasts at their limit strike. To expect them to be docile among humans, living together without attacking or devouring one another—is this not difficult? Yet even tigers and wolves, eagles and ospreys—the fiercest of birds and beasts—share the same blood and qi as humans, so their minds and intelligence are not so far removed from ours. If one can understand their desires, time their hunger and satiety, indulge their compliance without violating their opposition, there is none that cannot be tamed.

Moreover, to comprehend their joy and anger and indulge them—this is the art of nurturing them. But the essence of why they can stand before you, male and female, breeding in groups, different species living together without attacking or devouring one another—lies in the heart that harbors neither opposition nor compliance. Why? Understanding their joy and anger does not make them forget joy and anger. Joy and anger alternate like the movement of yin and yang: to oppose and provoke them is certainly impermissible, but to follow and indulge them is also not the mean. Though one may temporarily follow them to elicit joy, in the end, some violation will inevitably provoke their anger. Only when joy and anger do not manifest, and one harbors no opposition or compliance toward things, do things likewise harbor no opposition or compliance toward oneself. Thus, birds and beasts regard me as one of their own.

The reason birds and beasts scatter in alarm, fleeing and hiding, seeking only lofty forests, vast marshes, deep mountains, and secluded valleys is to avoid harm and remain with their kind. Now, regarding me as one of their own, they roam my gardens, rest in my courtyard, live alongside humans, walk beside them—what joy is this! Why would they seek the seclusion of mountains and forests or crave the peril of rivers and valleys? With no opposition or compliance in me, the subtlety of non-action and non-utterance silently prevails even over the fiercest of creatures. Those with horns are transformed and tamed—this may be called subtle indeed.

King Xuan of Zhou valued Liang Yang’s art and transmitted it, yet when it came to governance, he still conducted censuses of the people without applying this art—how profoundly he failed to reflect! If he had used this Dao to shepherd his people, achieving a world at ease and a society of contentment would have been effortless.

As for "Liang Yang": *Yang* is a paired bird, naturally docile and sociable; *Liang Yang* thus refers to one that advances and finds its place in the rafters. As for "Mao Qiu Yuan": *Qiu Yuan* suggests a place where feathered creatures thrive, a land of nourishment where they delight to dwell. This is why Liang Yang could raise wild birds and beasts, and why King Xuan ordered Mao Qiu Yuan to transmit his art.

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: “I once crossed the Shentang Abyss, where the ferryman handled the boat as if by divine skill. I asked him, ‘Can one learn to handle a boat?’ He said, ‘Yes—those who can swim can be taught. Those skilled at swimming can learn quickly. As for divers, they have never seen a boat yet handle it with ease.’ I asked him further, but he did not explain. May I ask what this means?”

Confucius said: “Speaking of medicine, you and I have long studied its texts, yet we have not grasped its essence—shall we now discuss the Way? Those who can swim may be taught, for they are light upon the water; those skilled at swimming master it swiftly, for they have forgotten the water. As for divers, though they have never seen a boat, they handle it with ease—this is because they see the abyss as level ground, and a capsized boat as no different from a cart overturned. Though ten thousand scenes of overturning lie before them, they do not enter their minds; where could they not act with leisure?

Explanation: Water bears the boat—those who learn to steer should know this fundamental principle. One may study the ferryman’s skill in handling a boat yet fail to learn what makes him a ferryman. Even the ferryman’s finest techniques can only transmit his methods, not exhaust his artistry. Those practiced in water and bold in swimming, though untrained in steering, may match the ferryman’s skill. For one unpracticed in water gazes at the river’s edge with trepidation and dread, fearing only drowning—how then could they steer a boat? Thus, those light upon the water may be taught, and those who forget the water master it swiftly. As for divers, they have never seen a boat yet handle it with ease. Never having seen a boat, they have not only forgotten the water but even the boat itself—thus they act with leisure in all things.

Crossing water is like traversing peril; governing the world is like steering a boat to ferry the people to a place without difficulty. Only those who embody the Way can lightly forget worldly affairs, moving through ten thousand changes without ever being obstructed—thus they act with leisure in saving the world. Yan Hui’s use and withdrawal, action and concealment, were the same as the sage’s, so Confucius discussed the essence with him after exploring the texts.

One who pries with clay is skillful; with a hook, hesitant; with gold, dull. The skill is the same, yet attachment to externals makes one weigh them heavily. All who weigh externals neglect what lies within.

Explanation: The more one values the object, the more the mind becomes constrained; a constrained mind dulls wisdom and loses skill. Skill resides within, while things lie without. Once skillful, attachment to externals makes one clumsy—why not settle the distinction between inner and outer? Treat gold as mere tile, and one will be skillful in all things, for what one values cannot then leave oneself. Yet this cannot be forced—only those who embody the Way act thus naturally.

At first, one may be skillful, but in the end, clumsy—thus skill turns to clumsiness. If one’s cunning and skill were absent, the weight of what one values would not be seen, and in handling affairs, one might not be unskillful. Then is not what the world calls skill truly great clumsiness? Thus, great skill appears as clumsiness.

*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Dao and Its Power, Scroll Three—Concluded*
*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Dao and Its Power, Scroll Four*

A student of the Inner College of Hangzhou Prefecture Academy, your subject Jiang Yu respectfully presents:

The Yellow Emperor and Confucius observed at Lüliang, where the water plunged from a height of thirty *ren*, its surging foam stretching thirty *li*, a place where soft-shelled turtles, alligators, fish, and turtles could not swim. Yet they saw a man swimming there. Believing him to be in distress and seeking death, they sent their disciples downstream to rescue him. After several hundred paces, the man emerged, his hair unbound, singing as he swam along the embankment. (The text should read "beneath the embankment" rather than "along the mulberry path.")

Confucius followed and questioned him: "At Lüliang, the water falls thirty *ren*, its foam surges thirty *li*, a place where soft-shelled turtles, alligators, fish, and turtles cannot swim. When I saw you entering it, I thought you were in distress and wished to die, so I sent my disciples downstream to assist you. Yet you emerged with your hair unbound, singing as you swam—at first, I took you for a ghost, but upon closer inspection, you are a man. May I ask if you have a method for treading water?"

The man replied: "No, I have no method. I began with what was familiar, grew with my nature, and fulfilled my destiny. I enter the water with the current and emerge with its flow. I follow the Way of water without imposing my own will—this is how I swim."

Confucius asked: "What do you mean by beginning with what was familiar, growing with your nature, and fulfilling your destiny?"

The man said: "I was born on the hills and am at ease on the hills—this is what is familiar. I grew up in the water and am at ease in the water—this is my nature. I do not know why I am thus, yet I am thus—this is destiny."

*Explication*: Creatures born in water do not struggle against its nature, just as humans are born on land and are at ease there. Yet this old man from Lüliang could swim where soft-shelled turtles, alligators, fish, and turtles could not—this was a particular ability of his nature. Only by possessing such a nature could he grow up in the water and be at ease in it. Though at ease in the water, if he did not reach the point of not knowing why he was thus, he would not yet be following the Way of water without imposing his own will, and his skill would not be so wondrous. Thus, his words ultimately speak of destiny. Such a man is like one of whom Zhuangzi spoke, who had attained the ability to roam great rivers.

When Confucius traveled to Chu, he came upon a hunchback in the forest who caught cicadas as easily as if plucking them. Confucius said: "You are skillful! Do you have a method?"

The man replied: "I do have a method."

In the fifth or sixth month, when I stack two pellets and they do not fall, I miss only a few; when I stack three and they do not drop, I miss one in ten; when I stack five and they do not fall, it is as if I were gathering them by hand. My stance is like a withered tree stump; my arm, like a dead branch. Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things many, I know only the cicada’s wings. I do not turn aside, nor do I let the myriad things distract me from the cicada’s wings—how could I fail to catch them?

Confucius turned to his disciples and said: "When the will is undivided, it coalesces into spirit." Is this not what the hunchbacked elder means?

The elder said: "You, a scholar in fine robes, what do you know of such matters? First refine what you practice, then you may speak of it."

Explanation: To learn cicada-catching by stacking pellets—the pellet, being round, rolls and turns without fixed inclination. If one can stack them without their falling, one’s bodily stillness, the precision of one’s grip, and the undivided focus of one’s will are already evident. With such mastery, catching cicadas is as easy as plucking them. Since the cicada’s wings are light and swift, to take them so effortlessly is to advance beyond mere skill—how could this belong to the realm of cleverness? Thus he says, "I have a method."

When his stance is like a withered tree stump and his arm like a dead branch, he has inwardly forgotten himself. When he does not let the myriad things distract him from the cicada’s wings, he has outwardly forgotten all else. With both self and things forgotten, how could he fail in catching cicadas? Thus Confucius said his will was undivided, coalescing into spirit. A divided will dissipates spirit; an undivided will concentrates it—this is what is meant by "guarding the pure qi." Such is the way of the hunchbacked elder, whose form was bent yet who sought no external refinement.

Confucius, by contrast, wore fine robes and cultivated the conduct of a Ru scholar in Lu. Thus the elder urged him to refine what he applied in the world and set it aside before speaking of such matters.

The cicada, as a creature, shuns filth, seeks purity, sings in season, eats nothing, and makes no demands. Those who have attained the Dao of bodily transformation find in it a fitting symbol for their practice.

There was a man by the sea who loved gulls. Each morning he went to the shore, and the gulls followed him, flocking in hundreds. His father said: "I hear the gulls all follow you—bring some back, and I will play with them." The next day, when he went to the shore, the gulls danced above but would not descend.

Thus it is said: "The highest words abandon words; the highest action is non-action." The knowledge of those who rely on cleverness is shallow indeed.

Explanation: The Classic says: "Once there is a deluded mind, it startles the spirit." Mind and spirit are as exterior and interior. If I startle my spirit, things will startle me in return. The man by the sea followed the gulls because his mind held no resistance. Once he harbored the thought of capturing them, he startled his spirit—hence the gulls danced above and would not descend. If one does not oppose things, all things will respond likewise—why only gulls? It was only because his fondness was for gulls that the gulls followed him.

Though gulls and humans are different species, when the human mind is wordless and actionless, it is closely observed by them. Then what need is there for words to be trusted or actions to be known? The knowledge of those who rely on cleverness is truly shallow.

When Liezi offered five jugs of wine first, it startled the recipient. When Viscount Xiang of Zhao led a hundred thousand followers to hunt in Zhongshan, they set fire to the forest, fanning flames across a hundred li. A man emerged from a stone cliff, rising and falling with the smoke and embers. The crowd called him a ghostly being. When the fire passed, he walked out unhurriedly, as if untouched by what he had endured.

Viscount Xiang, astonished, detained him and observed him closely: his form, complexion, and seven orifices were human; his breath and voice were human. He asked: "By what way did you dwell in stone? By what way did you enter fire?"

The man replied: "What do you mean by 'stone'? What do you mean by 'fire'?"

Viscount Xiang said: "What you just emerged from was stone; what you just passed through was fire."

The man said: "I do not know."

Explanation: What binds me to things, and what enables things to bind me, is the separation of form, preventing great unity. Things and I are not fundamentally different—our differences arise from knowledge and perception. Thus, knowing something as stone makes its hardness impenetrable; knowing it as fire makes its heat unapproachable. Yet the burning of fire and the hardness of stone cannot be ignored by those who are utterly without knowledge. Only when one knows through non-knowing, remaining serene and leaving no trace in the mind, do flesh and bone dissolve, merging into great unity. In great unity without self, nothing can obstruct one.

Here, a man emerges from a stone cliff, rising and falling with smoke and embers. When asked about his way of dwelling in stone and entering fire, he does not know—not only does he not know the way, he has even forgotten stone and fire. This is true non-knowing. Then what can stone or fire obstruct?

When Marquis Wen of Wei heard of this, he asked Zixia: "What sort of man is he?"

Zixia replied: "From what I have heard of the Master’s words, one who harmonizes attains great unity with things, so that nothing can harm him. Such a one may wander through metal and stone, tread through water and fire."

The marquis said: "Why do you not do this?"

Zixia said: "To hollow out the heart and discard knowledge—I have not yet attained this. Still, I can speak of it when there is leisure."

The marquis said: "Why does the Master not do this?"

Zixia said: "The Master is capable of it but chooses not to."

The marquis was greatly pleased.

A commentary states: When affirmation and negation complement each other, this is called harmony. To be obstructed by being is not harmony; to conceal oneself in non-being is also not harmony. For non-being is inaction and not principle, while being is obstruction and not the Dao. Only when one neither discards the function of being and non-being yet transcends both can one attain great unity with things.

This great unity means unifying through difference. When difference unifies, nothing is destroyed. The hardness of stone and the fierceness of fire cannot obstruct me. This is the Dao—what Zixia heard from the Master. Though Zixia himself has not yet hollowed out his heart and discarded knowledge, he still has leisure to speak of this. How much more so the Master?

That the Master does not practice this is not because he cannot, but because he chooses not to. Those who cannot, of course, do not practice it. But those who can yet cannot refrain from practicing it are controlled by their own ability and thus cannot truly do it. Only one who can practice yet does not, or practices without knowing why, is free from all inability.

I have often reflected: The man of Zhao who entered stone and fire did so because he did not know. Yet even grasses and trees, which lack knowledge, cannot avoid being scorched or drowned in water and fire, nor obstructed by metal and stone. This is because all things are bound to the Five Phases—wood bends, water weakens, metal hardens, fire blazes, earth balances—and spread through mutual generation, completion, restraint, and control. How can they escape transformation?

Humans are the spirit of the myriad things, endowed with the qi of harmonious balance. They can embody heaven and earth, yin and yang. What is called "not knowing" in them is not the same as the ignorance of things. By embracing harmony and guarding qi, they merge with the myriad things without perceiving difference. Then how could metal and stone, water and fire, ever obstruct their passage?

Look to the creatures of the world: birds soaring in the clouds, fish lurking in the depths, insects nesting in wood, rats moving through fire—all are manifestations of yin and yang. If those who grasp only one aspect of yin and yang can do such things, how much more the sage who unifies with yin and yang, holding creation within himself? He finds fulfillment in all things without needing to act to prove it.

A spirit-medium from Qi arrived in Zheng, named Ji Xian. He could foretell a person’s death or survival, fortune or misfortune, longevity or early demise, predicting the year, month, ten-day period, or day with divine accuracy. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled. Liezi met him and was enchanted. Returning, he told Hujiaozi: "I once thought your Dao was supreme, but now I see there is something even greater."

A commentary states: In ancient times, when people’s essence and spirit were undivided, gods would descend upon them. Among men, such a one was called a *wu* (medium), and if skilled in the Dao, a *shenwu* (divine medium). Named Ji Xian, he was one who had awakened to the Dao in youth. His mastery of the Dao allowed him to see clearly into others, while his youthful sensitivity left no emotion hidden. All beings confined to form are deluded by the calculations of life and death, fortune and misfortune, longevity and early demise. Yet Ji Xian could foresee these before they occurred—this is what the ignorant call the ultimate attainment.

Hujiaozi said: "I have shown you the outward form of the Dao, but not its true substance. Can one truly be said to have attained the Dao if one is like a flock of hens without a rooster—how could they produce eggs? If you set the Dao against the world, you will surely be exposed. That is why he could read your fate."

A commentary states: Form is the manifestation of affairs; substance is the reality of the Dao. To have form without substance is like having hens without a rooster—how can one lead all things and transform the myriad beings? Liezi set the Dao against the world, so his fate was certain and his exposure inevitable. Since he had grasped Hujiaozi’s outward form but not his true substance, how could he escape detection?

"Bring him to me and let him observe me."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Hujiaozi. Afterward, Ji Xian said to Liezi: "Alas! Your master is dead. He cannot live. He will not last ten days. I saw something strange—like damp ashes."

Liezi entered, weeping so that his tears soaked the bedding, and told Hujiaozi. Hujiaozi said: "Earlier, I showed him the pattern of earth—stillness without movement. This is likely what Zhuangzi called the *Dudeji* (Virtue Concealed). Bring him again."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Hujiaozi again. Afterward, Ji Xian said to Liezi: "Fortunate that your master met me! He is recovering. There is life in the ashes. I saw the *Duquan* (Virtue Balanced)."

Liezi entered and told Hujiaozi. Hujiaozi said: "Earlier, I showed him the pattern of heaven and earth—where names and realities do not enter, yet vitality stirs from the heels. This is the *Duquan*. He likely saw the *Shanzheji* (Virtue at Ease). Bring him again."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Hujiaozi again. Afterward, Ji Xian said to Liezi: "Your master is not in a state of fasting. I cannot read him. Let him fast, and at dawn I will observe him again."

Liezi entered and told Hujiaozi. Hujiaozi said: "Earlier, I showed him the *Taichong Mozhen* (Great Vacuity Without Trace). This is likely what I call the *Hengqiji* (Qi Balanced). When the *ni* fish swims in a whirlpool, it forms a deep pool; when still water swirls, it forms a deep pool; when flowing water swirls, it forms a deep pool; when overflowing water swirls, it forms a deep pool; when soaking water swirls, it forms a deep pool; when rushing water swirls, it forms a deep pool; when dammed water swirls, it forms a deep pool; when gushing water swirls, it forms a deep pool; when spring water swirls, it forms a deep pool—these are the Nine Abysses.

Bring him again."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Hujiaozi again.

He had not yet steadied himself when he lost his composure and fled. Hujiaozi said: "Pursue him." Liezi chased after him but could not catch up. Returning, he reported to Hujiaozi: "He has vanished, he is gone—I could not reach him."

Hujiaozi said: "Earlier, I showed him what had never yet departed from my source. I moved with him in emptiness and marvelous transformation, so that he knew not who or what I was. Taking me for drifting reeds, taking me for flowing waves, he fled."

The explanation states: What is shown lies with me; what is perceived lies with him. Where there is something shown, there must be something seen. When I showed him the patterns of earth, which incline toward yin, unmoved by shock, unstilled by quietude, he saw my virtue’s subtle stirrings sealed and unexpressed—thus he deemed me lifeless. When I showed him heaven and earth, which incline toward yang, though name and reality did not enter, there was still an "I"; though the subtle stirrings of qi outwardly manifested, their rest was profound—thus he saw my virtue emerge as goodness and deemed me alive. What is called "sealed potential" is this: at first, he saw only the patterns of earth, unaware that these were the sealed stirrings of virtue. Only upon seeing heaven and earth did he then take those sealed stirrings as mere expedience, fitting for the moment.

When I showed him the *Taichong Mozhen* (Great Vacuity Without Trace), perfect harmony surged forth, its bounds unfathomable; the subtle stirrings of qi remained ever-balanced, filling heaven and earth, responding to the myriad changes without ever being disturbed. His abiding in the stillness of the abyss is like the nine abysses of water—thus Ji Xian could find nothing to perceive in me. The nine abysses are the nature of water without human artifice. Though it bends or straightens with the land, takes shape with its vessel, swirls or stills, flows or overflows, rushes downward or seeps sideways, pools and returns, or bursts forth and streams away—though its waves transform ninefold—it remains ever-abyssal and self-possessed. When the Dao reaches this point, one enters the realm of neither death nor life. What Ji Xian knew was only life after birth—how could he fathom this?

Yet even the *Taichong Mozhen* still lodges in qi to reveal its subtleties. When I showed him what had never yet departed from my source, he became one who, though human, could act as heaven; though responding to the myriad things, remained deeply rooted in tranquil extremity. This is why Ji Xian lost himself and fled, and why Liezi could not catch up. The false before the true is like snow and frost melting at the sight of sunlight—thus he said, "He has vanished, he is gone."

Moreover, what had never yet departed from my source still did not leave the source, yet there was still something shown. For even the subtlest Dao does not fail to reveal itself—like sunlight shining everywhere, if the blind do not see, can the fault lie with the sun?

The one called Hujiaozi took emptiness as his essence and dwelt in a lofty central place. Dwelling in the center and on high, he seemed approachable, yet with emptiness as his essence, he never contended with the world—this is why none could fathom him.

Afterward, Liezi, believing he had never yet begun to learn, returned home. For three years he did not go out, cooked for his wife, and ate gruel as if feeding others. He grew indifferent to affairs, polished no more but returned to simplicity, stood alone in his form like an uncarved block, and guarded his heart with vigilance. Thus he ended his days.

The explanation states: "For three years he did not go out"—he cut off from things. "Cooked for his wife and ate gruel as if feeding others"—he forgot himself. "Indifferent to affairs"—he abandoned cunning and habit. "Polished no more but returned to simplicity, stood alone in his form"—he returned to his mandate, reverted to constancy, and let his true singularity shine. "Guarded his heart with vigilance"—though the myriad changes swirled, he kept to the constant path. "Thus he ended his days"—this endured through ten thousand generations unchanged.

Liezi advanced in this Dao, intending to show others how to return to the root—hence these words.

On his way to Qi, Liezi turned back mid-journey and encountered Bohun Maoren. Bohun Maoren asked: "Why have you returned?" Liezi replied: "I was startled. Why should I be startled?"

"I dined at ten porridge-stalls, yet five served me first." Bohun Maoren said: "If this is so, why then are you startled?"

Liezi replied: "When inner sincerity is not dissolved, the body’s demeanor radiates light, and this outward bearing overawes others’ hearts, making them slight the noble and the aged, and correct what troubles them. Now, these porridge-sellers merely trade in gruel for profit, with no great surplus gain; their advantage is meager, their influence slight—yet even so they act thus. How much more so with a lord of ten thousand chariots, who wearies himself for the state and exhausts his wisdom on affairs? He will entrust me with tasks and demand results from me. This is why I am startled."

Bohun Maoren explained: "Human nature is like water: frozen, it becomes ice; melted, it returns to water. Frozen, its form does not change; melted, its use is inexhaustible. The perfected person’s heart is vast as ice about to melt, reverting to its clarity—how could it be overawed by external radiance? But if one inwardly prides oneself on true wisdom, unable to dissolve the entanglements of things, then one’s demeanor becomes twisted and gleams with false light. Others can then scrutinize it closely, and even those as slight in power and profit as porridge-sellers will slight the noble and the aged, and meddle in what troubles them. *Chì* means disorder, referring to the many troubles that arise. Qi is a central state, a place where tasks are assigned and achievements demanded. Thus Liezi, on his way to Qi, was startled by the five porridge-sellers’ precedence and turned back mid-journey.

Yet this startlement differs from the so-called ‘startlement at favor and disgrace.’"

Bohun Maoren said: "Excellent is your observation! If you abide in yourself, others will safeguard you."

Not long after, when he went to visit, the shoes outside the door were filled to overflowing.

Bohun Maoren explained: "To be startled by five porridge-sellers serving first is to be adept at inward observation. ‘Abiding in oneself’ means not neglecting one’s form—this is why the shoes outside the door were so many."

Bohun Maoren stood facing north, leaning heavily on his staff and pressing it against his chin. After standing thus for a while, he departed without a word. The attendant informed Liezi, who rushed barefoot, holding his shoes, and upon reaching the gate asked: "Master, since you have come, have you not neglected your medicine?"

Bohun Maoren explained: "Standing facing north is to discern things. Leaning on his staff and pressing it to his chin is to forget himself. Departing without a word is to forget speech and discernment. Liezi, holding his shoes and rushing barefoot, has not yet silently grasped the wordless teaching; he is quick in seeking the Dao but forgets his feet. Moreover, rushing barefoot shows he has already strayed from ‘abiding in himself.’ The *Zhuangzi*’s *Virtue Complete and Perfect* repeatedly mentions the toe-less and the lame—perhaps this is akin to that."

Liezi said: "Enough. I had already told you: others will safeguard you. And indeed, they have safeguarded you."

You cannot make others safeguard you, yet you cannot prevent others from safeguarding you—why then this attachment to influence? Attachment breeds divergence. And if there must be influence, it unsettles your root and self—what meaning does it hold? Those who wander with you offer no true counsel; clinging to petty words poisons all. Neither awakened nor enlightened, how can they discern one another?

Bohun Maoren explained: One who does not lose their virtue yet has not attained the supreme virtue of non-virtue, who knows the Dao’s traces as something traversable yet has not reached the constant Dao’s effortless mind—such a one cannot prevent others from safeguarding them. And to fret over this inability to prevent safeguarding is already distinct from those who cause others to safeguard them. "Attachment" refers to preemptive action. Liezi never deliberately acted strangely to influence others, yet people were drawn to safeguard him—this must mean he unknowingly influenced them. Once influence takes hold, you exhaust your spirit, disturb your essence, and daily debate with those who leave their shoes at the door—what purpose does it serve? Thus it is said: unsettling your root and self holds no meaning. Those who wander with you are all mired in the exhaustion of human ways, so none can offer true counsel. Their petty chatter, though incessant, how can it serve as medicine for the ultimate teaching? In this state, they are all lost on the same path, where things and self remain unformed. Thus it is said: neither awakened nor enlightened, how can they discern one another?

Yang Zhu traveled south to Pei, while Lao Dan journeyed west to Qin, meeting by chance in the outskirts. By the time they reached Liang, they encountered the Old Master. Midway, the Old Master looked up to heaven and sighed: "At first I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot." Yang Zhu did not respond. Upon reaching their lodging, he brought water for washing, a towel and comb, removed his shoes outside the door, and advanced on his knees, saying: "Earlier, Master, you looked up to heaven and sighed, saying, ‘At first I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot.’ Your disciple wished to ask for your explanation, but your journey left no leisure, so I dared not. Now that you are at rest, may I inquire as to my fault?"

Bohun Maoren explained: Confucius said, "Unless one is a perfected person, one cannot humble oneself before others; and without humbling oneself, one cannot attain true understanding." Bringing water, washing, combing, removing shoes, and advancing on knees—all this was to seek true understanding. The Old Master said: "Your gaze is haughty and overbearing—who could bear to dwell with you?"

Bohun Maoren explained: Nothing in a person surpasses the eyes; the eyes cannot conceal their flaws, for they are separated into two orbs, the dwelling of the spirit. When those who have attained the Dao meet, their eyes meet and their intent is already conveyed. Those who know others often perceive truth in the space between brows and lashes. Thus Yang Zhu’s haughty and overbearing gaze, his improper vision, led the Old Master to deem him unteachable. Therefore, the *Book of the Yellow Emperor* says: "The mechanism lies in the eyes." Utmost clarity appears as disgrace; abundant virtue seems insufficient.

Bohun Maoren explained: Comprehending all four directions is called utmost clarity. The more one lowers oneself, the more evident it becomes—this is what appears as disgrace. Empty yet never full, thus abundant virtue seems insufficient. One who embodies this meets others with a gaze so unassuming it seems not to see them at all—how could there be haughtiness or overbearingness? Yang Zhu, his expression suddenly transformed, said: "I respectfully receive your instruction."

On his outward journey, when he stayed at an inn, the host welcomed him with the family, the master held the mat, the wife held the towel and comb, the guests yielded their seats, and those tending the stove withdrew. On his return, the inn’s guests vied with him for seats.

Bohun Maoren explained: Yielding a seat is not merely about non-contention. Vying for a seat is not merely about non-yielding. Upon hearing the teaching and returning, those who once yielded now contend. The distance between Qin and Liang is not great; the inn’s guests are the same as before, nor is there a second Yang Zhu. Yet their encounter changed so abruptly—one realizes then how the subtlest Dao operates in the ordinary, transforming form and altering perception, though people constantly rely on it without awareness. Traveling south to Pei, he hastened toward the convergence of things; on his return, he reverted to the root of his destiny. Whether yielding or contending depends solely on the direction of his journey.

Yang Zhu passed through Song and stayed at an inn on his way east. The innkeeper had two concubines—one beautiful, one ugly. The ugly one was honored, while the beautiful one was despised. Yang Zhu asked the reason. The innkeeper’s servant replied: "The beautiful one is beautiful in her own eyes, but I do not see her beauty; the ugly one is ugly in her own eyes, but I do not see her ugliness." Yang Zhu said: "Disciples, take note: act with worthiness but discard the conduct of self-regard—wherever you go, will you not be loved?"

Bohun Maoren explained: Honoring the beautiful and despising the ugly stems from personal bias; honoring the ugly and despising the beautiful reflects the impartiality of the Dao. Beauty and ugliness arise from deluded perception; honoring and despising them are not true principles. What is despised in the innkeeper’s concubine is not her beauty itself, but her reliance on it to be despised. What is honored in the innkeeper is not his ability to despise beauty, but his unawareness of it.
