If the innkeeper’s concubine did not rely on her beauty and lacked self-regarding conduct, there would be nowhere she would not be loved. The Old Master said: "When all under heaven know beauty as beauty, ugliness arises." It is not that beauty is despised, but that knowing it as beauty is despised.

Under heaven, there are ways that always prevail and ways that do not always prevail. The way that always prevails is called softness; the way that does not always prevail is called strength. Both are known, yet people do not understand them. Thus the ancients said: "Strength first appears in those not equal to oneself; when it reaches those equal to oneself, it is perilous. Softness first emerges from oneself; it is never perilous. With this, one prevails over individuals and multitudes alike. With this, one bears the world as if bearing nothing—this is called prevailing without striving to prevail, bearing without striving to bear."

Zhouzi said: "To desire hardness, one must guard with softness; to desire strength, one must preserve with weakness. Accumulating softness yields hardness; accumulating weakness yields strength. Observe what is accumulated, and you will know the abode of fortune and misfortune. Strength prevails over those not equal to oneself, but when it reaches those equal to oneself, it is brittle. Softness prevails by emerging from oneself—its power is immeasurable."

Lao Dan said: "When weapons are strong, they perish; when wood is strong, it breaks. The soft and weak are the companions of life; the hard and strong are the companions of death."

Explanation: The soft and weak are the root of the Dao. Guarding the root of the Dao is nothing more than self-mastery, and thus there is nothing it does not overcome. With this, one overcomes the self; with this, one bears the world—secure and without peril, blessed and without misfortune. Though one has never sought to precede others, none can precede them. This is the way of those who skillfully nurture life, abiding as long as heaven and earth.

The *Book of Changes* says: "Compliance is the means to exercise authority."
Zhuangzi says: "In this, the fish finds its advantage."
This principle accords with the present teaching.

Thus, Laozi and Zhouzi, in their writings, repeatedly emphasize this intent. When they cite the words of high antiquity, it is to show that this Dao has endured since ancient times. Appearance need not be childlike for wisdom to be childlike; wisdom need not be childlike for appearance to be childlike. The sage embraces childlike wisdom and discards childlike appearance, while the multitude clings to childlike appearance and distances itself from childlike wisdom. Those whose appearance resembles their own, they draw near and love; those whose appearance differs, they distance and fear.

A being with a seven-foot frame, differentiated hands and feet, hair on the head and teeth in the mouth, standing and moving—this is called a human. Yet humans are not without the hearts of beasts. Though they may possess the hearts of beasts, by appearance they are deemed kin. Creatures with wings and horns, fangs and claws spread wide, flying aloft or crouching to run—these are called birds and beasts. Yet birds and beasts are not without the hearts of humans. Though they may possess the hearts of humans, by appearance they are deemed distant.

Fu Xi, Nü Wa, Shennong, and the Xia sovereigns had serpent bodies and human faces, ox heads and tiger noses—these bore appearances unlike humans, yet possessed the great virtue of sages. Jie of Xia, Zhou of Yin, Duke Huan of Lu, and King Mu of Chu had the seven apertures of human features, identical to others in form, yet harbored the hearts of birds and beasts. Thus, the multitude, clinging to a single appearance in search of supreme wisdom, will never approach it.

Explanation: The so-called "appearances unlike humans" refer to forms that closely resemble others—such as wasps with the eyes of weasels or jackals with the voices of wolves, tigers with swallow-like jaws—examples recorded in recent histories, available for verification. These words align with the marvels of creation, yet people, deluded by shallow understanding, fail to grasp the profundity of childlike wisdom.

Liezi sought to clarify the ultimate Dao, and so spoke boldly, valuing only the truth of principle, heedless of the astonishment of the multitude. When the Yellow Emperor battled the Flame Emperor at the wilds of Banquan, he led bears, brown bears, wolves, leopards, lynxes, and tigers as his vanguard, while eagles, falcons, hawks, and kites served as his banners—this was commanding birds and beasts by force. When Yao entrusted Kui with music, striking and tapping stones, the hundred beasts danced in unison; when the nine movements of the *Xiaoshao* were complete, the phoenix came to pay homage—this was drawing birds and beasts by sound. If so, how do the hearts of birds and beasts differ from those of humans? Their forms and sounds differ from humans, yet people do not know the way to connect with them. The sage, knowing all and penetrating all, thus draws them forth and commands them.

Explanation: Observing the nine movements of the *Xiaoshao* and the phoenix’s homage, we see that commanding birds and beasts by force is no falsehood. The wisdom of birds and beasts naturally aligns with that of humans in some respects. In ordering their lives, they do not borrow wisdom from humans: male and female pair, mother and child bond; they avoid level ground and seek perilous heights, shun cold and seek warmth; when dwelling, they form groups, when moving, they arrange in ranks; the small stay within, the strong guard without; when drinking, they lead one another, when eating, they call in flocks.

In the age of great antiquity, they dwelled alongside humans and walked among them. In the age of emperors and kings, they began to scatter in fear and confusion. By the degenerate age, they hid and fled, evading harm. Now, in the land of the Jie people to the east, there are those who frequently understand the speech of the six domestic animals—this is the achievement of partial wisdom. The divine sages of great antiquity fully knew the dispositions of all things and completely understood the sounds of different species. They gathered and assembled them, trained and instructed them, making them equal to humans. Thus, they first united with spirits and demons, then connected with the peoples of the eight directions, and finally gathered birds, beasts, insects, and moths. This shows that the hearts and minds of creatures with blood and qi are not so distant.

The divine sages, knowing this, left nothing untaught in their instruction.

A commentary states: The stately strides of emperors and kings—whether the offices of the Yu clan or the courts of Tang and Wu—show that with each descending age, the Dao grows ever more constricted. In the time of great antiquity, birds and beasts dwelt alongside humans, walking among them without suspicion. As the ages declined and the Dao narrowed, they first grew startled and scattered, then fled and hid in concealment. That creatures of different kinds once lived and moved among humans—though this was so in the age of great antiquity—was also because the divine sages of that time had the means to gather and instruct them. Yet while the ages of past and present differ, how are the divine sages of old and present distinct? Later ages have also seen divine sages, yet we hear of none who gathered birds, beasts, insects, and moths to instruct them. Why is this? It is because the ages have declined, and creatures of different kinds, once startled, scattered, and hidden, can no longer be reassembled. Even with a sage ruler, one can only make birds, beasts, fish, and turtles docile. Thus, only when one has attained the age of a sage can one speak of the sage’s Way.

In Song there was a man called Master Ju, who loved macaques and kept them in troops. He understood their intentions, and they, in turn, grasped his. He reduced his household’s provisions to satisfy the macaques’ desires. Soon, however, he grew impoverished and sought to limit their food. Fearing the macaques would not submit, he first deceived them, saying: “Shall I give you taro—three in the morning and four at dusk—will that suffice?” The macaques all rose in anger. Then he said: “Shall I give you taro—four in the morning and three at dusk—will that suffice?” The macaques all prostrated themselves in delight. All things that are ensnared by cunning and baseness are like this. The sage, using wisdom to ensnare the multitude of fools, is no different from Master Ju using wisdom to ensnare his macaques. The name and reality remain unchanged, yet he makes them rejoice or rage at will.

A commentary states: The use of joy and anger never hits the mark. Joy inevitably turns to anger, and anger invariably reverts to joy. When Master Ju allotted taro, the name and reality were not diminished. Seeking to limit their food, he made them prostrate in delight, but first had to deceive them into rising in anger—thus he ultimately achieved their joy. Had he immediately given them four in the morning and three at dusk, not only would he not have gained their joy, they would surely have risen in anger. Moreover, the macaques’ joy and anger were not feigned. The numbers three and four, whether combined or separated, still make seven. Considering the macaques’ minds, they would rather eat more in the morning and less at dusk. Thus, the abundance or scarcity in morning and dusk—how could joy and anger not be employed?

It has been said that since the Great Simplicity divided, it transformed into seven—the numbers of heaven (three) and earth (four), one odd and one even, giving birth and completing all things. Nothing escapes this number. Those who do not understand it are enslaved by it; those not deluded by it can wield it. This is not only true of the macaques’ joy and anger. The governance of hegemons through pleasure and indulgence is deemed shameful at the gates of sages because they ensnare with cunning and baseness. Not only is this true of hegemons’ governance—even the sage’s response to emperors and kings cannot escape using the world as a cage. The difference lies only in their subtle and wondrous use of numbers, hence the depth or shallowness of their governance’s effects. Pushing this to its extreme, even the transformations of heaven and earth—day and night, waking and dreaming, cold and heat, death and life—cannot alter the number of the Great Expansion’s seven times seven, leaving one void. Thus, to call the macaques’ joy and anger delusion is to call all things delusion. To deem human joy and anger correct—how can the macaques’ joy and anger alone be wrong? People laugh at the macaques ensnared by Master Ju, yet they themselves have often been in cages without realizing it and thus do not laugh—this, too, is delusion. Therefore, for humans to laugh at macaques is like the *Mencius*’s analogy of the fifty-pace soldier mocking the hundred-pace one.

Moreover, the macaque is a creature skilled in its cunning, relying on its agility, and difficult to tame. Yet it is deluded by the alternating numbers of three and four and does not awaken—how much more so for other creatures? This may be why Liezi specifically took up the meaning of Master Ju’s taro allotment. Nevertheless, Master Ju, by reducing his household’s provisions to the point of poverty, had no choice but to limit the macaques’ food. From this, we know that the sage, using wisdom to ensnare the multitude of fools, does so because the ages have declined and the Dao is insufficient—is this not also a matter of necessity?

Master Jishengzi raised fighting cocks for King Xuan of Zhou. After ten days, the king asked: “Are the cocks ready to fight?” He replied: “Not yet. They are still vain and arrogant, relying on their qi.” After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: “Not yet. They still respond to shadows.” After another ten days, the king asked again. He replied: “Not yet. They still glare fiercely and bristle with qi.” After another ten days, the king asked again. He said: “Almost. Though other cocks may crow, this one shows no change. From a distance, it resembles a wooden cock. Its virtue is complete. No other cock dares respond—it simply turns and flees.”

A commentary states: The cock is a creature of wood. When wood rubs against wood, it ignites, hence the cock is a creature fond of fighting. Vanity and arrogance give it a haughty demeanor; reliance on qi gives it a domineering will. Though it has never seen another cock, it seeks opponents relentlessly. In such a state, it may be fearless, but can it be certain of victory? Thus, when asked if it was ready to fight, he said: “Not yet. It still responds to shadows.” It no longer relies on its qi but merely reacts to the direction of shadows. “It still glares fiercely and bristles with qi.” It no longer chases the delusion of shadows but merely responds to their direction. When an opponent arrives, it marshals its courage and waits. Yet its fierce glare unsettles its spirit, and its bristling qi disturbs its heart. Though it does not fight, its heart stirs within, and its spirit falters without—it merely endures without acting. Thus, he said: “Not yet.” Only when it was nearly ready...

When the cock crows thereafter without alteration, gazing upon it, it resembles a wooden kite—its virtue is complete. Within, it forgets its qi; without, it forgets its form. Though other cocks stand tall and crow, it no longer hears their voices—how does it differ from withered wood? Thus, the virtue of non-contention reaches its zenith, and so it is called complete. No cock under heaven can rival it; they but turn and flee in admiration.

Like the Supreme One’s pacification of the Hu barbarians, or Confucius’ mastery over his disciples.

The heart is the Crimson Palace, among birds, the Vermilion Bird. The sage nurtures it within, ensuring sufficiency without greed, satiety without contention—thus none under heaven can contend with him. The common folk merely cultivate externally, leaving the Vermilion Bird ever hungry, soaring across the eight wilds, seeking only profit. Without greed, it cannot fill its heart; without contention, it cannot satisfy its desires—only in death does it cease. How lamentable!

Hui Ang appeared before King Kang of Song.

King Kang stamped his feet and coughed sharply, speaking rapidly: "What pleases me are the brave and the strong—I take no delight in those who preach benevolence and righteousness. What instruction do you have for me?"

Hui Ang replied: "I possess a Way that renders men, though brave, unable to pierce with their blades, and though strong, unable to strike true. Does Your Majesty not wish to hear of it?"

The King of Song said: "Excellent! This is precisely what I long to hear."

Hui Ang continued: "Yet if blades fail to pierce and strikes miss their mark, this is still a form of humiliation. I possess another Way that makes men, though brave, dare not strike, and though strong, dare not attack. This ‘daring not’ does not mean they lack the will. I possess yet another Way that eradicates their will entirely. Without will, there can be no thought of love or profit. And I possess a Way that makes every man and woman under heaven joyfully desire to love and benefit one another. This surpasses mere bravery and strength—it stands above all four burdens. Does Your Majesty not wish to hear of it?"

The King of Song said: "This is what I truly desire to attain."

Hui Ang replied: "Such was the way of Confucius and Mozi. Though Confucius and Mo Di held no land as lords nor offices as ministers, every man and woman under heaven craned their necks and stood on tiptoe, yearning for their peace and benefit. Now, Your Majesty rules a realm of ten thousand chariots. If you truly embrace this will, all within your four borders will reap its benefits—your virtue will far surpass that of Confucius and Mozi."

The King of Song had no reply. Hui Ang bowed and withdrew.

The King then said to his attendants: "How persuasive the guest was in convincing me!"

**Exegesis:**
King Kang of Song delighted in valor and strength. When Hui Ang first appeared, the king stamped his feet, coughed sharply, and spoke harshly, resenting the imposition of benevolence and righteousness. His demeanor suggested resistance. Yet Hui Ang, seizing upon the king’s fondness for strength, tailored his words to that preference, gradually advancing his argument until he arrived at the teachings of Confucius and Mozi. Had the king accepted his words, he would have contradicted his own inclinations; had he rejected them, he would have found no fault in their reasoning. Thus, after Hui Ang’s departure, the king merely remarked to his attendants on the guest’s eloquence.

Let us consider this: within the realm of persuasive speech, there is no fixed right or wrong. Success makes one the head; failure, the tail. Take the likes of Zhang Yi and Su Qin, who mastered the art of vertical and horizontal alliances and roamed the Warring States. Though the lords of the six states recognized their eloquence, none could refute their arguments, trust their offices, or employ their strategies—yet they brought peace to the Central States for over a decade. What, then, is the true measure of such persuasion’s right or wrong? Even the *Liezi* is not free from rhetorical flourish; this passage merely reflects one facet of its author’s intent.

**The Yellow Emperor’s Exegesis**
Zhuangzi regarded the achievements of emperors and kings as mere byproducts of the sage’s work. To call them "byproducts" is not to dismiss their worth but to acknowledge that the sage’s Way is vast and profound, while the deeds of emperors and kings are but chaff and dregs. For the sage is a sage precisely because of such achievements, and the world looks up to the sage because of such governance. Thus, Zhuangzi also said: "Nothing is more divine than Heaven, nothing richer than Earth, nothing greater than emperors and kings." Heaven’s divinity and Earth’s abundance manifest in auspicious omens and transformations. How, then, can the sage’s stillness, which moves and penetrates the world’s affairs to respond as an emperor or king, ever be set aside? This is why the *Yellow Emperor* chapter was written.

After the decline of the Divine Farmer, the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun arose. The Dao emerged as virtue, and the august descended as emperors. The Yellow Emperor, the first of the emperors, though burdened with worldly affairs, did not yet match Yao’s magnanimity; though possessed of salvific virtue, he did not yet equal Shun’s luminous power. Yet having grasped the world’s patterns, he began to contemplate their origins—and thus arose the changes of fortune and misfortune.

At first, he rejoiced in the world’s devotion, but later grew anxious over its disorder. The five emotions roiled within, his complexion and flesh withered without. Had he not purified his mind and embodied the Dao, how could he have withstood the world’s ultimate changes, faced its greatest difficulties, and avoided the realm of misfortune, regret, and shame? Only the Yellow Emperor, by aligning his heart and form with the supreme Way, remained untouched by disgrace and harm, even as he traversed the paths of glory and peril. His governance nearly rivaled the land of Huaxu. The deeds of the divine beings of Mount Gushe mirrored the Yellow Emperor’s achievements; Liezi’s riding the wind embodied his supreme Way.

The perfected preserve pure qi; the sage conceals himself in Heaven. Shang Qiu Kai’s harmony with all things, Liang Yang’s indifference to favor or disfavor—all stem from the root of their power to inspire the myriad beings. The ferryman’s mastery of the oar, the swimmer’s dance in the rushing waters, the hunchback’s capture of cicadas, the Zhao man’s handling of stone and fire—all exemplify their resonance with the world. Through such resonance, what in the world could burden them? Through such inspiration, what among the myriad beings could disturb them? Such was the Yellow Emperor’s Way as an emperor.

Thus, in his discourse on the Dao, Liezi frequently cited the *Book of the Yellow Emperor* as its foundation. The chapter concluding with "Responding to Emperors and Kings" carries the same intent. Yet though Liezi composed the *Yellow Emperor* chapter, when he later praised the words of Yang Zhu, he claimed that the deeds of the Five Emperors were as indistinct as dreams, merging the worthy and the foolish in oblivion.

Of its remarks on Yao and Shun, it likewise declares them to have rotted alongside Jie and Robber Zhi. For when viewed through the Dao, even the Yellow Emperor’s governance did not depart from the Way; yet when observed through the achievements of emperors and kings, the true form of the Great Dao had already receded. How fleeting were their traces of merit and fame! Why should they not move with the times and perish together? In praising the words of Yang Zhu, did he not mean to discard the Yellow Emperor’s fame and merit while preserving the Great Dao?

*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Virtue, Scroll Four Concluded*
*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Virtue, Scroll Five*
*Presented by Your Subject Jiang Yue, Inner Student of the Hangzhou Prefectural School of the Song Dynasty*

**King Mu**

In the time of King Mu of Zhou, a *huaren* arrived from the westernmost land. He entered water and fire unscathed, pierced metal and stone; he reversed mountains and rivers, shifted cities and towns. Riding the void, he did not fall; touching the solid, he met no obstacle. His transformations were boundless, inexhaustible. Having altered the forms of things, he also changed the thoughts of men. King Mu revered him as a spirit, served him as a lord, prepared an open-air lodge for his dwelling, offered the three sacrificial beasts, and selected singing girls to entertain him. The *huaren* deemed the king’s palace crude and unfit to inhabit, his kitchen fare rank and inedible, his consorts foul and unlovely.

King Mu then rebuilt for him, sparing no craft in earth and timber, adorning the structure with ochre and whitewash. The five treasuries were emptied before the terrace was completed. Towering a thousand *ren*, it overlooked the summit of Zhongnan, and was named the Terrace of Central Heaven. He selected the most graceful maidens from Zheng and Wei—lithe and supple—anointed them with fragrant oils, painted their brows, adorned them with hairpins and earrings, dressed them in fine silk, trailing skirts of white linen, powdered and rouged, girdled with jade and rings. He filled the terrace with their fragrance and played the melodies *Steaming Clouds*, *Six Radiance*, *Nine Shao*, and *Morning Dew* to delight him. Month after month, jade robes were presented; day after day, jade viands were offered. Yet the *huaren* remained unmoved. Reluctantly, he descended to receive them.

Before long, he invited the king to journey with him. The king grasped the *huaren*’s sleeve, and together they soared upward until they reached the midpoint of Central Heaven, then arrived at the *huaren*’s palace. Its halls were wrought of gold and silver, strung with pearls and jade. It rose above the clouds and rain, its foundations unseen, appearing like a mass of clouds suspended in the azure. All that the eyes beheld, the ears heard, the nose inhaled, the mouth tasted—none of it belonged to the human realm. The king truly believed it to be the Purple Tenuity of the Clear Capital, the Celestial Vault of Broad Music, the abode of the Emperor on High.

When the king looked down, the palaces below seemed but heaps of earth and piles of brushwood. He had lived there for decades without a thought for his kingdom.

**Explication:**
The distance between what lies beyond the world and what lies within it is vast indeed. King Mu dwelt within the world; the *huaren* came from beyond it. The West governs metal, and metal is malleable—thus the *huaren* necessarily arrived from the westernmost land. Things fundamentally have no fixed form; the *huaren*, as master of creation, originates from the Great Void. Neither the six directions nor the five phases could restrain him. Thus he could gather heaven and earth within a millet grain or compress an eon into a single moment, with boundless room to spare. Entering water and fire, piercing metal and stone, reversing mountains and rivers, shifting cities and towns, altering the forms of things, changing the thoughts of men—these were but trivial matters to him.

King Mu, in his dotage, saw all things as sentient. His mind was not empty, so he was astonished by wonders and revered the *huaren* as a spirit, serving him as a lord. He exposed his bedding to the open air, offered the three sacrificial beasts, and selected singing girls, hoping to please him—yet he did not understand that what the *huaren* delighted in was true joy, which is joy without joy. Instead, he deemed the king’s offerings crude and foul, bowing his head in disdain without a glance.

The king then built the Terrace of Central Heaven atop Zhongnan, a thousand *ren* high, and filled it with maidens from Zheng and Wei. He played *Steaming Clouds*, *Six Radiance*, *Nine Shao*, and *Morning Dew* to entertain him, presented jade robes, and offered jade viands—yet he did not grasp that the *huaren* dwelt in no dwelling, savored no flavors, perceived no colors, heard no sounds. How could he take pleasure in what pleased men?

Seeing the king’s earnest and reverent heart, the *huaren* deemed him teachable, though not yet ready to ascend to the highest Dao. He tested him by gradually guiding him into the lesser vehicle, taking his sleeve to journey together. They ascended halfway to Central Heaven, arriving at the *huaren*’s palace, wrought of gold and silver, strung with pearls and jade. It floated beyond the clouds, with nothing above to grasp or below to support it, like a mass of clouds suspended in the azure, never falling.

All that the eyes beheld, the ears heard, the nose inhaled, the mouth tasted—none of it existed in the mortal world. The king believed it to be the Purple Tenuity of the Clear Capital, the Celestial Vault of Broad Music, the palace of the Emperor on High. Yet when he looked down from above, he saw that his former palaces were but heaps of earth and piles of brushwood—no different from the nests of bees or ants. Thus he abandoned the base and foul, seeking the lofty and pure, unwilling to return to his former capital. He lingered there for decades.

Alas! King Mu was once a companion of the Cinnabar Terrace, exiled to the mortal realm. The dust of the world had not yet deeply stained him, so he could dwell in the sacred realm. Even this lesser abode—could those born of flesh ever reach it? Even if they could, their souls would tremble and their spirits scatter, and they would seek to return.

The *huaren* again invited the king to journey with him. Wherever they went, he could neither see the sun and moon above nor the rivers and seas below. The play of light dazzled his eyes, and he could not see; the sounds assailed his ears, and he could not hear. His limbs and organs quaked, his mind wandered, his essence fled. He begged the *huaren* to let him return.

The *huaren* transported him, and the king fell as though into the void.

When he awoke, he was still seated where he had been before, and his attendants remained as they were. Before him, the wine had not yet cleared, nor had the dishes spoiled. The king asked where he had come from, and his attendants replied, "Your Majesty merely entered stillness."

From this, King Mu remained disoriented for three months before recovering. He questioned the *huaren* again, who said, "I journeyed with Your Majesty in spirit—how could your form have moved? Moreover, where we dwelled before—was it not your palace? Where we wandered—was it not your garden? What you deemed extraordinary was but a fleeting lapse in your usual state. At the extremes of transformation, in the space between swift and slow, can all be grasped?"

The king was overjoyed. He neglected state affairs, took no pleasure in his ministers or concubines, and indulged in distant travels. He commanded his chariot drawn by the Eight Steeds: on the right rein, *Lai* and *Liu*, and on the left, *Lü’er*; on the right trace, *Chiji*, and on the left, *Baiyi*. The charioteer was Zaofu, with *Yi* as his right-hand guard. The second chariot bore on its right rein *Quhuang* and on its left *Yulun*, with *Daoli* on the left trace and *Shanzi* on the right. The charioteer was Boyao, with Sanbai driving and Benrong as his guard.

They galloped a thousand *li* to the land of the Giant Sou clan. The Giant Sou presented the king with white crane’s blood to drink and offered the milk of cows and horses to wash his feet, as well as those of his two charioteers. After drinking, they departed and lodged at the slopes of Kunlun, on the sunny side of the Red Water. The next day, they ascended Kunlun’s peak to behold the palace of the Yellow Emperor and sealed it to bequeath to later generations. Then they were received as guests by the Queen Mother of the West, who feasted with the king upon the Jasper Pool. The Queen Mother sang for the king, and he harmonized with her—her song was mournful.

There, he beheld the place where the sun sets. In a single day, they traveled ten thousand *li*, and the king sighed, saying, "Alas! I, alone, am not replete in virtue yet am content in pleasure. Will later generations recount my faults?" King Mu was nearly a divine being—able to exhaust the joys of his lifetime, as though he had ascended in a hundred years.

**Explanation:**
The Way of the *huaren* is endlessly transformative, yet it does not depart from the One. Only those who attain this ultimate Oneness can truly realize the Way, leaping in an instant into the realm of nothingness. For gradual learners, their capacity is not yet ready to be instructed in the supreme Way of the sages. Thus, the *huaren* provisionally displayed the transformations of the middle heavens, allowing the king to witness the insubstantiality of the human world, to recognize the weariness of worldly burdens, and to forget his kingdom—only then did he reveal the true realm of the supreme Way.

Where he looked up and saw no sun or moon, height was insufficient to describe it; where he looked down and saw no rivers or seas, depth was insufficient to name it. The radiance that dazzled his eyes—his vision was confounded and could not see—yet the light of heaven arises within, to be perceived by spirit, not by the eyes. The sounds that assailed his ears—his hearing was overwhelmed and could not listen—yet the music of heaven resounds of itself, to be heard by *qi*, not by the ears. Thus, beholding the *huaren*’s palace was like gazing upon one’s own kingdom from the middle heavens.

King Mu was not yet ready to advance further, so his heart was unmoored, his spirit scattered, his mind bewildered, his essence lost—he begged the *huaren* to let him return. When the *huaren* again invited the king to journey with him, the places they reached were no different from the middle heavens, yet his perceptions were so altered. For the wondrous Way abides not apart from the present place, leaping beyond all existence. It was not merely unlike the *huaren*’s palace—where he dwelled, where he wandered, was no different from the king’s own palace or garden.

From this, we know that the distinction between the mad and the sage lies in name alone, not in reality; people are deluded, but the Way is not. It is like journeying in spirit while the form remains unmoved. When the *huaren* came from the western extremity, did he seek to display marvels and astonish the vulgar? No—his aim was to guide all people toward the wondrous Way. Had King Mu first awakened to the Way, the governance of the Yellow Emperor and the land of Huaxu might have been within reach—how could it have been merely his own amusement?

When King Mu emptied his five organs to serve the *huaren*, the *huaren* still did not rejoice. Did the *huaren* truly delight in the pleasures of sound, form, scent, or taste? No—he wished the king to awaken through these to the joylessness of worldly pleasures. Failing that, the *huaren* journeyed with him in spirit, displaying illusions to reveal the spontaneous arising and vanishing of transformations, that he might awaken to the natural principle of the spirit. Yet the king mistook these provisional displays for the Purple Tenuity of the Clear Capital and the grand music of the Highest Heaven, taking them as true delights—this was even further from the mark.

When the *huaren* again invited the king to journey with him, he revealed the true realm of the Way. Yet King Mu could not advance further; he begged the *huaren* to let him return. Why? The practice of the wondrous Way transcends form and body—how could one who has not attained the Way or realized its principle dwell in its domain? The *huaren* knew he would never awaken, so when the king sought to return, he did not stop him.

Nevertheless, when the *huaren* transported him, the king fell as though into the void—this too was a means to awaken him. Yet King Mu ultimately did not awaken. Thus, upon waking, he remained disoriented for three months. However, from this he recovered, questioned the *huaren* again, and was told of the principle of spiritual wandering. Only then did he begin to grasp the principle of transformation and rejoice.

Thereafter, he neglected state affairs and abandoned worldly concerns, took no pleasure in his ministers or concubines and distanced himself from others, indulged his desires without restraint, and wandered far without limit. Commanding his chariot drawn by the Eight Steeds, he galloped without bound. Charioteers like Zaofu, unparalleled in their generation, and the Eight Steeds, not of ordinary stock—traveling ten thousand *li* in a single day—how could the flight of wind and clouds compare to their swiftness?

Galloping a thousand *li*, he arrived at the land of Minister Sou. *Sou* means "to select," for he was still galloping and choosing his direction. The white crane is a creature of purity, soaring high; oxen and horses are beasts that bear heavy burdens and travel far. Offering the blood of a white crane for the king to drink was meant to alter his thoughts and urge him toward loftiness. Preparing the fresh milk of oxen and horses to wash the king’s feet was to cleanse his form and enable him to journey afar.

Kunlun is the mountain of the westernmost reaches. To call it Kunlun is to liken it to the Dao’s soaring brilliance and primordial vastness. North of the Red Water—*yang* containing *yin*—is the image of the mysterious and wondrous. At first, he reached the land of the Great Sou, but passed through without lingering. Lodging at the foothills of Kunlun and north of the Red Water, he merely sojourned briefly without staying long. On another day, he ascended Kunlun’s peak—this was progress in the Dao.

*Zhuangzi* speaks of Zhili Shu observing the hills of Mingbo, where the ruins of Kunlun served as the Yellow Emperor’s resting place. It tells how the Yellow Emperor, gazing south from Kunlun’s peak, returned only to lose his dark pearl—thus the symbolic significance of Kunlun as the Dao is clear. If King Mu could ascend Kunlun’s peak, then his unrestrained wanderings indeed reached far. Yet his travels could not dispense with chariots and horses, unlike the spirit-wandering of the *huaren*. Thus, though he traveled ten thousand *li* in a day, his journey remained measurable in time. Though he entered the westernmost reaches, he never surpassed Kunlun. He did not roam the Great Void and could not reach the land from which the *huaren* came.

The Yellow Emperor was a sage of the highest order, yet even when he sealed himself in his palace, none could see him. The Queen Mother of the West, a lineage of immortals and sages, though not bound by worldly rule, received him as a guest. They raised cups to each other at Yaochi, and she sang to him—surely he could have become intoxicated with the Dao and attained its joy. Yet King Mu’s harmonizing song was mournful, for he could only exhaust the pleasures of his own lifetime and did not attain the celestial joy.

Observing where the sun sets—the sun being a metaphor for the Dao—*Zhuangzi* describes the ten suns rising together, illuminating all things, as the flourishing of virtue. Thus, the sun’s setting in the westernmost reaches may symbolize the sage gathering the Dao and returning to the great origin and primordial source. Yet merely observing where the sun sets is only observation, not the attainment of the Dao. Thus, in the end, he sighed that his virtue was not full and his joy not complete.

*The Book of Zhou* records that he lived to a hundred, senile and dissolute—this is how later generations tallied his faults. Yet he exhausted the joys of his own lifetime and attained the great fast of longevity, which is what the world calls "ascending to the Dao through refinement." Thus, when later scholars examined the bamboo slips gnawed by bookworms, searched the stone chambers, and consulted *The Golden Cord*, *The Classic of Mountains*, *The Approaching Elegance*, and *The Great Commentary*, all recorded his tale.

I have said that the Yellow Emperor’s dream was a spirit-journey, and King Mu’s transformation was also a spirit-wandering—they were equal in their dreamlike nature. Yet their effects on governance differed: the Yellow Emperor’s dream arose from fasting the mind and disciplining the form, while King Mu’s transformation was merely a change wrought by the *huaren*. Moreover, the Yellow Emperor awoke naturally, whereas King Mu, unable to do so, had to ask the *huaren* to return. This is why the Yellow Emperor, upon waking, was content and at ease, achieving the governance of Huaxu, while King Mu, upon waking, remained disoriented for three months and could only exhaust the pleasures of his own lifetime.

Lao Chengzi studied illusion under Master Yin Wen for three years, yet was not taught anything. Lao Chengzi asked to be told his faults and sought to leave. Master Yin Wen bowed and led him into a chamber, dismissed his attendants, and spoke to him, explaining: "To desire to study illusion is to wish to use illusion to return and learn from illusion. After three years, could the extremes of transformation and illusion be fully imitated? Not teaching you the art for three years was itself the teaching."

Lao Chengzi could not fathom the principle of unspoken delight and instead asked to be told his faults and leave. Thus, Master Yin Wen had no choice but to speak to him, bowing and leading him into the chamber. The Dao is profound, so he dismissed his attendants to speak privately—those not meant to know were kept from seeing or hearing. Lao Chengzi was one who heard the great Dao late in life yet achieved mastery, so though he initially asked to be told his faults and leave, he ultimately transmitted the art. Master Yin Wen had inwardly attained the Dao and displayed this refinement to guide the masses, which is why Lao Chengzi studied under him.

He said: "When Lao Dan departed for the west, he turned to me and said: 'The *qi* of living beings, the forms of material things—all are illusion. What the Creator begins, what *yin* and *yang* transform, is called life and death. Exhausting the numbers and grasping change, following form to shift—this is called transformation, called illusion. The Creator’s skill is subtle, its work profound, inherently difficult to exhaust or complete. Following form, its skill is manifest, its work shallow, thus it arises and vanishes in an instant. Only when you understand that illusion and transformation are no different from life and death can you begin to study illusion. You and I are also illusion—what need is there to study?'"

The explanation: Life, death, transformation, and illusion—when discussed in general terms—are like a body’s shadow, like bubbles on water, like dew on garlic chives, like lightning’s flash: all are illusion. The subtle and profound, difficult to exhaust or complete, are called life and death. The manifest and shallow, arising and vanishing in an instant, are called transformation and illusion. To call them so is to name them based on their function. Thus, though their subtlety differs, their skill is the same; though their depth varies, their work is equal."

Then how do transformation and illusion differ from life and death? Only when you know that transformation and illusion are no different from life and death can life and death no longer be considered great. Transformation and illusion cannot be called shallow, nor can life and death alter transformation—transformation arises from the self. Thus, those who study illusion must base themselves on knowing illusion.

Yet knowing does not depart from awareness. To speak of the aware is not to depart from the realm of illusion, and even the unaware do not depart from illusion. Therefore, to study illusion through knowing is to use illusion to transform illusion. Though things may transform, both things and self remain within illusion. Once one possesses the knowledge of studying illusion, one already falls into the realm of illusion.

Only those who truly awaken through their inherent nature can extinguish all illusions. Without any deliberate intent, what need is there to study?

Thus, Master Yinwen’s refusal to instruct Laochengzi was the act of one who truly understood illusion. Laochengzi returned and, applying Master Yinwen’s words, pondered deeply for three months. Thereupon, he gained mastery over existence and nonexistence, commanding the seasons to shift at will. In winter, he summoned thunder; in summer, he conjured ice. What flew he made walk, and what walked he made fly.

Throughout his life, he never clung to his arts, and thus the world never transmitted them.

**Explanation:** That which adheres to form is never free from illusion. Though the workings of creation are wondrous, they do not depart from illusion. The illusions of creation arise constantly through human agency; the illusions of human agency, in turn, depend on heavenly principle. The illusions of creation do not depart from yin and yang; the transformations of humans do not depart from numerical change. Governed by yin and yang, even the real becomes illusion; exhausting numerical change, even illusion awakens. Awakening lies within the self—how could illusion belong to another? Once one attains this Dao, one not only illudes things without being illuded by them, but can also command the seasons to shift, conjure thunder in winter and ice in summer, reverse the flight of birds and the gait of beasts, and seize the illusions of creation itself.

Yet what, after all, gives rise to illusion? Fundamentally, it arises from the wondrous mind, which generates its substance—just as flowers in the sky emerge from emptiness. Though the substance of illusion appears, its principle remains wondrous. Appearance gives rise to illusion; wonder enables it. Only through appearance can it be spoken of; only through wonder must one ponder deeply for three months before attaining the Dao.

Moreover, those of old who studied illusion did not do so to astonish the vulgar. Their study aimed to awaken to the Dao through illusion. Thus, when speaking of their ability to illude, flight and gait could be reversed, thunder and ice could be summoned. Yet once they attained the Dao, they never clung to their arts throughout their lives, and so the world never transmitted them.

Alas! The wonder of transformation is such that even Master Yinwen did not presume to claim the Dao as his own, merely citing the words of the Old Master as he departed westward.

Master Liezi said: *"Those skilled in transformation keep their Dao hidden and ordinary, their achievements indistinguishable from others. The virtue of the Five Emperors and the accomplishments of the Three Kings were not necessarily the result of wisdom or courage alone—some arose through transformation. Who can fathom this?"*

**Explanation:** What makes heaven and earth heaven and earth is their transformation of the myriad things. What makes the sage a sage is their awakening to the Dao of transformation. The illusions of heaven and earth—yin and yang alternating, cold and heat measured—cause the myriad things to follow their Dao and attain their proper place, without boasting of commanding the seasons as an achievement. If heaven and earth were to illude by summoning thunder in winter and ice in summer, no living thing would survive. Thus, those skilled in transformation—why would they display marvels or strive to astonish the senses? Therefore, the sage’s transformations, though hidden and ordinary, cannot be swiftly discerned in their effects, yet they remain no different from others. This is why Master Laochengzi, though capable of illusion, never clung to his arts throughout his life. Confucius could have done the same but chose not to—this is the same Dao. The Five Emperors and Three Kings were all sages of old; whether yielding or contending, they adapted to their times. Though they displayed wisdom and courage, their silent, wordless wonder worked unseen. People could witness their achievements but could not fathom how they transformed—this is beyond the governance of emperors and kings.

**Awakening has eight signs; dreams have six conditions.**

What are the eight signs?
1. The familiar,
2. The intentional,
3. The attained,
4. The lost,
5. Sorrow,
6. Joy,
7. Life,
8. Death.
These eight signs are what form encounters.

What are the six conditions?
1. Ordinary dreams,
2. Troubled dreams,
3. Thoughtful dreams,
4. Waking dreams,
5. Joyful dreams,
6. Fearful dreams.
These six arise from the interplay of spirit.

**Explanation:** The signs of awakening and the conditions of dreams, though encountered by spirit and form, do not share a single principle. Fundamentally, they arise from the mind’s own creation. Those who do not recognize the origin of sensory change are perplexed when events arise; those who recognize it understand their cause. Understanding their cause, they are free from distress.

**Explanation:** The origin of sensory change does not exceed the signs of awakening and the conditions of dreams—this is the constant principle. Recognizing their cause and remaining free from distress, how much more so for those who know the Dao? Those who know the Dao see life and death as no different from themselves; their view of dreams and awakening is but a trifle.

The waxing and waning, ebb and flow of this single body all correspond to heaven and earth and respond to the kinds of things. Thus, when yin qi is strong, one dreams of wading through great waters in terror; when yang qi is strong, one dreams of traversing great fires and burning. When both yin and yang are strong, one dreams of life and death. When overly sated, one dreams of giving; when overly hungry, one dreams of taking. Those afflicted by floating emptiness dream of soaring; those afflicted by sinking solidity dream of drowning. Sleeping with a belt beneath one dreams of snakes; birds perching on one’s hair cause one to dream of flying. When yin prevails, one dreams of fire; when illness approaches, one dreams of eating. Those who drink wine dream of sorrow; those who sing and dance dream of weeping.

**Explanation:** Dreams are encounters of the spirit. The Dao of the supreme spirit is unfathomable by yin and yang, and since none can fathom it, none can control it. The human spirit gathers through essence, dwelling within the body, dependent on yin and yang. Bound to them, it is inevitably enslaved by them. Thus, the waxing and waning, ebb and flow of this single body—its spirit’s encounters become dreams, corresponding to heaven and earth, responding to the kinds of things, with no escape. Therefore, dreaming of wading through great waters or traversing great fires is governed by the strength of qi. Dreaming of giving when sated or taking when hungry is driven by desire. Dreaming of soaring or drowning is the spirit being gnawed by illness. Dreaming of snakes or flying is the spirit being stirred by the kinds of things. Dreaming of fire when yin prevails or of eating when illness approaches—such are the inversions of dream-thought. Dreaming of sorrow after drinking or weeping after singing and dancing—such are the alternations of sorrow and joy.

The body, though small, endures a hundred years of life, yet day and night divide its span. If even for a moment it fails to preserve its pure qi, then with the waxing and waning of time, the interplay of yin and yang, and the myriad things of day and night, it becomes besieged. The form toils without rest, the spirit dissipates without cease, and one spends a lifetime in ceaseless labor, transforming alongside the things of the world—how can one fail to awaken?

I have examined the principles of dreaming and waking, the constancy of night and day. What appears in dreams, though called an encounter of the spirit, is in truth the servitude of the form. If the form enslaves me, it is not that the form has the power to enslave, but that I enslave myself. By my own enslavement to the form, the form in turn enslaves me, and I, subjected to its servitude, cannot master it.

When dreaming, one does not know it is a dream; upon waking, one recognizes the dream, yet soon dreams again, still believing it to be waking. Night and day flow ceaselessly, and throughout life, one alternates between waking and dreaming without realization. Though the visions of water and fire, giving and taking, are initially without substance, the states of sorrow, fear, joy, and delight arise truly in the heart.

Yet waking knows the dream, while the dream knows not waking—thus waking is surely more real than dreaming. What waking does remains confined to thought and deliberation; what dreaming foreknows appears beyond thought and deliberation—thus dreaming is indeed more numinous than waking.

The waking of each day follows a familiar order in its deeds, while the dreams of each night present sights and sounds that rarely persist. Such is the fleeting distinction between dreaming and waking, yet their divergence extends to this degree. How much greater, then, is the transformation of life and death, the vast transition of departure and return? Without the proper person, can one hope to avoid being swept away in the currents of creation?

Nevertheless, if one awakens early to the distinction between dreaming and waking, then the comings and goings of life and death are no longer worth mentioning.

*Explication of the* Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing *(The Scripture of the Dao and Its Virtue in the Most High and Pristine), Scroll Five, concludes.*
*Explication of the* Chongxu Zhide Zhenjing *(The Scripture of the Dao and Its Virtue in the Most High and Pristine), Scroll Six.*

Minister Jiang Yu, an Inner Student of the Hangzhou Prefecture Academy of the Song Dynasty, respectfully presents:

**Under King Mu**

Master Liezi said: "When the spirit encounters, it becomes a dream; when form engages, it becomes an affair. Thus, daytime thoughts give rise to nighttime dreams—these are encounters of spirit and form. Therefore, when the spirit is concentrated, thoughts and dreams naturally dissipate. One who trusts waking cannot speak of the Dao; one who trusts dreams cannot be called enlightened—these are but the comings and goings of the transformations of things."

The men of old who were true persons forgot themselves in waking and slept without dreaming—how could this be mere empty words?

**Explication:**
Daytime thoughts and nighttime dreams—dreams arise from the mingling of the hun-souls, while waking is the unfolding of form. Day and night alternate in their course, the transformations of things coming and going like flowers in empty space, arising and vanishing in an instant. Thus, one who trusts waking cannot speak of the Dao, and one who trusts dreams cannot be called enlightened.

Yet though spirit and form encounter things, when one examines their origin, all is self-created. The inversions of thoughts and dreams, and the self-generated nature of thoughts and dreams—there is no other cause; it lies solely in whether the celestial spirit is concentrated or not.

The so-called true person does not depart from essence, for their spirit is concentrated. They do not delight in life nor detest death; their going forth brings no joy, their return no resistance. They do not discard the Dao with the heart, nor do they aid Heaven with human effort. They come and go with ease, unshaken by the transformations of things, and hold no trust in either dreams or waking. Thus, they forget themselves in waking and sleep without dreaming.

At the southwestern corner of the Western Limits lies a country whose borders are unknown, called the Land of Ancient Wilderness. The qi of yin and yang do not intersect there, so cold and heat are indistinguishable; the light of sun and moon does not reach it, so day and night are undifferentiated. Its people neither eat nor wear clothing but sleep often, waking once every fifty days. To them, what they do in dreams is real, while what they see in waking is illusory.

The Central Kingdom, equidistant from the four seas, spans north and south of the Yellow River and east and west of Mount Tai, extending over ten thousand li. Its yin and yang are measured precisely, so there is one cold and one heat; its divisions of dusk and dawn are clear, so there is one day and one night. Its people range from wise to foolish, its myriad things flourish, and its skills and arts are manifold. There are rulers and subjects, rites and laws. Their deeds are beyond counting. They regard what they do in waking as real and what they see in dreams as illusory.

At the northeastern corner of the Eastern Limits lies a country called the Land of Flourishing Settlements. Its earth is perpetually warm, and though the lingering light of sun and moon shines upon it, no fine crops grow there. Its people eat grass roots and tree fruits, knowing nothing of cooked food. Their natures are fierce and unyielding; the strong oppress the weak, valuing victory over righteousness. They run swiftly and rest little, remaining awake without sleep.

**Explication:**
The southwestern corner of the Western Limits is the direction of Kun and Dui. All things follow Kun’s labor and tend toward Dui’s joy and rest, so in that land, what is done in dreams is considered real. It is called the Land of Ancient Wilderness, for its Dao is vast and boundless, existing since antiquity—this is the nature of the southwest.

The northeastern corner of the Eastern Limits is the direction of Gen and Zhen. All things follow Gen’s paths to reach Zhen’s great thoroughfares, soon to advance toward Li’s mutual manifestation. Thus, its customs favor waking and eschew sleep. It is called the Land of Flourishing Settlements because things there grow abundantly and gather in communities—this is the nature of the northeast.

In the Central Kingdom, yin and yang are measured precisely, so there is one day and one night, one cold and one heat. They regard waking as real and dreams as illusory, yet none can escape the changes of yin and yang.

In the time of the Zhou, the House of Yin greatly expanded its wealth. Those who served beneath them toiled from dawn to dusk without rest. There was an old laborer whose strength was exhausted, yet he was pressed to work even harder. By day, he groaned as he toiled; by night, he sank into deep sleep, his spirit scattered and lost. Every night, he dreamed he was a lord, ruling over the people, managing the affairs of the state, wandering through palaces and gardens, indulging every desire—his joy was unmatched. Upon waking, he returned to his labors.

When others consoled him for his toil, the laborer said: "A human life spans a hundred years, divided equally between day and night. By day, I am a servant, and though it is bitter, it is bitter indeed; by night, I am a lord, and my joy is unmatched. What cause have I for complaint?"

The House of Yin was consumed by worldly affairs, its thoughts fixed on family wealth. Both mind and body were exhausted, and at night, it too sank into deep sleep. In dreams, it became a servant, running errands and performing every task, enduring scoldings and beatings without end. In sleep, it muttered and groaned until dawn.

Distressed, the House of Yin sought counsel from a friend. The friend said: "Your position brings honor, and your wealth is abundant—you surpass others by far. Yet to dream of being a servant is but the natural alternation of hardship and ease. How could you hope to have both waking and dreaming as you wish?"

Upon hearing this, the House of Yin eased the laborer’s workload and reduced its own burdensome thoughts. Its illness gradually abated.

**Explication:**
Day and night are divided, with form and spirit alternating in use. When the spirit labors by day, the body rests, so by night, the spirit rests while the body labors. When the body labors by day, the spirit rests, so by night, the spirit labors while the body rests. This is the Dao of yin and yang’s waxing and waning, of things reaching their extreme and then reversing.

Thus, the House of Yin and its servant experienced the alternation of hardship and ease, unable to have both in waking and dreaming. The ignorant, failing to understand the principle of fullness and emptiness, trust waking as real and dismiss dreams as illusory. They pursue the gains and losses of the day but pay no heed to the joys and sorrows of dreams. Little do they realize that if dreams were without awareness, it would be well, but if dreams *are* aware, then sorrow and joy, desire and aversion are no different from waking—how then can they be dismissed as illusory?

That the House of Yin recognized this as an illness and sought counsel from a friend was perhaps because the spirit had already received its warning.

As for easing the labor of one’s servants and reducing one’s own burdens of thought, if the illness finds even slight respite, then the principle may indeed be trusted. But if one truly ceases to weary the self, diminishing thoughts layer by layer, then the spirit may congeal, and dream-visions dissolve—how could this merely bring slight respite to the illness?

There was a man of Zheng who gathered firewood in the wilds when he encountered a startled deer. He chased it down and struck it dead. Fearing others would see, he hastily concealed it in a ditch, covering it with plantain leaves, overcome with joy. Soon after, he forgot where he had hidden it and took it all for a dream. As he walked along, he hummed of the affair. A passerby heard him, followed his words, and took the deer. Upon returning home, he told his wife, “A firewood-gatherer dreamed he had caught a deer but forgot where it was. Now I have it—he was truly dreaming!” His wife said, “Is it not you who dreamed of the firewood-gatherer catching the deer? Was there ever a firewood-gatherer? Now that you truly have the deer, is your dream not real?” The man replied, “I have the deer in hand—why trouble myself over whether he dreamed or I dreamed?”

The firewood-gatherer returned, vexed at losing the deer. That night, he truly dreamed of the hiding place and of the one who had taken it. At dawn, he followed his dream and found it. They took their dispute to court, where the magistrate ruled: “You first truly caught the deer but falsely called it a dream; now you truly dreamed of the deer but falsely call it real. He truly took your deer, yet you dispute it with him. Your wife claims both dreamed of the deer, so no one truly has it. Since the deer is now here, let it be divided in two.” He reported this to the lord of Zheng, who exclaimed, “Ah! Is the magistrate about to dream of dividing another’s deer?” He consulted his prime minister, who said, “Dream or not—a matter I cannot discern. To distinguish waking from dreaming, only the Yellow Emperor or Confucius could do so. Now that they are gone, who can decide? Yet perhaps the magistrate’s words may suffice.”

The explanation: It is human nature to trust waking as real, for it is what the body touches, and to dismiss dreams as illusory, for they are but the wanderings of the soul. Yet if one forgets what one does while awake, is this not akin to dreaming? And if what one encounters in dreams proves real, is this not akin to waking? For waking acts often stem from intention, so the spirit easily forgets them; but dreams may arise without intent, so the spirit remains sharp. In essence, dreaming and waking are not two different things—obscure them, and both are real; scrutinize them, and both are illusory. If one insists dreams are real, then while dreaming, why does one not know it is a dream? If one insists waking is real, then once awake, why does one still have dreams?

This man lived in the central kingdom, so he distinguished dreaming and waking thus. Had one spoken of this principle to the ancient tribes of the wilds, their judgments would surely differ. The firewood-gatherer of Zheng first took reality for a dream, then took a dream for reality. The one who took the deer followed his words and treated it as a dream, yet claimed the deer and disputed it. Dream and waking intertwined, truth and falsehood raced, right and wrong clashed—hence the lawsuit. The magistrate, who adjudicates disputes, faced a dilemma: if he sided with the taker, the deer originally belonged to the firewood-gatherer; if he sided with the gatherer, the taker had followed the dream to find it. Since it all arose from a dream, right and wrong were tangled, with no clear resolution. To divide the deer in two—how could this be a just verdict? This is why the lord of Zheng sighed and consulted his prime minister.

Yet the principle of waking and dreaming divides day and night equally. Trusting waking alone leaves one mute; trusting dreams alone leaves one lost. Only the Yellow Emperor and Confucius could discern the truth—without them, how could right and wrong be swiftly decided? Thus, though the magistrate’s division of the deer was a last resort, in the end, he neither trusted nor distrusted waking or dreaming—he trusted nothing, yet trusted all. Though he did not reach the point where dream-visions dissolve, he may be said to have borne the burden well. And was not the magistrate’s judgment, after all, acceptable?

The magistrate represents the law. Where there are forms and evidence, and the law must decide, right and wrong become tangled, with no clear answer. One can only act as circumstances permit—is this not acceptable when no better course exists?

In the Yang district of Song, there was a man named Hua Zi who, in middle age, suffered from forgetfulness. What he took in the morning he forgot by evening; what he received at dusk he forgot by dawn. On the road, he forgot to walk; at home, he forgot to sit. He did not recognize what came before or after. His household was distressed. They exhausted divination and sought omens, but to no avail.

They sought out shamans to pray for him, but the condition remained; they consulted physicians to treat him, but it did not cease. In Lu there was a Confucian scholar who offered his services, claiming he could cure Hua Zi. His wife and children offered half their property in exchange for the remedy. The scholar said: "This is not a matter for divination or omens, nor for prayers or supplications, nor for medicine or stone needles. Let me attempt to transform his heart and alter his thoughts—perhaps then he may recover."

Thereupon, they exposed him to the elements so that he sought clothing; they starved him so that he sought food; they confined him in darkness so that he sought light. The scholar joyfully told Hua Zi’s son: "The illness can be cured. However, my method is secret and not to be passed down to others. Let me send away all attendants and remain alone with him in seclusion for seven days." They complied. None knew what he did, yet an affliction of many years vanished in a single morning.

The explanation states: When principles are entangled with falsehood, divination may be consulted; when matters are bound to profit and harm, prayers may be offered; when illness arises from indulgence, medicine may be applied. But the malady of forgetfulness is so vast that one is unaware of the existence of heaven and earth—how could divination, prayers, or medicine cure it? Exposing him to seek clothing shows he had not forgotten cold and heat; starving him to seek food shows he had not forgotten the body; confining him in darkness to seek light shows he had not forgotten likes and dislikes. Thus, Hua Zi’s forgetfulness was merely an obstruction caused by illness, not true oblivion of worldly affairs. Therefore, the scholar rejoiced, knowing the illness could be cured. If it were true oblivion, even the workings of creation could do nothing—how could a Confucian’s shallow art suffice?

Confucians, who exhume graves under the guise of poetry and ritual, are the greatest corrupters of the Dao. Their methods subtly mislead the people, making them depart from truth and embrace falsehood without awareness. Thus, the scholar insisted his method was secret and must be transmitted only in strict privacy. After seven days together, the chaos within Hua Zi perished, and his sight, hearing, eating, and breathing became like those of ordinary men. The long-standing illness vanished in a single morning.

Once Hua Zi awoke, he flew into a great rage, cast out his wife, punished his children, and brandished a halberd to drive away the scholar. The people of Song seized him and demanded an explanation. Hua Zi said: "Before, when I was forgetful, I was vast and unaware of the existence of heaven and earth. Now, suddenly recalling the past, decades of life and death, gain and loss, sorrow and joy, likes and dislikes—all ten thousand threads surge forth. I fear that future matters of life and death, gain and loss, sorrow and joy, and likes and dislikes will disturb my heart like this. Can I ever again attain even a moment’s forgetfulness?"

Zi Gong heard of this and found it strange. He reported it to Confucius, who said: "This is not something within your grasp." Then he turned to Yan Hui and told him to record it.

The explanation states: The difficulty of encountering truth and the ease with which falsehood confuses people have persisted for a long time. When one’s joy lies in truth, the myriad things cannot alter one’s preferences, and even the love of wife and children may be forsaken. Nothing in the world can compare to its worth, and even the Confucian Dao is not worth adhering to. Thus, once Hua Zi awoke, he cast out his wife, punished his children, and drove away the scholar with a halberd. Knowing the joy of forgetfulness, he should have been able to forget worldly burdens without a second thought. Yet he still feared external things would disturb his heart and prevent even a moment’s forgetfulness—how much worse for those who never knew the joy of forgetfulness and daily rush toward the path of right and wrong? When Hua Zi’s family regarded his forgetfulness as a household affliction, and the scholar rejoiced at curing his illness, how utterly confused their hearts must have been!

The man called Hua Zi of Yang district in Song: "Yang" pertains to growth and nurturing, while "Hua" (flourishing) thrives under yang—both incline toward the path of turmoil. In middle age, he fell ill with forgetfulness, shedding his flourishing and returning to the root. But once awakened, he again rushed toward the path of entanglement. The reversal of the norm is the joyous sheen of *Dui*; the emergence of life is the hasty decisiveness of *Zhen*. Thus, Hua Zi flew into a great rage upon awakening.

Zi Gong belonged to the category of eloquence, considering himself superior to others in discernment, yet he was rebuked by Confucius. Yan Hui, however, could forget benevolence and righteousness, forget ritual and music, and repeatedly advanced until he attained "sitting in forgetfulness." Therefore, Confucius turned to Yan Hui to record this.

Among the Feng clan of Qin there was a man whose son was clever in youth but developed a bewildering illness in adulthood. He heard singing and mistook it for weeping; saw white and thought it black; smelled fragrance and deemed it rotten; tasted sweetness and found it bitter; walked correctly and believed it wrong. Wherever his mind wandered—heaven and earth, the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—nothing was not inverted.

The Yang family told his father: "The gentlemen of Lu possess many arts and skills—perhaps they can cure him? Why not seek them out?" His father traveled to Lu and, passing through Chen, encountered Lao Dan. He described his son’s symptoms. Lao Dan said: "Are you any less deluded than your son? Today, the people of the world are all confused by right and wrong, blinded by profit and harm. Those with the same illness are many, yet none are awakened. Moreover, the delusion of one person is not enough to overturn a household; the delusion of a household is not enough to overturn a village; the delusion of a village is not enough to overturn a state; the delusion of a state is not enough to overturn the world. If the whole world were deluded, who would overturn it? If all the people of the world were like your son, then you would be the deluded one. Joy and sorrow, sound and color, scent and taste, right and wrong—who can correct them?"

The explanation states: Singing and weeping, white and black, fragrance and stench, sweetness and bitterness—even heaven and earth, the four directions, water and fire, cold and heat—all these manifold names share a single wondrous root, originally without distinction. It is through deluded emotions that likes and dislikes arise, that things are distinguished, and that various false names are assigned. Seeking reality through names, clinging stubbornly without change—none are awakened.

"To examine but one aspect: to take white as white is to follow the multitude; to take white as black is to follow oneself. Following oneself invites doubt from the many; following the many brings confusion to oneself. Their words differ, mine differ—white and black bear distinct names. Though names and words diverge, the essence remains unmoved; it is only in names and words that distinctions arise. The many and the few contend, and the few cannot withstand the many. Leading delusion with delusion, all sink together and do not return—how can one hastily deem the shared affliction of the multitude as right and the solitary awakening of the one as wrong?

Master Yang believed his Dao could overturn the world, yet he still contended with the Confucians and Mohists over right and wrong, white and black. Thus, Feng was troubled by this and sent him to seek counsel from the many masters of Lu. Feng was one who met things and matched them, hence in youth he was clever, but in maturity he took delusion and confusion as his malady.

Moreover, my own words here are not necessarily free from delusion—how much less can the gentlemen of Lu, those couriers of delusion, resolve the delusions of others? Better to take your provisions and return home.

The explanation states: The world originally has no fixed right; the great Dao does not enter into verbal explanations. Yet the sages, in bequeathing teachings to the world, could not avoid such discourse. They also feared that learners would take the words of the multitude as wrong and the words of the sages as right, clinging to them without release, guarding them without forgetting—as the saying goes, 'Gold, though precious, becomes a film in the eye.' Thus Laozi said: 'My own words here are not necessarily free from delusion. How much less can the gentlemen of Lu, who establish teachings of benevolence, righteousness, loyalty, and trust, and bequeath the texts of the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music—the most deluded of the deluded—resolve the delusions of others?' Take your provisions and hasten home, for this is meant to turn you inward, to seek within.

A man of Yan was born in Yan and raised in Chu. In old age, he returned to his homeland and passed through Jin. His traveling companions deceived him, pointing to a city and saying, 'This is the city of Yan.' The man’s expression changed sorrowfully. They pointed to a shrine and said, 'This is the village shrine of your homeland.'"

Then he sighed deeply. Pointing to a house, they said, "This is your ancestors’ dwelling." He wept silently. Pointing to a mound, they said, "This is your ancestors’ grave." The man could not restrain his sobs. His companions burst into laughter, saying, "We deceived you before—this is but Jin." The man was overcome with shame.

When at last he reached Yan and truly beheld the city and village shrine of his homeland, when he truly saw his ancestors’ dwelling and grave, his sorrow had already grown faint. The explanation follows: Those who transmit teachings may be true or false; those who receive the Dao may do so early or late. What enters first becomes the master; what enters later becomes the guest. Today’s scholars, having first encountered a teacher who transmits false methods, cling to them and guard them, believing nothing could surpass them. Years later, should they suddenly meet a true teacher who imparts the true principle, they cling to disbelief instead, and to the end of their days remain unenlightened—how lamentable!

Thus Liezi set forth this parable of the Yan man passing through Jin. This man, born in Yan and raised in Chu, returned in old age. Passing through Jin, his companions deceived him, saying, "This is the city of Yan." His expression changed sorrowfully. Pointing to a shrine, they said, "This is the village shrine of your homeland." He sighed deeply. Pointing to a house, they said, "This is your ancestors’ dwelling." He wept silently. Pointing to a mound, they said, "This is your ancestors’ grave." He could not restrain his sobs. His companions burst into laughter, saying, "This is Jin—we deceived you before." The man was overcome with shame.

When at last he reached Yan and truly saw his ancestors’ dwelling and grave, his sorrow had already subsided and could not arise again. For the heart is moved by what it first encounters; upon seeing it deeply a second time, it regards it as ordinary. Moreover, one’s homeland is meant to be a place of joy in life, yet the nearer he drew, the more his distress grew, until at last he could only weep—where was the joy in living?

What he had taken for Yan was not Yan at all, but merely a city of Jin. They had deceived him with falsehood, and he had believed it as truth. From the deceivers’ perspective, this was truly cause for mockery. Thus we know that what people call inner and outer, near and far, joy and anger, sorrow and delight, are no different from the Yan man’s deception. In their attachments to near and far, sorrow and joy, they too are surely laughed at by the Creator, just as the Yan man was.

If one could awaken to the unreality of such things, one would surely reflect: in times when sorrow and grief arise, how could they persist? Yet near and far do not lie in things but in oneself; truth and falsehood do not lie in circumstances but in the heart. When the heart is true, even the false becomes true; when the heart is deluded, even the true becomes false.

Had the people of Jin never revealed their deception, the Yan man’s emotions would never have changed. But once he knew he had been deceived, even when he truly saw his ancestors’ dwelling and grave, his sorrow grew faint. Why? The human heart is not inherently untrue, but once led astray by others, it cannot return to truth. Thus the Yan man’s true feelings, once scattered, could never be restored. Though later they came to him in sincerity, he examined them for falsehood, and even when he forced himself to grieve, he could not—how could he not be cautious?

Tracing the teachings of Laozi and Zhuangzi, we find their great aim was to anchor the people in nameless simplicity, preventing them from being swayed by falsehood—not to govern them after their nature had already been corrupted. Thus the *Daodejing* concludes with nameless simplicity, teaching that one should not desire stillness, and the world will naturally turn toward uprightness. The *King Mu* chapter ends with this as well.

The *King Mu* commentary: From the August Thearch down to the achievements of kings, though it is said the Dao descended repeatedly, the responses of emperors and kings to the world all arose from the Dao and were the work of sages, differing only in their adaptation to the times. In the age of the Thearch, one could not but perform the works of the Thearch; in the age of the King, one could not but perform the works of the King. Governance reaches its end in the works of the King, and even the enduring virtue of the worthy nearly attains it. Thus Yu, Tang, Wen, and Wu all performed the works of kings. Qi’s worthiness was sufficient to carry on Yu’s Dao, and King Cheng’s mediocrity was enough to preserve the legacy of Wen and Wu.

By the time of King Mu, the Dao was insufficient to be transmitted, and the marvel of transforming others did not fill his virtue but harmonized with music—from this point, the Zhou Dao declined. In speaking of the Thearch, we speak of its flourishing; in speaking of the King, we speak of its decline—this is the principle of beginning and end.

Moreover, the virtue of the Five Thearchs and the achievements of the Three Kings, though their Dao was profound and constant, were at times illusory, like a dream. Thus this chapter vividly illustrates the principle of dreaming and waking, as if there were an ancient, wild land where people took their dreams as reality, or rustic folk who remained awake and never slept. Those who exhaust their thoughts by day dream nightly of being servants; those who labor their bodies by day dream nightly of being lords. As for the dispute over the deer, waking and dreaming become indistinguishable.

The traces of sages in responding to the world are like this. If one can discern the way of waking and dreaming, one knows that those who suffer delusion are not deluded by nature, nor are those who suffer forgetfulness forgetful by nature. Right and wrong, beauty and ugliness, are the same in the Dao. The transformations of the Dao, virtue, and achievement are all one in the end.

The dust and chaff of the world suffice to mold even Yao and Shun with abundance—how much more the achievements of kings? Tracing the origins of governance, it began with the Three Sovereigns. In that era, the Dao was left to nurture the world, and the people used knotted cords for records. At rest, they were at ease; in motion, they were content—this was the height of good governance.

Yet once the Dao was set aside for governance, the turning of the times inevitably led to the virtue of the emperors. The rule of the emperors—such as the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun, who governed the world by merely draping their robes—was indeed flourishing, yet their virtue was already manifest.
