It must progress to the kingship of Tang and Wu, where all recognize the kingly achievements appearing in the time of Yu of Xia, though none realize its beginnings trace back to before the Three Sovereigns, and its remnants endure for a thousand generations thereafter.

Thus, the descent of the Dao is like water leaving its source: its flow is ceaseless, and as it drifts ever farther from its origin, it can never return. Zhuangzi spoke of the Yu clan as those who "summoned benevolence and righteousness to disturb the world," and of the sages as those who "could not bear the harm of one age but thereby sowed the calamities of ten thousand generations"—this is what he meant.

Nevertheless, the sage who can wield the Dao to guide the times, not following the world’s descent, may bring even the kingship of the Zhou dynasty to a state where the people’s customs differ not from the age of utmost virtue. This is the teaching bequeathed by Master Liezi, a hope for all generations, an encounter that may come at any dawn or dusk.

*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Realized Virtue, Scroll Six—Concluded*
*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Realized Virtue, Scroll Seven*

Minister Jiang Yu, an inner student of the Hangzhou Prefecture Academy of Song, respectfully presents:

When Confucius was at leisure in his chambers, Zigong entered to attend him and saw him wearing a troubled expression. Zigong dared not inquire. *Explanation:* "At leisure after court" refers to the time after retiring from official duties; "at leisure in his chambers" means he had just returned from public service to take his meal. Thus his concern lay in governing the world and bequeathing guidance to future generations—this shows that though the sage was at leisure, his heart was never absent from the royal house, so steadfast was his loyalty.

To ask is to express what the heart desires to do but has not yet achieved. Confucius’ sorrow was something even Yan Hui could not fathom, how much less Zigong, who fell short of him? Thus he dared not ask.

He went out and told Yan Hui. Yan Hui took up his zither and sang. When Confucius heard it, he indeed summoned Yan Hui to enter. *Explanation:* Yan Hui’s taking up the zither and singing was meant for the Master to hear. That he indeed summoned Yan Hui to enter shows that the sage’s teaching was tireless.

He asked, “Why do you alone rejoice?”
Yan Hui replied, “Why does the Master alone grieve?”
Confucius said, “First speak of your aspiration.”
Yan Hui said, “I once heard the Master say: ‘Rejoice in Heaven and know fate, thus one has no sorrow’—this is why I rejoice.”
Confucius, his expression suddenly grave, said, “Was there truly such a saying? Your understanding is mistaken.”

"This was my teaching of former days—pray let my present words be taken as correct. You merely know that rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate bring no sorrow, yet do not know that rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate entail a sorrow of great magnitude."

*Explanation:* Yan Hui’s joy lies in the Dao, while Confucius’s sorrow concerns humanity. From the perspective of the multitude, only Yan Hui is uniquely joyful; from Yan Hui’s perspective, only Confucius is uniquely sorrowful. What Heaven bestows, one rejoices in and does not refuse—this is called rejoicing in Heaven. What fate ordains, one follows and does not resist—this is called knowing fate. To rejoice in Heaven yet not know fate, or to know fate yet not rejoice in Heaven, is insufficient to be free from sorrow. Though one who rejoices in Heaven and knows fate finds joy even in poverty, the joy of such a one is not in poverty or prosperity—this is why there is no sorrow. Yan Hui has attained this Dao, and thus, dwelling amid sorrows that others cannot bear, he remains unchanged in his joy.

Yet Confucius, having lived sixty years, underwent sixty transformations. What he once affirmed, he later deemed mistaken. To take rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate as freedom from sorrow was his former affirmation. To regard rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate as a sorrow of great magnitude is his present teaching. Compared to Confucius, Yan Hui grasped his conduct but not the reason for his conduct—hence he took up the zither and sang, yet could not share the sage’s sorrow.

Moreover, both are the words of a sage—how could there truly be contradiction or discord? Adapting to the times and suiting change, I merely ask that the present teaching be taken as correct. As for true joy and true knowledge, how can the words of past and present be distinguished?

Now I tell you the truth: cultivate the self, accept poverty or prosperity, know that going and coming are not of the self, and banish disturbance from the mind—this is what you call rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate without sorrow. Formerly, I cultivated the *Odes* and *Documents*, rectified rites and music, intending to govern the world and bequeath it to future generations—not merely to cultivate myself or govern the state of Lu. Yet the rulers and ministers of Lu daily lost their proper order; benevolence and righteousness grew ever weaker, human nature ever more shallow. If this Dao cannot prevail in one state or one generation, how much less in the world and future ages?

I first realized that the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music could not save the world from disorder, yet I did not know the means to reform them—this is the sorrow of one who rejoices in Heaven and knows fate. Nevertheless, I have grasped it now. The joy and knowledge of which I speak are not what the ancients meant by joy and knowledge. Without joy and without knowledge—this is true joy and true knowledge. Thus there is nothing not joyful, nothing not known, nothing not sorrowful, nothing not done. What need is there to abandon the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music? Why reform them?

*Explanation:* The sage is the utmost of virtue and the perfection of the Dao. To call one a sage is to say that the mind is congealed and form released, with nothing cherished and nothing feared—what sorrow could there be? Even the benevolent can be free from sorrow, how much more Confucius, who embodied the sage’s consummation? If he were not free from sorrow, he could not be called a sage. To say the sage has sorrow is to misunderstand the sage.

Yet now, manifesting such great sorrow in countenance—this is because the sage is the linchpin of the ten thousand things, the one on whom all transformation depends, and who bears the weight of the world. If, by rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate, I cultivate myself, accept poverty or prosperity, know that going and coming are not of the self, and still disturbances in the mind, then I am without sorrow—but how can I ensure that all the people are without sorrow? If the people are not entirely free from sorrow, how can the sage alone be without sorrow? His sorrow is not only for the present age but extends to future generations. The ages to come are endless, the people’s sorrows inexhaustible, and the sage’s sorrow likewise without end—is this not a sorrow of great magnitude?

Moreover, I cultivated the *Odes* and *Documents*, rectified rites and music, seeking to bring order, yet the Dao did not prevail. Seeing that the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music could not save the world from disorder, I thought to reform them but did not know the way—how could I be without sorrow? Why is this? The Six Classics are the traces left by the former kings—are they the essence of those traces? The footprints are what the shoes produce, but are the footprints the shoes? Though the traces cannot be clung to, the shoes cannot be discarded either. Thus, the *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music—if abandoned, there is no means to govern; if followed, they cannot bring governance. Now, to take rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate as freedom from sorrow is to abandon the shoes along with the traces—how then can governance be achieved?

Therefore, to call it rejoicing in Heaven is still to have something rejoiced in. To call it knowing fate is still to have something known. Having something rejoiced in necessarily entails sorrow; having something known necessarily entails omission. Only by attaining what is called true joy and true knowledge can there be no joy and no knowledge. Without joy and without knowledge, there is nothing not joyful, nothing not known, nothing not sorrowful, nothing not done—joy and sorrow both forgotten. Acting without acting, all is relinquished. The *Odes*, *Documents*, rites, and music need not be abandoned or reformed, yet the world can be governed and future ages bequeathed. This is what the *Book of Changes* means by "stirring the ten thousand things yet not sharing the sage’s sorrow." This is why Confucius edited the *Odes*, established the *Documents*, appended the *Book of Changes*, and composed the *Spring and Autumn*—earnestly transmitting these teachings.

Yan Hui bowed northward and said, "I too have grasped it." He went out and told Zigong. Zigong was lost in bewilderment, returned home, and brooded for seven days without sleep or food, until his bones stood out. Yan Hui went again to explain it to him, whereupon he returned to Confucius’s gate, strumming the zither and chanting the classics all day without cease.

*Explanation:* Yan Hui once asked Confucius to let him go to Wei, but Confucius thought it perilous, saying, "You would likely go and be punished." Was this perhaps because he had not yet grasped the meaning of rejoicing in Heaven and knowing fate as entailing a sorrow of great magnitude?

As for Yan Hui’s saying that "when employed, he acts; when set aside, he withdraws"—this being the same as Confucius—it was because he had attained this understanding. As for Zigong, though his wisdom sufficed to recognize the sage, he had not yet heard of the sage’s Heavenly Dao, and thus fell into bewilderment and self-loss. Nevertheless, his deep reflection over time, enduring without sleep or food until his bones stood out, showed that he had forgotten his self and merged with principle, making it possible to instruct him in the sage’s Way. Thus, upon receiving Yan Hui’s explanation, he released his doubts and returned to the Master’s gate, where he spent his days strumming the zither and chanting the classics. In his music, he found joy; in his recitations, he understood that the *Odes* and *Documents* need not be discarded.

A minister from Chen, on a diplomatic mission to Lu, privately visited the house of the Shusun clan. The Shusun lord said, "There is a sage in our state." The minister asked, "Is it not Kong Qiu?" "It is," he replied. "How do you know he is a sage?" The Shusun lord said, "I once heard Yan Hui say, ‘Kong Qiu can set aside the mind and employ the body.’"

The minister from Chen said, "There is also a sage in our state—did you not know?" "Who is this sage?" he asked. "Among Lao Dan’s disciples was one named Kang Cangzi, who attained Lao Dan’s Dao and could see with his ears and hear with his eyes." When the Duke of Lu heard this, he was greatly astonished and sent his highest minister with lavish gifts to invite him. Kang Cangzi accepted the invitation and arrived. The Duke of Lu humbly questioned him. Kang Cangzi said, "The transmission is false. I can see and hear without using my ears and eyes, but I cannot alter the functions of ears and eyes."

The Duke of Lu said, "This is even more extraordinary—what is your Way? I earnestly wish to hear it."

*Explanation:* Human perception and hearing do not depart from the body. Even the sage has no means to differ from others in this; only the sage’s perception and hearing rely on the body to manifest clarity, not being entirely bound by it. Precisely because he is not bound by the body, he can set aside the mind; precisely because he relies on the body, he still employs its form. "Setting aside the mind" corresponds to Kang Cangzi’s seeing and hearing without using ears and eyes; "employing the form" corresponds to his inability to alter the functions of ears and eyes. Since Kang Cangzi’s sageliness is no different from Confucius’s, we know that Confucius’s Dao is no different from the Old Master’s Dao.

Kang Cangzi said, "My body unites with the mind, the mind unites with qi, qi unites with spirit, and spirit unites with nothingness."

*Explanation:* In the Great Beginning, there was nothingness—nothingness without name—yet spirit moved within it. The Great Beginning presaged the inception of qi; qi harmonized and gave rise to life, and with life came the mind. The balance of creation is bestowed upon humanity in this way. The difference between the mad and the sage lies in what they unite with. The sage ever ascends and unites with nothingness; united with nothingness, there is nowhere he does not unite. The common person ever descends and unites with things; united with things, there is nowhere he is not obstructed. When the body unites with the mind, one forgets the form; when the mind unites with qi, one forgets thought; when qi unites with spirit, one becomes a single undifferentiated qi, sagely and unknowable. Yet though spirit is wondrous, it has not departed from being. Only when spirit unites with nothingness does it merge with the Great Void, neither ending in the vast nor neglecting the minute, with no thing escaping its mirror. Whether it be the faintest existence or the faintest sound—though distant in the eight wilds or near at the brows and lashes—whatever comes to me, I must know it. Yet this is not the awareness of the seven apertures and four limbs, nor the knowledge of the six viscera within the chest and abdomen; it is self-knowing alone.

The Duke of Lu was overjoyed. Another day, he told this to Zhongni, who smiled but did not answer.

*Explanation:* The eight wilds are the farthest reaches, where mountains and rivers spread and the ten thousand things intermingle, beyond the scope of sight and hearing. The brows and lashes are the nearest, where the viscera reside within and the orifices without, yet knowledge and perception cannot perceive themselves. When spirit unites with nothingness, the body merges with emptiness, and the appearances of things dissolve into the apex—all becomes the Great Void. Though the tiniest insect may be as visible as the slopes of Mount Song, how much more so the faintest existence? Though the faintest sound may be as audible as thunder, how much more so the faintest tone? The Old Master said, "The farther it extends, the less one knows." This principle accords with that. Nevertheless, the sage does not strive for excessive knowledge; whatever comes to me, I know it. Only by not pursuing external knowledge does one remain unaware and unknowing, yet one’s knowledge arises naturally, with nothing unknown. If one’s knowledge were obtained through perception, it would not depart from the body; if obtained through thought, it would not transcend reflection. How then could one know all without exception? Kang Cangzi’s words exhaust the matter—so perfectly aligned with the heart that no sound could contain it. Thus, when the Duke of Lu told this to Zhongni, Zhongni smiled but did not answer.

The Grand Steward of Shang visited Confucius and asked, "Is Qiu a sage?" Confucius said, "How would Qiu dare claim sageliness? Yet Qiu is one who has broadly studied and widely retained knowledge." The Grand Steward asked, "Were the Three Kings sages?" Confucius said, "The Three Kings were good at employing wisdom and courage—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know." He asked, "Were the Five Emperors sages?" Confucius said, "The Five Emperors were good at employing benevolence and righteousness—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know." He asked, "Were the Three Sovereigns sages?" Confucius said, "The Three Sovereigns were good at following the times—whether they were sages, Qiu does not know."

The Grand Steward of Shang was greatly astonished.

*Explanation:* The Dao is one alone. From the Sovereigns it descended to the Emperors; Yao transmitted it to Shun in this way, and Shun to Yu. Tang, Wen, and Wu were all ancient sages. Yet the simplicity of Fuxi, the yielding of Yao and Shun, the contention of Tang and Wu, and Confucius’s uncrowned kingship—all were responses to the times out of necessity, and thus the sageliness within them was concealed.

Thus, when Confucius was questioned by the Grand Steward of Shang, regarding himself he said, "I dare not," and regarding others, "I do not know." Nevertheless, those who skillfully follow the times manifest the Dao; those who skillfully employ benevolence and righteousness make virtue evident; those who skillfully apply wisdom and courage enrich their achievements; and those who broadly study and amass knowledge exhaust principle and fulfill their nature—all are the affairs of the sage, merely that he dare not claim to know.

Moreover, the Sovereigns descended to the Emperors, and the Emperors descended to the Kings. The Grand Steward of Shang was but an assistant to the kings; how could he, still occupied with following and upholding their governance, suddenly be told that one does not know whether the Emperors or Sovereigns were sages? How could he not be astonished?

He asked, "Then who is a sage?" Confucius changed his countenance and paused, then said, "In the West, there is a sage."

*Explanation:* The so-called sage is the Dao of the Sovereigns, the virtue of the Emperors, the achievements of the Kings, and the culmination of Confucius. What Confucius told the Grand Steward of Shang were all the affairs of the sage. The reason he did not claim sageliness was that the sage, in his sageliness, partakes of the numinous and is unknowable. When the sage acts and presages transformation, he becomes visible to all things. Yet what makes him a sage remains concealed; one may know his governance but not his Dao.

The Grand Steward of Shang sought the sage outside the sage—his confusion was profound. Confucius changed his countenance, intending to rectify his demeanor and enlighten him. When the change in countenance and pause brought no understanding, it became clear he would never grasp the words. Thus, Confucius told him, "In the West, there is a sage."

The so-called "person of the West" refers to the sage who does not depart from the numinous, the root origin of Heaven that has not yet presaged transformation. The West is the direction of returning to life and reverting to one’s nature. Moreover, the Way of Heaven moves from the West to the North; only upon reaching the North is the return to life complete. When *Liezi* speaks of the Dao of the sage, it often alludes to the West, aiming to dispel the decadence of the declining Zhou and the excesses of culture, urging the people to abandon superficiality and embrace substance. Thus, it speaks of the West as the origin of transformation and the destination of Lao Dan’s departure.

As for the distinction of the North, it will soon give rise to the arousal of *Zhen*. Such is the purport of these words.

Without governance yet without disorder, without speech yet self-trusting, without transformation yet self-acting—so vast and boundless that the people cannot name it. I, Qiu, suspect this to be sageliness, yet do not know whether it is truly sageliness or truly not sageliness.

To explain: The world originally had no affairs; those people abided in constant spontaneity. If one cannot bear the harms of an age and thus governs it, one disrupts the repose of ten thousand generations without hope of remedy. Therefore, those who respond as emperors and kings do so through the Dao, observing the transformations of words and the destinies of things to avoid entanglement. Yet where there are words, there will be those who doubt them; where there is transformation, there will be those who resist it. If names exist amid order and chaos, one strays ever farther from sageliness. Only that which is so vast and boundless that the people cannot name it may be likened to sageliness.

Old Master said: "The Dao is ever nameless and without beginning," for the Dao ought not to be named. The sage is the utmost of the Dao; if it can be named, it is not the Dao. From Fuxi to Confucius, have their responses to the world not been nearly akin to selling fame and reputation across the realm? No wonder Confucius did not recognize his own sageliness.

Yet if one takes the inability to be named as sageliness, then to name it "nameless" is already to have named it—how can it remain unnameable? That which ultimately cannot be named is the true nature of the Dao. To name it "unnameable" and thereby allude to its namelessness is the sage’s unavoidable concession. Thus, though Confucius said, "In the West there is a sage," he ultimately declared he did not know whether it was truly sageliness or truly not sageliness.

Nevertheless, this "unnameable" does not mean emptiness, non-action, or detachment from all designation as nothingness. Rather, it is to act without being burdened by action, as if nameable yet without the substance of a name. Thus, Confucius once praised Yao as "so vast and boundless that the people could not name him," yet did not deny his towering achievements.

Consider Confucius’ words to the Grand Steward of Shang: at first, he did not know him to be a sage; in the end, he still doubted whether he was a sage. None knew his traces at the beginning, nor his subtleties at the end—sageliness that cannot be known, therein it resides. The Grand Steward of Shang fell silent and pondered in his heart, "Kong Qiu has deceived me!"

To explain: Only where there is a fixed standard can deception occur. The Grand Steward of Shang, confronted with Confucius’ words, found his eloquence insufficient to refute their principle and his sincerity insufficient to trust his Dao, so he pondered in his heart that he had been deceived.

Zixia asked Confucius, "What sort of person is Yan Hui?"
The Master said, "Hui’s benevolence surpasses my own."
"What sort of person is Zigong?"
"The acuity of Ci surpasses my own."
"What sort of person is Zilu?"
"The courage of You surpasses my own."
"What sort of person is Zizhang?"
"The solemnity of Shi surpasses my own."

Zixia rose from his mat and asked, "Then why do these four serve you?"
The Master said, "Sit. I will tell you."

Hui excels in benevolence but cannot return to simplicity; Ci excels in eloquence but cannot be reticent; You excels in courage but cannot be timid; Shi excels in solemnity but cannot be harmonious. Were I to exchange my own qualities for the sum of theirs, I would not consent. This is why they serve me without divided loyalty.

To explain: The virtues of these disciples are like yin and yang; the Dao of the sage is like the harmonizing qi. The harmonizing qi is subtler than yin and yang, yet yin and yang draw upon it. Though each of the four possesses qualities surpassing my own, I would not consent to exchange my own for the sum of theirs. Thus, their excellence is precisely what makes them partial, and their deficiency is precisely what makes them wondrous—this is why they serve me without divided loyalty.

Master Liezi, having taken Huqiu Zilin as his teacher and befriended Boshi Maoren, then dwelled in Nanguo. Those who followed him there came daily in such numbers that they could not be counted.

To explain: After Liezi had advanced in the Dao of these two masters and returned riding the wind, his own cultivation had reached its utmost perfection. Yet he could not bear to keep the Master’s Dao solely for his own benefit, so he took up residence in Nanguo. Nanguo was a bustling district within the city walls. Once he had emerged to engage with the world, he could not prevent people from flocking to him, and the sandals outside his door piled up. Nevertheless, Master Liezi remained subtle in his way.

Each morning, he debated with others, and there was nothing he did not hear. Yet for twenty years, he shared a wall with Nanguozi and never paid him a formal visit. When they met on the road, their eyes seemed not to see each other. The disciples and servants in his household took it for granted that Liezi and Nanguozi were rivals.

To explain: Nanguozi embodied the subtlety of the Dao, dwelling amid the dust without being stained by it—this was why he and Liezi shared a wall. The Dao of these two masters was perfectly round and wondrously attuned, ever meeting without seeking, seeing without appearing. Why, then, would they need to pay formal visits? Not only did they not visit each other, but even when they met on the road, their eyes seemed not to see. For those who study the Dao, to have the Dao present at a glance is already the utmost. Yet to rely on such a glance is still not free from perception. Only when they met and their eyes seemed not to see was perception transcended. Without mutual engagement, without action, self and other both forgotten—Liezi’s Dao was perfected in this. Thus it is said: Master Liezi was also subtle. Subtle in that his words never departed from the Dao, his heart refined and wondrous.

Yet if each morning he debated with others and heard everything, was this love of debate? It was only because of those who followed him that he could not avoid it. His disciples and servants, seeing Liezi engaged in debate, took his lack of formal visits to Nanguozi as proof of rivalry.

A man from Chu came and asked Liezi, “In what way is your master a match for Nanguozi?”
Liezi said, “Nanguozi’s appearance is full, his heart empty. His ears do not hear, his eyes do not see, his mouth does not speak, his heart does not know, his form is without fear. What could he possibly do?”

To explain: “Appearance full” means his cultivation preserved his form without decline. “Heart empty” means he forgot his heart and things did not linger. “Ears do not hear” means he heard only himself. “Eyes do not see” means he saw only himself. “Mouth does not speak” means his words were not mere words. “Heart does not know” means true knowing is without knowing. “Form without fear” means he had no apprehensions or taboos. One like this has forgotten both teacher and disciple. What, then, could he do when he went forth?

“Nevertheless,” Liezi said, “let us go together.” He gathered forty disciples and set out.

To explain: Liezi had already spoken of Nanguozi’s subtlety, but fearing his followers would remain deeply confused about the ultimate Dao, he went with them to witness it firsthand. The forty disciples who accompanied him were like the many who come together with others.

When they saw Nanguozi, he seemed indeed like a lifeless effigy, impossible to engage. Looking back at Liezi, his form and spirit were not paired, and he could not be grouped with others.

To explain: Spirit gives rise to form, and form gives rise to spirit. The pairing of form and spirit is what gives rise to human life. When form and spirit are not paired, it is as if one has the form of a human but not the feelings—this is the kind of lifeless effigy Nanguozi resembled. A lifeless effigy is a clay figurine. One like this stands apart from things and people, solitary in his uniqueness. Thus he could not be engaged, nor could he be grouped with others.

Suddenly, Nanguozi pointed to one of Liezi’s disciples in the rear rank and spoke with him, his manner joyful and forthright, as if he were the very embodiment of strength. Liezi’s disciples were astonished. When they returned home, all wore expressions of doubt.

To explain: In its stillness, the Qian hexagram is single-minded; in its movement, it is direct. *Kankan* describes its harmony. To be *kankan* yet forthright, as if embodying the strength of Qian, is to embody the Dao of Qian, presiding over the transformations of the myriad things and guiding them into great harmony. Suddenly pointing to a disciple in the rear rank and speaking with him was a response to encounter, not a deliberate act. His Dao was vast, so they were astonished. When trust is insufficient, there is distrust. Thus, upon returning, they all wore expressions of doubt—quite different from those who withdraw to reflect privately and thereby gain insight.

Liezi said: “Those who grasp the meaning do not speak; those who advance in knowledge also do not speak. To use non-speech as speech is still speech; to use non-knowing as knowing is still knowing. Non-speech and not speaking, non-knowing and not knowing—these are also speech and knowing. They are also without anything not spoken, without anything not known, and also without anything spoken, without anything known. It is only thus. Why this groundless astonishment?”

To explain: Writing cannot fully express words, and words cannot fully express meaning—thus those who grasp the meaning do not speak. The knowing do not speak; those who speak do not know—thus those who advance in knowledge also do not speak.

Yet to regard non-speech and non-knowing as correct is to sever oneself from things. To take not-speaking and not-knowing as right is still not to forget the self. To take speech and knowing as right is to gain but shallow understanding. Speaking of speech, one may speak all day yet never truly speak. Not speaking of non-speech, one may remain silent all day yet never truly cease speaking. Nothing is left unspoken, nothing is left unknown—yet nothing is spoken, nothing is known. Such was the meeting of these two masters. This alone is the constant of the Dao, nothing extraordinary—why then this groundless astonishment?

All that Liezi ever said comes to this, in agreement with the Old Master’s words: “How do I know the nature of all under heaven? By this.” Scholars who grasp this may awaken to the supreme Dao; beyond this, duality is not the truth. From this we see clearly the teachings of Liezi.

When Liezi first studied under Old Shang, after three years his mind dared not entertain judgments of right and wrong, his mouth dared not speak of profit and harm—only then did Old Shang deign to glance at him. After five years, his mind again pondered right and wrong, his mouth again spoke of profit and harm—only then did Old Shang begin to relax his brow and smile. After seven years, whatever his mind thought held no right or wrong, whatever his mouth spoke held no profit or harm—only then did his master draw him near to share the same mat. After nine years, whether his mind wandered or his mouth uttered, he no longer knew his own right or wrong, profit or harm, nor that of others. Inner and outer had merged. Then his eyes became like ears, his ears like nose, his nose like mouth—all senses were undifferentiated. His mind congealed, his form dissolved, flesh and bone fused as one. He no longer sensed the body’s support, the feet’s tread, the mind’s thoughts, or the words’ concealment. Thus alone is principle nowhere hidden.

To explain: The madman and the sage dwell in different realms, yet upon close examination, their difference is but a hair’s breadth. The mind seeks to congeal but scatters; the form seeks release but remains bound. The mind races without return, the form is divided without communication. The mind is enslaved by the form, the form is driven by the mind. Even within a single body, the eye knows not the ear, the ear knows not the nose, the nose knows not the mouth—liver and gallbladder may as well be Chu and Yue. How much more so for the principles of the myriad things? He no longer senses the body’s support or the feet’s tread because his form has dissolved. He no longer knows the mind’s thoughts or the words’ concealment because his mind has congealed. Thus the ears, eyes, and nose mutually illuminate one another; flesh and bone fuse completely, merging with the spirit’s utmost emptiness. Whatever approaches me, my mind knows it—principle is nowhere hidden for this reason.

Liezi once spoke of riding the wind in this way, and again of principle being nowhere hidden. Mastery of the wind lies within me; illumination of principle lies in things. Though their functions differ, if one attains this, there is nowhere one cannot go. To speak of the Dao and reach the point where seeing and hearing do not rely on eyes and ears, where flesh and bone fuse completely—this may be called wondrous, yet it seems beyond what learning can achieve. Yet the reason it is so lies in first daring not to think of right and wrong, not to speak of profit and harm, advancing step by step until the great wonder of nine years is attained. How then can scholars regard the supreme Dao as unattainable as climbing the sky and not strive for it?

Initially, Liezi loved to wander.
To explain: The Dao of wandering is neither within nor without, nor does it dwell between the two. It moves above the myriad things, carefree between heaven and earth—this is the fullness and perfection of the Dao. Thus Liezi loved to wander, and Master Hu regarded wandering as the ultimate.

Master Huqiu asked: “Liezi loves to wander—what does he love about it?”
Liezi replied: “The joy of wandering lies in playing with the unchanging. Others wander to observe what they see; I wander to observe what transforms. Wandering, wandering—none can distinguish this wandering.”

Master Huqiu said: “Liezi’s wandering is fundamentally the same as others’, yet you say it is fundamentally different? Whatever one sees is always seen in its transformations. Playing with the unchanging nature of things, one does not realize one’s own unchanging nature. Focusing on external wandering, one does not focus on internal observation. Those who wander externally seek completeness in things; those who observe internally find sufficiency in themselves. Finding sufficiency in oneself is the perfection of wandering; seeking completeness in things is the failure of wandering.”

Thereupon Liezi never left his home for the rest of his life, considering himself ignorant of wandering.
To explain: Observing what one sees, one perceives the phenomena of things—light and dark, passage and obstruction, mountains and rivers, people—seeing their forms as not uniform. Observing what transforms, one looks at those same phenomena, seeing the great and the small, the existent and the nonexistent, knowing their ceaseless change. Yet emptiness does not complete seeing, and seeing does not depart from things. All that is seen is change, and change does not depart from seeing. Observing seeing and observing change are equally confined by things—what difference is there? Moreover, seeing and what is seen are equally illusory, both revolving with things. Seeking completeness through the endless transformations of things only distances one further from the Dao. Thus external wandering that seeks completeness in things is not as good as internal observation that finds sufficiency in oneself.

The so-called internal observation is not to seek seeing apart from things. Within this single body of mine, I allow things to shift and transform on their own. Things turn as things, and I do not chase after them. Within the body that pursues change, the nature of perfect clarity remains unaltered. Within this single nature, all transformations are fully encompassed. Without needing to look around, is this not the perfection of wandering? The deluded do not know to find sufficiency in themselves and regard observing the transformations of things as superior to merely observing what is seen.

Liezi sought to illuminate the subtlety of perfect wandering, thus positioning himself amidst the transformations of things. Borrowing the words of Master Huzǐ to dispel his obstructions, he remained within all his life, believing himself ignorant of wandering.

Master Huqiū said: "Is this not the perfection of wandering?"
The perfection of wandering knows not where it goes;
The perfection of observation knows not what it sees.
All things wander,
All things observe—
This is what I call wandering,
This is what I call observation.
Thus it is said: "Is this not the perfection of wandering? Is this not the perfection of wandering?"

Explanation:
Mencius said: "All things are complete within me. To turn inward and find sincerity—no joy is greater." This is what is meant by internal observation finding sufficiency in oneself. Having mastered internal observation, one does not depart from the ground of one’s nature yet pervades all within the seas—how could one know where one goes? One does not depart from the tip of an autumn hair yet clearly observes the ten thousand transformations—how could one know what one sees? Thus all things wander, all things observe.

For one who, through internal observation of the root, enables all things under heaven to wander and observe—is this not perfection? The reason for repeating "Is this not the perfection of wandering?" is that within subtlety there is yet subtler depth, and words cannot exhaust its perfection—akin to the *Book of Changes* saying of the *Qian* hexagram, "Is it not the sage alone?" That *Zhuangzi* begins with "Free and Easy Wandering"—is it not for its perfection?

*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Impalpable Dao, Scroll Seven—Concluded*
*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Impalpable Dao, Scroll Eight*

Submitted by the Inner College Student of Hangzhou Prefecture, Your Servant Jiang Yu:

When Zhongni and Long Shu spoke with Wen Zhi, [Long Shu] said: "Your arts are subtle indeed. I suffer from an affliction—can you cure it?" Wen Zhi replied: "I await your command."

*Explication*: The dragon is a creature that ascends and descends at will, unbound by restraint or domestication, capable of transformation. To call him "Long Shu" is not so profound as the "Old Dragon" spoken of in the *Zhuangzi*, who embodies perfect transformation. Perhaps Long Shu’s affliction stems from his sagely wisdom. Yet first, he speaks of the symptoms of his malady.

Long Shu said: "In my village, praise brings me no honor; in my state, slander brings me no disgrace. Gain does not gladden me, nor loss grieve me. I regard life as death, wealth as poverty, others as swine, and myself as but another man. Dwelling in my home, I see it as an inn for travelers; gazing upon my village, I view it as a land of barbarians. These many afflictions render me unmoved by rank or reward, unshaken by punishment or threat, unchanged by prosperity or harm, untouched by sorrow or joy. Thus, I cannot serve a lord, befriend kin, govern a wife and children, or command servants. What is this malady? And by what method can it be cured?"

The Explanation states: The Dao is that which nothing fails to penetrate. Having already attained the Dao of sagely wisdom, one may employ its truth to govern the self, and its surplus to order the state, while its unadorned essence may govern all under Heaven—nothing lies beyond its reach. Yet in Long Shu’s Dao, honor and disgrace, sorrow and joy cannot burden his heart; life and death, poverty and wealth cannot alter his thoughts. Within, he forgets the self; without, he forgets things. Neither punishments nor rewards sway him, neither profit nor harm change him, neither grief nor joy move him. His Dao has reached its utmost, its perfection—nothing can be added to it. Now, that his Dao cannot serve a lord, befriend kin, govern a wife and children, or command servants—this is to mistake the sage’s unwavering adherence to the root for the error of separating from transformation and dividing it into two.

To inwardly observe the root and outwardly perceive transformation—this alternation of emergence and withdrawal is neither different nor the same. If one were to take the great root and great origin of the Dao within oneself and extend it to govern all under Heaven and the state, flowing with things and harmonizing with their waves, then though one might encounter success or failure, creation or destruction, nothing would fail to engage one, yet one would never lose the Dao of supreme tranquility, and the myriad things would all attain their proper order. Yet Long Shu seeks to preserve the truth of his self-governance without engagement, hoping thereby to bring order to the myriad things—how can this be possible? This is why sagely wisdom is called an affliction.

Wen Zhi then instructed Long Shu to stand with his back to the light, while Wen Zhi himself faced the light from behind to observe him. The Explanation states: Commanding him to stand with his back to the light was to make him turn inward. Observing him from behind while facing the light was to examine whether he could remain without a mind responsive to things. After a moment, he said: Ah! I see your heart now—its square inch of space is empty. You are nearly a sage. Your heart has six apertures flowing freely, yet one remains unopened. Is it perhaps for this reason that you take sagely wisdom as an affliction? This is not something my shallow art can cure.

The Explanation states: In human life, the six senses join with the self to make seven, all of which the heart itself produces. Long Shu’s Dao levels all things equally—one might say his six apertures flow freely, yet he clings to the truth of his self-governance and cannot extend it to respond to things. Is this not the one aperture that remains unopened? It has been said that the heart and the body are the inner and outer of a single form, ever at odds like spear and shield. When all seven apertures are drilled open, Hun Dun dies; when all seven flow freely, sagely wisdom is complete. Those who embody the Dao regard having a body as the greatest affliction—does this not refer to this?

What is called Wen Zhi clings to his patterns to respond to things; the sage is one who perceives the signs of transformation. Thus, Long Shu necessarily sought a method from Wen Zhi.

That which arises without cause and yet is ever-generating is the Dao. That which arises from life and thus lives, though it may end, does not perish—this is constancy. That which arises from life and yet perishes is misfortune. That which arises with cause and is ever-dying is also the Dao. That which arises from death and thus dies, though it may not yet end, perishes of itself—this too is constancy. That which arises from death and yet lives is good fortune.

Thus, that which employs nothing and yet governs life is called the Dao; that which employs the Dao and attains its end is called constancy. That which employs something and thus dies is also called the Dao; that which employs the Dao and attains death is also called constancy.

The Explanation states: Once born, one should let go and entrust it, not clinging to life—this is to arise without cause and thus live constantly. When death approaches, one should exhaust its course and release it to its end—this is to arise with cause and thus die constantly. To speak of constant life and constant death is to say that though there is death and life, one has in truth never died or lived, and so enters into neither death nor life. This is why it is called the Dao.

To arise from life and thus live is to comprehend the essence of life, and so though one dies, one does not perish. To arise from death and thus die is to cling to life and lose principle, and so though one lives, one is as if dead. This is the constancy of principle.

To arise from life and yet perish—Yan’s early death is an example. To arise from death and yet live—Robber Zhi’s longevity is an example. These are matters of good or ill fortune.

Some, though dead, are called spirits because they have attained the Dao; some, though dead, are called ghosts because they follow constancy; some, though dead, are called things—these arise from death and thus live, yet though they live, they are as if dead.

When Ji Liang died, Yang Zhu gazed at his gate and sang. When Sui Wu died, Yang Zhu caressed his corpse and wept. When a servant is born, a servant dies—some sing, some weep. The Explanation states: Ji Liang did not dwell as the foremost of things; his Dao was sufficient above to uphold and below to shelter, transcending the waves to ferry the people to a place of no difficulty. Thus, in his death, he had advanced in the Dao of life and death. Therefore, Yang Zhu gazed at his gate and sang.

As for what is called Sui Wu—Sui as a tree has five calyxes and five bracts, yet its fruit does not sever its source. It is like one who can receive the center and establish destiny. Sui Wu, however, followed the transformations of things, truly unable to receive the center and establish destiny, and so sank into oblivion with things. Thus, in his death, Yang Zhu caressed his corpse and wept.

Servants know only to delight in life and hate death, yet do not understand the causes of life and death. Thus, they sing where they should weep and weep where they should sing—all is as it should be. Their singing and weeping alike are met with joy by those who possess the Dao, yet all are equally pitiable.

Alas! In human life, the myriad things are distinguished and ordered, yet even in the slightest smile or frown, whether it accords with measure or not, all follow and become right or wrong. Yet when it comes to the great transformation of life and death, they sing and weep without awareness or awakening—can this not be a cause for great sorrow?

One whose eyes are about to go blind first sees the autumn down; one whose ears are about to go deaf first hears the gnat in flight; one whose mouth is about to lose its taste first distinguishes the waters of the Zi and the Meng; one whose nose is about to be blocked first detects the scent of burning and decay; one whose body is about to stiffen first hastens to flee and rest; one whose heart is about to be deluded first discerns right and wrong. Thus, when things have not yet arrived, one does not turn back.

A commentary states: Yin and Yang illuminate, cover, and govern one another; the four seasons succeed, give birth to, and destroy one another, following the inherent principles of their sequence and the mutual employment of their cyclical movements. When exhaustion is reached, there is reversion; when an end is reached, there is a beginning—this is the natural principle. Thus, when brightness reaches its extreme, there is blindness; when hearing reaches its extreme, there is deafness; when taste reaches its extreme, there is insipidity; when smell reaches its extreme, there is blockage; when vigor reaches its extreme, there is stiffness; when discernment reaches its extreme, there is delusion. Therefore, withdrawing one’s sight and returning to inner listening, cutting off flavors and removing fragrances, dismissing vigor and abandoning discernment—then the spirit will not decline, and though one may live for thousands or tens of thousands of years, one can deeply root and firmly establish oneself.

In the gardens and marshes of Zheng, many worthies abound; in the eastern quarter, many men of talent. During the labor at the gardens and marshes, there was a man named Bo Fengzi who, passing through the eastern quarter, encountered Deng Xi. Deng Xi turned to his disciples and laughed, saying: "Let us perform a dance for him—what do you think of that one approaching?" His disciples replied: "We wish to know." Deng Xi said to Bo Fengzi: "Do you understand the meaning of nurturing? To receive nurture from others yet be unable to nurture oneself is the way of dogs and swine. To nurture things so that they serve one’s use is the power of man. To make your disciples eat their fill, clothe themselves, and rest is the achievement of governance. Yet gathering the young and old like grass, treating them as livestock for the kitchen and pantry—how is this different from the way of dogs and swine?" Bo Fengzi did not respond.

One of Bo Fengzi’s disciples then stepped forward out of turn and said: "Has the master not heard of the many ingenious men in Qi and Lu? There are those skilled in earth and wood, those skilled in metal and leather, those skilled in music, those skilled in writing and calculation, those skilled in military affairs, and those skilled in ancestral rites—all talents are assembled. Yet none can determine rank, none can command others. Those who assign rank lack knowledge; those who command lack ability. Yet knowledge and ability are both employed by them. The one who governs is but one whom we employ—why should you boast?" Deng Xi had no reply, so he glanced at his disciples and withdrew.

A commentary states: The worthy conserves the spirit; the man of talent covets fame and position. Thus, the distance between worthiness and talent is vast indeed. Bo Fengzi’s worthiness and Deng Xi’s talent met upon the road: Deng Xi, coveting fame and position, danced before Bo Fengzi, while Bo Fengzi, conserving his spirit, resisted Deng Xi. His followers, unable to forget words, stepped out of turn to respond: "Has the master not heard of the many ingenious men in Qi and Lu? Skilled in earth and wood, metal and leather, music, writing and calculation, military affairs, and ancestral rites—all are minor arts and those who serve. Those who assign rank lack knowledge; those who command lack ability. Those who lack knowledge and ability are emperors and kings. Those who possess knowledge and ability are ministers. Emperors and kings follow the Way of non-action; ministers hold the office of action. To serve the Way of non-action with the office of action—one who can square things cannot make them round, one who can make things white cannot make them black, one who can raise things high cannot bring them low, one who can make things dark cannot make them yellow. But to govern the office of action with the Way of non-action—then square and round, white and black, high and low, dark and yellow, none will be beyond mastery.

Things each take shape according to their essence, yet this has no form. Things each acquire names according to their sound, yet this has no name. Thus, Deng Xi called the achievement of nurturing others so that things serve one’s use the work of governance, not knowing that the one who governs is but employed by others and cannot employ others. How can talent be relied upon? How can talent be a cause for pride?"

Thus, when he heard the words of Bo Fengzi’s followers, though their arguments were sharp, he had no opening to interject—he merely eyed his disciples and withdrew.

Gongyibo was renowned among the feudal lords for his strength. Tangxigong spoke of him to King Xuan of Zhou, who prepared gifts and rites to summon him. When Gongyibo arrived and the king observed his appearance, he seemed but a feeble man. Perplexed, King Xuan asked, “What is the measure of your strength?” Gongyibo replied, “My strength can snap the leg of a spring katydid and crush the wing of an autumn cicada.” The king’s expression darkened as he said, “My own strength can rend the hide of rhinoceros and elephant, drag the tails of nine oxen, yet I still lament its weakness. You snap the leg of a spring katydid and crush the wing of an autumn cicada, yet your strength is famed throughout the world—how can this be?”

Gongyibo sighed deeply, withdrew from his mat, and said, “Excellent is Your Majesty’s question. I dare speak the truth. My master is one named Shangqiuzi, whose strength is unmatched in the world, yet even his kin do not know it, for he has never employed his strength.” The commentary states: Gongyibo was versed in the rites of public office, exemplifying the saying that “the good knight is not martial”—this is what it means to employ the strength of others. *Tang* signifies the high and level foundation, the vantage from which one oversees construction, the ground to which all look up in reverence. Tangxigong’s virtue was like a hall, able to embrace the feminine and become the world’s stream—this is how he recognized that Gongyibo did not rely on his own strength. King Xuan of Zhou was a sovereign of the middle revival, who would task men with affairs and test them by their achievements; thus, he sought those who were martial and mighty. Shangqiuzi embodied the constant virtue of the mean, holding fast to spirit in his nature—this is why, though his strength was such that even his kin did not know it, he became Gongyibo’s master.

Moreover, to snap the leg of a spring katydid and crush the wing of an autumn cicada is strength not worth relying upon; thus, he did not use his own strength but sought to employ the strength of others—this is why his strength was immeasurable. To rend the hide of rhinoceros and elephant and drag the tails of nine oxen is strength sufficient to meet any foe; yet if one relies on such strength, it does not increase, and those who surpass one will arrive. Is this not feebleness in the strong and courage in the weak?

“I pledged my life in service, and he instructed me, saying: ‘One who wishes to see what others cannot see must observe what others do not; one who wishes to obtain what others cannot obtain must practice what others will not. Thus, those who train their sight first perceive a cartload of firewood; those who train their hearing first hear the tolling of a bell. For one whose inner self is at ease, nothing is difficult without.’

“Since nothing is difficult without, his name did not spread beyond his own household. Now my name is known among the feudal lords—this is my violation of my master’s teaching, my display of my own ability. Yet my name is not built upon relying on my strength but upon my ability to employ the strength of others. Is this not better than relying on one’s own strength?”

The commentary states: To pledge one’s life in service is to cast aside limbs and dull the senses—this may be called possessing the substance. Thus, he instructed him. What is meant by “seeing what others cannot see” and “obtaining what others cannot obtain” is not that one perceives the obscure and hidden alone, but that one achieves what is difficult and impossible for others to do. What one sees does not depart from what all see, yet none perceive it. What one does does not exceed what all can do, yet none do it. Thus, nothing is more difficult to see than an autumn hair, yet nothing is easier than a cartload of firewood; nothing is more difficult to hear than a gnat in flight, yet nothing is easier than the tolling of a bell. If one exhausts the power of the eyes on an autumn hair, one’s sight will not extend beyond it; if one strains the power of the ears on a gnat in flight, one’s hearing will not surpass it. But if one borrows the clarity of the multitude, the eyes need not strain, and one’s sight will have clarity to spare; if one borrows the hearing of others, the ears need not exhaust themselves, and one’s hearing will have acuity to spare.

The common people see things but not the Way, so they constantly assail what is difficult. The worthy see the Way but not things, so they always do what is easy. When what is easy lies within, nothing is difficult without—when nothing is difficult, nothing is not easy. Who, then, can bestow a name upon one? Thus, one’s name does not extend beyond the Way.

Therefore, one who can employ the strength of others, though his strength could turn heaven and earth, the world does not witness his might; though his authority subdues the realm, none call him martial. The ancients had a saying: “One skilled in strength lifts an autumn hair; one skilled in hearing hears thunder”—this is what it means.

Moreover, to snap the leg of a spring katydid and crush the wing of an autumn cicada, though it may be called making the weak strong, still leaves traces of what has been snapped and crushed—its deeds can be seen, its name can be spoken. Thus, Gongyibo still took this as a display of his ability, violating his master’s teaching.

Zhongshan Gongzi Mou was a worthy prince of the state of Wei. He delighted in associating with the worthy but neglected state affairs, taking pleasure in the company of Gongsun Long of Zhao. The followers of Le Zhengzi Yu laughed at him. Gongzi Mou said, “Why do you laugh at my delight in Gongsun Long?” Ziyu replied, “Gongsun Long’s character is such that he acts without a teacher, studies without companions, is glib but misses the mark, rambles without a school, loves the strange and speaks recklessly. He seeks to confuse men’s minds and silence their mouths, practicing sophistry with the likes of Han Tan.”

Gongzi Mou’s expression changed as he said, “Why do you describe Gongsun Long’s faults so? Pray tell me the truth.” Ziyu said, “I laugh at how he deceived Kong Chuan, saying that a skilled archer can make the rear arrowhead strike the front nock, each shot following the last, arrow after arrow, so that the front arrow reaches the target without a break, and the nock of the rear arrow still holds the bowstring—appearing as one. Kong Chuan was astonished. Gongsun Long said, ‘This is not yet the marvel.’ Among the disciples of Feng Meng was one named Hong Chao, who, enraged at his wife, sought to frighten her. He drew the Wuhao bow and the Qiwei arrows, aiming at her eye. The arrow flew straight to her pupil, yet her eyelid did not blink; the arrow fell to the ground, and no dust rose.”

“Are these the words of a wise man?” Gongzi Mou said, “The words of the wise are inherently beyond the comprehension of fools. The rear arrowhead striking the front nock is merely the latter aligning with the former. The arrow flying straight to the pupil without the eyelid blinking—this is the full exertion of the arrow’s force. Why do you doubt it?”

Yue Zheng Ziyu said, “You are Gongsun Long’s disciple—how could you not gloss over his flaws? Let me speak of his greater absurdities. Long deceived the King of Wei, saying: ‘There is intention without mind, pointing without arrival, things not fully exhausted, shadows that do not move. A single hair can draw a thousand *jun*; a white horse is not a horse; an orphaned calf has never had a mother.’ His violations of categories and inversions of logic are beyond counting.”

Gongzi Mou said, “You take ultimate truths for absurdities—your error is the greater. Without intention, minds are the same; without pointing, all arrive; things fully known remain ever-present. Shadows that do not move are explained by transformation. A hair drawing a thousand *jun* is a matter of equal force. ‘A white horse is not a horse’ speaks to the separation of form and name. ‘An orphaned calf has never had a mother’—it is not an orphaned calf.”

Yue Zheng Ziyu said, “You take all of Gongsun Long’s clamor for reasoned argument. If he had spoken from some other orifice, you would still accept it.” Gongzi Mou fell silent for a long while, then took his leave, saying, “Let me return another day to discuss this with you further.”

**Explanation:**
Gongsun Long was a sophist—yet Gongzi Mou took his words for ultimate truths. Ultimate truths transcend words; though one may speak all day, one has not truly spoken. Thus, though one may expound endlessly upon the ten thousand things, discoursing without cease, multiplying without end, this does not impair their being the ultimate in speech. To see Yue Zheng Ziyu’s words to Kong Chuan as deceit is to have never fully investigated principle; to see his words to the King of Wei as deception is to have never heard the Dao. Why is this so?

A master archer can make the rear arrowhead strike the notch of the one before it, for he knows how to align the later with the earlier. When an arrow flies straight toward the pupil of the eye without the eyelid so much as fluttering, he has gauged the proper distance, assessed the strength of bow and arrow, and exhausted their potential without allowing the slightest excess or deficiency. All this can be deduced through reasoned principle.

Yet when words reside in the Dao, they leave form and abandon intellect, merging with the Great Thoroughfare. The intent is forgotten, the point negated. To exhaust the ten thousand things is ever to possess them, yet never to possess a single one—akin to the rod one foot in length, halved each day, never exhausted through ten thousand ages. The unmoving shadow: the former shadow is not the latter—akin to the arrow’s flight, so swift that there is a moment neither of motion nor of stillness. Equalize the potential of things, and a thousand jun are not heavy, a single hair not light. Depart from form and name, and whiteness cannot designate a horse, nor can "horse" name whiteness. A motherless calf has never had a mother. Calf and mother differ in bodily form and destiny, their principles not interchangeable. The subtlety of such words reaches this extreme—how could one who does not grasp the essence of language ever fathom their meaning?

Thus, before one has fully investigated principle, one laughs at them as deceit; yet when words reside in the Dao, one deems them perverse and contrary to reason. Though Gongzi Mou elucidated their meaning, Ziyu never awakened. Instead, he grew indignant, heaping scorn and reproach upon them. Gongzi Mou, knowing he could not speak of the ultimate Dao with him, fell silent and took his leave.

Yet Gongzi Mou had already come close to Ziyu’s level, still hoping that one day he might master himself and awaken. The ultimate words do not offend, yet he firmly rejected and utterly severed them. Hence he said, “Let me return another day to discuss this with you further.”

Gongsun Long’s disputations Gongzi Mou took for ultimate words; Liezi praised them, while Zhuangzi declared them capable of besting others in speech but not of winning their hearts. Liezi’s praise lauded their ultimacy. Zhuangzi, borrowing his arguments to dispel the traces of clinging to texts, at the end of his book both adopted his disputations and deplored those who, like him, made a spectacle of argument, lamenting their dissipation without attainment, their pursuit of the ten thousand things without return.

Their words differ, each appropriate in its own way. Yao governed the world for fifty years yet did not know whether the world was well governed or not, whether the countless masses wished to support him or not. He questioned his attendants—they did not know. He questioned the outer court—they did not know. He questioned those in the wilds—they did not know.

Emperor Yao then donned humble attire and wandered the broad thoroughfares, where he heard children singing a folk song:

*"You set our teeming people aright,
None but you establish the norm.
Unknowing, unperceiving,
We follow the Heavenly model’s form."*

Delighted, Yao asked, "Who taught you these words?"
The children replied, "We heard them from a minister."
He questioned the minister, who said, "It is an ancient poem."

Yao returned to his palace, summoned Shun, and thereupon abdicated the realm to him. Shun did not decline but accepted.

**Exegesis:**
In the age of the sage, there is no governance yet no disorder. Yao ruled the world for fifty years yet did not know whether it was well governed or not—this is precisely what is called *ultimate governance*. At that time, those at his side or in the outer court knew only to fulfill their duties faithfully; those in the wilds knew only their daily sustenance. Thus, from his attendants to the common folk, when questioned, none could say whether the realm was well governed or not. Yet Emperor Yao’s intent was to align Heaven with humanity, neither scorning the destitute nor abandoning the impoverished. He wished to know the truth, so he disguised himself and wandered the broad thoroughfares. In humble attire, he carried no outward authority to sway hearts; the thoroughfares were the crossroads of the nine provinces, where the sentiments of the four directions converged. The children’s song arose from natural joy, untainted by artifice. Its words—*"You set our teeming people aright, / None but you establish the norm. / Unknowing, unperceiving, / We follow the Heavenly model’s form"*—spoke of guiding the multitudes to a realm of ultimate principle, a Dao rooted in Heavenly virtue, far surpassing mere cleverness. The children had heard these words from a minister, who called it an ancient poem. That an ancient poem was now sung by children showed that present governance accorded with antiquity. Yao’s rule sought only to emulate the ancients—how could he not rejoice with his people upon hearing these words? This was the crowning achievement of Yao’s governance. The *Book of Documents* speaks of the people transforming into harmony—was this not that time?

Guan Yinxi said:
*"Abiding nowhere within,
Yet all things take form through it.
In motion, it is like water;
At rest, like a mirror;
In response, like an echo."*

**Exegesis:**
*"Abiding nowhere within"* means leaving no speck of dust in the heart’s chamber. When utmost emptiness resides in me, the principles of the ten thousand things are nowhere concealed—hence *"all things take form through it."* *"In motion, it is like water"* means it flows with change without constancy, yet its course is always fitting. *"At rest, like a mirror"* means it reflects things as they appear without grasping them. *"In response, like an echo"* means it answers every sound without anticipation or resistance. Thus, the Dao resembles things in this way. Things may stray from the Dao, but the Dao does not stray from things.

**Exegesis:**
The Dao in things neither exceeds the great nor falls short of the small. Its vastness encompasses all boundaries; its subtlety penetrates the foulest depths. It apportions to things with perfect balance, neither lacking nor excess, stopping precisely where it should. Being like things in this way is why it is likened to a mirror and water. Thus, the Dao in the world is like the sun and moon illuminating all, their light reaching the four directions without obstruction—yet the blind do not see. Is the fault in the sun? Things stray from the Dao; the Dao does not stray from things. The proof lies here.

Those who excel in the Dao neither employ the ears nor the eyes, neither strength nor the heart. To seek the Dao through seeing, hearing, form, or intellect is misguided. It may seem before you, then suddenly behind; in use, it fills the six directions; when set aside, its whereabouts are unknown. Neither those with intent can grasp it from afar, nor those without intent draw near. Only those who silently attain it and nurture it through their nature truly possess it.

**Exegesis:**
Devoid of sound or form, those who embody the Dao do not rely on ears or eyes. Without substance or function, they do not employ the heart or strength. Like following a trail, it may seem ahead, then suddenly behind. In use, one sees the Dao, not things—hence it fills the six directions. When set aside, one sees things, not the Dao—hence its whereabouts are unknown. How, then, does one excel in the Dao? Neither those with intent can grasp it from afar, for the Dao does not reside in non-action; nor can those without intent draw near, for the Dao is not exhausted by action. Only those who silently attain it and nurture it through their nature truly possess it. Silent attainment requires no words; nurturing through nature demands no action. Such possession is not the possession of others but the self’s own attainment. Only those who attain this neither drown in emptiness nor cling to existence. Within me, there is non-action yet nothing left undone; in things, there is non-use yet nothing unused.

Those who know forget emotion; those who are capable do not act—this is true knowledge and true capability. If one acts without knowing, how can there be emotion? If one acts without capability, how can there be action?

**Exegesis:**
Humans are esteemed above the ten thousand things for their knowledge and ability. Yet they are enslaved by creation because they are ruled by knowledge and ability. What is prized in knowledge and ability is that the Dao cannot be drawn near by those without intent. What is detested in knowledge and ability is that the Dao cannot be grasped from afar by those with intent. Knowing yet forgetting emotion removes the chisel of cunning; one’s knowledge becomes natural, knowing all without exception—this is true knowledge. Capable yet not acting avoids the burden of action; one’s ability pervades all things without limit—this is true capability. Only with sincerity and trust can one embody the Dao. Acting without knowing is not forgetting emotion but being incapable of emotion—how can this be the Dao? Only those who can harmonize yin and yang embody the Dao.

Unable to act—this is not "non-action" but true incapacity; how can this be the Dao? This is why the clumping of earth and the piling of dust are not the principle.

Consider Shang Qiu Kai’s treading upon water and fire—this is knowing yet forgetting emotion. Consider Confucius’s abstention from playing metal and stone instruments—this is ability yet not acting. Clumped earth and piled dust, though non-active, are not the principle.

Explanation: Clumped earth does not stir as the wild horses’ whirling gusts; piled dust does not rise as the flying motes—this may be called non-action. Yet though non-active, the generative principle is extinguished. What value, then, lies in non-action?

The Sage’s non-action is like the receptive Earth’s profound bearing: it fills the four voids, is without intent toward things, has never acted, yet the myriad things transform and grow, everlasting and unceasing—this is true non-action. From the Sovereign to the Emperors, from transmission to Confucius who perfected it, their Dao all originates here. Thus the chapter *"Zhongni"* concludes with this.

**Zhongni’s Explanation**
The Dao of Confucius is like the great light rising in the east—none, whether foolish or wise, fail to know its brightness. Yet only by examining the words of sages and worthies can one plumb its subtleties.

Zi Gong said: "In my view, the Master surpasses Yao and Shun by far."
Mencius said: "Confucius is the one who perfected the great synthesis." And further: "Since the birth of mankind, none has been greater than Confucius."
Yangzi said: "Is not the Dao of Heaven embodied in Zhongni?"

Zi Gong possessed wisdom sufficient to recognize the Sage. Mencius was a disciple of Confucius. Yangzi compared himself to Mencius. Their praise of the Sage is what the world and all generations take as their model.

Examining the classics to seek the Master’s Dao: when office is possible, serve; when cessation is possible, cease; when duration is possible, endure; when haste is possible, hasten—without leaning toward any one side. When the times are pure, be pure; when the times call for service, serve; when the times are harmonious, be harmonious—without clinging to any one corner. Able to be benevolent and to extend it, able to be eloquent and to be reticent, able to be courageous and to be timid, able to be solemn and to be affable—unconfined by any single path.

Confucius said: "I differ from others in this: nothing is absolutely permissible or impermissible." Yet later scholars lean toward one side, cling to one corner, are confined to one path, and fail to perceive the Sage’s completeness—this is why the chapter *"Zhongni"* was composed.

Yanzi grasped only the joy of knowing Heaven’s decree and the absence of sorrow, yet did not know the great sorrow inherent in knowing Heaven’s decree. Yanzi, though next to a Sage, still required instruction to understand—how much more so Zi Gong and his peers, who exhausted their thoughts to the point of emaciation!

Guan Yin said: "Those who are good at the Dao do not rely on ears, do not rely on eyes, do not rely on strength, do not rely on the mind. Only by silently recognizing one’s nature and allowing it to mature can one attain it."
Mencius said: "When office is possible, serve; when cessation is possible, cease; when duration is possible, endure; when haste is possible, hasten—this is what is meant by Confucius perfecting the great synthesis." These all understood Confucius.

In studying the meaning of this chapter, one sees the profoundest depths of Confucius.

Geng Sangzi perceived what lay beyond the eight wilds yet lost both hearing and sight. Lie Yukou studied for nine years until his bones and flesh dissolved—who but those who exhaust the divine and fathom the subtlest could achieve this?

The Grand Steward of Shang was deeply perplexed by the Sage of the West and believed he had been deceived by Liezi. He did not visit Nan Guo’s wall yet trusted in its rival—how could those of shallow knowledge and limited learning in the world presume to discuss this?

The heart closed in one aperture, and Long Shu’s illness was difficult to cure. A single hair lifted a thousand jun, yet Yue Zheng’s doubt remained unresolved. Deng Xi mocked Bo Feng’s companions yet was ensnared by his followers. Emperor Yao heeded the poem of a child and gained the trust of his ministers. Gong Yi Bo’s strength could lift a cicada’s wing, yet his fame resounded among the feudal lords. Shang Qiuzi’s strength rivaled the world, yet his merit and use sank among the six clans. When Ji Liang died, Yang Zhu leaned against his gate and sang; when Sui Wu died, Yang Zhu caressed his corpse and wept.

Such cases—how could they be easily fathomed? All are called the deeds of Confucius, yet the common people surely do not understand.

Those who study today, upon reading the *Zhuangzi* and reaching the chapters "The Fisherman" and "Robber Zhi," cast them aside and reject them, believing they slander and disparage Confucius and thus refuse to examine them—how could they know that Zhuangzi revered Confucius above all?

In this very section, the *Liezi* elucidates before and after, making the teachings of Confucius radiant through the ages without end—such is the power of its exploration. Zai Wo said: "In my view, the Master surpasses Yao and Shun by far." He also said: "Since the birth of humankind, none have been as great as Confucius." Yet Confucius remained modest and concealed his brilliance; when the Grand Steward of Shang inquired whether he was a sage, he demurred and declined the title.

As for Guan Yin Xi’s words on the goodness of the Dao—true knowledge lies in the absence of emotion, true ability in non-action—this further illuminates the Way of Confucius. For Confucius’ engagement with the world, his navigation of change and misfortune, never strayed from the real. He neither forced his counsel like a peddler hawking remedies nor embraced inert inaction like a clod of earth or a heap of dust. He ever dwelled in true knowledge and true ability, residing between the useful and the useless.

Thus, this section begins by speaking of his true joy and true knowledge, and concludes by expounding his true knowledge and true ability.

*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Primordial Emptiness, Volume Eight* concludes.
*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Primordial Emptiness, Volume Nine* begins.

Minister Jiang Yu, an Inner College Student of Hangzhou Prefecture, Song Dynasty, presents:

*Tang’s Inquiry*

Tang of Yin asked Xia Ge: “Did things exist in the earliest antiquity?”
Xia Ge replied: “In the earliest antiquity, there were no things. How could there be things now? Would later generations be right to say that there are no things now?”
*Explication*: Only after the existence of Heaven and Earth did the myriad things come to be. The myriad things fill the space between Heaven and Earth, and tracing their origin, they share a single qi. The movement of this qi is without end, and thus the generation of the myriad things continues without interruption. Through transformation and ceaseless flux, they change day by day and month by month. The names “now” and “ancient” are but provisional, and the presence or absence of things—how does it differ between now and ancient times?

That Tang, endowed with Heaven-bestowed wisdom, should pose this question to Xia Ge is because the transmission from the Three Sages—Yao, Shun, and Yu—reached Tang, and though the transition from Xia to Shang arose from adapting to the times, it was unprecedented in antiquity. To use what exists now to verify what did not exist in ancient times, and to trace it upward to the era of Fuxi, is to see the Dao gradually recede into simplicity and return to the primordial. Thus, the question of whether things had presence or absence in antiquity is the meaning of Tang’s inquiry to Xia Ge.

Tang of Yin said: “Then do things have no before or after?”
Xia Ge replied: “The beginning and end of things have no ultimate limit. What was a beginning may become an end, and what was an end may become a beginning. How can one know their order? Yet beyond things and before events, this is what I do not know.”
*Explication*: Viewed through form, things disperse into myriad distinctions. The past does not recognize the present, nor the future the past. Even in the briefest breath of coming and going, one cannot disorder their sequence of before and after. Viewed through nature, things are one in their true essence. What was a beginning may become an end, and what was an end may become a beginning. Even the covering and bearing of Heaven and Earth do not know the before or after of things.

Examining the transformations of things: the sparrow hawk becomes the goshawk, the goshawk becomes the cuckoo, and the cuckoo in time reverts to the sparrow hawk. The end of the sparrow hawk is the beginning of the goshawk; the end of the goshawk is the beginning of the cuckoo; and the end of the cuckoo is the beginning of the sparrow hawk again. This extends to the putrid transforming into the wondrous, and the wondrous reverting to the putrid. In their arising, none know whence they come; in their transformation, none know whither they go. It is like the coming and going of the sun and moon, the succession of the four seasons—does day precede night, or autumn precede spring? None can know their order. For all are confined within creation, equal within the turning wheel. How then can there be any distinction of before or after? To know what comes first, must one not look to that which is beyond things and events, and is undifferentiated and complete? Yet beyond things and before events, it is dark and obscure, silent and still—how could wisdom ever know it? Thus, he says, “This is what I do not know.”

Tang of Yin said: “Then do the eight directions above and below have limits?”
Ge replied: “I do not know.”
Tang pressed him further.
Ge said: “If there is nothing, then there is no limit; if there is something, then there is an end. How could I know this? Yet beyond the limitless there is no further limitlessness, and within the endless there is no further endlessness. Beyond the limitless there is no further limitlessness, and within the endless there is no further endlessness. Thus, I know it is limitless and endless, but I do not know that it has limits or ends.”
*Explication*: If there is nothing, then there is no limit; if there is something, then there is an end. Knowing this, the limitlessness of the eight directions above and below becomes self-evident. Yet reckoning that Heaven and Earth exist within the Great Void, though they have not departed from things, they are the greatest of things. Though greatest, they are still things. Thus, their existence differs from the existence of other things. One cannot call them limited, for they are vast; nor can one call them endless, for they exist. To say they are difficult to exhaust or fathom—this is nearly correct. Thus, Xia Ge says: “Beyond the limitless there is no further limitlessness, and within the endless there is no further endlessness.” The limitless and endless—this is already the utmost. That within the limitless and endless there is no further limitlessness or endlessness is how one perceives the magnitude of Heaven and Earth.
