Yet this so-called “nothing” is not true nothingness, but rather the absence of form. To call it “nothing” is to dispel the notion of “something.” Since Heaven and Earth have not departed from existence, one borrows “nothing” to reveal their vastness. In the end, however, since they already exist, they will return to an end. Thus, the beginning and end are ultimately lodged in the unknown.

Tang further asked: “What exists beyond the four seas?”
Ge replied: “The same as the Central States.”
Tang said: “How do you verify this?”
Ge replied: “When I traveled east to Ying, the people were the same. Asking about what lay east of Ying, it was still like Ying. When I traveled west to Bin, the people were the same. Asking about what lay west of Bin, it was still like Bin. From this, I know that the four seas, the four wastes, and the four poles are no different.”
*Explication*: The “uniformity of the four seas” refers to the Central States, where Heaven and Earth unite, the four seasons intersect, wind and rain gather, and yin and yang harmonize. Ying lies to the east of the sun, where shadows lengthen at dusk and winds are frequent; Bin lies to the west of the sun, where shadows lengthen at dawn and clouds are common. Though the land is remote and customs differ, the nature of the people is no different from that of the Central States. West of Bin and east of Ying, the land is even farther from the nourishing rains, yet the people are still no different from those of the Central States. Thus, beyond the four seas—though beyond the reach of footprints, boats, or carts—by inferring from one nature to another, why wait to see or hear before knowing? Thus, the great and small contain one another without end. That which contains the myriad things is like that which contains Heaven and Earth. Since it contains the myriad things, it is inexhaustible.

That which contains Heaven and Earth is thus without limit. How do I know that beyond Heaven and Earth there are not greater Heaven and Earth? This, too, is what I do not know.

**Explanation:** Within a single body, a single hair is contained by the skin, the skin by the whole, and the hundred parts by the body. Though a single hair is minute, it possesses the wholeness of the body; though it serves the function of the whole, it does not discard the combined use of the hundred parts. As for things: the pygmy cicada dwells within a mosquito’s eyelash, while the leviathan Peng soars through Heaven and Earth. The pygmy cicada is not deficient compared to the Peng, nor does the Peng have excess over the pygmy cicada—great and small contain one another, and that is all.

Yet the measure of things is easily exhausted, so what they contain has limits. Heaven and Earth are supremely vast, and what they contain is inexhaustible. Heaven and Earth still have form, but the Dao that contains them is without limit. Moreover, in speaking of great and small containing one another, it is evident that the great can contain the small, but how can the small contain the great? It is said that Heaven and Earth contain the myriad things, which can be perceived in form, yet what contains them is the Dao. Speaking in terms of the Dao, even the tiniest mustard seed contains the subtlety of Heaven and Earth. Thus it is said: that which contains the myriad things is like that which contains Heaven and Earth.

The statement *"How do I know that beyond Heaven and Earth there are not greater Heaven and Earth?"* is meant to expand the learner’s vision and prevent confinement within fixed boundaries, urging the awakened to know the Dao for themselves. Yet in the end, it is left unresolved, hence: *"This, too, is what I do not know."*

However, Heaven and Earth are also things. Things have deficiencies, which is why in ancient times Nüwa refined the five-colored stones to mend their flaws.

**Explanation:** Viewed through the principle of great and small containing one another, it is clear that Heaven and Earth are merely things. Being things, how could they be without formation or decay? This is why they have deficiencies that can be mended. The five colors are the essence of the Five Phases; stones are the firm and refined qi of earth. Refining the essential and refined qi of the Five Phases to harmonize the waxing and waning of yin and yang—this is what is meant by mending their flaws. At that time, the Dao of regulating and assisting was already manifest. She severed the legs of the giant turtle to establish the four pillars.

The explanation states: Heaven and Earth drift within the Great Void, floating and utterly minute—no more than a single thing borne upon the giant turtle’s back. Since they are deficient and mendable, this floating state first comes to rest and ceases movement. Thus the turtle’s legs could be severed, the Four Pillars established, and the positions of above and below, the eight directions, made immutable.

Later, Gonggong contended with Zhuanxu for the throne. Enraged, he struck Mount Buzhou, breaking the Pillar of Heaven and severing the Cords of Earth. Hence Heaven tilted to the northwest, and the sun, moon, and stars moved there; Earth sank in the southeast, and the hundred rivers and floodwaters flowed there.

The explanation states: The Pillar of Heaven is what Heaven relies upon to stand centered and not lean; the Cords of Earth are what Earth depends upon to maintain its four bounds and not collapse. This is the Dao before it departed from primordial undifferentiation. When the Dao descended and unified, one sign foretold the birth of Heaven and Earth. Once Heaven and Earth took their places, humanity emerged between them. Amidst the naturalness of emptiness and non-being, false clarity arose, giving rise to sameness and difference. Once sameness and difference were established, love and aversion intertwined. With love and aversion arose, contention and strife took root, and the qi of anger and resentment prevailed, destroying the Dao’s all-pervading completeness.

This is why Gonggong contended with Zhuanxu for the throne and struck Mount Buzhou. When the Pillar of Heaven was broken and the Cords of Earth severed, Heaven and Earth split, and the intermingling of their two primal qi made generation and transformation manifest. Thus Heaven tilted to the northwest, and the sun, moon, and stars moved there; Earth sank in the southeast, and the hundred rivers and floodwaters flowed there.

The northwest is the direction to which the myriad things return to their root. As the sun, moon, and stars move to the northwest, the essence of utmost yin converges below and is offered above, enabling the myriad things to draw upon its qi to live. The southeast is the direction in which the myriad things spread and flourish. As Earth does not fill the southeast, the essence of utmost yang converges above and descends below, enabling the myriad things to draw upon its moisture to take form.

Thus Heaven possesses essence, Earth possesses form; Heaven has its eight guiding principles, Earth its five measures, and together they become the mother of the myriad things. In humans, the right ear and eye are not as keen as the left, and the left hand and foot are not as strong as the right. Among things, though their forms and bodies undergo ten thousand changes, none can transgress the proper course of their transformation.

Tang further asked: Do things have greatness and smallness? Do they have length and brevity? Do they have sameness and difference?

Ge said: To the east of the Bohai Sea, beyond countless millions of *li*, there lies a great abyss, a truly bottomless valley called the Returning Gully. The waters of the Eight Vastnesses and Nine Wildernesses, along with the celestial Milky Way’s currents, all pour into it—yet it neither swells nor diminishes.

Within it stand five mountains: the first is Daiyu, the second Yuanqiao, the third Fanghu, the fourth Yingzhou, and the fifth Penglai. Each mountain rises and winds for thirty thousand *li*, its flat summit spanning nine thousand *li*. The distance between their midpoints is seventy thousand *li*, yet they stand as neighbors.

Explanation: Water is used as a metaphor for the Dao. The Dao, as a thing, is so vast it cannot be encompassed, so deep it cannot be fathomed—yet it is the source from which all good things arise. Thus the great abyss is truly a bottomless valley, called the Returning Gully. The great origin of the Dao is all-encompassing; though heaven and humanity pour into it, it never overflows. Therefore, the waters of the Eight Vastnesses and Nine Wildernesses, along with the celestial Milky Way’s currents, all flow into it—yet it neither swells nor diminishes.

The image of mountains dwelling within it does not depart from the great origin of the Dao, for they are the source of the myriad transformations and the abode of immortals and sages. *Yu* (Daiyu) pertains to the nature of wood; *qiao* (Yuanqiao) to the sharpness of fire; *fang* (Fanghu) to the substance of metal; *zhou* (Yingzhou) to the category of water; and Penglai to the principle of earth. The names of these mountains—some denoting affairs, some resembling things—do not share a single meaning, yet none depart from the principles of the Five Phases.

The number ten thousand signifies fullness, symbolizing the completeness of the Dao. All numbers begin with one, are established by three, completed by five, flourish at seven, and reside at nine. One, three, five, seven, and nine are all yang numbers, the path of transformation. Thus, the numbers in this passage are used as metaphors for the Dao.

Upon these mountains, their terraces and pavilions are all of gold and jade; their birds and beasts are pure white. Trees of pearl and coral grow in clusters, their blossoms and fruits all imbued with flavor. Those who eat of them never age or die. The inhabitants are all of the lineage of immortals and sages; day and night, those who fly back and forth among them are beyond counting.

Explanation: The immortals and sages, their true essence undisturbed, thus manifest transformations: their terraces and pavilions are all of gold and jade, their birds and beasts pure white, their trees of pearl and coral clustered together, their blossoms and fruits likewise. Moreover, their blossoms and fruits transform naturally, neither splitting their husks in spring nor ripening in autumn. Thus, those who eat of them do not follow decay in its changes, nor do they grow old and die with transformation’s passing.

The bodies of immortals and sages are utterly empty and unburdened, so they constantly fly back and forth. From this, we may observe: hills and brambles, perils and evils—these are not uniform. How do we know they are not self-created by the human heart?

Yet the roots of the five mountains have no fixed attachment, forever rising and falling with the tides, never resting even for a moment. The immortals and sages, distressed by this, appealed to the Emperor. Fearing the mountains would drift to the western extreme and lose the dwelling of the assembled sages, the Emperor commanded Yuqiang to dispatch fifteen giant turtles to lift their heads and bear the mountains. They took turns in three shifts, exchanging every sixty thousand years. Only then did the five mountains stand firm and unmoving.

A commentary states: Since the inability to stand firm even for a moment was deemed intolerable, while steadfast immobility was deemed secure, they necessarily relied upon humans and resorted to creatures to attain that stability. This is why the immortals and sages appealed to the Emperor, and why the Emperor commanded Yuqiang to act. Yuqiang is a divine spirit of the northern realm. Turtles serve as his envoys, thus Yuqiang dispatched the giant turtles to lift their heads and bear the mountains. Yet even these giant turtles, though mighty, possess limited strength, and their exertion must eventually falter. Therefore, their forces had to be combined, taking turns in three shifts, before they could sustain the weight.

Once a thing has been employed and relied upon for stability, that very thing must harbor what may harm it—and in turn, it may become harmful to us. Thus, alongside such turtles, there arose the kingdom of Longbo, whose people could hook six turtles with a single cast, hoist them upon their backs, and return home to sear their bones for divination. In the land of Longbo dwelt giants who, in fewer than a few strides, could reach the five mountains. With one cast, they hooked six turtles, bundled them together, hastened back to their kingdom, and seared their bones for divination. Thereupon, the two mountains Daiyu and Yuanqiao drifted to the northern pole and sank into the great sea, displacing countless immortals and sages. The Emperor, enraged, diminished the kingdom of Longbo to straiten its borders and shrank its people to shorten their stature. By the time of Fuxi and Shennong, the people of that land still measured several dozen *zhang* in height.

A commentary states: Daiyu and Yuanqiao are mountains of the southeast. The earth does not fill the southeast, so these two mountains drifted and sank. Having sunk into the great sea at the northern pole, they returned to their primordial origin and reverted to the heart of the supreme Dao. Thus, the immortals and sages lost their dwelling and were scattered. Later ages saw manifest governance, which is why the Emperor’s wrath flared and the great principles of the ancients became obscured.

Four hundred thousand *li* east of the Central States lies the kingdom of Jiao Yao, whose people stand but a foot and a half tall. Farther northeast dwell the Zhenren, who measure nine inches. South of Jing lies the realm of Mingling, where five hundred years make a spring and five hundred years an autumn. In high antiquity grew the Great Chun, for which eight thousand years make a spring and eight thousand years an autumn. Upon rotting soil sprout fungi and mushrooms that live from dawn to dusk. In the months of spring and summer appear midges, born with the rain and perishing at the sight of the sun.

North of the northernmost lands lies the Dark Sea, a celestial lake. There dwells a fish spanning thousands of *li* in breadth, its length commensurate, called the Kun. There also dwells a bird called the Peng, whose wings resemble clouds trailing from the sky, its body proportionate. Who in the world knows of such creatures? Great Yu encountered them in his travels, Boyi recognized and named them, and Yijian recorded them in his annals.

Along the riverbanks between the Yangtze and the sea breed tiny insects called Jiaoming. They swarm and alight upon the eyelashes of mosquitoes without touching them. They come and go, roosting and departing, yet the mosquito remains unaware. Li Zhu and Zi Yu, though they rubbed their eyes and raised their brows to peer by day, could not discern their form. Jiao Hu and Yu, Shi Kuang, though they cupped their ears and bent their heads to listen by night, could not catch their sound. Only the Yellow Emperor and Rongchengzi, dwelling upon Kongtong, fasted together for three months, stilling their hearts and abandoning their forms, then slowly perceived with their spirit and beheld them as clearly as the slopes of Mount Song; slowly they listened with their *qi* and heard them as resoundingly as thunder.

In the lands of Wu and Chu stands a great tree called the You. Its leaves are jade-green and sprout in winter; its fruit is cinnabar-red and tastes sour. Consuming its bark and sap cures the ailment of *fenjue*. The Central States prize it, yet when carried north across the Huai River, it transforms into the trifoliate orange. The stork does not cross the Ji River; the raccoon dog dies if it crosses the Wen River—such is the nature of earthly *qi*.

Nevertheless, though forms and *qi* differ, their natures are the same, and none can alter another. All life attains wholeness, and all portions are sufficient. How then do I discern their vastness or smallness, their length or brevity, their sameness or difference?

A commentary states: The myriad things fill the space between heaven and earth. They arise in different regions, transform at different times, and vary in their changes and measures. From the animate to the still, from the flying to the hidden, their shapes and forms are boundless. Can they ever be fully fathomed? Tracing them to their source, the workings of creation upon the ten thousand things all stem from spontaneity. And how can the ten thousand things choose their lot within creation? Whether they become an insect’s limb or a rat’s liver, they abide where they are placed.

As humans, born in the kingdom of Longbo, they cannot but be tall; as Jiao Yao or Zhenren, they cannot but be small. Among plants, as Mingling or Great Chun in Jing, they live long; as fungi upon rotting soil, they die young. Among animals, as Kun and Peng in the far north, they grow vast; as tiny insects along the riverbanks, they remain minute. The great do not exceed their nature by virtue of their size, nor do the small fall short of their nature by virtue of their smallness. Though long-lived, they must still perish, unable to add what their nature lacks; though short-lived, they still live, unable to diminish what their nature possesses. The trifoliate orange does not cross the Huai, the stork does not cross the Ji, the raccoon dog does not cross the Wen—all are constrained by earthly *qi*, yet their natures remain unalterable.

The chapter "Free and Easy Wandering" in the *Zhuangzi* elucidates this principle. I have often pondered: among creatures, none are greater than the giant turtles. Observe how they lift their heads to bear the mountains Daiyu and Yuanqiao—truly wondrous! Yet they cannot escape the fate of having their bones seared, so what advantage does their greatness confer? Among creatures, none are more minute than the Jiaoming. Though Li Zhu, Zi Yu, Jiao Hu, Yu, and Shi Kuang could neither see nor hear them, when the Yellow Emperor and Rongchengzi perceived them with spirit and listened with *qi*, they revealed an immeasurable vastness. Thus, what harm lies in smallness?

Thus, the vastness or minuteness, length or brevity, sameness or difference of things are not worth noting.

The Twin Mountains of Taihang and Wangwu spanned seven hundred *li* in breadth and rose ten thousand *ren* in height, originally situated south of Jizhou and north of Heyang. The Foolish Old Man of North Mountain, nearly ninety years of age, dwelled facing the mountains. Vexed by the northern barrier of the mountains and the circuitous paths for ingress and egress, he gathered his household to deliberate, saying: "Shall we join forces to level the perilous heights, opening a passage south to Yu and reaching the southern bank of the Han River—would this be feasible?" They assented in unison.

His wife raised a doubt: "With your strength, you cannot even diminish the mound of Kuifu. How then can you contend with Taihang and Wangwu? Moreover, where will you dispose of the earth and stones?"

The assembly replied: "We shall cast them into the far reaches of the Bohai Sea, north of Yintu."

Thereupon, he led his children and grandchildren—three men capable of bearing the yoke—striking rocks and tilling the soil, transporting the debris in baskets to the edge of the Bohai Sea. The widowed wife of their neighbor, Jingcheng, had an orphaned boy who, though only just shedding his milk teeth, leapt forth to aid them. From winter’s chill to summer’s heat, they made but one return journey home.

The Wise Old Man of the River Bend laughed and sought to dissuade them, saying: "How extreme is your folly! With the dregs of your years and remaining strength, you cannot even strip a single hair from the mountain—what then can you do with its earth and stones?"

The Foolish Old Man of North Mountain sighed deeply and replied: "Your heart is so obstinate, so utterly impervious—you are not even the equal of the widow and her frail child. Though I may die, I shall leave behind a son. My son will beget a grandson, and my grandson will beget a son..."

Child begets child, and child begets grandchild;
descendants stretch on without end.
Yet the mountain never grows—
how then shall it not be leveled?

The Wise Old Man of the River Bend had no reply.

The Spirit Who Grasps the Serpent, hearing of this, feared their unceasing resolve and reported it to the Emperor. Moved by their sincerity, the Emperor commanded the two sons of Kuafu to bear the two mountains away—one placed east of Shuō, the other south of Yōng. From that time on, south of Jì and north of the Hàn River, no ridges blocked the way.

Explanation:
At the edge of the Bohai Sea, north of Yǐn’s lands, there was indeed space enough to hold the heights of Tàixíng and Wángwū. With descendants unending and the mountain never growing, to level the perilous heights, open the road south of Yù, and reach the northern bank of the Hàn—this principle too is credible. Having both the principle and perfect sincerity, even the Spirit Who Grasps the Serpent, bravest of beings, heard and knew fear. The Emperor’s loftiness likewise was moved by their sincerity. Thus, though the Foolish Old Man and his frail child were weak, they could clear the ridges south of Jì and north of the Hàn. Moreover, it was precisely because they were the Foolish Old Man and his frail child that these ridges were removed: the old man’s thoughts were gentle and his sincerity absolute, while the child’s will was single-minded, his qi soft and unadulterated—this is how they moved heaven and earth, stirred gods and spirits.

Had they harbored inner doubts in the prime of their strength, they would have calculated their power insufficient to level even Kuífù’s mound and abandoned the task—this is why the old man’s wife voiced her doubts, and why the Wise Old Man of the River Bend laughed and sought to stop them.

In life, our vain calculations, the growth of the self, and the slow decay of the body—how are these barriers to the Dao any less than two mountains blocking the way? If one could likewise forget cunning and lay aside pride in one’s vitality, persist in sincerity without reckoning life or death, then in a single breath one might suddenly dissolve all obstructions and merge with the Dao. If not, it is only because sincerity has not yet reached its fullness.

Kuāfù, undaunted by his strength, sought to chase the sun’s shadow, pursuing it to the edge of Yú Valley. Parched with thirst, he rushed to drink from the Yellow River and the Wèi. When these proved insufficient, he turned north to drink from the Great Marsh, but before he reached it, he died of thirst on the way. He cast aside his staff, and where his flesh and fat soaked the earth, the Dèng Forest grew, spreading for thousands of miles.

**Exposition:**

What, after all, is the sun’s shadow? To pursue it without measuring one’s strength is to be enslaved by delusion. From delusion arises craving; with craving lodged within, even draining the Yellow River and the Wèi cannot quench the scorching heat of thirst—thus he perished on the road. Only after his death did he cast aside his staff. Where his flesh and fat soaked the earth, the Dèng Forest sprang forth, spreading for thousands of miles. Yet the moisture from a single body, seeping into the abandoned staff, could nourish a forest of thousands of miles, while it could not moisten his own parched bones—such is the ruin wrought by delusion.

*Explication of the* **Perfect Virtue of the Primal Void***, Scroll Nine—Concluded*
*Explication of the* **Perfect Virtue of the Primal Void***, Scroll Ten*
*Presented by your subject Jiang Yu, an Inner Student of the Hangzhou Prefectural Academy*

---

**Yǔ’s Inquiry**

The Great Yǔ said: “Within the Six Directions and the Four Seas, all is illumined by sun and moon, ordered by the stars, regulated by the four seasons, and governed by the Great Year. The beings born of the numinous take myriad forms—some die young, some live long—only the sage can fathom their Way.”

Xià Gé replied: “Yet there are those who arise without relying on the numinous, take form without depending on yin and yang, shine without awaiting sun and moon, perish without violence, live long without cultivation, eat without the five grains, clothe themselves without silk or floss, and move without boats or chariots. Their Way is spontaneous, beyond even the sage’s comprehension.”

**Exposition:**

When it is said that only the sage can fathom their Way, this does not mean the sage delights in comprehending things—rather, the Way is such that nothing lies beyond it. As for that which the sage cannot comprehend, it is not that the sage is incapable, but that the Way is naturally so, requiring no act of comprehension. Yet it is only because there exists that which the sage cannot comprehend that there arises what the sage *can* comprehend.

When Yǔ governed the waters and lands, he lost his way and strayed into a foreign realm. North of the Northern Sea, beyond countless thousands of miles from Qí Province, lay a land called Zhōngběi. Its borders were unbounded, untouched by wind, rain, frost, or dew. No birds, beasts, insects, fish, grasses, or trees grew there. All was level, encircled by towering ridges.

**Exposition:**

The north is the direction of darkness, where the myriad things withdraw, where the True One converges, and where the utmost spirit dwells. North of the Northern Sea lies the land of Zhōngběi—refined beyond refinement, numinous beyond the numinous. Unconfined by direction, it has no fixed borders. Unenslaved by qi, it knows no transformations of yin and yang. Independent of things, it produces no creatures, plant or animal. All is level—its Way utterly even. Encircling ridges leave no gaps beyond. How could even the divine Yǔ have reached such a land? Even he, had he not lost his way, could never have arrived, for it lies beyond the reach of foot, strength, boat, or chariot.

At the land’s center stood a mountain named Húlǐng, shaped like a carrying-pole with jars suspended at either end. At its summit was an opening like a ring, called the *Zī* Cavern. A spring gushed forth, named the *Shénfèn*, its fragrance surpassing orchid and pepper, its taste exceeding fine wine. From a single source, it divided into four channels, flowing down the mountain to irrigate the entire land. The soil’s qi was harmonious, and no plagues arose. The people’s nature was gentle, yielding to things without contention. Their hearts were soft, their bones weak—neither proud nor envious. Young and old dwelled together without rulers or subjects. Men and women wandered freely, without matchmakers or betrothals. They lived along the waters, neither plowing nor sowing. The soil’s warmth was just right, so they neither wove nor wore clothes. They lived a hundred years, neither dying young nor falling ill. Their population flourished without count, ever joyful, untouched by sorrow, aging, or suffering. Fond of song, they clasped hands and sang in turns, their voices never ceasing. When hungry or weary, they drank the *Shénfèn*, and their strength and spirits became peaceful. If they overindulged, they grew drunk, waking only after ten days. Bathing in the *Shénfèn*, their skin became sleek and glossy, their fragrance lingering for ten days.

**Exposition:**

At the center, above, hollow yet unobstructed, its flow endless, its outflow inexhaustible—such is the *Shénfèn* of the *Zī* Cavern, ever nourishing the myriad things. Its fragrance surpasses orchid and pepper, its taste exceeds fine wine—so its Way spreads, its scent delighting the heart. Irrigating the entire land, it is present in all things without exception. The absence of plagues shows its perfect harmony undispersed. The people’s gentle nature arises from the pure qi held within. Soft hearts mean the spirit is concentrated; weak bones mean the form is released. Young and old dwelling together, men and women mingling freely—such people know no marriage or officialdom. Neither plowing nor sowing, neither weaving nor wearing clothes—such people need no garments or food. Living a hundred years, they meet their end in due course. Their population flourishes, their life endless. Clasping hands and singing in turns, each attains true joy. Their ability to live thus stems from drinking the *Shénfèn* to transform their inner selves and bathing in it to permeate their outer forms.

When King Mù of Zhōu journeyed north and passed through this land, he forgot to return for three years. Upon his return to the Zhōu court, he longed for the land, lost in melancholy. He refused wine and meat, summoned no concubines, and only after months did he recover.

Guǎn Zhòng urged Duke Huán of Qí to journey through Liáokǒu, intending to reach this land and nearly succeeding. Xí Péng remonstrated: “My lord, you abandon the vastness of Qí, its teeming people, its scenic mountains and rivers, its abundant resources, its flourishing rites and music, its splendid robes, its palace filled with beauties, its court brimming with loyal ministers. With a shout, a million soldiers rise; with a gesture, the feudal lords obey. Why envy that land and forsake Qí’s altars of soil and grain to follow the ways of barbarians?”

Duke Huán, taking Xí Péng’s words to heart, desisted, and relayed them to Guǎn Zhòng. Guǎn Zhòng replied: “This is truly beyond Péng’s comprehension. I fear that land is beyond all knowing. Why cling to Qí’s wealth? Why heed Péng’s words?”

The explanation follows: King Mù of Zhōu once roamed as a spirit with a *huà-rén*, hence his later unrestrained wanderings, during which he passed through that land. For three years he forgot to return—such is the reception of those who are spiritually attuned. Yet upon his return, after mere months, he resumed wine and meat, summoned his concubines, and sought what was no longer of the spirit. From the sage-king Yǔ to King Mù’s time, governance shifted through the phases of loyalty, substance, and refinement, yet Mù’s journey and Yǔ’s arrival in that land revealed no difference in what they witnessed. Is this not proof that the workings of the spirit alone endure and remain whole?

How could the hegemony of Duke Huán or the worthiness of Xí Péng fathom this? Their narrow view of Qí’s proximity, believing nothing surpasses it, leads them to dismiss me as senile—like a frog in a shallow well, exulting in its pit’s joys, unaware of the Eastern Sea’s vast delight.

In the southern lands, people wear their hair loose and go bare; in the north, they wrap their heads and don furs; in the Central States, they wear caps and robes. Across the nine regions, livelihoods vary—some farm, some trade, some hunt, some fish—like winter furs and summer linen, boats for water and carts for land. These are acquired, shaped by nature, and made one’s own.

East of Yuè lies the land of Zhéxiū, where the eldest son, at birth, is sliced and eaten, deemed “fitting for younger brothers.” When a grandfather dies, they carry the grandmother away and abandon her, saying, “A ghost-wife cannot share a dwelling.” South of Chǔ lies the land of the Yán people, where upon a relative’s death, the flesh is left to rot before the bones are buried—only then is one called a filial son. West of Qín lies the land of Yíqú, where the dead are piled with wood and burned. The rising smoke is called “ascending afar,” and only then is one deemed filial. Such practices are upheld as governance above and custom below, yet they are not deemed strange.

The explanation follows: Where the five administrations extend and the seven tributes sustain, where heaven and earth converge, lies the Central States—hence its people wear caps and robes. Farmers, merchants, hunters, and fishers; winter furs and summer linen; boats and carts—all their deeds align with the central Way. Yet where the land leans toward yin or yang, so too do its customs. Thus, the southern lands, scorched by heat, favor loose hair and bare skin, while the northern lands, gripped by cold, wrap their heads and wear furs. In the farthest reaches of the four seas, the four wastes, the four poles, there exist lands like Zhémù, Yán, and Yíqú, where customs defy what eyes and ears know, startling human reason. Upheld as governance and embraced as custom, lived without doubt—all shaped by yin and yang. How deeply habit and custom can confound!

Confucius, traveling east, saw two children arguing and asked the reason. One child said: “I believe the sun is nearer at dawn and farther at noon.” The other insisted the sun was farther at dawn and nearer at noon. The first child argued: “At dawn, the sun is as large as a carriage canopy, but by noon it is like a plate—does this not prove that what is far appears small, and what is near appears large?” The second child countered: “At dawn, the sun is cool and refreshing, but by noon it is scalding like boiling water—does this not prove that what is near is hot, and what is far is cool?” Confucius could not decide. The children laughed: “Who says you are so wise?”

The explanation follows: The sun clings to the heavens and revolves with them. Above and below, in all eight directions, boundless and endless, impossible to exhaust—how can its distance be measured in an instant? The *Yuánmìng Bāo* states: “Heaven is incomplete in the northwest; yang culminates at nine, hence heaven’s circuit spans nine times nine, eighty-one myriad *lǐ*.” The *Lì Jì* says: “Numbers begin at one, stand at three, complete at five, flourish at seven, and rest at nine—thus heaven is nine myriad *lǐ* from earth.” Both schools inherit this teaching. If one accepts their claims, does it not align with the idea that what is near is hot and what is far is cool? Yet when tested against the carriage-canopy and plate analogy, it does not hold.

Thus, when the sun emerges from Fú Sāng as morning light, it is cool and refreshing, likened to a carriage canopy. When it faces Kūn Wú at high noon, it is like boiling water, likened to a plate. What should be large appears small; what should be cool feels warm; what should be near seems far. Though the children’s wisdom was slight, even they were confounded by these contradictions. To fathom the reason, even Confucius’s wisdom could not resolve it.

For the sun is like the Dao: deem it distant, and one may grasp it in a breath; deem it near, and one may remain lost for a lifetime. Speak of its vastness, and its use fills the void; speak of its smallness, and its absence goes unnoticed. Thus, to judge the sun by size is the mark of ignorance; to seek the Dao in refinement or coarseness is to not know the Dao.

Consider this: if one seeks fire from the sun using a fire-drill, it arrives in an instant—where then is the distinction between dawn and noon, near and far? Yet size and distance remain unfathomable—this is the sun’s mystery, its ceaseless motion through the ages.

Was Confucius truly unable to decide? He left it unexamined. The children hastily declared Confucius unwise, yet Confucius himself said: *“Do I possess knowledge? I possess no knowledge.”* If Confucius were truly wise, why would he be Confucius?

The petty squabbling over the sun’s size and distance is but childish bickering. *Equality*—this is the supreme principle of the world.

**Explanation:** To equalize the myriad things, allowing no distinction of high or low, is to merge self and other into oneness. In oneness, one aligns with the Dao—this is the supreme principle of the world. Hence, Zhuangzi’s *"On the Equality of Things."*

The same holds for physical objects. When a hair is evenly suspended, it may bear weight until it snaps—but if truly balanced, it will never break. Some dismiss this, yet there are those who understand its truth.

**Explanation:** In physical matters, the few cannot overcome the many, nor can the weak subdue the strong—this much is certain. Yet if one grasps the principle of *equality* and applies it, even the faintest hair can draw a thousand *jun* without snapping. Those who deny this are shackled by things; those who recognize it have penetrated the principle. Physical connections are but trifles—if one attains *equality*, the small can thus control the great. How much more so when one achieves the great *equality* of the supreme principle? What then could not be accomplished?

Zhan He once used a single silkworm’s thread for a line, a needle’s barb for a hook, a stalk of *jing* bamboo for a rod, and a grain of rice for bait—yet he drew fish as large as a cart from an abyss a hundred *ren* deep, amidst rushing currents, without the line breaking, the hook bending, or the rod warping. The King of Chu, hearing of this, summoned him in astonishment and asked the reason.

Zhan He replied: "I once heard my late master speak of Pu Juezi’s archery. With a weak bow and slender cord, he rode the wind and struck, bringing down two cormorants in the azure sky. His mind was focused, his hand in perfect balance. I took this as my model and practiced angling, spending five years mastering its way. When I stand by the river with rod in hand, my mind is free of distractions, fixed only on the fish. Casting the line and sinking the hook, my hand makes no distinction of light or heavy—nothing can disturb it. The fish see my hook and bait as mere drifting dust or foam, swallowing without hesitation. Thus, I can use the weak to control the strong, the light to draw the heavy."

"If Your Majesty governs the state with such skill, the world could be held in the palm of your hand—what further effort would be needed?"

The King of Chu said: "Well said."

**Explanation:** Using the weak to control the strong, the weak must break; using the light to draw the heavy, the light must be crushed. Why? Because their power is unequal. Yet if I, though weak, leverage the other’s strength to control it, that strength will not oppose me but serve me—thus, strength lies with me, and weakness with the other. Though I am light, if I harness the other’s weight to govern it, that weight will not contend with me but obey me—thus, weight lies with me, and lightness with the other. Then who can say the weak cannot control the strong, or the light draw the heavy? This is the principle by which Zhan He, with silk thread and needle-hook, drew cart-sized fish from an abyss a thousand *ren* deep, and Pu Juezi, with a weak bow and slender cord, brought down two cormorants in the azure sky.

Ah! Angling and archery are different arts, yet governance is not so distinct. Zhan He learned angling from Pu Juezi’s archery, then used it to instruct the King of Chu in statecraft. For one who attains the supreme principle of *equality*, though things may shift endlessly, how could they escape this principle? This is what Zhuangzi meant by *"Unify all things, and ten thousand affairs are complete."* Thus, the sage, with a body as insignificant as dust, governs the world as if holding it in one hand—how could this apply only to the state of Chu?

Gonghu of Lu and Qi Ying of Zhao both fell ill and together sought Bian Que’s treatment. Bian Que cured them...

Once their illnesses were cured together, Bian Que said to Gonghu and Qi Ying: "The ailments you suffered before were external invasions of your organs, which medicine and stone could remedy. Now, however, you bear congenital afflictions that have grown with your bodies. If I were to treat these for you, how would that be?" The two replied, "We would first like to hear the evidence."

Bian Que said to Gonghu: "Your will is strong but your *qi* is weak, so you excel in planning yet falter in decisiveness." To Qi Ying he said: "Your will is weak but your *qi* is strong, so you lack forethought and err in inflexibility. If I were to exchange your hearts, both would attain balance in virtue."

Thereupon, Bian Que gave them a poisoned wine to drink, rendering them unconscious for three days. He then opened their chests, extracted their hearts, exchanged them, and restored them. Applying a divine medicine, they awoke as before.

He explained: "Deliberation resides in the will, while decisiveness belongs to *qi*. The will commands *qi*—when the will is sufficient to lead *qi*, its expression is always measured. When the will is insufficient to command *qi*, it becomes enslaved to *qi* and disturbs the heart. Thus, excessive *qi* leads to inflexibility, while weak *qi* results in indecision.

I have often said that the will lies within oneself, untouched by transformation. Yet by clinging to the notion of a fixed self, one cherishes life and clings to the body. In cherishing the body, one becomes its servant. This is why Gonghu and Qi Ying’s will and thought were governed by the strength or weakness of their *qi* and physical form. That one’s will and thought should be enslaved to *qi* and body—truly, this is lamentable. Yet Bian Que could cure their ailments and alter the workings of creation—how wondrous is this!

Now, *Gonghu* suggests domineering arrogance, hence Gonghu’s strong will and aptitude for planning. *Ying* evokes infancy, hence Qi Ying’s weak will and lack of foresight. The two then took their leave and returned home.

Thereupon, Gonghu went to Qi Ying’s house and claimed his wife and children, but they did not recognize him. Likewise, Qi Ying went to Gonghu’s house and claimed his wife and children, but they, too, did not recognize him. The two households thus brought suit against each other, seeking Bian Que’s judgment to resolve the dispute. Bian Que explained the cause, and the dispute was settled.

He expounded: "Once, when Confucius traveled to Chu, he saw piglets nursing from their dead mother. After a moment, they glanced at one another, abandoned her, and fled. They did not see their mother as herself, nor did they find her like themselves. Thus, if something is not of one’s kind, even piglets will abandon their mother. But if it is of one’s kind, then even if Gonghu and Qi Ying returned to the wrong houses, their families would not doubt them.

Now, after Bian Que exchanged their hearts, only their forms remained enslaved to their original selves. Each returned to the other’s house yet did not recognize that their form was no longer their own. The two households, deceived by the change in appearance, failed to perceive that the two men were now of the other’s kind. Thus, they did not recognize them.

But tell me: in judging these two men, did the households seek them within their physical forms? Or did they seek them beyond their physical forms? If beyond, then why did they so readily accept Bian Que’s judgment? If within, then when they returned to their houses, how could they not recognize them? Why would they need Bian Que’s intervention?

Alas! From birth to death, the great transformations shift ceaselessly. From old age to infancy, how vastly do appearance, countenance, wisdom, and demeanor differ—far more than the exchange of Gonghu and Qi Ying’s forms! Yet the flow of great transformation is so subtle that people traverse it unawares. Bian Que’s exchange of their hearts was abrupt, so none could fail to be astonished by the change.

Moreover, when Gonghu and Qi Ying’s will and *qi* were exchanged, neither they nor their households could recognize one another. How much more so with the workings of creation upon the ten thousand things—born through transformation, dying through transformation, dying and being reborn without end, with no one knowing its origin! How could people possibly comprehend its reasons?

Once, when Yang Zhu set out, he wore plain white robes; upon returning, he wore black. His dog, not recognizing him, barked at him. This is why Yang Zhu stopped Yang Bu from beating his dog.

When Hu Ba played the zither, birds danced and fish leapt. Hearing this, Zheng Shiwen abandoned his home to study under Master Xiang. For three years, his fingers pressed the strings and hooked the pegs, yet he could not produce a single melody. Master Xiang said, 'You may return home now.'

Shiwen set down his zither and sighed: 'It is not that I cannot hook the strings or form melodies. What I seek does not lie in the strings, nor does my aspiration reside in sound. If my heart does not attain it within, and my instrument does not respond without, how dare I lift my hands to move the strings?' He asked for a little more time to observe what would follow.

Before long, he returned to Master Xiang. 'How is your zither playing now?' Master Xiang asked. Shiwen replied, 'I have attained it. Please, allow me to demonstrate.'"

Thus, when spring arrived, he struck the *shang* string to summon *Nanlü*, and a cool breeze suddenly arose, causing plants and trees to bear fruit. By autumn, he plucked the *jiao* string to rouse *Jiazhong*, and a warm wind gently swirled, making plants and trees flourish. In summer, he struck the *yu* string to invoke *Huangzhong*, and frost and snow descended together, while rivers and ponds froze over. By winter, he plucked the *zhi* string to stir *Ruibin*, and the sun’s rays blazed fiercely, melting solid ice in an instant.

As the piece neared its end, he commanded the *gong* string to harmonize the four, and the *jing* wind soared, auspicious clouds floated, sweet dew fell, and spring waters surged. Master Xiang then clapped his chest and leapt in joy, exclaiming, “How subtle is your playing! Even *Qingjiao* of Master Kuang or the pitch-pipes of Zou Yan could not surpass it. They would clutch their zithers and flutes to follow in your wake.”

The commentary states: The Dao may be transmitted but not received; it may be attained but not seen. Being unseen, it cannot be received; only what can be transmitted may be attained. A good teacher stops at what can be transmitted; a good student is thus able to gain it. In his learning, Shiwen sought to transcend the instrument and awaken to the Dao; in his teaching, Master Xiang used the instrument to convey sound. Therefore, when Master Xiang first sent him home, Shiwen still sought a little more time to observe what would follow. Once he had attained it, he could reverse the workings of creation, alter the sequence of the four seasons, make the *jing* wind soar, auspicious clouds float, sweet dew descend in streams, and spring waters surge—all without ever leaving the moment his fingers moved the strings. Thus, the turning of yin and yang lay within his grasp. How wondrous!

Master Xiang, at this, clapped his chest and leapt in joy, marveling at its subtlety. Had Shiwen merely followed Master Xiang’s transmissible techniques to become what Master Xiang already knew, he would never have earned such praise. Therefore, though the diligent student may say it is better to seek a teacher, the noble person desires self-attainment.

Alas! If the subtlety of a single art can reach such heights, then one who has attained the Dao could govern the world, bringing peace and prosperity—truly no difficult task.

Xue Tan studied singing under Qin Qing but had not mastered his teacher’s art when he declared himself proficient and took his leave. Qin Qing did not stop him but saw him off at the crossroads in the suburbs. There, he struck the rhythm and sang a mournful song, his voice shaking the trees and halting the passing clouds. Xue Tan then apologized and begged to return, never again daring to speak of leaving.

The commentary states: Those who study the Dao may be like Zheng Shiwen with Master Xiang—unaware of where his aspirations lay, sent home, only to return and earn his teacher’s marveling praise. Or they may be like Xue Tan with Qin Qing—believing they had mastered his art and leaving, only to apologize and beg to return, never again daring to speak of departure. This is what students must discern.

Qin Qing turned to his friend and said: “Long ago, Han E traveled east to Qi, running out of provisions. Passing through Yongmen, she sang for food. After she left, her lingering notes coiled around the beams for three days without fading, and the people nearby did not realize she had gone. At an inn, the innkeeper insulted her. Han E then sang a long, mournful lament, and the old and young of the village grieved, weeping and facing one another without eating for three days. They hurried to bring her back, and when she returned, she sang a joyful song. The villagers leapt and danced, unable to contain themselves, forgetting their former sorrow. They then richly rewarded her and sent her on her way. To this day, the people of Yongmen excel in singing and weeping, imitating Han E’s lingering voice.”

The commentary states: True sorrow is soundless yet mournful; true kinship is not smiling yet harmonious—meaning joy and grief cannot be feigned. Singing for food may not arise from sincere joy, and weeping in response to insult may not be true sorrow. Yet her art was so wondrous that the villagers, who had never known sorrow, wept and refused food upon hearing her lament; and those who had never known joy leapt and danced uncontrollably upon hearing her song. If feigned song and weeping can move people so deeply, how much more so when both performer and listener are genuine?

However, her art only worked in the village of Yongmen. Had she sung in Qi, there would surely have been those who recognized its falsity. Thus, her lingering voice was imitated only in Yongmen.

Bo Ya was skilled at playing the zither, and Zhong Ziqi was skilled at listening. When Bo Ya played with the intention of climbing a high mountain, Zhong Ziqi said, “How fine! Towering like Mount Tai!” When his intention turned to flowing water, Zhong Ziqi said, “How fine! Vast like the Yangtze and Yellow River!” Whatever Bo Ya conceived, Zhong Ziqi always grasped it.

Once, Bo Ya traveled to the northern side of Mount Tai. Suddenly, a violent storm arose, and he took shelter beneath a cliff. Overcome with sorrow, he took up his zither and played. First, he composed a piece for the ceaseless rain, then shifted to the sound of crumbling mountains. With each melody, Zhong Ziqi fully understood its essence. Bo Ya then set down his zither and sighed, “How fine! How fine! Your listening is such that your thoughts and images mirror my heart. Where can my music escape you?”

The commentary states: There are sounds, sounds of sounds, and that which sounds the sounds—all are heard. Once heard, the grandest does not exceed the *gong* note, and the subtlest does not go beyond the *yu*. By examining the purity or turbidity of *gong* and *yu* and comparing them to human affairs, where can sound escape? Thus, Ziqi’s skill in listening is not so extraordinary. Moreover, Bo Ya’s zither gained renown through Ziqi, while Ziqi’s listening would have no outlet for its artistry without Bo Ya. Liezi praised them for valuing the understanding of sound.

Yet Ji Zha’s observation of music surpasses even this.

*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Its Virtue, Scroll Ten Concludes*
*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Its Virtue, Scroll Eleven*

Presented by Jiang Yu, a *hesheng* of the Hangzhou Prefectural Academy of Song, for the imperial perusal.

**Tang Wen**

King Mu of Zhou, on his western expedition, crossed the Kunlun Mountains but did not reach Yan Mountain. On his return journey, before reaching the Central States, he encountered a craftsman named Yan Shi who offered his services. The king summoned him and asked, “What skills do you possess?”

Yan Shi replied, “I shall do as Your Majesty commands. However, I have already created something I wish you to see first.”

The king said, “Come with it tomorrow, and we shall observe it together.”

The next day, Yan Shi presented himself to the king. The king summoned him and asked, “Who is the person accompanying you?”

Yan Shi answered, “This is my creation, one capable of performing.”

The king scrutinized it closely. It walked, bowed, and raised itself—truly like a man. When the craftsman pressed its chin, it sang in perfect harmony with the pitch; when he lifted its head, it danced in precise rhythm. Its transformations were boundless, responding to every intention. The king took it for a real person and had his concubine, Lady Sheng, join him in observing it.

As the performance neared its end, the performer winked at the king’s attendants. Enraged, the king was about to execute Yan Shi on the spot. Terrified, Yan Shi immediately disassembled the performer before the king’s eyes, revealing it to be an assemblage of leather, wood, glue, lacquer, and pigments—white, black, and vermilion. The king examined it closely: within were liver, gallbladder, heart, lungs, spleen, kidneys, intestines, and stomach; without were sinews, bones, joints, skin, hair, teeth, and nails—all artificial yet perfectly formed. When reassembled, it appeared as before.

The king tested it: when he removed its heart, its mouth could not speak; when he removed its liver, its eyes could not see; when he removed its kidneys, its feet could not walk. Only then did King Mu smile in wonder and say, “Can human craftsmanship truly rival the workings of creation?”

He ordered a secondary chariot to carry Yan Shi back with him.

Ban Shu’s cloud-ladder and Mo Di’s flying kite were once deemed the pinnacle of skill. Yet when their disciples Dongmen Jia and Qin Guli heard of Yan Shi’s craft, they informed their masters, who thereafter never dared speak of their arts again and instead constantly wielded compass and square.

**Commentary:** Though an artificial construct, once taught to perform, it becomes an incitement to licentiousness. Thus, its ability to sing in harmony and dance in rhythm makes its winking unremarkable. Human skill indeed possesses marvels akin to flying kites or jade paper—yet these are mere objects. Man is the spirit of all things; surely he cannot be imitated by such assemblages. Yan Shi’s creation could walk, bow, and raise itself indistinguishably from a human, sing in perfect pitch, dance in exact measure, and transform endlessly at will—only then did it attain supreme mastery. Thus, even the skills of Ban Shu and Mo Di paled in comparison, and they dared not speak of their arts again, instead constantly wielding compass and square.

Alas! Human life—how could its potential be limited to Yan Shi’s craft? Yet men often pass through life without self-awareness, and when they encounter such marvels, they merely admire the skill, missing the deeper truth. How superficial!

Gan Ying, an ancient master archer, could draw his bow and cause beasts to crouch and birds to descend. His disciple Fei Wei learned archery from him and surpassed his teacher. Another disciple, Ji Chang, studied under Fei Wei.

Fei Wei said, “First learn not to blink, then we may speak of archery.”

Ji Chang returned home and lay beneath his wife’s loom, gazing upward at the moving shuttle. After two years, even when a needle’s tip brushed his eyelashes, he did not blink. He reported this to Fei Wei.

Fei Wei said, “Not yet. You must learn to see before you can shoot. See the small as large, the faint as clear—then come tell me.”

Ji Chang suspended a louse from a window with a hair and gazed at it from the south. Within ten days, it gradually appeared larger. After three years, it seemed as big as a cartwheel. When he looked at other things, they all appeared like hills and mountains. Then, using a bow of Yan horn and an arrow of northern reeds, he shot the louse, piercing its heart while the hair remained unbroken. He reported this to Fei Wei.

Fei Wei leapt up, striking his chest, and said, “You have mastered it!”

**Commentary:** Learning not to blink means not allowing external things to alter oneself. Mastering vision means transforming things through perception. If I am not altered by things, but things are transformed by me, then I can see the small as large and the faint as clear—thus, the ability to hit the mark lies within me. This is how Ji Chang pierced the louse.

After Ji Chang had mastered Fei Wei’s techniques, he calculated that only one man in the world could rival him and plotted to kill Fei Wei. They met in the wild, and as they exchanged arrows, their shafts collided midair and fell to the ground without raising dust. Fei Wei’s arrows were exhausted first, leaving Ji Chang with one final shot. When he loosed it, Fei Wei deflected it with the tip of a thorn branch, missing not a hair’s breadth. Thereupon, the two men wept, cast aside their bows, and bowed to each other on the road, pledging to become father and son. They swore an oath, cutting their arms, never to teach their arts to others.

**Commentary:** Mencius said, “Is the arrow-maker less humane than the armor-maker?”—meaning one must be cautious in choosing one’s craft. Thus, after Ji Chang mastered Fei Wei’s techniques, he plotted to kill him. For the learning of Ji Chang and the teaching of Fei Wei both ultimately aimed at harming others—this was their inevitable end. When Pang Meng learned archery from Yi and mastered his Way, he too killed Yi—this was the same principle. Mencius deemed Pang Meng’s killing of Yi justified, for Yi bore some blame in choosing unworthy companions. Had one studied archery like Master Gong’s disciple Si, how could one bear to use the Master’s Way to harm him? Fortunate was Fei Wei’s survival! Had he not deflected that final arrow with a thorn branch, he would not have escaped. Thus, the gentleman’s pursuit of learning is the archery of non-archery.

The teacher of Zaofu was named Tai Dou.

When Zaofu first began his apprenticeship in charioteering, he observed rites with utmost humility, yet Tai Dou remained silent for three years. As Zaofu’s reverence grew ever more meticulous, Tai Dou finally spoke: “The ancient poem says: *The son of a master bowyer must first weave baskets; the son of a master smith must first sew furs.* First observe my gait. When your stride matches mine, then you may take up the six reins and drive the six horses.”

Zaofu replied, “I shall follow your command.”

Tai Dou then erected wooden planks to form a path, barely wide enough for his feet, measuring each step as he placed them. He walked upon them, pacing back and forth without a single misstep. Zaofu learned this, and in three days mastered its art.

Tai Dou sighed, “How swift your grasp! You have attained it with such speed. All charioteering is like this. When you walked just now, your feet responded to your heart. Extend this to driving: harmony in the reins and bit, urgency and ease in the harmony of the lips, proper measure within the breast, and disciplined rhythm in the grip of the hands. When the center is inwardly aligned and outwardly attuned to the horse’s will, you can advance and retreat along the line, turn and curve within the compass, traverse great distances with energy to spare—truly, you have grasped the technique.

When the bit is mastered, the reins respond; when the reins are mastered, the hands respond; when the hands are mastered, the heart responds. Then you need not rely on sight, nor urge with the whip. The mind is at ease, the body upright, the six reins unconfused, and the twenty-four hooves land without error. In turning or advancing, retreating or halting, all is in perfect measure. Then the wheels leave no excess track beyond their path, the hooves no ground untouched. Neither mountain valleys nor level plains are perceived as different—they are as one. My art is exhausted. Remember this well.”

A commentary states: In all affairs under heaven, there are those that seem slow yet are urgent, appear delayed yet take precedence. What the foolish fail to perceive, the wise discern. Thus, in the art of charioteering, none do not assume that mastery begins with grasping the reins. Yet the teaching of Taidou first had the student observe its intent—just as archers first learn to see, bowmakers first craft baskets, and smiths first prepare furs. From this we know that even in the most minor of skills, without learning from a master, one will never recognize the essential first step. Even the wise cannot achieve mastery without cause. How swiftly Zaofu mastered the craft in three days! Yet had he not maintained utmost humility, enduring three years without instruction while observing ritual with ever greater care, his learning would not have been sincere, his thought not precise—how could he have attained it so swiftly? Extending what he learned to charioteering, no technique remained unmastered.

Now, to harness horses to a carriage and guide them with reins—six horses, twenty-four hooves—if a single hoof missteps, the excellence of all six is lost. Such is the difficulty of driving. Therefore, the skilled driver does not rely on sight, nor does he urge with the whip. To watch with the eyes is to see ever more confusion and fail to encompass all; to drive with the whip is to exhaust strength without achieving order. Only when inwardly attuned to the heart’s center and outwardly responsive to bit and reins can one remain at ease amid peril or level ground, urgency or leisure, the body ever upright whether advancing, retreating, turning, or curving. Then the wheels leave no excess track beyond their path, the hooves no ground untouched. "No excess track" does not mean no track at all, but that the wheels encounter no obstruction; "no ground untouched" does not mean no ground remains, but that peril and level ground offer no choice. When driving reaches this point, one no longer knows whether it is the self driving the horse or the horse drawing the carriage—all appears as one. Is this not wondrous? This is how Zaofu could command King Mu’s chariot, roaming freely to distant lands, crossing Kunlun, witnessing the sun’s descent, covering ten thousand *li* in a single day.

Alas! The subtlety of charioteering is profound indeed. Its art attains such marvels—how can mere "skill" suffice to name it? If Zaofu were to cast aside his reins and apply his mastery to the Dao, who could say it were impossible?

Yangzi said: "He who rules all under heaven must examine his driving." Examine this alone.

Wei Hei Luan killed Qiu Bingzhang out of personal enmity. Bingzhang’s son, Laidan, sought to avenge his father’s death. Laidan’s *qi* was fierce, his form exposed; he ate by counting grains and moved with the wind. Though enraged, he could not wield weapons to exact revenge. Ashamed to borrow strength from others, he swore to slaughter Hei Luan with his bare hands and sword.

A commentary states: Black is the color of *yin*; egg is the form of *yin*. Wei represents lofty prominence. Wei Hei Luan is the image of old *yin*. Bing means radiant flourishing; zhang, the completion of pattern.

Qiu represents land elevated at its center. Without Bingzhang, it is the image of old *yang*. Dan, discarding *yang*, becomes Laidan—thus the direction where young *yang* gradually grows. The *Yijing* states: "When *yin* doubts *yang*, there must be strife." *Yang* ever dwells in the great summer, its task being to give life, nurture, and foster growth, while *yin* retreats and lies concealed. Yet toward *yang* it cannot but harbor enmity. Thus, when *yin* comes to power in the depths of winter, it imprisons creatures, seizing and slaying Qiu Bingzhang. Yet even as *yin* flourishes, a single spark of *yang* qi has already begun to germinate in the palace of Yellow Bell—hence Laidan’s resolve to avenge his father’s death. *Yang* is inherently firm, so Laidan’s *qi* is fiercely potent, his form starkly exposed. Still in its germinal stage, he counts grains to eat and moves with the wind. Though enraged, he cannot wield weapons to exact revenge. Yet because his nature is unyielding, he feels shame in borrowing strength from others and swears to slaughter Wei Hei Luan with his bare hands and sword.

Wei Hei Luan’s will is fierce beyond all others; his strength matches that of a hundred men. His sinews, bones, skin, and flesh are not of mortal kind. He stretches his neck to meet the blade, bares his chest to receive the arrow; edges bend and points shatter, yet his body bears no mark. Confident in his might, he regards Laidan as a fledgling chick.

A commentary states: *Yin*’s task is to carve and restrain, and now is the season when it wields power amid unyielding ice. Thus his will is fierce, his strength peerless, his skin and bones inhuman; he meets blades and arrows without a scratch, and sees Laidan as but a fledgling chick.

Laidan’s friend, Shen Tuo, said: "Your hatred for Wei Hei Luan is extreme, and his underestimation of you is excessive. What plan will you devise?" Laidan, tears falling, replied: "I beg you to devise one for me." Shen Tuo said: "I have heard that Kong Zhou of Wei, a descendant of his lineage, obtained the treasured swords of the Yin emperor. When a mere boy wore one, he repelled the masses of three armies. Why not seek them?" Laidan then went to Wei, met Kong Zhou, and performed the rites of a servant-charioteer. He first offered his wife and children as hostages, then spoke of his desire.

A commentary states: The treasured sword is a divine instrument capable of governing and restraining. Yin means "center," akin to the *yin* of mid-spring. The treasured swords of the Yin emperor embody the harmonious *qi* that governs *yin* and *yang*, discerning and unerring. That his ancestor obtained them shows their Dao has endured since antiquity. The divine instrument is supremely subtle, with utmost softness driving the utmost hardness throughout the world. Thus a mere boy wearing one could repel the masses of three armies. Shen Tuo is one who can extend justice to those wronged, hence he devised this plan for Laidan. Kong Zhou is one who can navigate the utmost principles of human affairs, so Shen Tuo sent Laidan to seek the swords from such a man. Performing the rites of a servant-charioteer expresses utmost reverence. Offering his wife and children first pledges his sincerity.

Kong Zhou said: "I possess three swords; choose whichever you wish. None can kill a man, but first let me describe their forms. The first is called *Han Guang* (Containing Light): when you look, it cannot be seen; when you wield it, its presence is unknown. Where it touches, all boundaries dissolve; it passes through things, yet things remain unaware. The second is called *Cheng Ying* (Bearing Shadow): at the juncture of dawn’s first light and the break of day, or at dusk’s twilight and the onset of night, if you face north and observe closely, faintly, as if something exists, yet its form cannot be discerned. Where it touches, there is a faint sound; it passes through things, yet things are not harmed. The third is called *Xiao Lian* (Night Forged): in broad daylight, you see its shadow but not its light; at night, you see its light but not its form. When it strikes an object, it passes with a sound like *huo* (a sharp crack), then seals instantly. You sense its swiftness, yet it draws no blood. These three treasures have been passed down for thirteen generations, yet never put to use. They remain sealed in their cases, never once opened."

A commentary states: *Han Guang* preserves its light without shining—this describes the divine’s subtlety in all things. It cannot be seen because it has no form; its presence is unknown when wielded because it has no use. Where it touches, boundaries dissolve; it passes through things, yet things remain unaware—this is the Dao’s hidden constancy. *Cheng Ying* already has a shadow to bear. At the juncture of dawn’s first light and the break of day, or at dusk’s twilight and the onset of night—these are the times when *yin* and *yang* intersect. Seeking it by returning to the root, faintly, as if something exists, yet ultimately it cannot be discerned. Where it touches, there is a faint sound; it passes through things, yet things are not harmed—this is stirring the myriad things without opposition. *Xiao Lian* already has a form. In broad daylight, you see its shadow, for it is employed by *yang*. At night, you see its light, for it is governed by *yin*. Yet you see its shadow but not its light, its light but not its shadow—it does not yet appear as a distinct entity. When it strikes an object, it passes with a sharp sound, then seals instantly. You sense its swiftness, yet it draws no blood—it moves through the myriad things, giving life and nurturing, alternating flourishing and decline, its transformations endless. It compels those that live and transform to live and transform without cease, which may be called the swiftness of things. Yet the divine’s workings enter the formless through the seamless—this is the instant sealing after passing. Though swift, it does not harm things, nor can things harm it—this is drawing no blood. Passed down for thirteen generations, this speaks of traversing the cycles of *yin* and *yang*, enduring since antiquity. Never put to use, this is called the uselessness of the divine. Sealed in their cases, their concealment is profound. Never once opened, this is the absence of flaws in the divine.

Laidan said: "Even so, I must request the lowest of them." Kong Zhou then returned his wife and children and, after seven days of purification, at the time between dusk and darkness, knelt and presented the lowest sword. Laidan bowed twice and received it before departing.

A commentary states: The divine is that which subtly brings forth the myriad things.

Within the order of generation and transformation, when likened to form and appearance, there are those resembling the three swords. As for the *Xiao Lian*, it first emerges from the Great Simplicity, marking the inception of substance—thus Laidan necessarily requested the lowest of them, and Kong Zhou returned his wife and children, preserving the Way of mutual generation and pairing. The seven days of purification unify the will and forget the body. The time between dusk and darkness is the threshold of darkness and light, where the Dao is secretly transmitted. Laidan then took the sword and followed Black Egg. At that time, Black Egg lay drunk beneath the window, and from neck to waist, Laidan struck him thrice—Black Egg remained unaware. Taking Black Egg’s death as certain, Laidan hastened away. At the gate, he encountered Black Egg’s son and struck him three times, as though thrusting into emptiness. Black Egg’s son then laughed and said: "Why so foolish as to beckon me thrice?" Laidan, realizing the sword could not kill, sighed and returned home. When Black Egg awoke, he raged at his wife: "Drunk, you exposed me, leaving my throat sore and my waist stiff." His son said: "Earlier, Laidan came and met me at the gate, striking me three times—it also left my body aching and my limbs rigid. Did he seek to suppress me?"

A commentary states: Beneath the window lies the boundary of yin and yang. Drunk and sprawled, one is in a time of confusion and unawareness. When yin is at its zenith, only at the moment of their intersection and unawareness can it be truly harmed. Nevertheless, the sword of night-forged steel can only cause things to feel illness without drawing blood. Thus, when Laidan struck Black Egg with it, Black Egg raged at his wife, saying, "You exposed me while drunk, leaving my throat sore and my waist stiff." When he struck Black Egg’s son, the son said, "Earlier, you met me at the gate and beckoned me thrice—it also left my body aching and my limbs rigid." Laidan, realizing the sword could not kill, sighed and returned home. Yet though Black Egg remained unaware even as the blade struck, he still suffered a sore throat and stiff waist, his body gradually wasting away from that day. Thus, though there was a day of biting cold and a day of piercing frost, soon the spring sun rose warm, and the small departed while the great arrived. People are blind to the fact that the fourth month belongs to summer, unaware that its origin lay in Laidan’s subtle shift on that first day.

I have traced the Dao of yin and yang: their mutual generation is like father and son, their pairing like husband and wife. In their alternating use, they are ever born and ever die; in their struggle, they are ever enraged and ever hostile. When imprisoned, they become illness; when in power, they flourish. Though their Dao requires nothing external, its function resides in things—such is the nature of yin and yang. All things’ natures and human actions are but servants of yin and yang. If one examines human emotions and the changes of things, the nature of yin and yang is found to encompass all principles without exception. Thus, there are cases like Wei Black Egg, who killed Qiu Bingzhang out of private enmity, and Laidan, who vowed to slaughter Black Egg with his own sword. Yet the principle of yin and yang is such that as one waxes, the other wanes, and they can never be entirely severed. Thus, though Laidan harbored the intent to kill Black Egg, he could not slay him. Had Black Egg been killable, the principle of generation and transformation would have nearly ceased. If so, how could Wei Black Egg have killed Qiu Bingzhang? For Qiu Bingzhang had already been used and overcome by the flourishing force, and thus could be killed. But Wei Black Egg was then in power and flourishing—how could he be killed? Moreover, at that time, not only was the yang qi subtly stirring in Laidan, but Wei Black Egg already had his wife and son. Thus, though the Dao of yin and yang holds that yang is born at *zi* and yin at *wu*, within yin there is yang, and within yang there is yin—their arising, growth, decline, and cessation are beyond all reckoning.

He who understands these words of *Liezi* has grasped more than half of the Dao.

King Mu of Zhou launched a great campaign against the Western Rong, who presented him with the *kunwu* sword and fire-washed cloth. The sword measured a foot and a span, its red-hot steel blade cutting jade as easily as mud. The fire-washed cloth, when washed, must be cast into fire: the cloth turns the color of flame, while the grime turns the color of cloth. Removed from the fire and shaken, it gleams as white as snow. The prince believed no such things existed and dismissed their accounts as false. Xiao Shu said: "Does the prince cling so stubbornly to his own certainty, so stubbornly to his denial of principle?"

A commentary states: The *kunwu* sword and fire-washed cloth were gifts from the Western Rong, not possessions of the Central States. The prince, confined by what he had seen and heard, could not broadly comprehend the transformations and anomalies of things’ principles. To immediately dismiss such things as nonexistent and their accounts as false—is this not a denial of principle?

This chapter of *Liezi* wondrously extends beyond the bounds of heaven and earth. Whether the divine smelting of Nüwa’s stones, the Foolish Old Man’s moving of mountains, Kuafu’s pursuit of the sun, Bian Que’s healing of illness, Yanshi’s creation of the singing automaton, or Laidan’s wielding of the sword—nearly all expound the profoundest words of the boundless, clearing the obstructions of the mind, and reveal the wondrous principles of the infinite, opening what the eyes and ears cannot perceive. If even those of shallow knowledge and limited experience remain confined by what they see and hear, unable to deeply seek the ultimate principle, how can they ever free themselves from their shackles? Such is the intent behind concluding the chapter thus.

**Tang’s Inquiry: A Commentary**
All things emerge from and return to the mechanism, each following its inherent nature and inclinations, endlessly transforming into myriad forms and shapes. There are things even Yu the Great could not see, Bo Yi could not hear, and Yi Jian could not record. Who can exhaust their changes? Yet though their forms differ, their natures are the same. The people of Longbo’s land may be vast, but their minds and wisdom differ not from those of the tiny Jiao Yao. The Jiao Yao may be small in stature, but their likes and dislikes differ not from those of the Longbo. The midge is minute, yet its life is not lacking; the roc is immense, yet its nature is not excessive. The great *chun* tree may live long, yet it too dies; the ephemeral mushroom may perish young, yet it too has lived. The ignorant are bewildered by the unevenness of things’ transformations, unaware that their natures remain unchanging. Thus, they boast of longevity and lament early death, cling to love and shun aversion, entangled and confused, toiling endlessly without cease.

Therefore, *Liezi* employs *Tang’s Inquiry* to distinguish the great and small, same and different, vast and minute, long and short. In essence, if one seeks this chapter through the ultimate Dao, there are things like the sun’s distance, which a child can discern yet Confucius could not decide; things like Bian Que’s healing of illness, where Gong Yu and Qi Ying and their two households could not understand one another—all are deluded by changes in form, unaware that their fundamental nature is no different. If one knows what is shared, then everywhere is unity. Thus, Pujuzi’s archery can be used for angling, and the principles of archery and angling can be used to govern the state. Zheng Shiwen and Bo Ya achieved mastery of the zither through this, while Ziqi excelled in listening. Fei Wei and Ji Chang became renowned archers through this, and Zaofu attained precision in charioteering.

Yanshi’s creation of the automaton, Qin Qing’s mastery of song—these too arise from this same Dao. If these several masters had cast aside their arts to advance toward the Dao, who could say it was not so?

All these myriad transformations of the ten thousand things cannot escape the workings of yin and yang. Thus, in the end, Wei Hei Luan killed Bing Zhang of Qiu over a personal grudge, and Lai Dan plotted to avenge his father’s enmity.

Yet the inequality of things is the nature of things. If one wishes to make them equal, one must first grasp the Dao by which they may be made equal. To dismiss the transformations of the ten thousand things with indifference and claim one has achieved equality is like one who covers their eyes and plugs their ears, believing none can see or hear—how can this control the mind’s restless wanderings?

In the end, Prince Guo’s overconfidence in his own judgment and his obstinate distortion of principle stem from this very error.

*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Profound Virtue*, Scroll Eleven, concludes

*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Profound Virtue*, Scroll Twelve
Humbly presented by Jiang Yu, Inner Student of the Hangzhou Prefecture School of the Song Dynasty
*On Effort and Fate, Part One*

Effort addressed Fate, saying: "What are your achievements compared to mine?"
Fate replied: "What merit have you rendered to things that you would compare yourself to me?"
Effort said: "Longevity or early death, success or failure, nobility or baseness, wealth or poverty—these are all within my power."
Fate said: "The wisdom of Peng Zu did not surpass that of Yao and Shun, yet he lived eight hundred years. The talent of Yan Yuan did not fall below that of ordinary men, yet he died at thirty-two. The virtue of Confucius did not rank below that of feudal lords, yet he was besieged in Chen and Cai. The conduct of King Zhou of Yin did not surpass that of the Three Benevolent Ones, yet he occupied the throne. Ji Zha held no rank in Wu, while Tian Heng usurped the state of Qi. Boyi and Shuqi starved on Mount Shouyang, while the Ji clan grew richer than Zhan Qin. If these were within your power, why do the long-lived die young, the sagely suffer hardship while the perverse prosper, the worthy live in lowliness while the foolish enjoy honor, and the good remain poor while the wicked grow rich?"
