Effort said: "If it is as you say, then I have indeed no merit in things, yet why do things turn out thus? Is this not what you control?"
Fate replied: "Since it is called Fate, how can there be one who controls it? I merely follow the straight and yield to the crooked. Things live long or die young, succeed or fail, rise or fall, grow rich or poor—how can I comprehend them? How can I comprehend them?"

*Explication*:
Effort is what man does; Fate is what Heaven ordains. Heaven does not depend on man, nor does man complete without Heaven. That effort is constrained by Fate, and Fate arises from effort, cannot easily be distinguished in terms of merit. Comparing the weightiest of efforts with the most tenuous of Fates, how can one say effort’s merit is greater? Comparing the most abundant of Fates with the most meager of virtues, how can one say Fate’s merit is not profound?

Those who emphasize effort, though Fate governs, believe it arises from inherent nature and do not call it Fate. Those who emphasize Fate, though nature governs, believe it arises from Fate and do not call it nature. Both are one-sided views. Examining one aspect alone—Peng’s longevity and Yan’s early death—one might suspect they were determined by Fate. Yet not all like Peng live long, nor do all of Yan’s talent necessarily die young; perhaps this arises from effort. Thus, to say effort has merit in things yet plays no part in Fate is untenable; to say all things are governed by Fate with no role for effort is equally untenable.

Nevertheless, what arrives without being summoned, what occurs without knowing why—this is Fate. Since it is called Fate, it governs the ten thousand things without heeding them. If there were one who controlled it, how could it be called Fate? Thus, it follows the straight without exhaustion through all ages; it yields to the crooked, completing the ten thousand things without omission. Though one speaks of following, there is none who follows; though one speaks of yielding, there is none who yields. The straight is straight of itself; the crooked is crooked of itself. Longevity or early death, success or failure, nobility or baseness, wealth or poverty—these arise neither from Heaven nor from man. Like the raven’s blackness, the swan’s whiteness, the *chun* tree’s longevity, the mushroom’s brevity—all are self-determined. Who is the agent? Only that things act of themselves, with none acting upon them. Thus, these eight conditions in man are like plants whose roots determine their sprouts. Fine fruit follows blossoms; the finest grain’s fruit comes from its excellent seed. Though there be foul shoots, their growth must follow their roots. Tracing to their source, not even a hair’s deviation is found. How can one know that today’s abundance of Fate does not arise from past abundance of virtue? Or that today’s abundance of virtue does not accumulate for a future abundance of Fate? These are all unknowable.

If so, then Fate is not necessarily distinct from effort, nor effort from Fate. How then can one assert the existence of Fate? How can one deny it? Even Fate does not know why it is so. This is what makes it Fate. How can one hastily take the crude calculations of alleyway rustics, demanding recompense from the Creator, insisting that the worthy live long and the wicked die young, the wise be honored and the foolish debased, the good grow rich and the evil remain poor? Their failure to comprehend Fate is extreme.

Only those who truly know Fate follow Heaven’s natural principle, cultivate human effort without neglect, forget both longevity and early death, delight in both success and failure, adapt to both honor and wealth, and remain unchanged by poverty or riches. This is what the sage calls "rejoicing in Heaven and knowing Fate," and the reason *Liezi* composed the chapter "Effort and Fate." In *Zhuangzi*, the *Great Master* concludes with Zisang’s discourse on Fate, deeming this the ultimate teaching of the *Great Master*. Beigongzi said to Ximenzi: "You and I live in the same age, yet you are successful. We belong to the same clan, yet you are respected."

We share the same appearance, yet you are beloved. We speak alike, yet your words are valued. We act the same, yet your conduct is deemed sincere. We serve in office together, yet you are honored. We till the soil together, yet you grow rich. We trade together, yet you profit. I wear coarse hemp, eat coarse grain and oysters, dwell in a thatched hut, and walk on foot. You wear embroidered silks, feast on fine millet and meat, live in a mansion with towering beams, and ride in a carriage drawn by four horses. At home, you dismiss me with cheerful disdain; at court, you display open contempt. You do not visit me, nor do we travel together—this has been so for years. Do you truly believe your virtue surpasses mine?"

Ximenzi replied: "I have no way to know the truth of this. You pursue affairs and remain poor; I pursue them and succeed. Is this proof of our differing worth? Yet you claim we are the same—your face is shameless indeed."

Beigongzi had no reply. He left in distress. On the road, he met Master Dongguo, who asked: "Where have you been, that you return with such a halting step and a look of deep shame?"

Beigongzi recounted what had happened. Master Dongguo said: "I will relieve your shame. Let us go together to question Ximenzi. Ask him: 'Why do you so deeply humiliate Beigongzi?' Press him to explain."

Ximenzi said: "Beigongzi claims we share the same age, clan, appearance, speech, and conduct, yet differ in rank, wealth, and poverty. I told him: 'I have no way to know the truth of this. You pursue affairs and remain poor; I pursue them and succeed. Is this proof of our differing worth? Yet you claim we are the same—your face is shameless indeed.'"

Master Dongguo said: "Your notion of 'worth' refers only to differences in talent and virtue. My understanding of 'worth' is different. Beigongzi is rich in virtue but poor in Fate; you are rich in Fate but poor in virtue. Your success is not due to wisdom, nor is his poverty due to folly. Both are Heaven’s doing, not man’s. Yet you boast of your favorable Fate, while Beigongzi feels shame for his virtue—neither of you recognizes the principle of what is inherently so."

Ximenzi said: "Enough, Master. I dare not speak further."

After Beigongzi returned home, he put on his coarse hemp robe and felt the warmth of fox and badger fur. He ate his coarse beans and tasted the savor of fine grain and meat. He sheltered in his thatched hut as if beneath the shade of a grand hall. He rode in his humble cart as if adorned with ornate carvings.

All his life, he remained at ease, unaware of honor or disgrace as belonging to others or to himself.

Master Dongguo heard of this and said: "Beigongzi has slept long indeed—yet a single word could awaken him. How easily his sorrow was dispelled!"

Explanation:
The movement of the Heavenly Dao proceeds from west to north. You (酉) is the gate through which the myriad things close, hence the name Ximenzi. The north is where the myriad things are stored and where the essence of transformation is most profound, hence the name Beigongzi. Ximenzi, facing inward toward his dwelling, is thus rich in Fate but poor in virtue. Beigongzi, however, conceals himself deeply, and so is rich in virtue but poor in Fate.

Master Dongguo has labored through the trigram Kan and emerged into Zhen—he does not abide in non-action but moves into stillness through activity. Thus, he could dispel Beigongzi’s shame and awaken him.

I have examined Fate: it arises from no governing agency yet arrives inevitably, something no living being can escape. Even the sages Yao, Shun, Yi, and Qi, or Confucius; the worthies Jizha, Zhan Qin, and Yanzi—all were bound by Fate and could not alter it throughout their lives. No wonder Beigongzi felt shame for his virtue, and Ximenzi boasted of his favorable Fate.

Yet if we say Fate arrives from no agency, then its coming is natural, with no one to bring it. If the bringer is fundamentally nonexistent, then who receives its constraints? Whether constrained or not depends on oneself alone. Thus, if one does not accept Fate, one is constrained by it; if one understands Fate, one constrains it rather than being constrained by it.

From this, sages may be impoverished and the benevolent may die young; the good may be poor and the worthy may be lowly. Yet we have never heard of Fate altering the conduct of sages and worthies. Therefore, when Beigongzi heard Master Dongguo’s words and recognized the principle of what is inherently so, he remained at ease all his life, unaware of honor or disgrace as belonging to others or to himself.

If this is so, can Fate truly constrain him? This is the achievement of one who has attained the Way of Fate.

Guan Yiwu and Bao Shuya shared a bond of profound friendship, dwelling together in Qi. Guan Yiwu served Prince Jiu, while Bao Shuya served Prince Xiaobai. The Qi ruling house was rife with favored clans, and the lines of legitimate and illegitimate succession ran parallel. The people of the state feared chaos. Guan Zhong and Shao Hu escorted Prince Jiu in flight to Lu, while Bao Shuya escorted Prince Xiaobai to Ju. Soon after, Gongsun Wuzhi staged a rebellion, leaving Qi without a ruler, and the two princes vied to return. Guan Yiwu clashed with Xiaobai on the road to Ju, loosing an arrow that struck Xiaobai’s belt hook. Once Xiaobai ascended the throne, he coerced Lu into executing Prince Jiu; Shao Hu died for him, while Guan Yiwu was imprisoned. Bao Shuya said to Duke Huan: “Guan Yiwu is capable—he can govern the state.” Duke Huan replied: “He is my enemy. I wish to kill him.” Bao Shuya said: “I have heard that a worthy lord harbors no private grudges. Moreover, one who can serve his own master can surely serve a lord. If you seek hegemony over kings, none but Yiwu will suffice. You must pardon him.” Thereupon, Guan Zhong was summoned. Lu returned him to Qi, and Bao Shuya welcomed him beyond the city gates, releasing him from captivity. Duke Huan treated him with reverence, placing him above the ministers Gao and Guo. Bao Shuya humbled himself before him, entrusting him with state affairs, and he was styled “Uncle Zhong.” Duke Huan thus became a hegemon.

Guan Zhong once sighed: “In my youth, when I was poor and distressed, I once traded goods with Bao Shu, taking more than my share of the profits. Bao Shu did not deem me greedy, knowing I was impoverished. I once devised a plan for Bao Shu that ended in utter failure, yet he did not think me foolish, knowing that circumstances may be favorable or unfavorable. I was employed three times and thrice dismissed by my lords, yet Bao Shu did not consider me worthless, knowing I had not met with the right moment. I fought in three battles and fled all three, yet Bao Shu did not think me cowardly, knowing I had an aged mother. When Prince Jiu was defeated, Shao Hu died for him, while I endured imprisonment and humiliation. Bao Shu did not think me shameless, knowing I did not scorn minor setbacks but felt shame if my name were not renowned throughout the world. My parents gave me life, but Bao Shu truly knew me.” Thus the world praises the fine friendship of Guan and Bao, and Xiaobai’s skill in employing the capable. Yet in truth, there was no fine friendship, nor any skill in employing the capable.

In truth, there was no fine friendship, nor any skill in employing the capable—not because there existed some other fine friendship or some other skill in employing the capable. Shao Hu did not choose to die; he had no choice but to die. Bao Shuya did not choose to recommend the worthy; he had no choice but to recommend them. Xiaobai did not choose to employ his enemy; he had no choice but to employ him.

Explanation: In the affairs of the world, whatever succeeds beyond the reach of human foresight, whatever arrives without being summoned—this is Fate. When Guan Yiwu and Bao Shuya first shared their profound bond, their hearts could be said to have known no distance. Later, when Yiwu served Prince Jiu and Bao Shu served Prince Xiaobai, though their lords differed, their hearts remained unchanged. When the two princes contended for entry and clashed on the road to Ju, and Guan Zhong’s arrow struck Duke Huan, at that moment, how could Yiwu have harbored any intention of serving Duke Huan? After Duke Huan ascended the throne and coerced Lu into killing Prince Jiu, he even petitioned Lu to surrender Guan Zhong as his enemy, wishing to vent his hatred by mincing him—how then could Duke Huan have harbored any intention of employing Uncle Zhong? Even if Bao Shuya could forget the enmity of the Ju road and not abandon their former friendship, he should still have refrained from recommending Guan Zhong’s worth, given Duke Huan’s hatred. Moreover, Guan Zhong, in serving Prince Jiu, had failed to achieve success beforehand and failed to die for him afterward—his worthiness was hardly exemplary. Yet now, Bao Shuya disregarded Qi’s suspicions and recommended him; Duke Huan forgot his lack of service to Prince Jiu and, setting aside his enmity, employed him; Guan Zhong, too, did not feel shame for his past imprisonment and humiliation, nor did he decline his position, but devoted himself to Qi, forgetting his former service to Prince Jiu. None of this could have been foreseen by human foresight.

Once Guan Zhong was employed by Qi, he filled the granaries, clarified rites and norms, enriched the state, and strengthened its armies. He turned misfortune into fortune and defeat into victory, ultimately uniting the feudal lords nine times and rectifying the world. The people have reaped his benefits to this day. Thus Confucius praised him, saying: “Were it not for Guan Zhong, we would still be wearing our hair loose and fastening our robes on the left.” Was Guan Zhong’s contribution to later generations and the world at large a trifling one? Indeed, Guan Zhong did not cling to the petty fidelity of common men and women, nor did he drown himself in a ditch. Had Bao Shuya’s words gone unheeded and Duke Huan’s enmity remained unresolved, how could Bao Shuya have preserved their friendship? Where could Guan Zhong have applied his talents?

Nevertheless, Guan Zhong ultimately achieved the great feat of uniting the feudal lords and rectifying the world, so that the people have reaped his benefits to this day. Thus Bao Shuya’s recommendation of his enemy, Duke Huan’s employment of his enemy, and Guan Zhong’s enduring humiliation in Lu to devote himself to Qi—all these were matters of necessity, not choice. From this perspective, when the world praises the fine friendship of Guan and Bao and Xiaobai’s skill in employing the capable, it is not that there was any fine friendship or any skill in employing the capable. All was the work of Fate, not something within human power. If one claims there was fine friendship, then what was the state of Guan Zhong’s friendship during the battle on the road to Ju? If one claims there was skill in employing the capable, why did Duke Huan’s employment of Guan Zhong depend on Bao Shuya’s words? The point is not that only Duke Huan and Guan and Bao were thus. In all cases, the trust between friends and the duty between lord and minister are no different. Yet upon close examination, the conduct of these several men was all marked by impartiality of heart. With hearts fixed on perfect impartiality, friendship need not be preserved to be preserved, enmity need not be abandoned to be abandoned, and loyalty need not be demonstrated to be demonstrated.

This was the foundation upon which Duke Huan established his hegemony.

Alas! If one can but make one’s heart impartial, then in all things it will naturally accord with the spontaneous workings of Heaven’s mandate. Why, then, should one debase oneself and bend the Way in petty pursuit of conformity to the whims of others?

Moreover, even the governance of a hegemon—achieved through cunning strategy and force—was not something within human power to accomplish. Extending this principle upward, we see that the Way of the August Sovereigns, the virtue of the Divine Emperors, and the radiant governance of the True Kings were all spontaneous and self-arising, not made by human effort. This much is evident.

When Guan Yiwu fell ill, Duke Xiao Bai visited him and said, “Your illness, Uncle Zhong, has grown grave. There is no need to avoid speaking of it. If it should become critical, to whom shall I entrust the state?”

Guan Yiwu asked, “Whom does my lord desire?”

Duke Xiao Bai replied, “Bao Shuya would be suitable.”

Guan Yiwu said, “He is not. He is a man of integrity and purity, a fine gentleman. Yet toward those who do not measure up to him, he does not associate with them. Once he hears of another’s fault, he never forgets it. If you place him in charge of the state, above he will probe the ruler, and below he will oppose the people. His offense against you, my lord, will not be long in coming.”

*Explanation:* People often cite Guan Zhong’s refusal to entrust the state to Bao Shuya as evidence of his judgment. In truth, Guan Zhong knew Bao Shuya’s talents were not suited to governing the state, for he feared Bao Shuya would offend the ruler. Is it not better to spare him the burden of governance and allow him to remain whole, rather than to place him in office only to incur guilt? This was Guan Zhong’s way of preserving their friendship.

Duke Xiao Bai asked, “Then who would be suitable?”

Guan Yiwu replied, “If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do. His character is such that above he forgets his lord, and below the people do not rebel. He feels shame for not equaling the Yellow Emperor and pities those who do not equal himself. One who distributes virtue to others is called a sage; one who distributes wealth to others is called a worthy. To rule others with one’s worthiness is never to win their allegiance; to humble oneself before the worthy is never to fail in winning their allegiance. In governing the state, he does not pry; in managing his household, he does not spy. If you must choose, then Xi Peng will do.”

Thus, Guan Yiwu did not slight Bao Shuya out of preference, nor did he favor Xi Peng by choice. To favor one at the outset may lead to slighting him in the end; to slight one in the end may follow from favoring him at the start. The ebb and flow of favor and disfavor do not depend on me.

*Explanation:* “Above he forgets his lord” refers to his governance being unobtrusive. “Below the people do not rebel” refers to his people being simple and sincere. Feeling shame for not equaling the Yellow Emperor means he does not presume upon his own superiority. Pitying those who do not equal himself means his heart is set on rescuing others with goodness. To distribute virtue to others is to enable the people to return to their true nature and restore their original condition—this is the Way of the sage. To distribute wealth to others is to ensure the people lack nothing in providing for their parents above and nurturing their children below—this is the virtue of the worthy. To rule others with one’s worthiness is like a mountain’s barren slopes; to humble oneself before the worthy is like a marsh’s fertile growth.

As for “in governing the state, he does not pry; in managing his household, he does not spy,” this does not mean he truly fails to see or hear. Rather, his Way is sufficient to embrace all things. Herein lies the reason Xi Peng was suitable and Bao Shuya was not.

An ancient saying goes: “Without blindness and deafness, one cannot achieve great success.” This is precisely the point.

In the *Classic of Poetry*, the preface to the *Gejü* speaks of the Wei ruler’s treacherous miserliness and narrow impatience, while the poem itself says: “It is this narrow heart that is the target of reproach.” The harm of a narrow heart to governance is thus made clear.

When one walks the path with others, the ebb and flow of favor and disfavor follow the principle of perfect impartiality and the natural order of things, not one’s own will. Only Guan, Bao, and Xi understood this. Thus, the waxing and waning of their relationships adhered to Heaven’s principle and did not follow personal inclination. Was this not the reason the governance of Qi could honor the Zhou and strengthen the state?

*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Primal Virtue*, Scroll Twelve—Concluded
*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Primal Virtue*, Scroll Thirteen

Submitted by your humble student, Jiang Yu, an Inner Hall scholar of the Hangzhou Prefecture Academy of Song:

**On the Interplay of Effort and Fate**

Deng Xi wielded the doctrine of "two possibilities," crafting endless arguments. During Zichan’s governance of Zheng, he composed the *Bamboo Penal Code*. The state of Zheng adopted it, yet Deng Xi repeatedly challenged Zichan’s administration. Zichan yielded to him. Zichan then seized and executed him, swiftly putting him to death.

Thus, Zichan did not *choose* to employ the *Bamboo Penal Code*—he had no choice but to use it. Deng Xi did not *choose* to humble Zichan—he had no choice but to yield. Zichan did not *choose* to execute Deng Xi—he had no choice but to kill him.

*Explication*:
Zichan served as Zheng’s minister for three years, and the virtuous submitted while the wicked were transformed—his governance should have been unassailable. Yet Deng Xi repeatedly challenged and humbled him. Zheng adopted Deng Xi’s *Bamboo Penal Code*, yet though they should have cherished its author, they ultimately executed him. Such are the workings of principle beyond human reckoning.

The world claims that Zichan’s execution of Deng Xi for composing the *Bamboo Penal Code* was an act of personal agency, failing to recognize that there were, in truth, forces at play compelling him to use the code, to yield, and to kill. Emperor Wen of Han, moved by Lady Tiying’s plea, abolished corporal punishment—yet none in later generations could restore it. The same principle applies.

According to *Xunzi* and the *Zuozhuan*, Si Zhuan’s execution of Deng Xi occurred *after* Zichan’s death, leading scholars to suspect an error in the classics. Yet the *Liezi* concerns itself solely with illuminating the Dao and elucidating principle—what it grasps is the essence, while the incidental is left aside. Why quibble over minor discrepancies in its words?

To live when life is possible is Heaven’s blessing.
To die when death is due is Heaven’s blessing.

To live when life is possible yet not to live is Heaven’s punishment.
To die when death is due yet not to die is Heaven’s punishment.
To be able to live or to die, and thus to live or to die—this exists.
To be unable to live or to die, and thus to die or to live—this too exists.
Yet life and death, neither of things nor of the self, are all a matter of fate, beyond the reach of wisdom’s contrivance.

**Explanation:**
To live in health and peace, embracing virtue—this is to live.
To die in fullness of years, completing one’s fate—this is to die.
These are the cases of being able to live or to die, and thus to live or to die.
The *Hongfan* speaks of the Five Blessings—these are what are called Heaven’s blessings.
To live in misfortune, cut short by calamity, or to live in sickness, evil, sorrow, or poverty—these are what the *Hongfan* calls the Six Adversities, what are called Heaven’s punishments.
To live or die as one should is the constant principle of things.
To die or live unexpectedly is a matter of fortune or misfortune.
Life and death are one—some deem it Heaven’s blessing, some Heaven’s punishment; some arise from the constant, some from the unexpected.
Even the wisest should be able to discern their distinctions.
Yet the cause of life in life, the cause of death in death—at the very moment of receiving life, the determinant of death is already set.
To extinguish me in death, there is also a determinant of life.
Life and death, life and death—externally, not to be seized by things; internally, not to be controlled by the self.
All is commanded by Heaven, beyond the reach of wisdom.
What then is to be done?
Only by understanding this can life and death remain unaltered.

Thus it is said:
Profound and boundless, the Dao of Heaven converges of itself.
Vast and undivided, the Dao of Heaven moves of itself.
Neither Heaven nor Earth can transgress it, neither sages nor wisdom can interfere with it, neither ghosts nor spirits can deceive it.
The natural is silently accomplished, leveled and pacified, guided and welcomed.

**Explanation:**
"Boundless" is the residue of division; "convergence" is the gathering of movement.
"Profound" speaks of what is hidden and hard to perceive; "vast" speaks of what is distant and without limit.
The manifest life of things converges with the Dao.
Subtle and in harmony with the Dao, it moves with the constant Dao of Heaven.
Converging and moving of itself, the myriad things likewise live and die of themselves.
Though Heaven’s spirit and Earth’s abundance,
they cannot transgress its natural course;
though the subtlety of sages and wisdom,
they cannot interfere with its natural course;
though the numinous power of ghosts and spirits,
they cannot deceive its natural course.
Thus it is silently accomplished without words or action,
leveled and pacified without bias or deviation,
guided and welcomed without beginning or end.
Such is the ultimate of what fate enacts.

Season Liang, a friend of Yang Zhu, fell gravely ill after seven days. His sons gathered around weeping and sought physicians. Season Liang said to Yang Zhu, "My sons are so unworthy—why do you not sing for them to enlighten them?" Yang Zhu sang:

"Heaven does not know—
how could man awaken?
Not blessed by Heaven,
nor cursed by man.
Is it I or you
who does not understand?
Physicians or shamans—
do they truly know?"

His sons did not comprehend and ultimately summoned three physicians. The first was Master Jiao, the second Master Yu, and the third Master Lü. They examined his illness.

Master Jiao said to Season Liang, "Your cold and warmth are imbalanced, your deficiency and excess disordered. Your illness arises from irregular eating and drinking, indulgence in sensual pleasures, and the scattering of essence and thought—neither Heaven nor ghosts are to blame. Though grave, it can be treated." Season Liang said, "A common physician." He promptly dismissed him.

Master Yu said, "From the beginning, your prenatal qi was insufficient, while your postnatal nourishment was excessive. The illness did not arise in a single day; its causes accumulated gradually. It cannot be cured." Season Liang said, "A skilled physician."

And he was about to eat.

Master Lü said, "Your illness arises neither from Heaven, nor from man, nor from ghosts. When you received life and took form, there was already one who ordained it, and one who knew it. What can medicine and stone do for you?"

Season Liang said, "A divine physician." He then sent him away with great respect.

Soon after, Season Liang’s illness resolved of itself.

Explanation: The meaning of *jiǎo* is to grasp what is crooked and straighten it, which is not natural. Master Jiao’s medicine sought to attack the illness after it had already arisen in life—this is the way of common physicians. Master Yu spoke of compliance, so his medicine lay in the beginning of life, deeming the illness incurable—this is the way of skilled physicians. Master Lü spoke of synthesis, so his medicine equated life and death as one. His words emerged from before the receiving of life and form, entering the spirit with profound meaning—this is the way of divine physicians.

Thus did Season Liang accept life and death with such equanimity. Was it not for this that Yang Zhu, upon seeing his gate after his death, sang?

Life is not preserved by valuing it, nor is the body enriched by cherishing it. Life is not cut short by despising it, nor is the body diminished by slighting it. Thus, valuing it may not preserve life, despising it may not bring death, cherishing it may not enrich the body, and slighting it may not diminish it. This seems contradictory, yet it is not. Life and death, enrichment and diminution, arise of themselves. Or valuing it may preserve life, despising it may bring death, cherishing it may enrich the body, and slighting it may diminish it. This seems compliant, yet it is not. Life and death, enrichment and diminution, still arise of themselves.

Explanation: Life and death, enrichment and diminution, are already ordained before the receiving of life and form. How can valuing or despising, cherishing or slighting, add or subtract to them after life has begun? The body is the entrusted form of Heaven and Earth, and life is the entrusted compliance of Heaven and Earth. Since Heaven and Earth have already entrusted transformation to us, they cannot violate the natural boundaries of our allotment—how much less can these things, which are not yours to possess, be preserved or destroyed by valuing or despising, enriched or diminished by cherishing or slighting?

Nevertheless, valuing and despising, preservation and destruction, cherishing and slighting arise from perception. False distinctions of sameness and difference: the many perceive sameness, the solitary perceive difference. Taking sameness as compliance and difference as opposition—if one follows the inherent nature, where lie opposition or compliance? To call them opposition or compliance is but semblance, not reality. In their essence, all things are as they are.

Thus, tracing the origin of life and form, though life knows not the body, and the body knows not life, how much less can they know valuing or despising, cherishing or slighting? Nevertheless, Liezi discusses this to clarify that the subtleties of life and form all arise from the natural, intending to dispel the world’s delusions—those who cling to life in pursuit of principle, or who enslave their forms to profit.

As for those who honor life and revere the root, desiring to be entrusted with the world, they would rather tread the path of seeming compliance than act with carelessness.

Zhai Xiong said to King Wen: "What is long cannot be lengthened, what is short cannot be shortened. What is lost in calculation—what is it?"

Laozi said to Guan Yin: "Who knows the reason for what Heaven detests? To anticipate Heaven’s will, to weigh profit and harm, is not as good as ceasing."

Explanation: That length and shortness cannot be altered is like the legs of ducks and cranes, which cannot be cut or extended. Before life and form existed, there was already one who ordained it. Calculation is like wisdom—what can wisdom do? All is Heaven alone.

Thus, what man detests is what Heaven detests. Does Heaven privately detest man? The reason must have its origin, but it is not limited to what the ears and eyes perceive, nor can it be known in an instant. To receive it compliantly is acceptable.

But to use wisdom as a chisel, to weigh and sharpen it, exhausting the spirit with reckless conjecture—*The Classic of Virtue* calls this "foreknowledge," the flower of the Dao and the beginning of folly. Thus, he told Guan Yin that anticipating Heaven’s will and weighing profit and harm is not as good as ceasing.

To conjecture and often hit the mark—this is why Confucius detested Zigong.

Yang Bu asked: "Here is a man whose age, words, talents, and appearance are like his brother’s. Yet their lifespans are like father and son, their status like father and son, their reputations like father and son, their affections and aversions like father and son. I am perplexed."

Yangzi said: "The ancients had a saying, which I once understood and will tell you: What is so without knowing why it is so—that is fate. Now, in darkness and obscurity, in confusion and chaos, whether acting or not acting, days pass and come—who can know the reason? All is fate."

Explanation: What lies within us is nature; what lies with Heaven is fate. Nature may be cultivated but not neglected; fate may be heeded but not interfered with. The noble person conducts himself with proper conduct and awaits fate—why would he desire to subtly seek this fortune through such means?

Yet Yang Zhu, in his petty calculations, measured the thickness and thinness of years, virtue, talent, and appearance, and reckoned the differences in lifespan, status, reputation, affection, and aversion, likening father and son to brothers. Brothers are spoken of in terms of following one another in age, while father and son are spoken of in terms of the inequality of honor and humility. This is called adding delusion to delusion—great delusion! He did not understand that fate is at work.

In darkness and obscurity, beyond the reach of wisdom’s illumination; in confusion and chaos, beyond the power of reason to discern—whether acting or not acting, one does not hide in nothingness nor stagnate in being. Days pass, moving with transformation; days come, appearing with the times. Who can know the reason? This is how creation subtly works all things.

If creation were to calculate what a person should do in life and respond accordingly, its way of generating all things would be shallow indeed.

One who trusts in fate knows neither long life nor early death; one who trusts in principle knows neither right nor wrong; one who trusts in the heart knows neither adversity nor compliance; one who trusts in nature knows neither safety nor peril. This is called having nothing to trust and nothing not to trust. Truly sincere and earnest—what should one reject, what should one embrace? What should one grieve, what should one rejoice? What should one do, what should one not do?

Explanation: When we speak of knowing neither long life nor early death, right nor wrong, adversity nor compliance, safety nor peril, it is not that these do not exist. Consider how Yan Hui lived briefly while Robber Zhi lived long—even if a man of the Dao could truly trust in fate, how could he grossly misjudge others, calling Yanzi long-lived and Pengzu short-lived? He would simply allow life and death to follow their natural course without claiming them as his own. Only by not claiming them do both long life and early death proceed unimpeded—this is what it means to say they are "not known." The same applies to right and wrong, adversity and compliance, safety and peril.

To say there is nothing to trust refers to knowing neither long life nor early death, neither right nor wrong, neither adversity nor compliance, neither safety nor peril. To say there is nothing not to trust refers to trusting in fate, principle, the heart, and nature. In this state, likes and dislikes do not arise, so there is nothing to avoid or pursue. Sorrow and joy do not manifest, so there is no grief or delight. Following what is not done, there is nothing done; following what is done, there is nothing not done. This may be called true and earnest. "True" means pure and without falsity; "earnest" means real and without delusion.

The book of the Yellow Emperor states: The perfected person dwells as though dead, moves as though mechanical. He does not know why he dwells, nor why he does not dwell; he does not know why he moves, nor why he does not move. He does not know why others observe changes in his countenance, nor does he say that others’ lack of observation leaves his countenance unchanged. Alone he comes, alone he goes, alone he enters, alone he departs—who can obstruct him?

Explanation: To dwell as though dead means the heart is like dead ashes—it rests of itself, without intention. To move as though mechanical means to act like a crossbow’s trigger—it responds to things before moving. Moving or resting with the times, dwelling or not dwelling, acting or not acting—all follow their natural course, and none are known. In this state, self and things are both forgotten, and seeing and hearing are both extinguished—what observation remains? Flesh and bone are wholly dissolved, and countenance is but a temporary vessel—what change could there be? Transcending all, solitary and unique, with no counterpart—alone he enters, alone he departs, alone he comes, alone he goes. Who could obstruct him?

The conduct of ordinary people differs entirely. Their distinction between inner and outer is unsettled, their boundary between honor and disgrace unclear. They take having a name as honor, having no name as disgrace. Whether their countenance changes or not depends on whether others observe them. Thus they fear authority, fear punishment, fear ghosts, fear others, their five viscera knotted with sorrow, their bodies shackled in restraint. All their lives they toil without respite, perishing with the transformations—is this not lamentable? Why not reflect: I am myself—what need have I of others? If one truly examines one’s own self, then others’ observation or lack of it is of no concern, and one’s countenance need not change. Thus the perfected person does not depart from the real, while ordinary people cannot perceive the solitary—is there any other reason? It lies solely in oneself.

*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of Primordial Emptiness, Scroll Thirteen Concluded*
*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of Primordial Emptiness, Scroll Fourteen*

Submitted by Your Servant Jiang Yu, Inner Hall Student of the Hangzhou Prefecture Academy

**On the Distribution of Power and Fate**

Mo Xi, Shan Zhi, Tan Xuan, and Bie Fu—these four roamed the world together, each fulfilling their aspirations. Though they spent their lives in one another’s company, they never truly knew one another’s hearts, for each believed their own wisdom to be profound.

Qiao Ning, Yu Zhi, Nü Zhuo, and Pian Pi—these four roamed the world together, each fulfilling their aspirations. Though they spent their lives in one another’s company, they never spoke of their arts, for each believed their own cunning to be subtle.

Yu Ya, Qing Lu, Jian Ji, and Ling Sui—these four roamed the world together, each fulfilling their aspirations. Though they spent their lives in one another’s company, they never awakened to one another’s insights, for each believed their own talent to be perfectly realized.

Mian Xuan, Zhui Wei, Yong Gan, and Qie Yi—these four roamed the world together, each fulfilling their aspirations. Though they spent their lives in one another’s company, they never exposed one another’s faults, for each believed their conduct to be without transgression.

Duo Ou, Zi Zhuan, Cheng Quan, and Zhi Li—these four roamed the world together, each fulfilling their aspirations. Though they spent their lives in one another’s company, they never exchanged glances, for each believed the times to be perfectly suited to them.

Such are the manifold dispositions of the world. Though their appearances differ, all are ultimately gathered into the Dao—this is the decree to which they return.

*Explication:*
Mo Xi and Shan Zhi represent the divergent temperaments of composure and trepidation. Tan Xuan and Bie Fu embody the contrasting demeanors of leisurely delay and hasty impulse. Qiao Ning and Yu Zhi differ as cunning flattery and blunt honesty. Nü Zhuo and Pian Pi vary as stubborn obstinacy and fawning compliance. Yu Ya and Qing Lu diverge in their excess and superficiality of emotion. Jian Ji and Ling Sui contrast in their reticence and eloquence. Mian Xuan and Zhui Wei differ in their preference for either clarity or complexity. Yong Gan and Qie Yi oppose bold resolve and timid hesitation. Duo Ou is one who echoes others; Zi Zhuan, one who trusts only himself. Cheng Quan exploits authority to dominate others; Zhi Li relies solely on his own strength without aid.

Ever since the Great Simplicity dissolved, the people have raced along the paths of right and wrong, profit and harm, their dispositions manifold and beyond enumeration. Liezi here summarizes twenty distinctions based on the inclinations to which their natures cling, the arts they transmit, the talents they employ, the conduct they display, and the times they encounter. Those of temperament believe their wisdom to be profound; those of art, their skill to be subtle; those of talent, their abilities to be perfectly realized; those of conduct, their actions to be without fault; those of timing, their circumstances to be perfectly suited. In their confusion, each affirms what they affirm and rejects what they reject, fulfilling their aspirations throughout their lives—so varied are the world’s dispositions.

Yet when one examines their origins, success and failure are equal in the realm of the living. Beauty and ugliness, goodness and foulness, all meet the same end. This is why they are moved by the Dao and converge in fate. Though all are gathered into the Dao, they do not comprehend it; though all return to fate, they do not trust it. Relying on private wisdom, clinging to partial views, they pursue only the interests of their small selves, forgetting the great impartiality of the world. In this, their resemblance to heaven and earth differs not from the mutual affinity of the one-legged Kui, the serpent, and the wind-eyed creature—what worth is there in being the most intelligent of the living?

Only those who embody the Dao and attain to fate achieve a mind congealed and a form released. When the mind congeals, there is no room within for cunning or affectation; when the form is released, there is no trace without of expression or countenance. These are the wellsprings of the world’s dispositions, yet those who transcend them wander carefree between heaven and earth, their hearts at ease. Looking down upon the world’s dispositions—is this not lamentable?

Things that seem to succeed do so only by chance, not by true success. Things that seem to fail do so only by chance, not by true failure. Thus delusion arises from chance, and in the moment of chance, one is benighted. If, in the moment of chance, one is not benighted, then one is neither startled by external misfortune nor delighted by internal fortune. Moving with the times, halting with the times—even wisdom cannot fathom this.

*Explication:*
The principles of the world are most subtle yet most luminous. Before signs appear, they are subtle; yet their principles are utterly clear. The worthy are blind to what has not yet sprouted; the common people are blind to what has already come to pass. If they are blind even to what has already occurred, how much more so to the incipient signs of success and failure? It is fitting that they remain benighted and know nothing of them.

Yet so-called chance success and chance failure are not truly chance at all—the principles of success and failure are silently determined before any form appears. Though it is said that delusion arises from chance, in truth, chance arises from delusion. Only those who cannot perceive the unformed signs of success and failure are bewildered in the moment of chance. Even in success, they dare not be certain of it, trembling lest it slip away; even in failure, they do not accept it as failure, hoping still for some gain. How, then, could they not be startled by external misfortune and delighted by internal fortune?

If one can remain unbewildered in the moment of chance, then success is simply success, and failure simply failure. Regarding fortune and misfortune as the alternation of day and night, the succession of cold and heat—seeing the outward, one knows the inward; observing the past, one knows the future. What room is there for joy or sorrow in between? Such a one has advanced beyond wisdom and aligned with the Dao. Even the great matters of life and death hold no change for them—how much less the mere beginnings of profit and harm?

One who trusts fate harbors no duality between self and other. Those who maintain such duality would do better to cover their eyes and plug their ears, turning their backs to cliffs and facing chasms—they would not fall. Thus it is said: life and death come from fate; poverty and hardship come from the times. Those who resent early death do not understand fate; those who resent poverty do not understand the times. Facing death without fear, enduring hardship without sorrow—these are those who understand fate and accept the times.

*Explication:*
When Shang Qiu Kai walked through water and fire, declaring that nothing could oppose him, his mind was unified—this illustrates how one who trusts fate harbors no duality between self and other.

Without trust in fate, one clings to the self, and with the self established, "other" and "this" arise. With "other" and "this" in place, likes and dislikes take form; as likes and dislikes intertwine, sorrow and joy alternate—though one may never have turned their back to cliffs or faced chasms, the fear of falling remains ever-present. Those who cover their eyes and plug their ears do not truly forget what they see and hear; yet with perception momentarily obstructed, even if they truly turned their backs to cliffs and faced chasms, they would not fall. This is how those who understand fate and accept the times face death without fear and endure hardship without sorrow.

When the clever weigh profit and harm, assess emptiness and fullness, and gauge human sentiments, their gains and losses may still align with fate. When the foolish do not weigh profit and harm, do not assess emptiness and fullness, and do not gauge human sentiments, their gains and losses may also align with fate. What difference, then, lies between weighing and not weighing, assessing and not assessing, gauging and not gauging? Only when there is nothing to weigh and nothing not to weigh does one remain whole and free from loss. It is not that one knows wholeness or loss—one simply remains whole, loses, or perishes of their own accord.

*Explication:*
Weighing success and failure in profit and harm, assessing presence and absence in emptiness and fullness, gauging likes and dislikes in human sentiments—these are the ways of the clever. The unwise do the opposite. Yet the clever do not always succeed, nor do the foolish always fail. Thus, weighing or not weighing, assessing or not assessing, gauging or not gauging—none differ in outcome. Still, one cannot abandon cleverness because it does not always succeed, nor cling to foolishness because it does not always fail. Only when there is nothing to weigh and nothing not to weigh, when one employs wisdom without being enslaved by it and relies on wisdom without depending on it, do gain and loss both vanish. One remains perpetually whole, yet neither knows nor cares why this is so.

Duke Jing of Qi wandered on Ox Mountain, gazing northward over his capital. Tears streamed down his face as he sighed: "How beautiful is this land! So lush and verdant—how could I bear to leave it and die?" If the ancients had never died, where would I go after departing this realm? His attendants, Shi Kong and Liang Qiu Ju, wept alongside him, saying: "We rely on Your Lordship’s grace. Though we eat coarse grains and tough meat, ride broken-down horses and rickety carriages, we still do not wish to die—how much less Your Lordship?" Only Yanzi laughed beside them. The duke wiped his tears and turned to Yanzi: "Today’s outing has left me sorrowful. Kong and Ju wept with me—why do you alone laugh?" Yanzi replied: "If worthy rulers had always guarded this land, then the Great Duke and Duke Huan would still rule it. If brave rulers had always guarded it, then Duke Zhuang and Duke Ling would still rule it. Had these lords remained, where would Your Lordship be but standing in the fields in straw raincoat and hat, tending to affairs and fretting over death? How would you have come to occupy this throne? Since rulers have succeeded and departed one after another, and you alone weep for it, this is unkind. Seeing an unkind ruler and flattering ministers—these are the two things that made me laugh in secret." Duke Jing grew ashamed. He raised his cup to punish himself and forced his two ministers to drink two cups each as well.

*Explication:*
The penalty of wine replenishes what is lacking in one’s qi. Duke Jing, gazing over his capital, admired its beauty and longed to preserve it forever, yet had no means to do so—his sorrow and tears knew no end. Upon hearing Yanzi’s words, he realized his cultivation was insufficient. Thus, he raised his cup to punish himself and forced his two ministers to drink two cups each. The double cups served to emphasize his inadequacy.

There was a man of Wei named Dongmen Wu whose son died, yet he felt no grief. His steward asked: "No one in the world loved their child more than you. Now that your son has died, why do you not grieve?" Dongmen Wu replied: "I once had no son, and when I had no son, I felt no grief. Now that my son has died, it is the same as when I had no son—why should I grieve?"

*Explication:*
All who possess human form have human emotions; only the people of the great antiquity could forget them, while those of later ages could not match this. If one has not attained the forgetting of emotions yet remains indifferent and untroubled, they fall short of true emotion. How can one without emotion still be called human? Even Confucius, who engaged with the world, wept bitterly at the death of Yan Hui, asking, "For whom else would I grieve if not for him?" His inability to forget emotion was thus. Yet Dongmen Wu felt no grief at his son’s death—was he truly one who had forgotten emotion?

Farmers rush to the seasons, merchants chase profit, artisans pursue skill, and the benevolent seek power—circumstances compel them. Yet farmers face droughts and floods, merchants encounter gains and losses, artisans meet success and failure, and officials experience favor and neglect—fate compels them.

*Explication:*
This is the chapter "Force and Fate." Liezi exhaustively demonstrates that all living things are governed by fate, yet he also warns against dismissing effort as futile and sinking into inaction. His purpose is to ensure that force and fate proceed together without losing their mysterious operation.

*Force and Fate Explained*
Mencius called benevolence, righteousness, propriety, and wisdom "fate," for they are rooted in human nature—thus the noble person does not call them fate. He regarded sensory pleasures as "nature," for they are subject to fate—thus the noble person does not call them nature. What pertains to nature can be cultivated through human effort; what pertains to fate cannot be altered by heaven’s principle. Though some aspects of nature may be influenced by habit, human effort cannot be abandoned. Though heaven’s decrees depend on human action, heaven’s mandate cannot be entrusted entirely to fate. What fate governs may reside in nature; what nature possesses may be governed by fate. Nature and fate always proceed together; heaven and humanity constantly interact. Human lifespan, prosperity, rank, wealth, and poverty—all arise from this. Thus, Liezi composed the chapter "Force and Fate."

Having examined the words of Liezi, one finds that while he places primacy on fate, he does not deny the efficacy of human effort in shaping things—yet neither does he wish to abandon all human endeavor and surrender entirely to fate. The essential point lies in not being burdened by matters of longevity or early death, nobility or lowliness, success or failure, wealth or poverty, for the power to shape fate resides within oneself.

Moreover, when force is set against fate, it is simply the natural course of things. They depend upon one another and cannot exist without each other; viewed through the lens of the Dao, all is fate. Thus, what one desires to do is fate; what one does not do is also fate. What succeeds when pursued is fate; what fails when pursued is also fate. Whether one advances directly or yields to circumstance, matters of longevity or early death, success or failure, nobility or lowliness, wealth or poverty—all arise of themselves.

Since these are said to arise of themselves, with no external force governing them, then even in matters of longevity or early death, who is there to augment or diminish? Even in matters of success or failure, what cause is there for joy or sorrow? Thus, one who understands fate accepts and receives these things as they come.

Consider Confucius, whose sageliness was unmatched since the dawn of humankind—yet once bound by fate, he was twice exiled from Lu, had trees felled over him in Song, faced hardship in Shang and Zhou, and was besieged between Chen and Cai, ultimately serving not a single ruler. Though heaven had endowed him with near-sagely gifts, his teachings remained mere empty words, never realized in action—this may indeed be called fate. As for the Master, he cultivated himself, accepted success or failure as it came, recognized that comings and goings were not his to control, and stilled the turmoil of his heart and thoughts. His joy in heaven and understanding of fate were thus. Therefore, he found joy in adversity and joy in prosperity, yet what he rejoiced in was neither adversity nor prosperity.

However, short of being a sage, none can truly comprehend the constraints of fate. Not only do they fail to understand, but they also persist in calculating what human effort has yet to achieve, rolling up their sleeves in defiance—how lamentable! Thus, Bao Shu initially treated Guan Zhong with generosity, yet Guan Zhong later slighted him; Xi Peng initially slighted Guan Zhong, yet Guan Zhong later treated him with generosity. Deng Xi opposed Zichan’s governance, yet Zichan adopted Deng Xi’s penal code; Zichan first employed his laws, and Deng Xi later met execution under them. Generosity arose from their own nature; slight arose from their own nature. The use of laws was unavoidable; the imposition of punishment was unavoidable. All was fate, not something within human power to control. Yet these men, in their time, claimed their actions as the work of their own wisdom and ability, failing to recognize the inherent principle of things. This is why the governance of Duke Huan and Duke Wen is deemed shameful in the Confucian school, and why Zichan’s benevolence is criticized by Confucius.

Consider Ji Liang, who revered the divine physician—even matters of life and death could not sway him. Or Duke Jing of Qi, who wept upon seeing his capital’s walls—yet remained confused by the very thresholds of benefit and harm. All was fate, and the distinction between understanding and ignorance was thus. This is why Liezi could not escape debate.

Nevertheless, Beigongzi once felt shame for his disregard of fate when compared to Ximenzi, who achieved success through his endeavors. Yet upon awakening, he forgot both honor and disgrace, remaining at ease for the rest of his life. If this is so, then even one who disregards fate—can fate truly constrain him?

Liezi’s intent was to clarify his own awakening in order to awaken those who had not yet understood, urging them to seek enlightenment. Though the central theme of his chapter dispels the confusion between force and fate, he ultimately concludes that force must not be abandoned and fate must not be ignored—this is the ultimate truth of fate. Thus, he concludes with the teachings on official service, farming, craftsmanship, and commerce as the workings of fate.

*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Profound Virtue*, Scroll Fourteen—concluded.
*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Profound Virtue*, Scroll Fifteen.

Submitted by Jiang Yu, an Inner Hall Student of Hangzhou Prefecture School in Song

**Yang Zhu, Chapter One**

Yang Zhu traveled to Lu and lodged at the Meng residence. Meng asked him, “Man is but man—why then pursue fame?”
Yang Zhu replied, “Fame is sought for wealth.”
“Once wealthy, why not cease?”
“For nobility.”
“Once noble, why not cease?”
“For death.”
“After death, what remains?”
“For descendants.”
“What benefit does fame bring to descendants?”
“Fame is as one’s own body, straining the heart. Those who ride fame bring blessings to their clans, profit to their villages—how much more to their descendants? Yet all who pursue fame cultivate frugality, and frugality leads to poverty. Those who pursue fame must yield, and yielding leads to lowliness.”

Meng said, “When Guan Zhong served as minister of Qi, the lord indulged, and so did he; the lord was extravagant, and so was he. Their wills aligned, their words followed, and the Way was enacted—Qi became a hegemon. Yet after his death, only the Guan clan remained. When the Tian clan served Qi, if the lord was full, they diminished themselves; if the lord taxed, they gave. The people all turned to them, and thus they came to possess Qi. Their descendants have enjoyed it to this day without end. If true fame impoverishes, then false fame enriches.”

Yang Zhu said, “Truth has no fame, and fame has no truth. Fame is but pretense. Long ago, Yao and Shun feigned yielding the world to Xu You and Shan Juan yet did not lose it, reigning for a hundred years. Boyi and Shuqi truly yielded the rulership of Guzhu, yet their state perished, and they starved on Mount Shouyang. The distinction between truth and pretense is thus made clear.”

*Explication*:
The constant Dao has no name; names arise only after things are formed and their numbers fixed. The wise detest the chaos of affairs and things, and so, out of necessity, they impose names upon them. Names are not natural—all within the realm of the nameable is but pretense. Yet though names issue trust, they must rely on reality; reality does not manifest itself and must borrow names. The noble-minded do not resent following names to tread reality; they resent only clinging to names and burdening reality.

The careless multitude, unaware that their bodies do not truly belong to them, pursue wealth and nobility in life. Unaware that their descendants do not belong to them, they compete for empty fame after death. At first, they seek reality through fame; in the end, they seek fame to the detriment of reality. If reality is not fame, then why did Guan’s extravagance bring no benefit to his descendants? If fame is not reality, how did Tian’s generosity lead to their possession of Qi? Names cannot be discarded, nor can they be pursued. Pursuing names destroys reality; amassing reality establishes names.

Moreover, if one pursues the present, then Boyi and Shuqi’s yielding is not as good as Yao and Shun’s pretense. If one cares for posterity, then Guan Zhong’s extravagance is not as good as the Tian clan’s generosity. To seek both fame and reality—how is this possible?

Liezi did not value worldly wealth and nobility, nor was he unaware that Yao, Shun, Boyi, and Shuqi did not seek fame yet gained it, becoming objects of universal praise for later ages. Even the sage’s engagement with the world, daily entanglement in affairs, inevitably produces names—all within the realm of judgment. Liezi spoke thus to urge scholars to devote themselves to the nameless Dao.

If one feigns benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and teaching to steal the world’s empty fame, not only will they fail to gain fame, but they will lose even the profit they might have had—far worse than thieves who at least indulge their desires in life. This is perhaps an overcorrection, a necessary exaggeration.

Yang Zhu said, “A hundred years is the utmost span of life. Among a thousand, not one attains it. Even if one does, half is spent in infancy, dotage, and old age. Half of what remains is lost to sleep at night and distraction by day. Half of what is left is consumed by pain, illness, sorrow, loss, worry, and fear. In the few years that remain, moments of ease and self-contentment, free from care, are rare indeed. For what, then, does man live? For what joy? For fine food and abundance. For music and beauty. Yet fine food cannot always satisfy, nor can music and beauty always be enjoyed. Then one is bound by rewards and punishments, advanced or restrained by fame and law. Restlessly, one competes for fleeting reputation, schemes for posthumous glory, anxiously obeys the sights and sounds of the senses, and clings to the judgments of body and mind. The supreme joy of the present is lost, and one cannot indulge even for a moment. How is this different from a prisoner in heavy shackles?”

*Explication*:
A hundred years of life are worn down by cares. Yin and yang assail from without; desires gnaw from within. Nothing is strong or enduring—all becomes illness and vexation. At night, the spirit labors in sleep; by day, the body toils in wakefulness. How can one not harbor fleeting anxieties even for a moment? Yet these anxieties, when held, bring worry and fear; when released, bring ease and joy. Holding or releasing them lies within oneself. Why, then, do people so often tread the path of care? Do they willingly embrace suffering? No—it is because they take fine food and beauty as joy that they compete for reputation, scheme for glory, guard their senses, and cling to judgments. Anxious and restless, they are bound by rewards and punishments, advanced or restrained by fame and law, daily mired in care without self-awareness.

Thus, in a hundred years of life, one fails both to attain the inner joy of heaven and to indulge even for a moment—both are lost. How is this different from a prisoner in heavy shackles? Zhuangzi also called this a long illness, a prolonged affliction from which one does not die. Did Liezi truly wish for the people to indulge in the pleasures of music and beauty?

Indeed, those who scramble in vain for hollow reputations bring no benefit to themselves—far better to cast off restraint and pursue the joys rightful to life. Yet is this not itself a corrective overreach?

The people of the great antiquity knew life as a fleeting arrival and death as a fleeting departure. Thus they followed their hearts in action, never resisting what nature inclined them to love. The pleasures of the present body were not to be forsaken, so they were not swayed by the lure of fame. They roamed in accord with their nature, never opposing what the myriad things inclined them to love. Posthumous renown was not to be sought, so they were not touched by the reach of punishment. Fame and infamy, long life and short—these were not matters for their reckoning.

The *Explanation* says: Death and life are but a going and a returning. The people of great antiquity, their great simplicity yet undispersed, their primal integrity uncarved by human artifice, could trace things back to their origin and return to their end, thus knowing the discourse on death and life. Thereby they followed their hearts in action and roamed in accord with their nature, going nowhere without a sense of ease and self-possession.

Nature is to the heart as substance is to function. To depart from nature and then follow the heart is to act in accord with the heart, thus never resisting what nature inclines one to love. To roam in accord with one’s nature is then to never oppose what the myriad things incline them to love. Moreover, action may be constrained, but roaming is ever fitting. Following the heart in action, one does not forsake the pleasures of the present body—this is not to seek the good of near-term fame, so one is not swayed by fame. Roaming in accord with one’s nature, one does not scheme for posthumous renown—this is not to seek the evil of near-term punishment, so one is not touched by punishment. Those who are thus do not see the changes of life and death as anything more than the constant alternation of night and day. How then would they have leisure to calculate the precedence of fame and infamy or measure the length of life and span of years?

Yang Zhu said: What distinguishes the myriad things is life; what they share is death. In life there are the worthy and the foolish, the noble and the base—this is what distinguishes them. In death there is putrefaction and dissolution—this is what they share. Yet the worthy and the foolish, the noble and the base are not within one’s power; putrefaction and dissolution are likewise not within one’s power. Thus life is not what one lives, death is not what one dies, worthiness is not what one makes worthy, foolishness is not what one makes foolish, nobility is not what one makes noble, baseness is not what one makes base. Yet the myriad things are equally born, equally die, equally worthy, equally foolish, equally noble, equally base. Whether one dies at ten or a hundred, whether one is a sage or a tyrant, a fool or a brute—all alike become rotting bones. Rotting bones are one and the same: who knows their distinctions? Why then hasten to the present life and trouble oneself over what comes after death?

The *Explanation* says: Life and death alternate, originally without sameness or difference. Petty wisdom, self-serving in its smallness, falsely sees difference within sameness, then posits sameness on the basis of difference—thus the sameness and difference of life and death become confused and indistinguishable. Yang Zhu sought to equalize the changes of life and death and make them one. Thus, taking the common view that sees life as different and death as the same, he ultimately returns to a state beyond sameness and difference. If one were to abruptly speak to them of the ultimate unity of the Way, they would be thrown into confusion amid their doubts, and their delusions would remain unresolved. This is the sage’s constant skill in saving people.

Moreover, in equalizing the changes of the myriad things, one must speak of Yao and Shun, Jie and Zhou—only by dispelling the world’s deepest delusions can one properly use the extremes of the mad and the sage, those whom all under heaven and all generations trust. To say that Yao and Shun are the same as Jie and Zhou is no idle claim. The traces of Yao and Shun’s response to the world, their adaptation to the times and compliance with change, could not avoid some degree of attachment—how then could their traces fail to converge in rotting bones? As for what made Yao and Shun truly Yao and Shun, this is what Confucius called “so vast that the people could find no name for it”—how could this be the same as the rotting bones of Jie and Zhou?

Yang Zhu said: Boyi did not lack desires—his pride in purity led him to starve to death. Zhan Zi did not lack passions—his pride in integrity led him to leave his lineage diminished. The errors of purity and integrity are thus manifest in goodness itself.

The *Explanation* says: In human life, passions give rise to desires, and desires give rise to love. If desires are not moderated, they overflow into cravings, nurturing likes and dislikes, and the natural endowment of life and destiny becomes diseased—this is how goodness itself becomes error. If what one prides oneself on is purity and integrity, then one can restrain one’s passions and moderate one’s desires—how then is this an error of goodness? Yet Boyi and Zhan Zi, though they bore the names of purity and integrity, harbored the intention to correct excess and remedy flaws, so their traces could not avoid some degree of attachment—thus they too were enslaved by passions and desires. To the point of starving to death or leaving one’s lineage diminished—is it not fitting to call this an error of goodness? Thus the sage follows the course of the mean as his constant principle and does not go to extremes.

Yang Zhu said: Yuan Xian was impoverished in Lu; Zigong amassed wealth in Wei. Yuan Xian’s poverty harmed his life; Zigong’s wealth burdened his person. If poverty is not permissible and wealth is not permissible, where then lies the permissible? It lies in delighting in life and in freeing the body. Thus one who is good at delighting in life is not impoverished; one who is good at freeing the body is not wealthy.

The *Explanation* says: In human life, one must rely on things for sustenance—plowing to eat and weaving to clothe oneself are unavoidable. Even the people of great antiquity were no exception, save that they did not cast their gaze left and right to snare profit amid riches and honor, nor did they allow officials to monopolize the fields. The former path avoids poverty—this is how one delights in life. The latter path avoids wealth—this is how one frees the body. For one who is impoverished wears a hempen cap and straw sandals, leaning on a staff of brambles—how can one speak of delighting in life? For one who is wealthy is weighed down by cares and anxieties, ever seeking more—how can one speak of freeing the body? From this one knows that the Way of Liezi does not go to extremes and has never been wholly unmindful of the peace or peril of the world’s course.

Yang Zhu said: There is an ancient saying: "In life, we cherish one another; in death, we abandon one another." This saying reaches the utmost truth. The way of cherishing in life is not merely about affection; it is through diligence that one attains ease, through hunger that one finds satiety, through cold that one knows warmth, through poverty that one achieves success. The way of abandonment in death is not that one feels no sorrow; it is that no pearls or jades are placed within, no patterned brocades are worn, no sacrificial animals are offered, and no funerary objects are set out.

The commentary states: The establishment of later kings and lords to govern the people of the world is meant to ensure they do not neglect their duties—this is the way of cherishing in life. But when it comes to death, all is simplified; even good friends do no more than sigh in eternal lament—this is the way of abandonment.

Yan Pingzhong asked Guan Yiwu about nurturing life. Guan Yiwu replied: "Let it be, without obstruction or restraint." Yan Pingzhong asked: "What does this entail?" Yiwu said: "Indulge the ears in what they wish to hear, the eyes in what they wish to see, the nose in what it wishes to smell, the mouth in what it wishes to speak, the body in what it wishes to rest, and the mind in what it wishes to do. If the ears desire to hear sounds but are not allowed to listen, this is called stifling hearing. If the eyes desire to see beauty but are not allowed to look, this is called stifling sight. If the nose desires the scent of pepper and orchid but is not allowed to smell, this is called stifling scent. If the mouth desires to speak of right and wrong but is not allowed to speak, this is called stifling wisdom. If the body desires comfort but is not allowed to follow it, this is called stifling ease. If the mind desires to act freely but is not allowed to do so, this is called stifling nature. All these stiflings are the tyrants of oppression. To cast off these oppressive tyrants and await death in joyful ease—whether for a day, a month, a year, or ten years—this is what I call nurturing life. To be bound by these oppressive tyrants, clinging to them without release, and living in sorrow for a long life—whether a hundred years, a thousand years, or ten thousand years—this is not what I call nurturing life."

The commentary states: When Master Liezi studied under Old Shangzi, after three years, his heart dared not contemplate right and wrong, and his mouth dared not speak of profit and harm. Thus, in what his mouth wished to speak and his mind wished to do, he could not indulge freely. For this reason, Old Shangzi first relaxed his countenance and smiled upon seeing him. After nine years, whatever his heart contemplated and whatever his mouth spoke, he could indulge and act freely without obstruction or restraint. Thus, Old Shangzi acknowledged his progress in both inner and outer cultivation. The so-called indulgence of the ears in hearing, the eyes in seeing, the nose in smelling, and the body in resting is like this—it does not mean indulging in sounds, sights, scents, and tastes to violate what human principles abhor, and only then is it called indulgence. One who can advance in this way has truly heard the Dao. "To hear the Dao in the morning, one may die in the evening." Thus, even a life of a day or a month is sufficient for nurturing, so why cling to a long life in sorrow? This is Liezi’s ultimate principle of nurturing life. What Guan Zhong, Yanzi, and Zeng Xi did not do—how could they have advanced in this Dao?

Yan Pingzhong’s pork shoulder never filled his plate—he was one who practiced frugality in person. Guan Yiwu had three returning cups and a wine stand—he was one who loved luxury. Yan Pingzhong’s question and Guan Yiwu’s answer were naturally as they were. Their exchange was like the principle of fruits and melons; their words happened to align with the Dao. Thus, Liezi adopted their sayings to convey the ultimate Dao, not intending for scholars to emulate what Guan and Yan did.

Guan Yiwu said: "I have already told you about nurturing life. What of sending off the dead?" Yan Pingzhong replied: "Sending off the dead is a matter of simplicity. What more is there to say?" Guan Yiwu said: "I still wish to hear of it." Pingzhong said: "Once dead, what is it to me? Burning is acceptable, sinking is acceptable, burying is acceptable, exposing is acceptable, wrapping in firewood and casting into a ravine is acceptable, or adorning with embroidered robes and placing in a stone outer coffin is acceptable—whatever one encounters."

Guan Yiwu turned to Bao Shu and Huangzi and said: "The way of sending off the dead—we two have advanced in it."

The commentary states: For one who has attained the Dao, sending off the dead means taking heaven and earth as the coffin and outer coffin, the sun and moon as paired jades, the stars as pearls and ornaments, and the myriad things as offerings. In this, what encounter could not be fitting?

*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Vacuous and Unwavering Classic, Scroll Fifteen, concludes.*
*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Vacuous and Unwavering Classic, Scroll Sixteen.*

The memorial submitted by Jiang Yu, an inner student of the Hangzhou Prefecture Academy of Song:

During Yang Zhu’s era, Zichan governed Zheng, monopolizing the state’s administration. After three years, the virtuous admired his transformation, the wicked feared his prohibitions, Zheng was well-ordered, and the feudal lords stood in awe. Yet he had an elder brother named Gongsun Chao and a younger brother named Gongsun Mu. Chao was fond of wine, and Mu was fond of women.

In Chao’s residence, a thousand *zhong* of wine were amassed, fermented dregs piled into mounds. From a hundred paces beyond the gate, the stench of lees and swill assailed the nostrils. When he was lost in drink, he knew nothing of the world’s peace or peril, the regrets and humiliations of human affairs, the prosperity or ruin of his household, the closeness or estrangement of his nine clans, or the sorrow and joy of life and death. Even if water and fire, blades and weapons clashed before him, he remained oblivious.

Mu’s rear courtyard held dozens of chambers, each filled with young, supple beauties. When he was immersed in sensuality, he cast aside close kin, severed friendships, and fled to the inner quarters, sating himself day and night. He emerged but once every three months, yet still felt unsatisfied. If a maiden of surpassing beauty dwelled in the village, he would bribe and summon her, employ matchmakers to entice her, and only desist if he failed to obtain her.

Zichan grieved over this day and night. Secretly, he sought Deng Xi’s counsel, saying: “I have heard that governing the self leads to governing the household, and governing the household leads to governing the state—this speaks of proceeding from the near to the far. I have brought order to the state, yet my household is in chaos. Is my Way perverse? What method can save these two?” Deng Xi replied: “I have long found this strange but dared not speak first. Why not admonish them in due time, enlightening them on the weight of life and destiny, enticing them with the dignity of ritual and righteousness?”

Zichan heeded Deng Xi’s words. Seizing an opportunity, he visited his brothers and said: “What elevates humans above beasts is wisdom. What wisdom guides is ritual and righteousness. When ritual and righteousness are perfected, fame and rank follow. If you act on impulse, indulging your desires, your life and destiny will be imperiled. Heed my words, and by evening, Chao will repent and receive emolument.”

Chao and Mu replied: “We have long known this and long deliberated upon it. Do we need your words to recognize it? Life is hard to encounter, death easy to reach. With a life so difficult to attain, awaiting a death so easily come—how can one not ponder this? To exalt ritual and righteousness to impress others, to feign nature and temperament for fame—we deem this worse than death. We wish to exhaust a lifetime’s joy, to plumb the year’s utmost pleasure. Our only fear is a belly too full to drink to our heart’s content, strength too spent to indulge our passions. We have no leisure to fret over foul reputations or perilous lives.”

“Moreover, you boast of your governance to sway others, seeking to confuse our hearts with fine words, to delight our minds with honor and emolument—is this not base and pitiable? We wish to part ways with you. Those who excel at governing the external may not bring order to things, yet their persons suffer. Those who excel at governing the internal may not bring chaos to things, yet their natures find ease. Your governance of the external may be temporarily enforced in one state, but it does not accord with human hearts. Our governance of the internal can be extended to all under heaven, and the Way of ruler and minister will cease. We have long wished to instruct you in this art, yet you turn and teach us yours instead.”

Zichan was left speechless. Later, he recounted this to Deng Xi, who said: “You have dwelled with true persons yet do not recognize them. Who calls you wise? Zheng’s order is but a coincidence, not your achievement.”

*Explication states:* Indulging passion in sensuality is what deludes and enthralls human sentiment, what human principles deem most shameful. Gluttonous drinking is what all human sentiment desires, what the former kings warned against. The common person’s sentiment craves the sight of beauty. When denied to the point of blindness, it is not that they truly suppress desire, but that they fear peril to life and are restrained from indulgence. The mouth craves fine flavors. When denied to the point of satiation, it is not that they truly forget preferences, but that they shun foul reputations and are deterred from indulgence.

Thus, exalting ritual and righteousness, feigning nature and temperament, may appear outwardly as if one can be serene and effortless. Yet in their seated reverie, their thoughts race in an instant beyond the four seas, their aspirations and intentions reaching everywhere, accomplishing anything. If so, a hundred years of life will inwardly torment their hearts and minds, outwardly afflict their forms and bodies. Where is the joy in such life?

As for Chao and Mu’s conduct, it is simply genuine. Their so-called gluttonous drinking is not drowning in wine. Their so-called indulgence in sensuality is not wallowing in lust. For Chao and Mu have long understood the peace and peril of the world’s ways, the gains and losses of human principles, and long deliberated upon them. Desiring to exhaust a lifetime’s joy, to plumb the year’s utmost pleasure, they drink to their heart’s content and indulge their passions. Though their reputations may be foul, they do not fret over perilous lives, nor do they spare a thought for it.
