This is what is called governing the internal without governing the external, having no shame before the Dao and virtue, daring to act on perverse impulses without adhering to the conduct of benevolence and righteousness.

Their genuine Way of governing the self, if extended and practiced, could bring the world to effortless order. Yet Zichan busies himself ferrying people across the Zhen and Wei rivers, his governance inevitably criticized by the people and humbled by Deng Xi. This is what is called excelling at governing the external—things may not be ordered, yet the person suffers. Though his methods may be temporarily enforced in one state, they do not accord with human hearts. How could he understand the genuineness of these two?

To fail to comprehend this is one thing, yet to further confuse the mind with clever words, to let honor and disgrace sway the will—such sincerity is contemptible, and such intent pitiable. Ruling Zheng in this manner and monopolizing its governance, even if the virtuous submit to its transformation and the wicked fear its prohibitions, they remain ignorant of the true principles of order. Any success is merely accidental—how can it be called achievement? In the court of Mu, Zichan stood between the ranks of Ji and Meng, yet his conduct and aspirations were unequal, and his governance of internal and external affairs diverged so starkly. Thus it is said: if the Way could be conveyed to others, none would fail to share it with their brothers.

As for Deng Xi, initially he had not yet examined the Way of the court of Mu, so upon hearing Zichan’s words, he shared his sorrow. Later, having grasped the Way of the court of Mu, he heard Zichan’s words and diverged in understanding. Alas! Without Deng Xi’s words, later observers of the court of Mu would nearly all share Zichan’s sorrow and never perceive the truth.

Wei’s Duanmu Shu was a descendant of Zigong, who inherited his family’s wealth, amassing tens of thousands in gold. Unconcerned with worldly affairs, he indulged his every inclination. Whatever the people desired to do or fancied to enjoy, he did and enjoyed without restraint. His mansions, terraces, gardens, ponds, feasts, carriages, robes, music, and concubines rivaled those of the lords of Qi and Chu. Whatever his heart desired, his ears wished to hear, his eyes wished to see, or his mouth wished to taste—even if it came from distant lands or remote states, beyond what Qi’s soil could produce—he procured it all as readily as if it were within his own walls. In his travels, though mountains and rivers blocked the way, the paths were long and the distances vast, he ventured without hesitation, as easily as taking a single step. Daily, hundreds of guests filled his halls, his kitchens never ceased their smoke and flame, and his halls and corridors never stilled their music.

From the surplus of his provisions, he first distributed to his clansmen; what remained, he gave to his village; and what still remained, he scattered throughout the state. At sixty, his vitality waning, he abandoned his household affairs and dispersed his storehouses of treasures, carriages, robes, and concubines. Within a year, all was gone, leaving no wealth for his descendants. When he fell ill, he had no medicines stored; when he died, no resources for burial. The people of the state who had received his bounty gathered to contribute, returning his wealth to his descendants.

Qin Guli heard of this and said: "Duanmu Shu is a madman, a disgrace to his ancestors." Duangan Sheng heard of it and said: "Duanmu Shu is an enlightened man, his virtue surpassing his ancestors." What he did and how he lived startled the common mind, yet the truth of principle affirmed it. Many of Wei’s gentlemen clung to ritual and teaching, yet this was hardly sufficient to grasp the heart of such a man.

A Commentary states:

Zigong was a man burdened by his mercantile pursuits. While amassing wealth, he hoarded it without daring to spend, clinging to it without release. His heart was filled with anxiety and distress, ever seeking more without cease—truly a life of sorrow. With Zigong’s riches, he could have lived out his days in grand estates, fine robes, rich flavors, and fair companions, lacking nothing. Yet his endless pursuit of gain arose from scheming for his descendants’ endless future. Alas! Grandchildren are not your possessions; to claim and hold them as such is delusion. Moreover, he tormented his body, forwent sweetness, and denied his own sustenance, only to be cast aside by the sages for his mercantile ways. In striving to suit his own desires, was this not a great tragedy?

As for Duanmu Shu: inheriting his family’s wealth, he never knew the toil of commerce yet bore the weight of ten thousand gold. Having it, he knew how to use it. Thus, he indulged his every inclination, doing all he wished and delighting in all he fancied. In ease and contentment, his aspirations fulfilled, he rivaled the lords of Qi and Chu. Not only did he know how to use wealth, but as his vital qi neared its decline, he scattered and exhausted all he had. By common judgment, he was most unkind to his descendants’ legacy. Yet later, those who received his bounty returned his wealth to his descendants—so he was not without bequest after all.

Alas! Duanmu Shu lived free from the burden of commerce, exhausting a lifetime of joy. In death, he left no wealth for his descendants yet did not lose it for them. His conduct and deeds startled the common mind, yet the truth of principle affirmed them. Where truth resides, only the sage can fully grasp it. Those bound by teaching cannot but be astonished by such spirit.

Between madness and sagacity lies a gulf—how could it be less than heaven and earth? To call the enlightened mad is great confusion indeed.

Yangzi declared the great sage to be difficult to comprehend—was this not the reason? Meng Sunyang asked Yangzi: "Suppose there is a man here who values life and cherishes his body, seeking not to die—would this be acceptable?" Yangzi replied: "In principle, there is no not-dying." "Then seeking long life—would this be acceptable?" "In principle, there is no long life. Life is not preserved by valuing it, nor is the body enriched by cherishing it. Moreover, what is the purpose of long life? The five passions of love and hate were the same in ancient times as now; the safety and peril of the four limbs were the same in ancient times as now; the bitterness and joy of worldly affairs were the same in ancient times as now; the alternations of order and chaos were the same in ancient times as now. Having heard of these, having seen them, having experienced them, one finds even a hundred years wearisome in their excess—how much more the suffering of long life!"

Meng Sunyang said: "If this is so, then swift death is preferable to long life. Thus, treading upon blades, plunging into boiling water—one would attain one’s desire." Yangzi replied: "Not so. Once born, one should relinquish and entrust it, exhausting one’s desires while awaiting death. When death approaches, one should relinquish and entrust it, exhausting one’s course to its fullest end. There is nothing not relinquished, nothing not entrusted—why hastily distinguish between swift and slow?"

Explanation: Confined by having life, life does not struggle against form, yet form must ultimately decay. Enslaved by transformation, transformation constantly flows through form—how can form endure? Thus, a hundred years is the greatest span of longevity, yet among a thousand, not one attains it. In principle, one may not achieve long life—how much less immortality? Examining life’s existence and cessation, it does not originally belong to me. Observing life’s cares and troubles, one clings to long life. While one has life, within your form, the five passions’ loves and hates churn within; within your body, the four limbs’ safety and peril press without. In a single lifetime, the myriad affairs’ bitterness and joy converge before you. The changes of a day are no different from those of a month; the shifts of a year are no different from those of a hundred. Having heard and known them, having seen and recognized them, having experienced and passed through them—how can one still seek long life?

Yet though death and life are but morning and evening, why rejoice in life? Why grieve over death? One should not, because life is insufficient cause for joy, weary of long life; nor should one, because death is insufficient cause for sorrow, delight in swift death. Thus, those who attain the Dao regard life and death: once born, they relinquish and entrust it, exhausting their desires while awaiting death, not binding themselves to the ditches’ self-strangulation. When death approaches, they relinquish and entrust it, exhausting their course to its fullest end, not clinging to the longevity of exhaling the old and inhaling the new. Though they have no intention of long life, if they attain the longevity of Peng, they do not weary of it. Though they have no intention of swift death, if they meet the early demise of Yan, they follow transformation. Nothing is not relinquished, nothing is not entrusted—thus it is, and no more.

Yang Zhu said: "Bo Cheng Zigao did not use a single hair’s benefit for others. He abandoned his state and retired to plow in seclusion. Great Yu did not use his own body for self-benefit, leaving one part of his body withered. The ancients would not sacrifice a single hair to benefit the world, nor would they take the whole world to serve a single body. If each person does not sacrifice a single hair, and each person does not benefit the world, the world will be well-ordered."

Explanation: In the *Yi*, diminishing the lower to enrich the upper is called "diminishing"; diminishing the upper to enrich the lower is called "enriching." Enrichment must entail diminishment, and diminishment must ultimately lead to enrichment. Diminishment and enrichment are the principles of fullness and emptiness, growth and decline. As for the generation of the myriad things, all equally embody the supreme principle, without deficiency or excess. Add a single hair, and nature has no room for excess; remove a single hair, and nature has no surplus. Thus, to enrich is to diminish, and to diminish is to enrich—both miss the mark. What could be more chaotic than calling this "order"? Only one who does not employ diminishment or enrichment attains the way of mutual benefit for self and others.

*Zhuangzi* speaks of the time from Rong Cheng to the era of Shen Nong, when the people savored their food, admired their clothing, delighted in their customs, and rested content in their dwellings, living to old age without visiting one another—this could be called each person not sacrificing a single hair, each person not benefiting the world. In such a time, perfect order prevailed.

Qinzi asked Yang Zhu: "Would you pluck a single hair from your body to save the world?" Yangzi replied: "The world is certainly not saved by a single hair." Qinzi said: "Suppose it could be saved—would you do it?" Yangzi did not respond. Qinzi went out and told Meng Sunyang, who said: "You do not understand the Master’s heart. Allow me to explain. If someone could gain ten thousand gold by harming your skin, would you do it?" "I would." Meng Sunyang said: "If someone could gain a state by severing one of your joints, would you do it?" Qinzi fell silent for a while. Meng Sunyang said: "A single hair is finer than skin, and skin is finer than a joint—this is evident. Yet it is by accumulating hairs that skin is formed, and by accumulating skin that joints are formed. A single hair is but one ten-thousandth of the body—how can you regard it as light?" Qinzi said: "I cannot answer you. However, if you asked Lao Dan and Guan Yin with your words, your words would be correct; if I asked Yu or Mo Di with my words, my words would be correct." Meng Sunyang then turned and discussed other matters with his disciples.

Explanation: Those who speak of Yangzi claim his way is centered on self-interest, thus even if it meant plucking a single hair from his body to save the world, he would not do it. Yet *Liezi* records his words differently. Yangzi’s meaning was this: the greatness of the world cannot be saved by a single hair. Since a single hair is insufficient to save the world, why speak of a hypothetical salvation?

Qinzi’s question was likewise ill-considered, and so Yangzi did not respond. Yangzi’s intent was this: though a single hair and the skin differ in magnitude, the skin is but an accumulation of hairs, and since both are parts of my body, both are equally worthy of love—why then value a joint over a single hair? If one could lead every person to honor life and cherish its root without slighting even a single hair, the world would be more than well-governed.

Yangzi’s love for a single hair was not love for the hair itself, but love for his own body. All people love their bodies yet fail to cherish even a single hair; if they do not cherish a single hair, the neglect accumulates until they harm their very bodies without realizing it. They approve of loving the body but disapprove of loving a single hair—how thoughtless this is!

I have observed that in human life, the noble rule the base, the low serve the exalted, and all spend their days toiling, laboring for things external, never reserving even the slightest for themselves. Have they never considered whether their lives exist for themselves or for others? If their lives are truly their own, why do they not act for themselves? And if they exist for others, yet cannot even govern themselves, how can they govern others?

*Liezi* deeply despised those who chase after the ten thousand things without turning back, and so his book often attributes to Yangzi the doctrine of self-interest. Qinzi never grasped Yangzi’s meaning; instead, he claimed that if his words were put to Yu or Mo Di, they would be correct. But this merely shows he saw the traces of Yu and Mo Di, for he understood neither Yangzi nor Yu and Mo Di. Meng Sunyang then turned away and discussed other matters with his disciples, for Qinzi’s words were not of the same kind.

Yang Zhu said: The world’s praise is heaped upon Shun, Yu, the Zhou kings, and Confucius, while its blame falls upon Jie and Zhou. Yet Shun tilled the fields at Heyang and made pottery at Leize, his limbs never knowing rest, his mouth and belly never tasting fine fare. His parents did not love him, his siblings did not befriend him. At thirty, he married without informing them. When he received Yao’s abdication, he was already old, his wisdom in decline. When Shangjun proved unworthy, he abdicated to Yu, living in sorrow until his death—such was the bitter end of this man revered as a sage. Gun failed in his governance of flood and earth, and was executed at Feather Mountain. Yu inherited his work and avenged his father, laboring endlessly over barren lands. He had no time to raise his son, passed his own door without entering, his body withered, his hands and feet calloused. When he received Shun’s abdication, he humbled his palaces and adorned his robes and crowns, living in sorrow until his death—such was the toil of this man revered as a sage. After King Wu’s death, King Cheng was young and weak, and the Duke of Zhou took up the regency. The Duke of Shao was displeased, and the four states spread rumors. The Duke of Zhou lived in the east for three years, executed his brother, and exiled another, barely escaping with his life, living in sorrow until his death—such was the peril of this man revered as a sage. Confucius expounded the Way of the sage-kings and responded to the summons of rulers of his time. He was driven to fell a tree in Song, erased his traces in Wei, impoverished in Shang and Zhou, besieged in Chen and Cai, humiliated by the Ji family, insulted by Yang Hu, living in sorrow until his death—such was the harried life of this man revered as a sage.

All four of these sages lived without a single day of joy, yet died with names revered for ten thousand generations. But a name is not something one can truly possess. Though praised, they knew it not; though rewarded, they knew it not—no different from a clod of earth or a block of wood.

Jie inherited the resources of generations, sitting upon the throne of the Son of Heaven. His wisdom was sufficient to overawe his ministers, his might enough to shake the realm. He indulged his ears and eyes in every pleasure, exhausted his thoughts in every desire, living in bliss until his death—such was the wanton ease of this man reviled as a tyrant. Zhou too inherited the resources of generations, sitting upon the throne of the Son of Heaven. His power knew no bounds, his will no resistance. He gave free rein to his passions in the Leaning Palace, indulged his desires through long nights...

They did not torment themselves with ritual and righteousness, yet blissfully met their execution—such were the reckless among heaven’s people. Those two fiends enjoyed the pleasures of desire in life, but in death bore the name of folly and brutality. For reality is not something a name can confer; though reviled, they knew it not; though praised, they knew it not—how did they differ from a clod of earth or a block of wood?

Those four sages, though the fairest of all, toiled to the end of their days, yet all alike met death. Those two fiends, though the foulest of all, reveled to the end of theirs, yet all alike met death.

Explanation: Shun was the most illustrious of emperors, Yu the foremost of kings, the Duke of Zhou the paragon of loyalty and sagacity, and Confucius the brightest expositor of the Dao—all were the highest exemplars of sagehood, revered and cherished by all under heaven for ten thousand generations. Yet Shun’s bitter hardships, Yu’s cares and toils, the Duke of Zhou’s fears and trepidations, and Confucius’ haste and distress—when examined against the records of the Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou dynasties, and weighed against Confucius’ own words, the truth of their suffering is beyond doubt. To say they lived in constant sorrow until death is no exaggeration.

As for Jie and Zhou, their wanton ease and reckless indulgence, their unbridled delight in the pleasures of ear and eye, their exhaustive pursuit of every desire, their unrestrained passions in the Leaning Palace, their unchecked desires through long nights—this may indeed be called blissful fulfillment of every wish. All under heaven ascribe beauty to Shun, Yu, the Duke of Zhou, and Confucius, calling them the Four Sages; all under heaven ascribe evil to Jie and Zhou, calling them the Two Fiends. The Four Sages bore the empty name of ten thousand generations, while the Two Fiends enjoyed the real benefits of their own lifetimes. For reality is not something a name can confer, nor is a name something reality can claim. In the end, what we call praise or blame is merely passed down through the ages. How can those who praise or blame know the truth of what came before? And how can those who are praised or blamed know what comes after? Though there be praise or blame, how does it differ from a clod of earth or a block of wood? Is it not fitting, then, to say that beauty and evil alike meet the same end in death?

Liezi speaks thus not to urge the world to abandon the names of the Four Sages and pursue the reality of the Two Fiends, but to make seekers of the Dao discern that both name and reality are equally illusory, and to recognize that sorrow and joy are equal burdens. Thus, he uses the common judgments of all under heaven for ten thousand generations to show that one should forsake the traces of the sages and seek the Dao of the sages. Moreover, the Four Sages, content with heaven and knowing fate, were never truly troubled. What we call their hardships, cares, and fears were but unavoidable responses to the world, sharing in the fortunes and misfortunes of the people, and grieving the people’s sorrows. The name of sagehood and wisdom was bestowed upon them by others, and they did not refuse it. Only by understanding this can one grasp the meaning of Liezi’s words, for he walks the same Dao as the Four Sages.

*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Vacuous and Unwobbling Classic*, Scroll Sixteen—Concluded
*Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Vacuous and Unwobbling Classic*, Scroll Seventeen

**Submitted by Jiang Yu, an Inner Student of Hangzhou Prefecture School in Song**

**Yang Zhu, Part Two**

Yang Zhu met with King Hui of Liang and said, "Governing the world is as easy as turning one’s palm." The king replied, "You cannot even govern one wife and one concubine, nor tend to a garden of three *mu*—how can you claim that governing the world is as easy as turning one’s palm?"

Yang Zhu answered, "Have you not seen a shepherd? A hundred sheep in a flock, led by a five-foot boy carrying a switch, will go east when he wishes and west when he wishes. But if Yao were to lead a single sheep while Shun followed with the switch, they could not move forward. Moreover, I have heard: a fish that can swallow a boat does not swim in shallow streams; the great swan soars high and does not alight in filthy ponds. Why? Because its reach is vast. The deep tones of *huangzhong* and *dalü* cannot accompany frivolous dances. Why? Because their resonance is too distant. Thus, those who govern the great do not govern the petty; those who achieve great deeds do not fuss over small ones. This is what I mean."

*Explication:* Only when one understands what it means to govern as easily as turning one’s palm can one truly speak of governance. Yangzi said, "The world is vast, yet its governance lies in the Dao; the Four Seas are distant, yet their governance lies in the heart." If one truly believes these words, then the Dao exists even without descending to the level of one’s belt—how much more effortless than turning one’s palm! If one can embody this Dao, then one may act without action, and the people will govern themselves; one may love stillness, and the people will correct themselves. Thus, by not governing, one governs. But if one insists on governing through governance, then even one wife and one concubine will overwhelm one’s capacity, and a garden of three *mu* will exhaust one’s strength—this is like Yao leading the sheep while Shun follows with the switch. Therefore, it is said: those who govern the great do not govern the petty; those who achieve great deeds do not fuss over small ones.

Yang Zhu said, "The affairs of the distant past have vanished—who records them? The deeds of the Three Sovereigns exist as if they exist not; the affairs of the Five Emperors seem like waking dreams; the undertakings of the Three Kings appear now hidden, now revealed—of a hundred, not one is known. Even the events of one’s own lifetime are sometimes heard, sometimes seen—of ten thousand, not one is known. The matters before one’s eyes may endure or fade—of a thousand, not one is known. From the distant past to the present, the years are too numerous to count. Yet since Fuxi’s time, over three hundred thousand years have passed, and all distinctions of worth and folly, beauty and ugliness, success and failure, right and wrong have dissolved—only the swiftness or slowness of their passing remains. To pride oneself on fleeting praise or blame, to exhaust one’s spirit and body in pursuit of a name that may linger a few hundred years after death—how can this moisten dry bones? Where is the joy in life?"

*Explication:* Whatever can be spoken or done is nothing but affairs. If one does not depart from the realm of words and deeds, one cannot escape the workings of time and fate. Even the governance of the distant past involved affairs; the Way of the Sovereigns, the virtue of the Emperors, the achievements of the Kings—with each descending age, affairs grow more entangled. Judging by what the ears and eyes perceive, the knowledge of one may be double, fivefold, tenfold, a hundredfold, or even a thousandfold that of another. Extending this upward to the Yellow Emperor, some events exist as if they exist not, some seem like waking dreams, some appear or vanish—of a hundred, not one is known. As for the distant past, all is already lost—who records it? Thus, the traces of good and evil alike are all abandoned. If one believes goodness is to be pursued, then the name of goodness will also fade in time; if one believes evil is to be avoided, then the sound of evil will also vanish in time—only the swiftness or slowness of their passing remains. How can one be so deluded as to race endlessly after such fleeting distinctions?

Yet the affairs of the Sovereigns, Emperors, and Kings in responding to the world did not depart from the realm of the nameable—were they truly right? Were they truly wrong? The traces of emperors and kings arose from stimulus and response, compelled to act, without intent for fame, yet others attributed names to them. This differs from those who pride themselves on praise or blame to seek renown. Thus, though their worldly affairs passed with the times, what made them sages endures eternally. If it were not so, why would the governance of sages be esteemed?

Yang Zhu said, "Humans resemble the category of Heaven and Earth, possessing the nature of the Five Constants. Among beings, the most intelligent are humans. Yet humans lack claws and teeth sufficient for defense, skin tough enough for protection, speed enough to flee harm, or fur and feathers to shield against cold and heat. They must rely on external things for sustenance, depending on wisdom rather than strength. Thus, what wisdom values is preserving the self; what strength devalues is encroaching upon others. Yet the body does not belong to oneself—once born, one cannot but preserve it; things do not belong to oneself—once obtained, one cannot but part with them. The body is the master of life, just as things are the master of sustenance. Though one preserves life, one cannot possess the body; though one does not discard things, one cannot possess them. To possess things or the body is to wrongly claim private ownership of the world’s body and the world’s things—only the sage does not do this. To regard the world’s body and the world’s things as public—only the perfected person does this. This is what is called the utmost of the utmost."

*Explication:* In life, one must rely on things for sustenance—this is the foundation of nurturing life, the principle of safeguarding life, the truth of fulfilling life, and cannot be avoided. The body is the master of life, so to live, one must first not separate from form; things are the master of sustenance, so to nourish form, one must first rely on things. Yet there are those with surplus things who do not nourish their form, so though one does not discard things, one cannot possess them. There are those whose form remains intact yet whose life perishes, so though one preserves life, one cannot possess the body. People of the world do not know how to nourish form—how can they truly preserve life? To wrongly claim private ownership of the world’s body as one’s own, to wrongly claim private ownership of the world’s things for sustenance—this is to pursue what life has no use for."

If the body remains intact yet the principle of life perishes, what is there in the world worth pursuing? Only the sage, perhaps, can abandon affairs and relinquish life’s concerns to attain wholeness of form and restoration of essence. Yet even the sage is but a harbinger of transformation, unable to forget the self. As for the perfected one who never strays from the true—such a one makes the world’s body public, so the body is no different from things; makes the world’s things public, so things are none other than the self. This is what *Zhuangzi*’s "Attaining Life" calls "essence refined to the utmost," and what may be termed the ultimate of the ultimate.

Yang Zhu said: The people’s inability to rest stems from four causes—first, for longevity; second, for fame; third, for rank; fourth, for wealth. With these four, one fears ghosts, fears others, fears authority, fears punishment—this is what is called the fugitive. Life and death are determined by external forces. If one does not defy fate, why covet longevity? If one does not prize nobility, why covet fame? If one does not seek power, why covet rank? If one does not crave riches, why covet wealth? This is what is called the compliant. The world has no counterpart; fate is mastered from within.

Explanation: At the beginning of life, all possess the way to longevity, and those who attain their constant nature live long. Holding fast to the everlasting and loving virtue, fame then follows, rank is achieved, and wealth becomes abundant—this is the sequence of the four. Human longevity naturally ranges from those above like Peng Zu to those below like the Five Hegemons of Yu. How can people’s greed for life ever cease? As for the martyr who dies for fame or the miser who dies for wealth—before obtaining, they fret over gain; after obtaining, they fret over loss. Their minds are tormented, their bodies labored, their lives spent in haste—when do they know a moment’s peace? Of the four, people can seldom possess them all. Even if one attains one, though they toil their whole lives, it is never enough to satisfy their desires. How much less can they satisfy their insatiable cravings if they pursue all four? This is why the people cannot rest.

With these four, one advances in pursuit and retreats in avoidance. In the unseen, they fear ghosts. In society, they feign emotions to avoid others’ reproach. Authority need not be wielded against them, yet they tremble in dread of its reach. Punishment need not be set for them, yet they quiver in fear of transgression. In the slightest movement, their frail selves are controlled by things, and the true master within is lost—this is what is called the fugitive. Little do they know that equalizing the changes of life and death allows one to forget longevity and brevity; discerning the wealth of contentment makes wealth and possessions unnecessary to pursue. Without carriages and robes to bind them, blades and saws do not touch them. Indifferent to order and chaos, they know neither demotion nor promotion. With nothing to covet within, they have nothing to fear without. Dwelling between heaven and earth, they move alone, enter alone, come alone, go alone—the world has no counterpart. This is called "possessing alone." One who possesses alone is called the most noble.

Thus the saying goes: If one neither marries nor enters office, half of human desires are lost. If one neither eats nor wears clothes, the way of ruler and subject ceases. Explanation: Hunger drives one to eat, cold drives one to wear clothes—those with life cannot escape desire. With desire unfulfilled, strife arises, and the distinction between ruler and subject is thereby clarified. All people must eat and wear clothes, but not all marry or enter office. Marriage brings many troubles of human relations; office stirs the heart of envy. Is this not the root of the people’s inability to rest? One who neither marries nor enters office, though not entirely free of desires, is far removed from the common folk.

As for the cessation of the way of ruler and subject—this means both ruler and subject are at ease, neither knowing who serves whom nor seeking favor or praise. Thus the duty between ruler and subject cannot be abolished, but its way may cease. A Zhou proverb says: "A farmer can be killed by sitting." Rising at dawn and returning at dusk, he takes this as the constant of his nature; sipping beans and chewing greens, he takes this as the height of flavor.

A body with coarse, thick flesh and sinews knotted in tension, suddenly placed beneath soft silk curtains and fed with fine grains, meats, orchids, and citrus—such a heart grows fevered, the frame restless, and illness brews within. Should the lords of Shang and Lu share the toil of a field laborer, they would not endure even a single season before exhaustion. Thus, what the rustic finds at ease, what he deems most excellent, he calls unmatched under heaven.

**Explanation:** All are human, yet if a field laborer were to receive the provisions of a ruler, he would fall ill; if the lords of Shang and Lu were to share the labor of a field hand, they would be spent. To abandon fine grains and follow a diet of greens is indeed difficult for human nature; to exchange greens for fine grains seems easy. Yet one cannot readily exchange the ease of the field laborer, for habit reshapes a person and cannot be swiftly altered. How much more so for those drowned in external things, content with vulgar learning—how can one expect them to change their habits and find rest in the supreme Dao? Surely those who have not pondered this deem it distant.

Long ago in Song, there was a field laborer who wore only coarse hemp to survive the winter. When spring came and he tilled the eastern fields, he basked in the sun, unaware of the world’s grand halls, sheltered chambers, silk floss, or fox-fur robes. Turning to his wife, he said: *"The warmth of the sun’s embrace is known to none. If I offered it to our lord, he would surely reward me richly."* A wealthy villager told him: *"Once, a man praised the fine taste of wild beans, sweet spring celery, and duckweed seeds to the village elders. The elders tasted them, only to find their mouths stung and their bellies pained. The crowd laughed and reviled him, and the man was deeply ashamed. You are of the same kind."*

**Explanation:** One who wears coarse hemp knows nothing of the warmth of grand halls, sheltered chambers, silk floss, or fox-fur robes; one who praises wild beans, sweet spring celery, and duckweed knows nothing of the richness of fine grains. Cloaked in comfort, they remain unaware of the Dao’s way of clothing all things; seduced by worldly flavors, they fail to know the Dao’s blandness, which is flavorless—so it is with these men.

Yang Zhu said: *"Grand houses, fine clothes, rich flavors, and beautiful women—with these four, what more could one seek? Yet those who possess them and still seek more are of insatiable nature. Such insatiability is the worm that gnaws at yin and yang."*

**Explanation:** Grand houses, fine clothes, rich flavors, and beautiful women are all things beyond one’s proper share. To pursue them relentlessly is to be of insatiable nature. Why must one seek beyond these to be deemed insatiable? Mencius considered the eyes’ colors, the ears’ sounds, the nose’s scents, and the limbs’ ease as human nature. The teachings of Liezi sought harmony with nature and contentment in simplicity, despising those who distort their true feelings to chase empty fame. Thus, to possess these four and still seek more is to be the worm that gnaws at yin and yang. Moreover, to speak of having these four is to imply wealth and sufficiency. If one must seek them to possess them, what greater insatiability could there be?

Loyalty is insufficient to secure a ruler’s peace and only endangers the self; righteousness is insufficient to benefit others and only harms one’s life. Peace for superiors does not come from loyalty, and so the name of loyalty fades; benefiting others does not come from righteousness, and so the name of righteousness perishes. When ruler and subject are both at ease, when self and others both profit—this was the Way of antiquity.

**Explanation:** Loyalty dares to confront the ruler’s displeasure; righteousness is resolute in shaping things. Loyalty may overreach in self-discipline, and others will turn against it; righteousness may err in severity, and the unworthy will respond with malice. But to serve a ruler with the Dao is to bear a fine name oneself while the ruler enjoys renown—is this not ruler and subject both at ease? To respond to things with the Dao is to remain without action oneself while the people transform naturally—is this not self and others both profiting? Laozi said: *"When the great Dao declines, there is benevolence and righteousness; when the state is in chaos, there are loyal ministers."* This is the same idea.

Master Yu said: *"Abandon fame, and there is no sorrow."* Laozi said: *"Fame is the guest of reality."* Yet the careless pursue fame without end. If fame cannot be abandoned, can it not at least be treated as a guest? Now, to have fame is to enjoy honor; to lack fame is to suffer disgrace. Honor brings ease and pleasure; disgrace brings worry and hardship. Worry and hardship violate one’s nature; ease and pleasure accord with it. These are tied to reality. How can fame be abandoned? How can it be treated as a guest? The danger lies in clinging to fame and burdening reality. Clinging to fame and burdening reality will lead to irreparable peril—how could it be merely a matter of ease and pleasure or worry and hardship?

**Explanation:** Master Yu’s abandonment of fame does not mean its absence but its non-attachment. Laozi’s treating fame as a guest does not mean its removal but its non-mastery. Since life exists, so does the self; since the self exists, so do burdens. Where things and self interact, every affair involves fame, and every fame involves reality. Joy and suffering in one’s nature are tied to fame—how could fame be entirely eradicated? The careless, enamored of empty fame, pursue it endlessly and thus lose their grasp on reality. Those who covet the name of benevolence may go so far as to kill themselves; those who covet the name of righteousness may go so far as to destroy their kin. Jie Zitui died for loyalty; Weisheng died for trust—both clung to fame and burdened reality, courting irreparable peril.

This chapter of Liezi repeatedly expounds the principle of fame and reality. Yet lest scholars mistakenly believe the Dao seeks to eradicate all fame under heaven, it further clarifies: the danger lies in clinging to fame and burdening reality. If one can avoid clinging to fame and burdening reality, this is Master Yu’s abandonment of fame, Zhuangzi’s treating fame as a guest, and the sage’s so-called namelessness. In this lies the fullness of the Way of conducting oneself and responding to things.

**Yang Zhu’s Explanation:** Master Liezi’s classic elucidates the essentials of the great Dao, transmitting the orthodox lineage of the Yellow Emperor, Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Kings Wen and Wu, the Duke of Zhou, and Confucius.

Yang’s doctrine of "for me" is a heretical teaching that deludes the people, corroding the Way of the sages more grievously than any other. Thus, later scholars of the sages took the ability to refute Yang and Mo as the mark of a true disciple of the sages. Yet observe how Liezi takes the name *Yu-kou* (Master Yu)—this too signifies his self-assumed duty to safeguard the Way of the former sages. His book repeatedly cites the words of Yang and Mo as instruction, even equating Confucius with Mozi as those whom the world wishes to bring peace and benefit. Here, he further offers the teachings of *Yang Zhu* as a separate chapter. Does the author of *Liezi* truly regard Yang Zhu’s Way as unopposed to the sages and thus fit to instruct the world? Or does he recognize it as a doctrine that obstructs benevolence and righteousness—why then would he adopt its words?

The intent of *Liezi* may be inferred. Yang’s "for me" is a lament for those in the world who, chasing external things, lose themselves, unaware of what abides within. In life, they pursue longevity, fame, rank, and wealth—none of which truly benefit the self. Even in death, they still seek blessings for their descendants. Yet descendants are but the cast-off husks of heaven and earth—what have they to do with me? Thus, men cautiously weigh what they see and hear, cling to right and wrong, are restrained by rewards and punishments, and advance or retreat by the measures of fame and law. Restless and anxious, they spend their lives no different from prisoners in heavy shackles, never awakening to the fact that creation brought me into being for *myself* alone. My life ought to be lived for me, ensuring that the self is not lost—why then torment body and mind to seek what others seek, to suit what others suit, and in doing so, lose what is truly mine?

From this, we know that *Liezi* does not wish the world to embrace Yang’s heretical doctrine. Rather, he desires that men not be enslaved by external things, that they know only to preserve the self. If one can avoid losing the self, then governing a state or ordering the world becomes but the overflow of one’s surplus—how magnificent would that be!

Nevertheless, Master Liezi’s instruction is subtle. His book clarifies that all beings arise from utmost emptiness, taking *zong-cang-gu* (the storehouse of the ancestral) as its principle, equating all paths to the lost sheep. Thus, he takes the most extreme of Yang Zhu’s heretical teachings, merges them with the Way of the sages, and presents them as one discourse, hoping that scholars will not follow the traces of the sages but seek their minds instead. Therefore, every passage that invokes Yang Zhu’s words conveys nothing but the essence of the supreme Way, even going so far as to equate the Four Sages and the Two Villains as sharing the same ultimate end.

Later readers, upon reaching this point, may well doubt that *Liezi* considers Yao and Shun as truly outside the Way, or that he genuinely aligns with Yang’s heresy. This is the fault of reading the text without grasping its circumstances. Little do they realize that this very chapter is where *Liezi* exerts his utmost effort, forming a continuous thread with Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Kings Wen and Wu, the Duke of Zhou, and Confucius. Confucius said: *"Is it only the* Spring and Autumn Annals *that understands me? Is it only the* Spring and Autumn Annals *that condemns me?"* The chapter *Yang Zhu* in *Liezi* is of the same nature.

*Commentary on the* True Classic of Perfect Virtue *concludes, Volume Seventeen.*
*Commentary on the* True Classic of Perfect Virtue *begins, Volume Eighteen.*

Minister Jiang Yu, an Inner Student of the Hangzhou Prefecture Academy of Song, respectfully presents:

*Explaining the Symbols, First Fascicle*

Master Liezi studied under Master Huqiu Zilin. Master Huqiu Zilin said: "If you understand how to hold to what comes after, then we may speak of how to hold to the self." Liezi said: "I wish to hear about holding to what comes after." He replied: "Observe your shadow, and you will know."

Liezi turned and beheld his shadow: when his form bent, the shadow curved; when his form straightened, the shadow aligned. Thus, crookedness and straightness follow the form and do not reside in the shadow; bending and extending depend on things and not on me. This is called "holding to what comes after and thereby abiding in what comes first."

*Explanation:* The *Explaining the Symbols* elucidates the sage’s response to the world. The sage’s response to the world arises only after being moved, acts only under compulsion, and rises only when there is no alternative. Taking things as the form and oneself as the shadow, the shadow always follows the form in its crookedness or straightness, just as I always yield to things in bending or extending. When they come, I come with them; when they go, I go with them. What once moved now halts; what once sat now rises. Others all seize what comes first, while I alone take what comes after, as though lacking any fixed principle. Yet by not contending with things, none in the world can contend with me—this is how one abides in what comes first.

Master Liezi’s teacher was Master Huqiu Zilin. Once, when his disciples paid their respects, he asked: "What did Master Hu say?" Here, he recounts Master Hu’s teaching on holding to what comes after. In the *Heavenly Signs*, the sage’s Way proceeds from Heaven to man, hence it is said to be wordless. In the *Explaining the Symbols*, the sage’s Way proceeds from man to Heaven, hence it cannot avoid being spoken. At first, wordlessness proceeding to Heaven is the sage’s original heart; in the end, speech proceeding to man is the sage’s unavoidable necessity.

Moreover, Liezi’s Way of life arises from knowledge and self-attainment—why would he need to study? Yet to leave instruction for all ages and become the teacher of ten thousand generations, he must, from beginning to end, employ the Way of master and disciple in his discourse. This is akin to Confucius, who did not claim sageliness for himself but said, "I love antiquity and am earnest in seeking it."

Guanyin said to Master Liezi: "Beautiful words produce a beautiful echo; ugly words produce an ugly echo. A tall body casts a long shadow; a short body casts a short shadow. Name is the echo; body is the shadow." Thus he said: "Be cautious in your words, for they will be answered; be cautious in your conduct, for it will be followed."

*Explanation:* The *Book of Changes* says: "Words and actions are the hinge and spring of the noble person. The release of the hinge and spring determines honor or disgrace. Words and actions are how the noble person moves Heaven and Earth—thus the noble person is cautious of them." Therefore, the sage sees the outward and knows the inward, observes the past and knows the future—this is the principle by which he attains foreknowledge.

*Explanation:* Outward and inward, past and future—these are one mechanism. People are often blind to the subtlest illumination and only recognize outward and inward, past and future when things have taken shape and their patterns are fixed. Who can see the outward and know the inward, observe the past and know the future? Only the sage who, from the root source, discerns the portents of transformation.

The *Book of Changes* says: "The inchworm bends to seek extension. The dragon and snake store their power to preserve themselves." The measure lies in oneself; the verification lies in others. If others love me, I must love them; if others hate me, I must hate them. Tang and Wu loved all under Heaven, thus they reigned as kings; Jie and Zhou hated all under Heaven, thus they perished—this is what is verified. When verification and measure are both clear yet not acted upon, it is like leaving without using the door or walking without following the path. Seeking profit thus—is it not difficult?

I have observed that the Divine Farmer possessed the virtue of blazing clarity. Verifying it against the books of Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou, and measuring it by the words of exemplary scholars and worthies, I find that those who preserved or ruined, abolished or established, yet did not proceed by this Way—there have been none.

*Explanation:* Before the Great Primal separated, the ten thousand images were a chaotic whole. After the Two Modes divided, each thing found its place. Not even a hair’s breadth could be disordered; not even one or two could be misaligned. Sound moves and echo responds; form arises and shadow follows. What lies in me can be measured; what lies in others can be verified. Following Yao and Shun, the imperial enterprise can be traced; following Tang and Wu, the kingly achievement can be inherited. The Divine Farmer possessed the virtue of blazing clarity—he attained this alone. The books of Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou record this alone. The words of exemplary scholars and worthies debate this alone. Even the most sagely of persons, subtle and profoundly penetrating, unfathomably deep—when they emerge to respond to things, there has never been one who did not govern by this Way.

Mencius, who claimed to understand words, said: "If the sages were to arise again, they would surely follow my words." His discourse on the Way of the former sages also said: "If the sages were to arise again, they would not alter my words." Regarding the sages who had not yet appeared, he predicted what they would surely do, for they would not go beyond verification and measure.

As for the saying, "If others love me, I must love them; if others hate me, I must hate them"—the sage does not love or hate others based on their love or hatred of him. Rather, if others love me, it must be because I have something worthy of their love; if others hate me, it must be because I have something worthy of their hatred.

Yan Hui said: "Those who ask about the Way do so for the sake of wealth. Now that I have obtained the pearl, I am wealthy—why would I need the Way?" Master Liezi said: "Jie and Zhou valued profit and slighted the Way, thus they perished. Fortunately, I have not yet spoken to you of this. A person without righteousness, who eats and nothing more, is like a chicken or dog. Those who forcibly seize food and lock horns, with the stronger prevailing—these are beasts. To be like chickens, dogs, or beasts, yet desire others to honor oneself—this cannot be. If others do not honor oneself, then danger and disgrace will befall one."

*Explanation:* Zhuangzi once used the "mysterious pearl" to illustrate the Yellow Emperor’s Way.

The pearl, as an object, is perfectly round and luminous—it is the ultimate treasure. Its roundness allows nothing to obstruct it, and its clarity reflects all things without exception. Thus, obtaining the pearl serves as a metaphor for the wealth of the Way. Only those who attain the Way possess all things in abundance, which is what Zhuangzi called "wealth beyond measure." To value profit as wealth leads only to peril and disgrace.

Liezi had mastered archery and sought instruction from Master Guanyin. The master asked: "Do you know why you hit the mark?" Liezi replied: "I do not." Guanyin said: "Then you are not ready." Liezi withdrew and practiced for three years before returning to report. Guanyin asked again: "Do you now know why you hit the mark?" Liezi answered: "I do." Guanyin said: "Now it is acceptable. Hold fast to this and do not lose it. This principle applies not only to archery but to governing a state and cultivating oneself." Thus, the sage does not examine whether things endure or perish but investigates the reasons behind them.

*Explanation:* An archer who hits the target without prior intent cannot be called a skilled archer, for he does not understand why he succeeded. Once he grasps the reason, his success lies within himself, and even the skill of Gan Ying or Fei Wei becomes attainable. Governing a state and cultivating oneself follow the same principle. By discerning the causes of survival and ruin, one can wield the Way to navigate the times, ensuring enduring existence. If the nature of survival and ruin has already manifested, even scrutiny will be of no avail.

Liezi said: "Those whose vitality is abundant grow arrogant, and those whose strength is abundant grow reckless—such people cannot be spoken to of the Way. Thus, one does not discuss the Way with the young and vigorous, let alone practice it."

*Explanation:* Those whose vitality is abundant grow arrogant, and those whose strength is in full bloom are in their prime—a time when external things assail them and their virtue naturally declines. How could they be spoken to of the Way? By the time their hair turns gray, their desires soften and their bodies begin to rest, making them fit to hear of the Way and practice it. Yet this speaks only of those who are subject to the great transformations of nature. There are also those who follow the great transformations without being swept along by them, who never lose the heart of an infant. Though strong, they are not arrogant; though aged, they do not decline. In speaking of the Way, they are never at a loss.

Thus, those who act recklessly will find no one to guide them. Without guidance, they become isolated and lack support. The wise entrust matters to others, so even in old age they do not decline, and even when their wisdom is exhausted, they do not fall into chaos.

Therefore, the difficulty in governing a state lies in recognizing the worthy, not in considering oneself wise.

*Explanation:* Those who do not take on tasks but exhaust their strength in labor will not decline with age. Those who do not hoard wisdom but employ the counsel of the wise will not fall into chaos when their own wisdom is spent. Even with the intelligence of Yao and Shun, Yao worried over not obtaining Shun, and Shun worried over not obtaining Yu and Gaoyao. The difficulty of governing a state lies solely in this—how can one rely on the conduct of self-proclaimed wisdom?

A man of Song crafted a mulberry leaf out of jade for his lord, taking three years to complete it. Its edges, veins, and fine details were so lifelike that when mixed with real mulberry leaves, it could not be distinguished. As a result, this man lived off his craft in the state of Song.

When Liezi heard of this, he said: "If heaven and earth took three years to produce a single leaf, few things would have leaves at all." Thus, the sage relies on the transformative power of the Way, not on cleverness or skill.

*Explanation:* Carving jade into a mulberry leaf so indistinguishable from real leaves demonstrates how human skill can rival the workings of creation. Yet the jade carver took three years to complete one leaf, while the transformations of nature—with yang qi circulating subtly through heaven and earth—bring forth the flourishing and withering of all things, shaping countless forms without being called clever. Even the skill of the Song artisan only secured his livelihood in one state—how much less for those whose skill does not match his? Thus, the sage relies on the transformative power of the Way, not on cleverness or skill.

Liezi was impoverished, his appearance bearing the marks of hunger. A visitor mentioned this to Ziyang, the ruler of Zheng, saying: "Lie Yukou is a man of the Way. That he lives in your state in such poverty—does this not suggest you do not value scholars?" Ziyang immediately ordered an official to send grain to Liezi. Liezi went out to meet the messenger, bowed twice, and declined the gift. The messenger departed.

When Liezi returned home, his wife gazed at him and, striking her breast, said: "I have heard that the wives and children of those who possess the Way all enjoy ease and happiness. Yet you bear the marks of hunger. The ruler passed by and sent you food, but you refused it—is this not fate?"

Liezi laughed and replied: "The ruler does not know me personally. He sent me grain only because of another’s words, and if he were to punish me, it would also be on account of another’s words. This is why I did not accept it." Later, the people indeed rose in rebellion and killed Ziyang.

**Explanation:** The sage is never careless in accepting or refusing. If something does not accord with the Way, he would rather die than accept it—how much less would he do so for the sake of his wife’s offerings? By navigating the world in this manner, he avoided disaster.

The Lu family of the Shi clan had two sons. One loved learning, the other loved warfare. The one who loved learning offered his arts to the Marquis of Qi, who accepted him and made him tutor to the noble scions. The one who loved warfare went to Chu and presented his methods to the King of Chu, who was pleased and appointed him military commander. Their salaries enriched their household, and their ranks honored their kin.

The neighboring Meng family had two sons as well, who pursued the same studies, yet suffered from poverty. Envying the Shi family’s prosperity, they sought advice on how to advance. The two Shi sons truthfully told them what they had done.

One of the Meng sons went to Qin and offered his arts to the King of Qin. The king said: "Nowadays, the feudal lords contend with force, and all they concern themselves with are soldiers and provisions. If I were to govern my state with benevolence and righteousness, it would be the path to ruin." He then castrated the man and banished him.

The other Meng son went to Wei and presented his methods to the Marquis of Wei. The marquis said: "I rule a weak state, hemmed in between great powers. I serve the great states and soothe the small ones—this is the way to seek security. If I were to rely on military power, destruction would soon follow. If I let you return unharmed, you would go to another state and become a grave threat to me." He then had the man’s feet cut off and sent him back to Lu.

Upon their return, the Meng father and sons beat their breasts and reproached the Shi family. The Shi father said: "Those who seize the moment prosper; those who miss it perish. Your path was the same as ours, yet your results differ—this is because you missed the moment, not because your conduct was flawed. Moreover, in the world, principles have no constant right, and affairs no constant wrong. What was once used may now be discarded; what is now discarded may later be used. This matter of use and disuse has no fixed right or wrong. Seizing opportunities and adapting to circumstances—this depends on wisdom. If wisdom is insufficient, even if one were as learned as Confucius or as skilled as Lü Shang, where could one go and not meet with failure?"

The Meng father and sons relaxed their expressions, free of resentment, and said: "We understand. Say no more."

**Explanation:** The Guo clan of Qi and the Xiang clan of Song differed in wealth, for the Xiang clan did not grasp the Guo clan’s way. Beigongzi and Ximenzi met with different fortunes in their undertakings, for their virtue and fate may have differed in depth. As for the Shi and Meng families of Lu, their pursuits were the same—unlike the Guo and Xiang clans, who followed different paths, or Beigongzi and Ximenzi, whose virtue and fate may have differed. Qi inherited the customs of the Grand Duke and continued with the governance of Guan and Yan; Wei was enfeoffed by Kangshu and Duke Wu, who maintained its administration, so Wei abounded in noble men. Thus, the states of Qi and Wei concerned themselves only with benevolence and righteousness. Chu, dwelling among the barbarians, had King Wu who once wished to display his worn armor to observe the governance of the Central States, and King Zhuang who reviewed his troops at the Luo suburbs to inquire about the Zhou tripods. From Duke Xiao of Qin onward, Qin devoured the six states. The kings of Qin and Chu concerned themselves only with soldiers and provisions. The Shi family applied the methods the Meng family had used in Wei to Chu, and presented the techniques the Meng family had used in Qin to Qi, so they met with success everywhere. Had the Meng family likewise applied the methods used in Wei to Qin and the techniques used in Qin to Wei, they would have achieved the same results as the Shi family. Why did they reverse their methods? Thus, the Shi family attributed their failure to a lack of timely wisdom, and the Meng family, too, released their resentment.

The virtue of governing with benevolence and righteousness is profound, so those who offended were punished severely. The art of military power in a strong state is shallow, so the punishment stopped at amputation. Nevertheless, seizing opportunities and adapting to circumstances depend on wisdom. Yet in the world, there are matters beyond the reach of wisdom—thus, the differing fortunes of the two families were also a matter of fate. Yet when Liezi recounted their words, it was because the *Shuofu* does not depart from the scrutiny of forms and names. If one were to attribute all success and failure to fate, scholars would drift toward the inaction of clods and dust, which is not the Way. Therefore, in this case, he did not dismiss the wisdom of seizing the moment.

Duke Wen of Jin went out to a meeting, intending to attack Wei. Gongzi Chu looked up to the sky and laughed. The duke asked why he laughed. He replied: "I laugh at a neighbor who was escorting his wife to her parents’ home. Along the way, he saw a woman picking mulberry leaves, was pleased, and spoke with her. Yet when he glanced back at his wife, someone was beckoning to her as well. I secretly laughed at this."

The duke understood his meaning and called off the attack. He turned the army back, but before they arrived, someone had already raided their northern border.

**Explanation:** People’s thoughts are not far apart. What I desire, others desire; what I know, others know. To pursue one’s own ambitions without considering others’ feelings is delusion.

The state of Jin suffered from bandits. There was a man named Xi Yong who could recognize bandits by their appearance, discerning their intentions from their eyebrows and eyelashes. The Marquis of Jin sent him to identify bandits, and he did not miss a single one among hundreds. The marquis was overjoyed and told Zhao Wenzi: "I have found one man, and all the bandits in the state are as good as gone. Why would I need more?"

Wenzi said: "Relying on spies and scrutiny to catch bandits, Your Lordship will never eradicate them. Moreover, Xi Yong will not meet a good end."

Soon after, the bandits plotted among themselves: "The one who has us cornered is Xi Yong." They then joined forces to ambush and kill him.

When the Marquis of Jin heard this, he was greatly alarmed. He immediately summoned Wenzi and told him: "Just as you predicted, Xi Yong is dead. But what method should we use to capture the bandits?"

Wenzi replied: "There is a saying in the Zhou proverbs: 'He who sees the fish in the abyss brings misfortune; he who predicts hidden things invites calamity.' If Your Lordship wishes to eliminate bandits, nothing is better than elevating the worthy and entrusting them with governance, so that instruction is clear above and transformation flows below. When the people possess a sense of shame, what need is there for bandits?"

Thereupon, the marquis appointed Sui Hui to administer the government, and the bandits fled to Qin.

**Explanation:** Those who rely on spies and scrutiny catch bandits only after the crime is committed, while those who clarify instruction and transformation prevent banditry before it arises. Once a man has become a bandit, where is the virtue in that? Thus, Xi Yong’s scrutiny of bandits led to his untimely death. But when transformation is enacted and the people come to know shame, as when Sui Hui was entrusted with governance, the bandits fled to Qin. Yet driving bandits away only to have them flee to Qin is like those who control floods by making a neighboring state their drainage ditch.

To govern the world by the Dao is to have the people dwell without knowing what they do, move without knowing where they go, harmonized in desirelessness—this is called "pristine simplicity." When covetous ambition does not arise, who would become a bandit? The state of Jin relied on spies and scrutiny, so I addressed its error and guided it toward governance, but I did not proceed to expound the fullness of the Dao.

Confucius, returning from Wei to Lu, halted his carriage by a bridge over the river to observe. There was a waterfall thirty *ren* high, its swirling currents flowing ninety *li*, where fish and turtles could not swim, and *yuan* and *tuo* could not dwell. Yet a man was about to ford it. Confucius sent someone along the bank to stop him, saying: "This waterfall is thirty *ren* high, its currents swirl for ninety *li*, where fish and turtles cannot swim, and *yuan* and *tuo* cannot dwell. Surely it is too perilous to cross?"

The man paid no heed, waded in, and emerged safely. Confucius asked him: "Is this skill? Or do you possess some technique? How were you able to enter and exit?"

The man replied: "When I first entered, I began with loyalty and trust. When I exited, I followed with loyalty and trust. Loyalty and trust placed my body amidst the waves, and I dared not act for private ends. That is how I was able to enter and emerge again."

Confucius said to his disciples: "Remember this, all of you. If even water can be crossed by entrusting oneself to loyalty and trust, how much more so with people?"

**Explanation:** Loyalty follows the Dao of water without self-interest; trust rests in water without doubt. Thus, entering and exiting the water becomes something one does without knowing how—this is how he was able to enter and emerge again. The *Huangdi* chapter once spoke of this, attributing it to following the principles of nature and destiny; here, it is called loyalty and trust. "Placing one’s body amidst the waves" means that loyalty and trust are the very principles of nature and destiny. The previous chapter clarified the spontaneity of the imperial Dao, hence it spoke of "nature and destiny." This chapter demonstrates the correspondence of natural principles, hence it speaks of "loyalty and trust." Confucius once told Zizhang that loyalty and trust could be practiced even in the lands of the Man and Mo barbarians. His teachings on loyalty and trust are manifold, so here he bids his disciples to remember them.

*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Pristine Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue*, Scroll 18—concluded.
*Explication of the Perfect Dao of Pristine Emptiness and Ultimate Virtue*, Scroll 19.

**Submitted by Jiang Yu, Inner College Student of Hangzhou Prefecture, Song Dynasty, on the "Explanation of Correspondences" Section**

Bai Gong asked Confucius, "Can one engage in subtle discourse with others?"
Confucius did not respond.

Bai Gong asked again, "If a stone is cast into water, what then?"
Confucius said, "A skilled diver from Wu could retrieve it."
Bai Gong said, "If water is poured into water, what then?"
Confucius replied, "When the waters of the Zi and Meng rivers merge, Yi Ya could distinguish them by taste."

Bai Gong said, "Is it truly impossible to engage in subtle discourse with others?"
Confucius said, "Why would it be impossible? Only one who knows the nature of words can do so. For one who knows the nature of words does not speak with words. Those who contend for fish get drenched; those who chase beasts run swiftly—not because they delight in it. Thus, the most profound words transcend speech, and the highest action is non-action. What shallow minds contend over is but trifling."

Bai Gong, unable to bear it, later died in the bathhouse.

*Explication:*
What sound cannot encompass, the mind cannot grasp. If it can be spoken, it can be known. I, possessing knowledge, can speak; they, hearing the words, can understand. Once form and voice manifest—even if like the merging of the Zi and Meng rivers, difficult to distinguish—Yi Ya could discern them by taste. Thus, when Bai Gong first inquired about subtle discourse, Confucius, already knowing his intent, did not respond. Such is the impossibility of concealing words.

Yet one who speaks without intent speaks as if not speaking; one who speaks with intent, though seeking subtlety, only amplifies it. Thus, speech is not inherently unsuited to subtlety—seeking subtlety itself precludes it. Where there are words, there is action; where there is action, there is contention. I bring resentment; they return harm—like those who contend for fish or chase beasts, drenched and running, compelled by circumstance.

But one who knows the nature of words does not speak with words. Without words, there is no action. Compared to the contentions of the shallow-minded, it is as trivial as the clash of insects. Is this not trifling?

Though Bai Gong heard Confucius’ words, he ultimately died in the bathhouse—was it not because, bound by his father’s enmity, he had no choice?

---

Zhao Xiangzi sent Xin Gan Muzi to attack the Di, defeating them and capturing the cities of Zuoren and Zhongren. A messenger was dispatched to report the victory. Xiangzi was eating but wore a troubled expression. His attendants said, "Two cities captured in a single day—this is cause for joy. Why does my lord appear troubled?"

Xiangzi said, "The greatness of rivers and seas lasts no more than three days; violent winds and torrential rains do not endure past morning; the midday sun does not linger. Now, the virtue and conduct of the Zhao house have not been cultivated over time. Two cities captured in a day—will ruin not soon befall us?"

Confucius heard of this and said, "The Zhao house will flourish. For worry is the means to flourishing, and joy is the path to ruin. Victory is not the difficult part—maintaining it is. A worthy ruler sustains victory thus, and his blessings extend to later generations. Qi, Chu, Wu, and Yue all once triumphed, yet ultimately perished, for they did not grasp how to maintain victory. Only a ruler of the Dao can sustain it."

Confucius’ strength was such that he could force open the gate of a state’s capital, yet he refused to be known for his power. Mozi devised defensive strategies, and Gongshu Ban conceded, yet he refused to be known for his military skill. Thus, one who excels at maintaining victory turns strength into weakness.

*Explication:*
Attacking the weak with one’s strength, opposing the few with one’s multitude, assaulting the guilty while oneself blameless—this is the way of absolute security. Yet fullness must decline, completion must decay, and prosperity must wane—this is the inevitable principle. Thus, victory is not difficult; maintaining it is.

Speaking of victory, Qi, Chu, Wu, and Yue all once triumphed. Speaking of maintenance, only a ruler like the startled teal can be deemed capable. Why? Victory relies on strength; maintenance relies on weakness. When armies clash, though strength prevails, it is not strength that makes one strong—rather, the most yielding Dao directs one’s strength. Victorious at the enemy’s gates, one must preserve with weakness. It is not that weakness preserves strength, but that the most yielding Dao, accumulated, becomes firm.

Thus, *Laozi* says: "In life, humans are supple and weak; in death, they are rigid and strong. In life, plants are supple and weak; in death, they are withered and brittle. Thus, those who rely on military strength do not prevail; trees that are strong are felled." Only one who embodies the Dao comprehends the principles of heaven and earth’s waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness. Though immensely strong, he refuses to be known for his power; though capable of subduing others, he refuses to be known for his military skill. He takes softness, weakness, humility, and lowliness as his outward form, always abiding in a place of non-contention. Thus, none in the world can contend with him—not only can none contend, but all will gladly support him without resentment. What greater way is there to maintain victory? This is how Confucius knew the Zhao house would flourish.

---

In Song, there was a man devoted to practicing benevolence and righteousness, unwavering for three generations. His family’s black ox unexpectedly bore a white calf, so he asked Confucius about it. Confucius said, "This is an auspicious omen; offer it to the Supreme Emperor."

A year later, the man’s father unexpectedly went blind, and the ox again bore a white calf. The father again instructed his son to ask Confucius. The son said, "Last time, you asked and lost your sight—why ask again?"

The father said, "The words of a sage first oppose, then align. The matter is not yet resolved—let us ask again."

The son again asked Confucius. Confucius said, "It is auspicious," and again instructed him to perform the sacrifice. The son returned and relayed the message. The father said, "Follow Confucius’ words."

A year later, the son unexpectedly went blind. Later, when Chu attacked Song and besieged its capital, the people exchanged their children to eat and broke their bones for fuel. The able-bodied climbed the walls to fight, and more than half died. This man, however, because both father and son were afflicted, was exempted.

When the siege was lifted, both father and son’s afflictions were restored. The commentary states: A black ox bearing a white calf is the natural order of things—only when it occurs without cause does Heaven perhaps intend some portent. The ox is the sacrificial beast for Heaven and Earth, so its birth without cause is an auspicious omen for great matters. The ox is a creature of water, black is the color of water, and white is the color of metal; metal generates water, so black giving birth to white reverses its origin, making its generation inexhaustible. This is how those who practice benevolence and righteousness without cease rely on such signs to escape death in Chu’s siege. Moreover, black is obscure while white is manifest—this too resembles the pattern of first losing sight and then regaining it. The words of sages are all explanations that exhaust principle and fulfill nature, thus they never fail to align. When they appear to oppose, it is not that they do not align, but that the matter has not yet reached its conclusion. How can later readers of the sages’ works, failing to grasp their intent, hastily apply them to affairs and seek immediate results?

Consider this case: a household’s black ox bore a white calf twice without cause, and Confucius deemed it auspicious, instructing them to offer sacrifice to the Supreme Emperor. Such extraordinary omens should have brought extraordinary blessings. Yet it only spared father and son from the duty of scaling the walls—from a Daoist perspective, the distinction between fortune and misfortune differs in minor wisdom, so its arrival also varies in weight according to the times. During the decline of Zhou, the six states’ sole concern was military power. Even Confucius, born in that era, nearly could not escape the hardships of Chen and Cai. Thus, for the people of Song to avoid death in battle, unless they had practiced benevolence and righteousness without cease for three generations and thereby moved Heaven and Earth, they could not easily have obtained such a fate.

In Song there was a man called Orchid Child who offered his skills to Duke Yuan of Song. The duke summoned him to perform. He bound two rods twice his height to his shins, ran swiftly while keeping pace, and juggled seven swords, tossing them in succession so that five were always in the air. The lord was greatly astonished and immediately bestowed gold and silk. Another Orchid Child, skilled in swallow-play, heard of this and also sought an audience with the duke. The duke flew into a rage: "Formerly, a man with extraordinary skills sought my favor, but his skills were of no use—it just so happened I was in high spirits, so I rewarded him with gold and silk. He must have heard of this and come seeking a similar reward. Seize and execute him!" A month later, he was released.

A commentary states: The marvel of a skill may not differ, yet rewards and punishments vary—external things cannot be relied upon. Thus, the noble person focuses only on what lies within themselves.

Duke Mu of Qin said to Bo Le, "You are advanced in years. Do you have among your kin someone who can be sent to seek horses?"

Bo Le replied, "A fine horse can be discerned by its form, appearance, sinews, and bones. But the horse of all under heaven—vanishing as if extinguished, lost as if gone, like this one—leaves no dust in its wake, outstripping all bounds. My sons are all of lesser talent; they may be taught to recognize fine horses, but not the horse of all under heaven. I have one with whom I once shared a roof while gathering firewood and vegetables—Jiu Fang Gao. In judging horses, he is no inferior to me. I beg you to meet him."

Duke Mu received him and sent him to seek a horse. After three months, he returned and reported, "I have found one. It is in Shaqiu."

The duke asked, "What kind of horse is it?"

He replied, "A yellow mare."

The duke sent someone to fetch it, but it was a black stallion. Displeased, Duke Mu summoned Bo Le and said, "You have failed. The one you sent to seek a horse cannot even distinguish color or sex—how could he know a horse?"

Bo Le sighed deeply and said, "Has it truly come to this? This is why he surpasses me ten thousandfold without measure. In Jiu Fang Gao’s observation, it is the workings of heaven. He grasps the essence and forgets the coarse; he sees within and disregards the outer. He perceives what is to be seen and does not see what is not; he gazes upon what is to be gazed upon and overlooks what is not. In his judgment of horses, there is something more precious than the horse itself."

When the horse arrived, it was indeed the horse of all under heaven.

A commentary states: Judging horses is but the least of skills. Even Bo Le, with all his mastery, could not teach it to his sons, nor could his sons receive it from him. Why? What can be transmitted does not go beyond rules, compasses, and measuring cords; what can be judged does not go beyond form, appearance, sinews, and bones. As for the horse of all under heaven—it vanishes as if concealed between existence and nonexistence, lost as if dwelling in the indistinct and vague. It transcends all bounds, leaving no trace. Unless one disregards its outward traits, observes its heavenly workings, attains it inwardly in the heart, and matches it outwardly in the horse’s body, one may seek the state’s fine horses but never obtain the horse of all under heaven.

Thus, when Jiu Fang Gao sought the horse, though it was a black stallion, he called it a yellow mare. Did Jiu Fang Gao truly not know its color or sex? He took it as a mare because what he valued was its masculine power; what he held to was its feminine essence. He called it yellow because what was black was its surface; what he preserved was its inner truth. This is what is meant by seeing what others do not see, gazing upon what others do not gaze upon—thus, there is something more precious than the horse itself. For this reason, Duke Mu, upon hearing his words, thought him a failure. Yet Bo Le, though he recognized this, knew he surpassed him ten thousandfold without measure. When the horse arrived, it was indeed the horse of all under heaven.

For in Jiu Fang Gao’s judgment of horses, he silently grasped the marvel of the unspoken. In Bo Le’s understanding of Jiu Fang Gao, he was in perfect accord without a word. Without Jiu Fang Gao, the horse could not have been found; without Bo Le, Jiu Fang Gao could not have been known. The horse of Shaqiu had never been absent, yet Bo Le and Jiu Fang Gao do not appear in every generation. Even if the horse of all under heaven exists, it may not be recognized as a fine steed, while mediocre and sluggish horses may still be yoked together beneath the carriage. Alas! The horse of all under heaven is so difficult to know—how much more so the scholars of the world who wander beyond the realm of the knowable?

Xu Wugui met Marquis Wu of Wei and spoke to him of judging horses. This is why the marquis was greatly pleased and laughed.

King Zhuang of Chu asked Zhan He, "How should one govern the state?"

Zhan He replied, "I am enlightened in governing the self but not in governing the state."

King Zhuang said, "I have been entrusted with the ancestral temples and the altars of the state. I wish to learn how to preserve them."

Zhan He replied, "I have never heard of a self well-governed yet a state in chaos, nor have I heard of a self in chaos yet a state well-governed. Thus, the root lies in the self. I dare not answer with what is but a branch."

The king said, "Well said."

A commentary states: The Yellow Emperor asked Guangchengzi, "I wish to gather the essence of heaven and earth to aid the five grains and nourish the people. I also wish to regulate yin and yang to bring fulfillment to all living things."
