Guangchengzi said, "With a heart as restless as a meddler’s, how could you be worthy of speaking of the supreme Dao?" Later, when the Yellow Emperor returned to entreat him, asking how to govern the self to attain longevity, Guangchengzi approved of his question and spoke to him of the supreme Dao.

For when the root is secured, the branches need not be feared. Thus, when King Zhuang asked Zhan He about governing the state, Zhan He answered with governing the self.

The elder of Huqiu said to Sunshu Ao, "There are three things that incur resentment—do you know them?"

Sunshu Ao asked, "What are they?"

He replied, "Those with high rank are envied; those with great office are resented by their lord; those with generous emoluments are beset by grievances."

Sunshu Ao said, "The higher my rank, the more I humble my aspirations; the greater my office, the smaller I make my heart; the more generous my emoluments, the more I extend my benefactions."

Can this avert the three resentments? The explanation is as follows:

When rank is high, others envy it—because it overshadows them. When office is great, the lord resents it—because it encroaches upon him. When emoluments are generous, grievances follow—because it implies hoarding.

Yet if high rank is met with humble aspirations, none perceive its loftiness; if great office is held with a small heart, the lord does not嫌 its magnitude; if generous emoluments are matched with broad benefactions, none begrudge their abundance. This is the Way to distance oneself from resentment.

When Sunshu Ao fell ill and was near death, he admonished his son: "The king has repeatedly offered to enfeoff me, but I have refused. After my death, the king will surely enfeoff you—you must not accept fertile land. Between Chu and Yue lies a place called Qinqiu; though its terrain is unfavorable, its name is most inauspicious. The people of Chu revere ghosts, and the people of Yue are superstitious—only this land can be held in perpetuity."

After Sunshu Ao’s death, the king indeed offered his son fine territory. The son declined and requested Qinqiu instead, and it was granted to him.

To this day, his holding has not been lost.

Explanation: To dwell where the multitude shuns is to avoid contention; only by avoiding contention can one escape blame, and thus indeed endure long. Now, Sunshu Ao—his rank and emoluments were sufficient to honor his person and keep resentment at bay, his benefits and blessings extended to his descendants, enabling them to hold their land in perpetuity. Was this by some other means? No, it was simply that amid wealth and nobility, he discerned the principle of waxing and waning, fullness and emptiness, ensuring that loftiness did not reach peril, that plenitude did not overflow. This is what is meant by "though rich and noble, one does not use abundance to harm oneself."

As for Yan He, who truly despised wealth and nobility, his case surpasses even this.

Niu Que was a great Confucian of Shangdang. Descending to Handan, he encountered bandits in the thickets of Ou Sha, who took all his clothing and carriage. Niu walked away, gazing back with a cheerful countenance, free of sorrow or regret. The bandits pursued and asked the reason. He replied: "The noble man does not harm what he nurtures by clinging to what nurtures him." The bandits exclaimed: "Ah! How worthy he is!" Then they conferred among themselves: "With his worthiness, if he goes to see the Lord of Zhao and speaks of us, he will surely bring trouble upon us. Better to kill him." So they pursued and slew him.

When the people of Yan heard of this, they gathered their clans and warned one another: "If you encounter bandits, do not be like Niu Que of Shangdang." All took heed. Soon after, Niu Que’s younger brother traveled to Qin. At the pass, he too encountered bandits. Remembering his brother’s warning, he struggled fiercely with the bandits. When this proved futile, he pursued them, pleading humbly for his belongings. The bandits grew enraged: "We spared your life—how generous!—yet you pursue us without end, leaving tracks for all to see. Once a bandit, where is the room for mercy?" They killed him and, in the process, slew four or five of his companions as well.

The Explanation states: Niu Que, though truly capable of non-attachment, was slain for it; the younger brother of the Yan man, recalling his brother’s warning and feigning reluctance, brought disaster upon himself. Though one may say that shifts between safety and peril arise from unseen causes, in essence, the actions of these two men were excessive and thus invited such outcomes.

Why so? When Niu Que encountered the bandits, his joyful demeanor, free of distress or reluctance, would have sufficed. Yet when they pursued him to question his motives, he declared, *"The noble man does not harm what he nurtures through what he nurtures,"* thereby revealing his own traces. The one who recalled his brother’s warning fought fiercely but failed—he might have stopped there. Instead, he pursued them with humble pleas for his belongings, thus revealing *their* traces. Once both their traces were exposed, the bandits feared retaliation, which is why these two men were killed.

Yet when *his* traces were evident, the harm was distant, and so only Niu Que perished. When *their* traces were evident, the harm was near, and thus four or five of his companions were slain as well.

The Yu clan was a wealthy family of Liang. Their household overflowed with abundance, their wealth and silk beyond measure, their treasures beyond estimation. They ascended a high tower overlooking the main road, where they set up music and wine, gambling on the upper floor. As their retinue of swordsmen passed below, one of the gamblers loosed an arrow, striking the bright jade target squarely. Turning to grasp a pair of fish-shaped tokens in laughter, a kite suddenly dropped a rotten rat, hitting the mark. The swordsmen conferred among themselves: *"The Yu clan has long enjoyed wealth and pleasure, yet they have always held others in contempt. Though we have not provoked them, they now insult us with a rotten rat. If we do not avenge this, we shall have no standing in the world."* They vowed as one: *"Let us unite in purpose, lead our followers, and annihilate their household to set an example."* All agreed.

On the appointed night, they gathered their forces and weapons, launching an assault on the Yu clan and utterly destroying their household.

The Explanation states: The Way of excess is what Heaven diminishes, Earth transforms, spirits harm, and humanity despises. Only the sage perceives the shifts in all things, thus avoiding excess to preserve life, eschewing extravagance to curb indulgence, and rejecting arrogance to prevent ruin, dwelling only within proper bounds. The Yu clan’s wealth had already accumulated resentment through their lack of benevolence, yet they further ascended a high tower to provoke the gaze of spirits, stood over the main road to offend the disdain of the masses, set up music and wine, and indulged in gambling, giving free rein to their dissipation. How could this endure? Thus, though they harbored ambitions to dominate others, their downfall arose from a kite dropping a rotten rat.

Did the kite, having seized the rotten rat, deliberately cast it down? The travelers on the road were many—why did it strike precisely upon the swordsmen? Was it not that their arrogance and extravagance were despised by gods and men alike, and some unseen force caused it to fall thus, inciting the swordsmen’s wrath?

In the East there was a man named Yuan Jingmu. While traveling, he collapsed from hunger on the road. A bandit from Hufu named Qiu saw him and offered a gourd of food to feed him. After Yuan Jingmu ate three mouthfuls, his vision returned. He asked, *"Who are you?"* The other replied, *"I am Qiu of Hufu."* Yuan Jingmu exclaimed, *"Ah! Are you not a bandit? Why do you feed me? I will not eat a bandit’s food!"* Pressing his hands to the ground, he retched violently but could not expel it. Choking, he collapsed and died.

The man from Hufu was indeed a bandit, but the food was not stolen. To reject it because the giver was a bandit, deeming the food itself tainted, was to lose something precious.

The Explanation states: When he could not yet see, he accepted the food; once he had eaten and regained his sight, his deluded heart led him to perceive the food as stolen. He died for it without hesitation. From this, we know that the knowledge of heart and eye often brings little benefit to the self but much harm.

He was called Yuan Jingmu because his eyes could distinguish clearly. *The Zhuangzi* says: *"No thief is greater than virtue when the heart has eyes."* *The Laozi* says: *"The sage acts for the belly, not for the eyes."* Could this be the reason?

Zhu Lishu served Duke Ao of Ju. Believing the duke did not appreciate him, he left and dwelt by the sea.

In summer, he ate water caltrops and water chestnuts; in winter, he ate acorns and chestnuts. When Duke Ao of Ju faced peril, Zhu Lishu parted from his friend and went to die for him. His friend said: "You left because you believed the duke did not appreciate you. Now you go to die for him—this makes no distinction between appreciation and lack of it." Zhu Lishu replied: "Not so. I left because I believed he did not appreciate me. Now, by dying for him, I prove he truly did not. I will die to shame those rulers of later generations who fail to recognize their ministers. For if one is appreciated, one dies for them; if not, one does not—this is simply walking the straight path." Zhu Lishu may be called one who acted urgently to fulfill his resolve.

The Explanation states: Zhu Lishu was one who, possessing knowledge, forgot his own body. Human knowledge arises from deluded perception. Taking the body as the self, one values and cherishes it, seeking not to die. Thus, one worries over things that may harm the self, desiring to know all things without exception. Yet once knowledge exists, it inwardly deludes the mind, and resentment accumulates within, leading one to forget the body without realizing it. At first, one seeks knowledge to cherish the body, but in the end, knowledge turns to harm it. Thus, it is clear that knowing is no better than not knowing.

For the noble person, death may be as light as a goose feather or as weighty as Mount Tai. To sacrifice oneself to manifest virtue is to treat death as light; to resent and forget oneself—what benefit does it bring? Therefore, what the sage values lies in knowing what one does not know, and what the sage deems harmful lies in not knowing what one knows. Yang Zhu said: "Benefit given brings true reward; virtue extended invites harm." What arises within finds response without—this is the law. Thus, the worthy are cautious in what they put forth.

The Explanation states: Observing Yuan Jingmu and Zhu Lishu, one sees that resentment extended invites harm—how could the worthy act thus?

*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Virtue, Scroll Nineteen* concludes.
*Explication of the Perfect Dao and Virtue, Scroll Twenty* begins.

A student of the Inner College of Hangzhou Prefecture in Song, your subject Jiang Yu respectfully presents *Explaining the Symbols, Part Three*:

Yangzi’s neighbor lost a sheep. Having already led his own kinsmen in pursuit, he further requested Yangzi’s servants to join the chase. Yangzi said: “Hmm! To lose one sheep—why so many pursuers?” The neighbor replied: “There are many branching paths.” Upon their return, Yangzi asked: “Did you recover the sheep?” The neighbor said: “It is lost.” Yangzi asked: “How was it lost?” The neighbor said: “Among the branching paths there were further branches. I did not know which way to go, so I turned back.”

Yangzi’s countenance fell into sorrow, and for a long while he did not speak; for the rest of the day, he did not smile. His disciples found this strange and asked: “The sheep is a lowly beast, and moreover not yours—why has this so diminished your words and laughter?” Yangzi did not answer, and his disciples could not fathom his intent.

Disciple Meng Sunyang went out and told this to Xin Duzi. Another day, Xin Duzi entered together with Meng Sunyang and asked: “Formerly there were three brothers who traveled between Qi and Lu, studying under the same master and learning the Way of benevolence and righteousness before returning home. Their father asked: ‘What is the Way of benevolence and righteousness like?’ The eldest said: ‘Benevolence and righteousness make me cherish my life and only then seek fame.’ The second said: ‘Benevolence and righteousness make me sacrifice my life to achieve fame.’ The youngest said: ‘Benevolence and righteousness make me preserve both life and fame.’ These three methods are opposed, yet all derive from the Ru—who is right and who is wrong?”

Yangzi said: “There was a man who lived by the river. Skilled in water, bold in swimming, he plied a boat to ferry passengers and thus provided for his hundred dependents. Those who packed provisions to study from him formed a multitude, yet nearly half drowned. They originally studied swimming, not drowning—yet the benefit and harm turned out thus. If you were to judge, who is right and who is wrong?”

Xin Duzi silently departed. Meng Sunyang reproached him: “Why did you ask such a roundabout question, and why did the Master answer so obliquely? My confusion has only deepened.” Xin Duzi said: “The Great Way loses the sheep through many branching paths; the learner loses life through many methods. Though the root of learning is not different, though the root is not divided, the branches diverge thus. Only by returning to unity and reverting to the One can loss and gain be determined. You have long stood at the Master’s gate, studied the Master’s Way, yet do not grasp the Master’s circumstances—how lamentable!”

The Explanation states: The *Book of Changes* says: “What does the world ponder, what does it deliberate? The world converges on the same destination by different paths, attains unity through a hundred deliberations.” What does the world ponder, what does it deliberate? It ponders so as to have a destination. If different paths lead to the same destination, why deliberate to attain it? If a hundred deliberations yield one governance, why deliberate at all? It is like those who set out for Yan driving north, or those bound for Yue taking the southern road. Once the fixed positions of Yan and Yue are clearly discerned, and one faithfully follows the Way without cease, even with many branching paths, one will not be confused. Only because learners do not clearly see the Way, do not steadfastly follow the Way, seek shallow achievements and immediate results, abandon the level thoroughfare of the Great Way and favor shortcuts, rely on deluded views to create distinctions, and become lost in the convergence of unity and the divergence of a hundred deliberations—like the man who lost the sheep, they ultimately do not know where they are going and turn back. This is why Yangzi was troubled by the lost sheep and diminished in words and laughter.

The hour of *chen* belongs to the sheep, and *chen* is the correct position of earth. Its association is with the spleen, which is the palace of intent. To lose the sheep is to harm the keeper of intent. Thus Yangzi was moved by this. Zhuangzi’s saying that storing grain and herding sheep are equal to losing the sheep also carries this meaning. Xin Duzi was one who could preserve the mind and guard intent, so he knew to ask about those who studied benevolence and righteousness under the same master yet followed different methods. Yangzi, then, answered with the parable of those who studied swimming yet half drowned. Meng Sunyang, though he stood at the forefront of things, rushed into action and became fragmented from the Way—unlike what Zhuangzi called “Mengzi’s reversal.” Thus he deemed Xin Duzi’s question roundabout and Yangzi’s answer oblique.

The analogy of learning to swim: those who study the Way aim to transcend life and death, to ferry the people through the realm of no difficulty. Yet learners lose their lives through many methods—are they not like those who study swimming yet drown? Nevertheless, those who drown are not at fault for the water, nor is the loss of life the fault of the Way. If one takes ferrying for profit, one cannot avoid drowning; if one seeks the Way through many methods, one cannot avoid losing life. If one follows the Way of water without selfishness, how can there be drowning? If one follows the level thoroughfare of the Way and does not favor shortcuts, how can there be loss? This is Yangzi’s aspiration.

Yang Zhu’s younger brother was named Bu. He went out wearing plain white clothes. When it rained, he removed the white clothes and put on black ones before returning. His dog, not recognizing him, ran out barking. Yang Bu grew angry and was about to strike it. Yang Zhu said: “Do not strike it. You are just the same. If your dog had gone out white and returned black, how could it not be startled?”

The Explanation states: When the color of clothes changes from white to black and the dog does not recognize it, the man is ready to strike the dog. When external things change and one does not perceive it, one’s joy and anger vary accordingly—whom should one blame? Only those who follow great changes without being submerged can speak of this.

Yang Zhu said: “To practice goodness not for the sake of fame, yet fame follows; fame does not seek profit, yet profit comes; profit does not seek conflict, yet conflict arrives. Therefore, the noble person must be cautious in practicing goodness.”

The Explanation states: If one begins with practicing goodness and ends in conflict, is this so-called goodness truly good? Or is it not good? Therefore, the noble person must be cautious in practicing goodness. To be cautious in practicing goodness does not mean that goodness should not be practiced, nor does it mean that one should merely avoid practicing goodness for the sake of fame. If one does not seek fame, then no one in the world can contend with one.

Formerly, there was a man who claimed to know the Way of not dying. The Lord of Yan sent someone to learn it. Before success was achieved, the teacher died.

The Lord of Yan was furious and intended to punish his envoy. A favored courtier admonished him, saying: “Of all human concerns, none is more urgent than death; of all things one values, none surpasses life. That man lost his own life—how could he have enabled the lord to avoid death?” Thus, the envoy was spared.

There was a man of Qi who also wished to learn this Way. When he heard that the teacher had died, he struck his chest in regret. Fuzi, upon hearing this, laughed and said: “He desired to learn the Way of not dying, yet when the man himself died, he still regretted it—this is to not understand the very purpose of learning.”

Huzi said: “Fuzi’s words are mistaken. There are those who possess arts they cannot practice, and there are those who can practice arts they do not possess. In Wei, there was a man skilled in calculation who, on his deathbed, explained the method to his son. The son memorized his father’s words but could not put them into practice. Another man asked him, and he recounted what his father had said. The questioner applied the words and practiced the art, achieving results no different from the father’s. If this is so, how could the dead be incapable of transmitting the art of life?”

The Explanation states: *Zhuangzi* says: “If the Way could be offered as a gift, no one would fail to present it to their lord.” Thus, the Lord of Yan could not compel the man who claimed to know the Way of not dying to offer his art. It also says: “If the Way could be given to others, no one would fail to pass it on to their children and grandchildren.” Thus, the skilled calculator of Wei explained it step by step to his son, yet his son memorized the words but could not practice it.

However, the Way can be transmitted but cannot be received. Precisely because it can be transmitted, those who can practice it cannot lack its method; precisely because it cannot be received, some may possess the method yet fail to practice it. The writings of Liezi belong to this category. Since the Way cannot be directly taught or given to others, he had no choice but to embed it in a book, intending that those who awaken may apply its words, practice its method, and thereby align with the Way without discrepancy.

The people of Handan presented doves to Jianzi on the first morning of the new year. Jianzi was greatly pleased and richly rewarded them. A guest asked the reason.

Jianzi said, "Releasing living creatures on the first morning of the new year demonstrates benevolence."

The guest replied, "The people know you wish to release them, so they compete to capture them, and many die. If you truly wish to preserve their lives, it would be better to forbid the people from capturing them. To capture and then release them—your benevolence cannot compensate for the harm."

Jianzi said, "Indeed."

Explanation: The *Yinfu Jing* states: "Benevolence arises from harm, and harm arises from benevolence. To treat kindness as empty is precisely what harms creatures." Heaven and Earth, in their relation to the myriad things, and the sage, in his relation to the hundred clans, assist them through natural spontaneity, without any mind of love or benefit. They regard all things as straw dogs—this is the Way.

The Tian family of Qi held an ancestral sacrifice in their courtyard, hosting a thousand retainers. Amid the feast, someone presented fish and wild geese. Tian gazed at them and sighed, saying, "How generous Heaven is to the people! It cultivates the five grains and gives life to fish and birds for their use."

The assembled guests echoed his words in unison. But the son of the Bao family, a boy of twelve seated among them, stepped forward and said, "It is not as you say. Heaven and Earth, the myriad things, and we humans all arise together as the same kind. There is no hierarchy of noble and base among kinds—only differences in size, intelligence, and strength that lead to mutual domination and predation. Things are not born for one another. Humans take what is edible and eat it—how could Heaven have originally created them for human use? Moreover, mosquitoes and gnats bite our skin, and tigers and wolves devour flesh—did Heaven originally create humans for mosquitoes and gnats, or flesh for tigers and wolves?"

Explanation: *Zhuangzi* says, "The myriad things and I are one." Thus, the intelligence of birds and beasts naturally aligns with that of humans; only their forms make them seem distant. How could they be born for one another? The divine sages of high antiquity, in their dealings with different kinds, gathered and instructed them just as they did with people, for their minds and intelligence were not far removed from ours. Later generations began to dominate one another through differences in size, intelligence, and strength, preying upon each other. If we claim that creatures are born for human consumption, then mosquitoes and gnats that bite our skin must also be born for the sake of other creatures. Tian’s thousand retainers were not as perceptive as the young son of the Bao family, who had long possessed understanding. This shows that the location of the Way is not restricted by age or youth. The boy of the Bao family may be said to have encountered a thousand men’s worth of insight.

In Qi, there was a poor man who often begged in the city. The city’s residents grew weary of his frequent appeals, and none would give him anything. He then went to the stables of the Tian family, worked as an assistant to the horse doctor, and borrowed food. Someone in the city mocked him, saying, "Working for the horse doctor for food—is that not shameful?"

The beggar replied, "Of all shames in the world, none surpasses begging. If begging is not shameful, how could working for the horse doctor be?"

Explanation: Though laboring for the horse doctor is a lesser shame than begging for food, the poor man of Qi did not initially abandon his resolve because of the shame of begging. He did so only because no one would give him anything. Precisely because he was so content with poverty, even mockery about honor and disgrace could not sway his heart. How much less would one who truly understands the distinction between honor and disgrace be moved by gain and loss, advantage and harm?

A man of Song was traveling on the road when he found a lost contract. He took it home and hid it, secretly counting its notches. He told his neighbor, "My wealth is assured."

Explanation: A lost contract cannot ensure wealth, just as stale words cannot convey the Way. This is like Duke Huan reading a book, prompting the wheelwright to lay down his hammer and chisel and ask him, "What you are reading is but the dregs of the ancients."

A man had a withered parasol tree. His neighbor, an old man, said that withered parasol trees were inauspicious, so the man hastily cut it down. The old neighbor then asked to take it as firewood. The man became displeased and said, "The old neighbor only wanted firewood and thus urged me to cut it down. To live next to such a treacherous person—how can this be?"

Explanation: The old neighbor’s remark about the inauspiciousness of the withered parasol tree was not necessarily made with firewood in mind. But by asking to take it as firewood, he trod a suspicious path. The man then deemed him treacherous. Yet who can say whether it was truly the neighbor who was treacherous, or whether the man himself was the source of treachery? In the end, treachery or its absence lies within oneself.

A man lost his axe and suspected his neighbor’s son. He observed the boy’s gait—it was that of an axe thief; his complexion—it was that of an axe thief; his speech—it was that of an axe thief. Every movement and mannerism was that of an axe thief. Later, he dug in his grain cellar and found his axe. The next time he saw his neighbor’s son, none of his movements or mannerisms resembled those of an axe thief.

Explanation: The neighbor’s son had always been the same, but the man who lost the axe harbored suspicion within. Thus, he saw every action as that of an axe thief. Once his suspicion was dispelled, he saw nothing of the sort. From this, we see that the distinctions among the myriad things are all created by our own minds. If scholars can make their intentions sincere, like the man who lost the axe, then they will be in the Way wherever they go. How could external things intrude?

Bai Gongsheng was plotting rebellion. After leaving court, he stood motionless, leaning on his staff upside down, its metal tip piercing his chin. Blood flowed to the ground, yet he was unaware. Someone from Zheng heard of this and said, "If he could forget his chin, what could he not forget?" When the mind is fixated, one may stumble over a stump or pit, or knock one’s head against a post without realizing it.

Explanation: *Zhuangzi* says, "The perfected person has no self." Those who do not know the Way cling to the notion of a self. They look upon their own bodies—all the limbs, the nine orifices, the six viscera—all present within. How could they not have a self? Why not observe Bai Gongsheng? When perverse thoughts fester within, one may even forget one’s chin—what could one not forget? How much more so when one inwardly attains the Way? Then the body merges with the great unity, and the absence of self becomes self-evident.

Once, there was a man of Qi who coveted gold. At dawn, he dressed in his finest robes and went to the market. Arriving at the gold-seller’s stall, he seized the gold and departed. The authorities captured him and asked, “With so many people present, how could you take another’s gold?” He replied, “When I took the gold, I saw no one—only the gold.”

Explanation: To see an object and seize it like gold is to have no Dao beyond things. To see the Dao and seize it like gold is to have no things beyond the Dao. The *Liezi* concludes with this passage, for the eight chapters all use things to illuminate the Dao. Whether later readers awaken to it or not depends entirely on the interplay of desire and non-desire, perception and non-perception.

The *Shuofu* chapter explains: The essence of the Dao admits no single thing, transcending all speech. Its function excludes no thing, residing in form and number. Where there is form, there is name; where there is number, there is order. The constancy of waxing and waning is as unchanging as a tally’s reliability; the verifiability of beginning and end is as precise as a tally’s alignment. Nothing is more numinous than Heaven, whose Dao accords with yin and yang; nothing is more abundant than Earth, whose principle accords with softness and hardness. Nothing is greater than the emperors and kings, whose virtue accords with benevolence and righteousness. Like a shadow to its form, crookedness and straightness follow without alteration; like an echo to its sound, high and low depend without deviation. The virtue of Shennong and the Flame Emperor embodied this alone; the books of Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou recorded this alone; the words of legalists and worthies elucidated this alone. Thus, the teachings of Liezi, having exhausted the ultimate of the supreme Dao from the *Yang Zhu* chapter onward, necessarily conclude with the *Shuofu*. For the ultimate of the supreme Dao is not merely obscure and silent—it is through this that one responds and penetrates the world’s affairs, hence the matters of the *Shuofu*.

The meaning of the *Shuofu* lies in this: for oneself, there are measures; for others, there are standards. In navigating the world, one must esteem the Dao; in governing, one must recognize worthies. In responding to affairs, wisdom is key; in securing victory, the Dao is fundamental. Governing the state begins with cultivating the self; averting resentment arises from humility. Benefits bestowed bring true returns; resentments incurred invite harm—thus the worthy are cautious in their actions. Fame does not seek profit, yet profit follows; profit does not seek conflict, yet conflict arises—thus the noble person is cautious in doing good. All its words concern the peace and peril of the world’s ways, the gains and losses of human principles. All its deeds are those that speech can discuss and intention can discern. Therefore, to speak of the Dao without the *Shuofu* is insufficient to constitute the Dao.

Yet Liezi begins with the *Tian Rui* and concludes with the *Shuofu*. The *Tian Rui* is the tally of nature; it speaks of Heaven’s wondrous Dao, which aligns with creation and transformation, proceeding from Heaven to humanity. The *Shuofu* speaks of human affairs manifestly verifying the wondrousness of the supreme Dao, proceeding from humanity to Heaven. The great Dao’s course is like a ring’s ceaseless revolution, without accumulation—its boundaries cannot be exhausted, its path cannot be fathomed. It is ever-generating, ever-transforming, and nothing more.

Examining the *Liezi* from the *Tian Rui* onward, the sequence reveals the essence of the great Dao. The movement of Heaven’s Dao necessarily begins with the *Tian Rui*. The sage’s response to the world is greatest in emperors and kings, thus the *Yellow Emperor* follows the *Tian Rui*, and the *King Mu* follows the *Yellow Emperor*. Of the Three Dynasties’ kings, none surpassed Wen and Wu; by King Mu’s time, the royal enterprise had declined. Not speaking of its flourishing but of its decline clarifies that once the wondrousness of the great Dao unfolds, it does not remain purely constant but inevitably deteriorates. After the governance of the Yellow Emperor, there must follow the decline of King Mu. With the governance of emperors and kings established, were it not for Confucius—the primordial sage—who edited the *Odes*, fixed the *Documents*, struck the *Changes*, and composed the *Classics*, the achievements of emperors and kings would not be known to later ages. Thus, the Dao of emperors and kings culminates in Confucius alone as the great completer of their enterprise. Therefore, the *Confucius* follows the *King Mu*, and from Confucius onward, the transmission of the Dao’s sequence is exhaustive.

Liezi, fearing that later ages might diverge unevenly and fragment the Dao, thus employed the *Tang Wen* to exhaust its variations, preventing things from obstructing the Dao. In essence, the myriad things’ changes all arise from power, and their outcomes are all governed by fate. Hence, the *Li Ming* presents questions and answers, ultimately returning to nature, desiring that people not be controlled by external fate. As for the *Yang Zhu* chapter, it dispenses with the emptiness of the myriad things’ names to grasp the Dao’s ultimate attainment. When the Dao reaches this point, it is complete and cannot be surpassed. Yet the Dao cannot end in nothingness, so the *Shuofu* concludes it.

From this perspective, Liezi’s teachings emerge as the *Tian Rui* and return as the *Shuofu*—thus transmitting the Dao of the Yellow Emperor, Yao, Shun, Yu, Tang, Wen, Wu, Zhou, and Confucius. He is what was called a “vast and authentic perfected person of antiquity.” That he styled himself Master Liezi was because he was a master, like Confucius, distinct from the masters of the various schools. Later scholars who failed to grasp the circumstances of his work deemed him unaligned with the Dao of emperors and kings, lumping his classic with the various masters. This is merely using a tube to view the sky—such a view is narrow, hardly worth mentioning. Scholars should devote their hearts to it.

*The Explication of the Perfect Virtue of the Vacuous and Authentic Classic, Scroll Twenty, concludes.*
